This bestselling book—now in its fourth edition—has become the gold standard for 
sales engineers, who engage on the technical side of the sales and buying process 
and are the people who know how everything works. The book helps you navigate 
a complex and ever-changing technical sales environment and become an effective 
bridge-builder between the business/commercial interests and the technical details 
that support the sale.  
Written by one of the foremost experts in this field, this book presents everything 
you need to improve your skills and increase your value to the sales team. Chapters 
are written in a modular fashion so that you can choose topics most relevant to you 
at the moment—or follow them in order as they build upon each other and give you 
the complete A to Z on your role. Each chapter is short enough so that you can read 
through it in 10-15 minutes and apply the learning the next day. You’ll find actionable 
hints, case studies, and anecdotes illustrating the topics with lessons learned, both 
positive and negative. 
The book helps you to understand the unique role of the sales engineer, from the 
broad picture to the nuances of the job; develop skills needed to become a valuable 
consultant to your team and the customer team; utilize best practices for creating and 
completing winning RFPs; effectively integrate global practices into your day-to-day 
activities; increase your ability think on a more strategic level; and become a trusted 
advisor to executive customers.  
With this completely updated and expanded edition of Mastering Technical Sales 
in hand, you will achieve a better win rate, experience higher customer satisfaction, 
hit revenue targets, and feel greater job satisfaction. Newly added and revised 
chapters guide you through today’s challenges, including the impact of the cloud 
and everything-as-a-service, new sales models (monthly vs annual revenue commits), 
and the virtualization and automation that is now part of the sales engineer’s world.  
This book is a must-have resource for both new and seasoned sales engineers within 
tech software, hardware, mechanical, and civil engineering vendors, along with 
management and leadership in those organizations, and anyone who must present, 
demonstrate, or sell hi-tech items for a living. 
John Care is the Managing Director of Mastering Technical Sales LLC. He holds 
a BSc with honours in Chemical Engineering from Imperial College London and is 
a former contributing member of the MBA Advisory Council for the Fox Business 
School of Temple University Philadelphia.

Acknowledgments
I
t is impossible to mention everyone who contributed to the ideas and con-
cepts within this book. At least twenty-five thousand sales engineers (SEs) 
have passed through the Mastering Technical Sales program since 2014. 
Thank you to those thousands of SEs at companies such as Google, Red -
Hat, Palo Alto Networks, Salesforce.com, Juniper Networks, Veeam, Ruckus, 
Cisco, HP, Microsoft, and Oracle. To everyone who ever stopped me in the 
hallway during a workshop, contacted us through LinkedIn or email, con -
tributed a fantastic idea in class (in whatever language), or said, “you should 
write about . . . , ” I hope you’ll find your contribution somewhere on these 
pages. SEs are pack animals, and we love to share our toys—so thank you so 
much and know that it means the world to me. 
First, thanks to the editorial team at Artech House of David Michelson 
and Natalie McGregor. Next, a shout out to the fantastic facilitation team 
we’ve built up over the years—that’s Chris Daly, Angelo Rodriguez, Art 
Fromm, Jonathan Whiteman, Martin Gregory, JJ Song, Brian Conway, Dr. 
Jim Wagstaff, Rafique Ahmed, Masaru Someya, David Zhu, and CJ Ng. The 
constant stream of cultural modifications and constructive feedback from 
the workshops has made a better and more diverse manuscript. It’s been 
a humbling experience. I must also include Susan Tan and Jaslyn Soh, our 
operations and finance team in Singapore. 
This book is about the personal experience of being a sales engineer. 
Much of the foundation was laid by the SE teams and my peers at Sybase, 
Oracle, Business Objects, Vantive, CA, HP, and Clarify. I know that I learn far 
more from my clients than they ever learn from me, so kudos to Lynn Gates, 
Chris Bridge, Ashley Hall, Dan Hodge, Michael Cooper, Bobby Hyam, Marco 
Guidobaldi, Megan Tomko, Kakali Bhattacharjee, Lys Arya, Romain Vivier,

## Page 18

xvii
 
Introduction
The first step towards getting somewhere is to decide you’re not going to stay 
where you are.
                    —John Pierpoint Morgan
I
n 1849, French writer Jean-Baptiste Alphonse Karr wrote plus ça change, 
plus c’est la même chose —the more things change, the more they stay the 
same. So much has changed since the third edition of this book hit the streets 
in 2014. Everything as a service (EaaS) is ubiquitous, and cloudification—
converting applications, data storage, and compute cycles to take advan -
tage of cloud computing—is now mainstream technology. In addition, most 
technology companies directly measure a large proportion of their revenue 
on a monthly consumption model rather than a quarterly billing cycle. As a 
result, the technical barriers to customer entry and exit are lower.
Yet the underlying professional skills we use to conduct the sales process 
remain remarkably the same. We ask questions; we listen; we answer ques-
tions; we seek out pain and gain, build relationships, and attempt to position 
our portfolio as uniquely qualified to solve the business problems we have 
jointly identified with the customer or partner. There is, thankfully, more of 
an emphasis on the buying process (rather than selling) and certainly a focus 
on the value that a sales engineer (SE) can bring to the customer.
What has changed is the technology that surrounds those profession -
al skills. Although an SE may still conduct product demonstrations, make

---

## Page 19

xviii Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Introduction  xix
technical slide presentations, deliver custom whiteboards, or run a proof 
of concept, there are now multiple tools to make life easier. Companies are 
integrating automation and artificial intelligence (AI) into the SE sphere of 
influence at a breakneck speed. Virtual reality is commonplace; bots are ev-
erywhere; and long before the fifth edition of this book comes out, we’ll 
have AI assistants listening in on live sales calls and offering suggestions on 
how you can improve your presentation, phrasing, and overall pitch. 
These changes mean that I have written six new chapters, eliminated 
five chapters, split two chapters in half, and significantly revised or rewritten 
every other chapter in the book. 
Y ou, the Audience
If you are reading this book, you are undoubtedly responsible for navigating 
through a complex technical sales environment. The techniques I cover are 
remarkably independent of what you are explaining and selling. Whether 
it is hardware, software, or services; a physical data center or virtual cloud; 
networking, security, storage, financial services, medical equipment, or so -
lar-powered devices, it does not matter. What does matter is that you team 
up with your sales counterpart, and your job is to sell your portfolio to the 
end customer. The complexity of the sales environment means that there 
are two sets of buyers: a business buyer and a technology/domain expert 
gatekeeper. So, there are two sets of sellers: one focuses on the business and 
commercial aspects, and one focuses on bridging the technical aspects across 
to the business needs. The sales engineer is that bridge builder.
A quick Google search for sales methodologies yields more than 50 million 
hits. Amazon shows more than 100,000 books within its sales category. So 
why do we need yet another book? Unfortunately, 99.9% of those materials 
focus on old-school selling1 and target the quota-carrying salesperson. This 
book is for the sales engineer—those who engage on the technical side of 
the sales and buying process, who are usually the people who know how 
everything works.2 Some of the more complex transactions I see today in -
volve multiple customer teams of 20–30 people, each specializing in some 
particular aspect of the technology or the business. Many have advanced 
degrees in systems architecture or a similar field. 
1. Think ABC: Always Be Closing.
2. Don Carmichael, one of the thought leaders in the SE world, once proposed a job 
title “the person the customers really want to speak with.”

---

## Page 20

xviii Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Introduction  xix
Using the Book
The book is modular, so that you can pick and choose the topics of most 
relevance to you at the moment. For the brand-new SE, I would suggest 
that you mostly follow the chapter order, as the chapters build upon each 
other and follow a somewhat logical sequence. The experienced SE can 
read through the Contents and select the top three chapters of interest. You 
do not need to run through the book in sequential chapter order. It is not 
a murder mystery novel (spoiler: the salesperson is always the killer any -
way!), so you can skip chapters as you wish. Each chapter is short enough 
so that you can read through it in 10–15 minutes and apply the lessons the 
next day. When reading through the material, you should personalize each 
technique to fit the genuine and authentic you. You will also need to con -
sider country- and culture-specific modifications. Tactics that work well in 
the United States do not always apply to Europe and Asia-Pacific and vice 
versa. 
The chapter format is simple and designed to help leverage the mate -
rial. First, review the list of goals at the start of each chapter. Then use those 
goals (usually three) as a framework to internalize the contents. Next, read 
the chapter, and please highlight any materials that jump out at you and 
make an impact. Whether reading a paper copy or an e-copy, I expect to 
see annotations, highlights, and sticky notes throughout the material. Next, 
read the Skill Building table at the end of the chapter and use that summary 
to put all the concepts together and create a list of action items. The skills 
tables have two sections: the new SE and the more experienced SE or SE 
manager. Finally, create a plan to make it happen.
Many Mastering T echnical Sales customers form a book club. They as -
sign different chapters to pairs of SEs and ask them to create a short 5- to 
10-minute summary presentation of the key learning points from the chap-
ter. That usually leads to an animated discussion and a collection of to-dos 
and modifications to current systems and materials. It is even more fun 
when the salespeople or business development representatives join in, as 
that helps promote a deeper understanding of the different roles. 
In addition to the standard text, you will see two additional features. 
The first are hints. These are specific applications of the techniques that I 
have gathered from students. The second is some more extended case stud-
ies. These are either personal stories or stories from customers that illustrate 
one or more of the points within the chapter text.

---

## Page 21

xx Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
The Bottom Line
Over the past 20 years, more than 40,000 sales engineers and other techni -
cal personnel have participated in our workshops. We have delivered these 
workshops in eight different languages in at least 35 different countries. I 
know that if you embrace the techniques in the book that you will achieve a 
better win rate, experience higher customer satisfaction, hit revenue targets, 
and feel greater job satisfaction. The book intends to help you accomplish 
the following:
1. Make enough money to be satisfied;
2. Improve your skills;
3. Keep your sanity and composure.
Enjoy the read.

## Page 22

1
CHAPTER
1
Chapter Goals What Is a Sales Engineer?
What you choose also chooses you. 
     —Kamand Kojouri
W
hat is a sales engineer (SE), and what do 
they do? That existential question brings 
to mind the cocktail party question. You meet 
someone at a party, and they ask you what 
you do for a living. You proudly reply, “I am a 
sales engineer!” Unfortunately, they look at you 
blankly. That is the fundamental problem with 
our job. If you were to respond that you are a 
nurse, a lawyer, a teacher, an architect, or even 
(gasp) a salesperson, you would receive a nod of 
understanding. But a sales engineer? You now 
need a few extra sentences to explain. You are 
hoping that your newfound friend does not lose 
interest and decide to refresh their drink instead. 
What Is a Sales Engineer?
A sales engineer is a unique blend of four dis -
tinct skill sets or personalities. There is no perfect 
mixture of these personalities. What is essential 
is that the SE possesses each trait and can quickly 
morph between any combination of them. The 
variability of the skill sets shows itself in the mul-
titude of job titles for the profession. The generic 
and historical term is that of a presales engineer, 
Explain who and what is a 
sales engineer.
What are the critical 
attributes of a sales 
engineer?

---

## Page 23

2 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook What Is a Sales Engineer? 3
dating back to a clear division between the activities before the sale and sup-
port/installation activities after the sale. There are now well over 50 different 
job titles, including the most popular sales engineer, solutions engineer, so-
lutions consultant, solutions architect, systems engineer, customer engineer, 
and technical account manager. Add in other variations with cloud, value, 
and success in the titles, and the result is general chaos. The final complica-
tion is that engineers and architects are protected titles in some countries, so it 
is almost impossible to agree on a common title within one company, never 
mind the entire industry. 
Reverting to the four skill sets, they revolve around the technical engi -
neer, the salesperson, the trusted advisor, and the storyteller/explainer. Let 
us examine each of those in a little more detail.
Role #1: The Technical Engineer
There is a misconception that an SE needs a technical background, can pro-
gram or code in multiple languages, and knows every product feature within 
the portfolio. That is simply not the case. Some of the best SEs in the busi -
ness have backgrounds in English literature, philosophy, ancient Roman his-
tory, or journalism, just as many practitioners have degrees in information 
technology, science, engineering, or mathematics. As a hiring manager, I 
have recruited a full range of personnel, ranging from a published Shake -
spearean scholar to a rocket scientist from NASA. What matters is the ability 
to learn just enough about a technical topic to sound knowledgeable. That 
in itself is a significant skill that leads many SEs to think of themselves as a 
mile wide and an inch deep. 
The SE must then translate that knowledge into a language that every -
one else can understand. Most commonly, that is speaking technically with 
the technicians, explaining business outcomes to management, and debat -
ing strategy and options with the executive suite. The engineer component 
of the title means that we pride ourselves on our technical knowledge. Our 
technical credibility becomes one of the keys to building successful and win-
ning relationships with those around us. 
Role #2: The Salesperson 
Make no mistake about it; you have an obligation to sell your company’s 
products and services. Most often, that occurs more indirectly when partner-
ing with a direct quota carrying salesperson. This concerns some individuals 
with a predominately technical background, as they have an aversion to 
sales, which they perceive to be dirty and deceitful. An optimistic view is 
that you are the catalyst to spark the relationship between your customer 
and your company. You are selling yourself, your products, and your com -

---

## Page 24

2 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook What Is a Sales Engineer? 3
pany every time you interact with the customer, just in a softer and kinder 
way. 
For a prospective or new SE, the basic rule is do no harm. As long as you 
understand the sales and buying processes, have a clear demarcation of roles 
and responsibilities, and have open communication with your sales partner, 
you are in good shape. If you can learn technology, you can quickly absorb 
enough about the art and science of selling to become a highly competent 
SE. As you’ll see later in the book, a proportion of SEs lateral across to a 
sales role and do exceptionally well, as many of the skills are transferable. 
Role #3: The Trusted Advisor and Consultant
The well-rounded SE seeks to understand their customer, both personally 
and professionally. The consultant aspect applies to learning about how 
things work, understanding the current and anticipated set of problems, 
and establishing a golden future where problems are solved and business 
outcomes are gained. That is all accomplished with patience, discipline, cu-
riosity, and a tight linkage between the technology features and the busi -
ness drivers. 
It is not enough to provide advice. The customer must trust your ad -
vice. Your customer must listen and then (we hope) accept and apply your 
advice. You are part consultant, teacher, psychologist, and counselor, with a 
sprinkling of financial expertise as seasoning. This is yet another example of 
the multifaceted role that an SE plays during the entire customer lifecycle. 
Paying more attention to this component of the job softens the sales 
aspect of the profile. Most SEs naturally lean into this skillset as it fits the 
desired technical/business mixture and allows for a gravitational close 1 in-
stead of a hard sales close. 
Role #4: The Explainer of All Things (the Storyteller)
The previous three personalities are all severely diminished without practi-
cal communication skills. Understanding the customer’s business and de -
signing innovative solutions is no use if you cannot explain those solutions 
(and subsequently sell them) to others. The consummate SE needs to be 
multilingual and fluently switch between Techie, BizTalk, and ExecSpeak. 
Imagine explaining a network packet sniffer to those three different types of 
customers! Unless you are highly technical and are in a room with a dozen 
other technicians, deep-diving into the intricacies of speeds, feeds, and fea-
tures will not progress the sales opportunity. Your mission is to explain ex-
1. Everything naturally and inevitably comes together to win the deal. Most sales -
people are (rightfully) paid to speed the deal along, which is where the conflict 
occurs.

---

## Page 25

4 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook What Is a Sales Engineer? 5
actly how your portfolio will help your audience make money, save money, 
or mitigate risk. For an individual, that can be as simple as the promise that 
they will go home on time every evening. 
One of the most effective communications methods is using stories. In 
addition to livening up a meeting, a well-constructed story will make you 
memorable, interesting, and compelling. That is a fantastic position for any 
SE in any sales situation. So, the final extension is to be the explainer of all 
things and not be limited to the explainer of all technical things. The feeling 
when your customer says, “I see what you mean,” is priceless. 
All the Other Skills
I like to think of the SE team as the oil that keeps the engine of sales run -
ning. In fact, we are the lubricant that powers the entire company. As an SE, 
you have the opportunity to interact with every other department. You will 
touch sales, support, corporate marketing, product marketing, product man-
agement, engineering, education, professional services, and even finance, 
legal, and HR. The smaller the company, the greater your reach. There is no 
other position within the company outside of the executive suite that casts 
such a vast shadow as the SE team. In reality, you use all of your skills in 
dealing with prospective customers internally every day. 
Romain Vivier, a solutions consulting manager, uses a graphic repre -
sentation (Figure 1.1) during the hiring process to illustrate the modality of 
Figure 1.1  A graphic representation of a sales engineer. (Reprinted with 
permission from Romain Vivier.)

---

## Page 26

4 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook What Is a Sales Engineer? 5
the SE—known within his company as a sales consultant. Couple all these 
attributes with a positive attitude, persistence, curiosity, plus a desire to 
win—and you have a world-class SE. I especially like the graphic as it does 
emphasize the unique skill of listening. Too many engineers want to hear 
themselves talk, yet the practical engineer knows how to ask the right ques-
tion and then shut up and listen. 
At heart, we are fixers and pleasers. We like to make people happy, and 
we want to solve problems. If neither of those outcomes motivates you, 
then sales engineering is probably not the right job for you. Similarly, if you 
require a routine and predictable day, want certainty over your schedule, 
and to know precisely what you will be doing on Thursday, you should look 
elsewhere. 
CASE STUDY: Family Dynamics
As a personal example, when my daughter (an engineer) and I decide to fix 
something or start a project, my wife (teacher/psychologist) and son (lawyer) 
know that the best thing to do is to get out of our way. The engineers always 
prevail through a combination of brute force, stupidity, and the occasional flash 
of brilliance. It is not always pretty or elegant, but the outcome of our efforts 
solves a problem. Of course, the rest of the family will say that we do not always 
listen very well or solve the right problem—but that is a story for another book. 
E E E E
Summary
When I ask hiring managers for a word that describes what they are looking 
for during the interview process, the most popular reply is attitude. Howev-
er, when I ask for a second word describing the perfect SE once onboarded, 
that response is adaptable. That flexibility encapsulates the fundamental role 
of the SE. You must readily switch from the technical to the business aspects 
of your portfolio; you must build relationships with the hundreds or thou -
sands of people you meet each year and do that with charm and grace. You 
must accomplish all that with the understanding that most people do not 
know what you do or how you do it and are clueless about the implications 
of the job title.

Process
If you want to know how to sell more, then you better 
know why customers buy.
              —Steve Ferrante
T
here are hundreds of books written about 
sales processes and methodologies. Each fo -
cuses on bringing a unique structure and disci -
pline to a sales opportunity yet provides minimal 
guidance to the SE. A new SE’s first exposure to 
the sales process usually comes from sitting in the 
back of the room during sales training directed 
at the field sales representatives. That training is 
boring and mostly irrelevant. This chapter briefly 
examines the major phases involved in introduc-
ing a new product, market introduction through 
the salesforce, and later purchase by a customer. 
For each stage in the sales cycle, more detail is 
provided in the next set of chapters in the book 
(Chapters 3–15). The sales process is usually far 
more fluid than presented in this chapter, as ev -
ery company and even every product is different 
enough to allow for minor variations. Use this 
overview to identify those areas where you have 
the least exposure or experience and consider 
starting with those chapters. 
Understand the various 
phases of the sales process.

sales stages.

sales process.

## Page 29

8 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook An Overview of the Sales Process 9
Definition of the Market
Before the salesforce is ever engaged in selling a product, a marketing man-
ager or senior executive (in many small companies, the founder) comes up 
with an idea for a new product or service. In theory, that person has inter -
acted with the potential audience and researched the addressable market to 
define a viable product. Ideally, you have customers seeking a product rather 
than a product in search of customers. In practice, this is not always the case, 
and you may have technology looking for a business problem it can solve. 
The marketing department will have one or more target customer profiles  or 
personas that they hope will define the initial set of customers for the sales -
force to attack. There should be a set of standard use cases mapping common 
problems and challenges facing potential customers at this stage.
The Marketing Campaign
Someone within your company handles demand generation. They build a 
campaign to reach potential purchasers of your product (based on those tar-
get customer profiles) to make them aware of what you have to offer. The 
marketing team will likely use a variety of channels and approaches to reach 
these customers. Some are more targeted than others. I’ll look at five meth-
ods and how they may impact the success and positioning of your product 
in the sales cycle. 
1. Email lists and outbound calling campaigns.  The email list is now the 
number one most effective channel 1 for early-stage lead genera -
tion. A potential customer is sent an email containing some brief 
yet enticing details about your product in the hopes of eliciting 
enough curiosity to drive a follow-on step. That step may be click -
ing through to a website, filling out a form, directly responding 
to an email, or even calling an inbound sales number. Typically, a 
response will drive an outbound call from your sales center back to 
the potentially interested customer and thus begins a sales cycle of 
indefinite length and depth. These leads are usually hard to gener-
ate and qualify, yet they can turn into real pipeline opportunities. 
The potential periodic great lead they may uncover is worth your 
while in spending a little time with the inbound and/or outbound 
telesales team to help train and focus them on proper qualification. 
1. Some 53% of marketers say email is the most effective channel for early-stage 
marketing. (HubSpot Demand Generation Report, 2021. www.hubspot.com/
resources.)

---

## Page 30

8 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook An Overview of the Sales Process 9
2. Customer events. New products are introduced at international, re -
gional, or local customer events. Think of the annual Apple de -
velopers conference as an example. These events may vary from 
a brief breakfast session to a full multiday offsite boondoggle. The 
payback is receiving direct feedback from current and potential 
customers about the utility of your value proposition. Note this 
feedback is subject to sampling error as the views of your existing 
customers may conflict with the desires of potential customers. It 
is a delicate balance. Nevertheless, these events can be an excellent 
opportunity for the SE to meet new and existing customers and 
gather feedback for product marketing and even the development 
team.
3. Partners. A partner (such as a systems integrator, distributor, or 
value-added reseller) may bring your sales team into a deal. These 
types of arrangements have built-in advantages and disadvantag -
es. The positive is that the deal is usually qualified; otherwise, the 
partner is unlikely to engage your help. The negative is that you 
may not have direct access to the end customer (the partner is a 
buffer in the middle), which can pose problems around account 
control. Note that some companies sell exclusively through the 
channel, and this is then one of the major lead sources.
4. Trade shows and seminars. Your company may host a booth (physi -
cal or virtual) at a trade show. The result can be hundreds and 
thousands of poor to moderate quality leads. Marketing depart -
ments may differ with me, but experience shows that less than 
5% of those leads materialize into anything tangible that can enter 
the sales pipeline. Attending a trade show, dependent upon loca -
tion, is either thought of as a rite of passage for an associate SE or 
as a reward for a top-performing SE. Trade shows can be helpful 
for new product introductions or marketing campaigns to experi -
ment with A/B alternate messaging for effectiveness. For the SE, 
it is an opportunity to practice your craft when it comes to brevity 
and on-the-spot adaptability while also affording you the chance 
to conduct some market and competitive research.
5. Online website-based trials. It’s now prevalent for technology vendors 
to provide a free trial of their products. It may be a free limited-use 
version or a 14- to 28-day full trial of an entire product set. The 
incremental financial cost of spinning up a standard configuration 
in the cloud is minimal. The actual cost to the sales organization 
lies in the monitoring and follow-up required once the trial is 
underway. Unless a small trial grows into a more significant

---

## Page 31

10 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook An Overview of the Sales Process 11
opportunity, the SE organization is usually distanced from these 
low-end activities. 
Lead Qualification
The initial qualification has two main components. The first is the prelimi -
nary qualification performed by your telemarketing support personnel. This 
step validates the basic business requirements and gathers an impression of 
the overall approval process. There are a variety of qualification acronyms 
in use, the two most common being budget, authority, needs, and timing 
(BANT) and metrics, economic buyer, decision process, decision criteria, 
identify pain, and champion (MEDDIC). If the lead passes this gate and is 
large enough, it passes onto your sales partner. The sales representative must 
then build an account plan to secure the initial sale.
Hint: Call the MEDDIC
Many companies use MEDDIC as a more formal part of their sales process, not just re -
stricted to leads. Therefore, it is a handy mnemonic to keep in the background to ensure 
that all the preparatory sales work is completed before devoting significant time and effort 
to an opportunity. 
The second phase of qualification falls upon you and the sales represen-
tative. This step involves reviewing the lead qualification criteria in more 
detail. The SE is responsible for the technical validation of customer require-
ments. Confirmation of the business criteria and business issues (“pain”) 
may be handled by the rep, the SE, or a combination of the two. Assuming 
that the project has a sufficient budget, business needs, and a viable techni-
cal environment, you progress to the next sales cycle phase.
Request for Proposal
Larger clients or those operating in a regulated environment 2 may submit 
a formal request for details surrounding your company, products, proposed 
solution, and other critical decision-making criteria. This request may take 
the form of a request for information (RFI) or a request for proposal (RFP). 
An RFI is a preliminary request to get general information, while an RFP 
is more detailed and may vary from a brief dozen pages up to hundreds of 
pages and thousands of questions. 
2. This includes most government organizations.

---

## Page 32

10 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook An Overview of the Sales Process 11
In the situation where you are a key supplier for your customer, you 
may avoid the entire RFP process. This action is driven by understanding 
the purchasing process and the overall level of trust between you and the 
prospect. In addition, most companies have strict rules regarding when to 
issue an RFP (some companies never issue an RFP and others will issue one 
100% of the time for any purchase over a set amount), who receives the 
RFP, and what the communications protocols are once the RFP is out for a 
response.  
If your company is a major player in the market or marked as up and 
coming by a research organization like Gartner in their Magic Quadrant, 
you will receive unsolicited RFPs. This likely signifies that a competitor, or 
a systems integrator, has sold the concept of a solution to the prospect. The 
prospect is now trying to ensure that they review all products in the mar -
ket. This is a problematic area in which to compete, as a significant amount 
of SE time can be spent in pursuit of random RFPs which have a minimal 
chance of transforming into revenue.
Fortunately, depending upon the country and area of focus, only a 
small percentage of deals are initiated through an RFP. 
Discovery and First Customer Engagement
You will progress to the discovery or needs analysis phase once engaged 
with the customer, either due to an RFP or lead qualification. This phase 
is the most critical of the sales cycle as you now have the chance to learn 
enough about your customer to construct a personalized solution for them. 
It is also your first real opportunity to interact with the prospect and start 
the relationship-building aspect of the sale. Your ability to create that re -
lationship and discover both the pain and the gain points so that you can 
respond with a compelling solution proposal is a true measure of your SE 
skills. I think discovery is both the most challenging and the most intel -
lectually rewarding part of the entire sales cycle. It is undoubtedly the area 
where the genuinely skilled SE is willing to spend a few extra hours, know-
ing that it will pay off later.
That said, you don’t always get the time to conduct discovery before 
moving into the next phase of present, demonstrate, and propose. If your 
company uses a highly transactional model or is a single product company, 
then you’ll frequently get placed in a zero-discovery demo situation. All is 
not lost. More on that later.

---

## Page 33

12 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook An Overview of the Sales Process 13
Present, Demonstrate, and Propose
We now move into the outbound part of the sales cycle. A greater pro -
portion of the communications flow from you to the customer. Discovery, 
when you are asking questions and then listening extremely hard, is more 
of an inbound process. Although discovery never truly ends (as you should 
always be learning more about your customer), we can now focus on areas 
such as presenting via slides or a whiteboard, demonstrating, and providing 
an overall proposal for a solution design. 
1. Presentation. Once you have completed your discovery process (both 
technical and business), you must present your findings. These pre-
sentations should adapt based on the specifics about your custom -
er, environment, and needs. This time is also an excellent point 
in the sales cycle to introduce some competitive advantages and 
other options and alternatives. Again, by nature of their title and 
knowledge, the SE is frequently better positioned to do this than 
the salesperson. 
2. Demonstration. If you are new to the role, you may think (and may 
even have been told) that the demonstration is the most critical 
and challenging part of the SE job. Assuming you are blessed with 
even an average product portfolio, that is not the case. The higher 
the quality of your discovery, the easier the later phases of the sales 
cycle become. Giving a demo is easy; giving a high-quality demo 
that meaningfully shows your solution’s value is difficult. Many 
SEs never rise above simply listing the features of their product in 
an “and then” linear fashion. Ultimately, it is your ability to select 
the correct messages and components of your solution that deter -
mines whether you are chosen or your competitor gets the busi -
ness. 
3. Proposal. Some prospects may take a further step and cut out more 
vendors from their process, asking for proposals from only a final 
two or three. The proposal should reflect the configuration/bill of 
materials you recommend based on your interaction with the pros-
pect. The proposal will also include pricing and legal terminology 
describing the way your company does business. This text is known 
as terms and conditions (T&Cs) and is almost always based upon 
the customer’s legal framework rather than yours. You will usu -
ally have to put together a document describing the products and 
services you are recommending and detailing interactions and de -
pendencies for the customer. Examples of that may be storage, pro-
cessing, power, or release level requirements. You may also need

---

## Page 34

12 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook An Overview of the Sales Process 13
to include documentation to satisfy other basic customer require -
ments for areas such as security (penetration testing, ISO process 
adherence) or data processing (such as HIPAA or GDPR). 
Most SE teams stay far away from pricing, even though many experi -
enced SEs will know the intricacies of the pricing model better than most 
of the salespeople and sales managers. Typically, you confirm a technical 
architecture to ensure that all the required components are priced into the 
contract. You are, in effect, the technical conscience of the salesforce and 
need to ensure that nothing is forgotten. 
Proof of Concept
Even though you may be selected as the best choice (known in the trade 
as the rather dubious technical win), many clients will still require another 
high-touch step. The proof of concept (POC) enables potential customers to 
try the product(s) out either in their environment or in an isolated sandbox. 
Various alternative names exist for this step, such as a pilot, proof of value, 
trial, or evaluation. Many companies have advanced, self-administering pi-
lot kits—now watched by AI bots to ensure everything stays on track, noti -
fying SEs only when they see a potential issue. 
Most complex POCs will require some degree of SE interaction, mainly 
involving multiple client systems, the import/export of data, a physical in -
stall of hardware, or a significant amount of revenue at risk. As with most 
things in the realm of the SE, the more repeatability and discipline around 
the POC process and mapping out customer success criteria, the more likely 
you are to achieve success. 
Negotiations: Closed Won or Closed Lost 
Your job is (almost) done, and it is up to your sales management team to 
close the deal. You may be brought in for last-minute clarification of tech -
nical points, although the technical sale should be complete. That does not 
mean that you detach entirely from the deal. On the contrary, you should 
maintain your relationships under the traditional medical principle of do 
no harm, as you may obtain information not available to the sales team be-
cause of your position of trust within that customer.  It also makes the next 
step much more straightforward.

---

## Page 35

14 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook An Overview of the Sales Process 15
Post-Sales Support and Account Management
Just because you have won the deal does not mean you can wash your 
hands and walk away from the customer. Many contracts are now monthly 
recurring revenue (MRR) rather than the old-style one large upfront sum. 
That means that continued customer satisfaction is essential. Although a 
considerable part of that burden should fall upon your company’s other 
functions, such as support and customer onboarding/success, it is still es -

sential that you remain connected. The relationships are crucial, and there 
is always one more deal in the pipeline from all but the smallest one-time 
customer!  
Summary
This chapter provides an overview of a typical enterprise sales cycle. Within 
your process, some stages may be totally omitted, yet others may be further 
subdivided. You will notice that although the bulk of your activity occurs in 
the middle stages of the cycle (see Table 2.1), it is essential that you remain 
engaged throughout the sales cycle. It is a trend over the past few decades 
that the spread of SE involvement has gradually expanded further back into 
business value discovery and yet forward into customer engagement and 
making the executive connection. Prioritize your reading of this book based 
on the chapters related to the sales cycle phases where you spend the most 
time and have the maximum impact.
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Concentrate on how the sales process works within your company to better balance  
    your efforts across multiple opportunities. 
 2. Find where the formal written process differs from the real-life version! 
 3. Watch and learn how the experienced SEs spend their time.
 4. Meet your partners.
 5. Visit your counterparts in support. Buy lunch if possible.
For the experienced SE:
 1. There is not much to gain by deepening your knowledge of the sales process.
 2. Track time and activity for a typical month and see if that matches your expectations.
 3. If you desire to move into sales, shadow a salesperson during the early lead generation 
    and the later negotiation stages. Y ou will learn something—even if it is only how hard the  
    sales job really is!
For the SE manager:
 1. Understand the sales cycle so that you can effectively distribute work across your team.
 2. Look for areas where the supporting functions are not delivering as expected.
 3. Meet your partners.
 4. Meet your peers from other cross-functional areas (support, services, and so on).
 5. Be prepared for escalations—both from clients and from salespeople.

CHAPTER

Chapter Goals Lead Qualification
There is no easy button in sales. Prospecting is hard, 
emotionally draining work, and it is the price you have 
to pay to earn a high income. 
                —Jeb Blount
T
hink of a lead as an individual or an entity 
that has come into contact with your busi -
ness. They may or may not have any intention of 
purchasing from you and eventually becoming a 
customer. A lead is the first step in the sales pro-
cess. Leads are vital, as they populate the front 
end of the sales funnel or pipeline and may then 
convert, once qualified, into a prospect with a 
corresponding opportunity. Therefore, lead qual-
ification is a crucial step, as it is essentially a fil -
ter to weed out those individuals and businesses 
who are unlikely to become customers. 
Both lead generation and initial lead quali -
fication are traditionally thought of as being in 
the domain of the sales representative. However, 
that is a dangerous assumption, as the sales engi-
neer needs to contribute to lead generation and 
should undoubtedly be aware of the lead quali -
fication process. 
Understand the different 
roles and motivations in the 
lead-qualification process.
Comprehend the concept 
of lead quality.
Recognize that an unofficial 
part of your job is to 
generate and qualify leads.

---

## Page 39

18 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Lead Qualification 19
Lead Generation
The previous chapter covers some of the ways sales and marketing organiza-
tions can generate leads. However, one unwritten responsibility of the SE is 
to generate leads for their sales counterparts. That is the sales in sales engi-
neer. That does not mean making cold calls, 1 blasting out emails, and run -
ning social media campaigns. However, it does mean looking for additional 
options both within your current customer base (made famous by salesforce.
com as land and expand) and by leveraging your relationships to ask, “Is there 
anyone else we should be speaking with?” Generating the occasional lead 
for your sales counterpart is a wonderful way to win friends and influence 
people. It’s also a great thing to mention when looking for a promotion or 
pay raise!
Initial Lead Qualification
In most companies, the classic inside sales/telesales organization conducts 
any initial lead qualification. Euphemistically known as a business develop-
ment rep (BDR), they will run through a series of scripted questions to clas-
sify the lead and make a first determination as to whether it is worth pursu-
ing. In addition, most sales methodologies have a set of gating questions and 
a corresponding acronym to guide initial qualification. For example, I will 
use one of the simplest: budget, authority, needs, timing (BANT). The goal 
is to gain positive answers to each of these questions to progress and qualify 
the lead.
1. Budget. The fundamental question is, “Has this project/initiative 
been approved, and does it have budget/funding allocated?” Al -
though a yes is the preferred answer, a no does not disqualify the 
lead. If a budget has been allocated, the next set of questions are 
how much, who owns the budget, and what is the overall purchas-
ing process. You are trying to determine if this is a just-looking situ-
ation or there is some serious intent. It is a rare occurrence when 
you get an exact figure, so even learning about a range (e.g., “be -
tween one and two million”) is sufficient.
2. Authority. Does this person have the authority to make a decision 
and progress the project? The answer is usually a no, as the indi -
vidual is in information-gathering mode and following instructions 
from their management. Follow-up questions involve the organi -
1. Calling complete strangers to get a sales appointment.

---

## Page 40

18 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Lead Qualification 19
zational hierarchy and who would be involved in the purchasing 
process and their role (often expressed as approver, recommender, 
influencer, gatekeeper, user, and the like).
3. Needs. An initial set of questions to determine the high-level busi -
ness issues driving the project. What are they trying to accomplish, 
and why are they doing it now? Needs may also touch upon tech-
nical requirements and contingencies. For example, your product 
may require a specific database, development environment, or 
cloud supplier to function, so any compatibility issues need to be 
drawn out early in the process to disqualify the lead.
4. Timing. Is there a timeframe for either making a purchase or having 
an operational solution? What is driving the timescale? Salespeo -
ple often refer to this as the compelling event that propels the overall 
sale. A compelling event comes in many forms, such as legal and 
compliance, new product introductions, end-of-year budget, and 
so on. 
Your sales organization should have a standard set of these qualification 
questions. You should familiarize yourself with them for two reasons. First, 
you may have to use them yourself. Not every lead comes in via the tele -
sales team. Some may arrive through partners or directly from an existing 
customer. Second, you now have expectations of the research performed 
before a lead is considered qualified and passed across to the main sales 
force.
You will also discover that many prospects are reluctant to provide this 
information. The view is that the more they share with the vendor, the 
more likely that information will be used against them at a later stage of 
the sale. They may also creatively exaggerate their position or their needs 
to get attention from the vendor. These actions happen more often than 
salespeople would like to admit, especially when someone firmly states that 
they are the final approver or the recommender, and that is not the case. 
Hint: Learn the Qualification Process
You may view lead qualification as an activity that is clearly outside the realm of the 
sales engineer. However, I can guarantee there will come a time in your career when it is 
a valuable skill to know. Take the time to learn the basic steps and observe some of your 
gifted sales partners going through the conversation. Remember MEDDIC from the previ-
ous chapter.

---

## Page 41

20 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Lead Qualification 21
Lead Quality
Not all leads are equal, so based upon the answers to the qualification script, 
plus other factors such as company size and vertical industry, the lead will 
be ranked and marked as qualified or unqualified. Except in exceptional 
circumstances, an SE should never be working, or even see, an unqualified 
lead. There are far more valuable activities where you can direct your time. 
The ranking attempts to quantify the potential value of that lead to your 
company. Different sales organizations use different scales, so you may see 
leads categorized as A/B/C or 30/90/180 days or even L/U/N (for likely/
unlikely/never). 
Monitoring the Incoming Leads
As new leads arrive and populate your sales force automation (SFA) sys -
tem, you should check them and match the lead quality to the eventual 
outcomes. Many telesales teams subscribe to the “if in doubt, send it out” 
philosophy and will err on the side of optimism. That is good behavior as 
salespeople are paid to progress opportunities, and it is better to scrub out a 
deal a little later in the cycle rather than never pursue a good lead early on. 
That said, look for chances to tighten some of the qualification questions, es-
pecially around technical fit. That may sound like a management issue, but 
if you are a subject matter expert or working in a territory/country far from 
headquarters, you understand local issues far better than anyone else. One 
or two additional questions asked upfront can eliminate a stream of unquali-
fied leads, consuming sales time and never yielding a win.  
Suppose you are an inside SE (you conduct all your business remote -
ly) and support a group of inside salespeople. In that case, the boundaries 
between initial and further qualification can be blurred. A call can rapidly 
morph from qualification through discovery to a demo within 60 minutes. 
This progression makes lead qualification even more essential in order to 
avoid a rapid-fire demo to an unqualified prospect. For context, the standard 
ratio of inside SE:salesperson is about 11:1, so time is a precious commodity. 
CASE STUDY: Get the Questions Right!
Franck, a sales leader from Europe, makes a telling point about the need to local-
ize lead generation. “We released a brand-new box into the small- to medium-
sized business (SMB) market. It had an incredibly competitive price-performance 
ratio, and our partners and direct sales teams were very excited about selling the 
new hardware. However, it had an engineered hard limit of 64 maximum users. 
There was no way around it. We also had a legal licensing restriction that pro -
hibited us from selling it directly in certain countries like the United Kingdom

---

## Page 42

20 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Lead Qualification 21
and Ireland. Marketing built the qualification scripts in our Americas HQ, so we 
always had huge issues localizing the questions. For example, in some smaller 
countries, a 64-user system was enterprise-level hardware rather than SMB, 
and in Europe, we had to find out where both the end users and the data center 
were located. We had to requalify every lead passed across to us for account size 
and location for ten weeks. It was a tremendous waste of time.”
E E E E
Summary
This is one of the shorter chapters, as most of the processes discussed hap -
pen well before you ever see the deal. At this point, you now have a quali-
fied lead passed across to you and your sales partner. Some organizations 
now consider this a good opportunity, and others still require the primary 
account salesperson to confirm the lead (again) before it converts into an 
opportunity. There is now much work to be done, which we will cover in 
the chapters dealing with technical and business value discovery. The deal is 
now primed for the sales engineer to engage and work their magic. 
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Learn the qualification criteria the inside sales/BDR team use.
 2. Always look for opportunities to expand your install base and speak with new contacts  
    inside and outside your immediate customer area.
 3. Practice running through BANT , MEDDIC, or an equivalent in case you need it.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Monitor the quality of incoming leads.
 2. Look for refinements and improvements to the question script.
 3. Offer up a lunch-and-learn with the inside team.

Chapter Goals The RFP Process
The sweetest joy, the wildest woe is love. What the 
world needs is more love and less paperwork.
             —Pearl Bailey
A 
standing joke amongst the worldwide SE 
community is that RFP is an acronym for 
really fast paperwork  instead of request for propos -
al. In effect, how quickly can you get through 
a document and creatively reply ”yes” to every 
question? An RFP is both a positive and a nega -
tive event. It is potentially an opportunity to win 
business, yet it can also waste your time and de-
tract from other more profitable tasks. I will go 
on record and state my bias right up front in that 
I firmly believe that most RFPs are a total waste 
of time and money. 1 This chapter explains the 
creation of an RFP from the customer viewpoint; 
exposes some common RFP myths; describes 
how to put some science into a go/no-go deci -
sion; provides strategies for avoiding an RFP; and 
then finishes with best practices for the comple -
tion and presentation of the final output when 
forced to go through the process. 
1. The average technology vendor responds to 150 
RFPs annually (Loopio Survey 2021, https://loo -
pio.com/blog/rfp-statistics-win-rates).
