## Page 45

24 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 25
The Creation of an RFP
As noted in Chapter 2, some organizations must issue an RFP for every sig -
nificant technology purchase above a specific financial limit. This process is 
quite common in regulated industries (such as utilities) and most national, 
state, county, and local governments. They have no choice and must follow 
a rigorous and proscribed process to interact with each vendor to defend 
themselves from any charges of favoritism.
Creating an RFP allows a company to describe its requirements with the 
expectation that one or more vendors can satisfy those requirements. Those 
requirements are usually sandwiched between a business case, some eco -
nomic/financial data, some technical and business backstory, plus a mass of 
legal terminology and purchasing standards. The RFP can come in many dif-
ferent flavors primarily defined along an axis of technical content (focusing 
on the how and what) instead of the business content (focusing on the how 
many and what of eventual results). The business-to-technical ratio (BTR) 
indicates which department within the customer organization had the most 
input and drove the RFP. The lower the BTR, measured as business pages or 
questions divided by technical pages, the more likely IT created the RFP, and 
it is a highly technical document (BTR << 1). An added byproduct is that the 
SE will be far more engaged and have much more work. A high BTR scoring 
RFP means that business outcomes are prioritized over the technology and 
consequently will mean more work for the salesperson versus the SE.
CASE STUDY: The Programmer Productivity Software RFP (Part 1)
Back in my days as an IT executive, I was also responsible for a development 
organization. The development engineers created a set of products that we sold, 
through our salesforce, to end users. It was certainly a novel organizational set-
up. Unfortunately, we suffered from appalling productivity and quality issues 
in the development organization, to the point that we launched a program to 
buy programmer productivity software. Our CFO asked that we issue an RFP, so 
we created one in the space of a week and gave it to the top five vendors in the 
space. We also set a ten-day deadline for responses. Having been on the other 
side of the fence, I felt that was unreasonable, but now the purchasing group 
was fully engaged and having fun. 
 We were also instructed to hold a single vendor meeting to answer any sup -
plemental questions to provide a level and fair playing field for all concerned. 
That was ridiculous, and I insisted on allowing each vendor to spend time with 
my team and conduct their discovery. I explained this to the CFO and COO by 
telling them that we needed to reward the intelligent vendors willing to invest 
more time and ask more profound and strategic questions. We got our way. And 
then . . . only one vendor took me up on that offer and spent more time with my 
development manager and her team.
 All five vendors met the deadline and supplied rather professional-looking re-
sponses to our RFP. There was almost zero differentiation between the vendors,

---

## Page 46

24 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 25
so we took the plunge and invited all five of them into headquarters the follow-
ing week to outline their proposal and give us a demonstration. Things were 
getting interesting. (The story continues in Chapter 7.) 
E E E E
The Truth Behind Three RFP Myths
There are many long-standing and generally accepted truisms or rules about 
RFPs, some of which are not completely accurate. Three of the most bla -
tant myths can cost you considerable time and money if you believe them. 
They’ve been present in the industry for at least three decades and continue 
to live on, as they are rarely challenged. Let us take a closer look:
1. Ninety percent of issued RFPs are rigged and biased toward a vendor. No 
sales team likes to admit that they were outsold or wasted time 
on an impossible-to-win opportunity. It is always someone else’s 
fault. You’ll hear, “it was clearly rigged to favor our competitor,” 
or “they’d already decided before the RFP was even issued.” Here 
is an analogy that has served me well for more than thirty years: 
I train a youth football (soccer) team. We play in an extremely 
competitive league and are one of the very best teams in the area. 
We win most of our games. Sometimes we lose. When we lose, 
the parents either blame the referee for making bad decisions and 
favoring the other team or blame the coaches 2 for making poor 
tactical decisions. Yet sometimes the players make mistakes and 
play poorly (they are under-12s—it happens!). Sometimes we lose 
to an inferior team that does just everything right and beats us, 
and sometimes we are simply beaten by a better team. Yet in the 
eyes of the parents, it is always someone else’s fault—never their 
children.
  This attitude is precisely the same viewpoint that sales teams 
take when they lose an RFP. When speaking with current and for-
mer CIOs about the RFP process, their experiences align with my 
own. 
John, we never ever bias an RFP or preselect a vendor. In today’s 
business environment, we could not afford the consequences if 
that happened and it was discovered. We would lose our jobs and 
2. If you wonder why people even volunteer to do this, it is because the children 
truly enjoy the game, and their smiles and happiness are worth it.

---

## Page 47

26 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 27
endanger the careers of our staff. There are far more subtle ways of 
influencing a final decision.
  There are certainly RFPs issued where the customer is personally 
biased toward (or against) a solution. People are biased; RFPs are 
rarely biased. Over the past 15 years, our cumulative data yields a 
number closer to 40% in terms of personal bias toward a particular 
vendor. That alone is not enough to dictate a winner, but it cer -
tainly helps. The bias can manifest itself in several ways. A senior 
Wall Street managing director once told me:
This is what we do if we absolutely need to change a score. We rate 
every factor on a scale of 1 (low) through 5 (high) and then apply 
a weighting. Deciding what a 1, a 2, or a 5 is fairly clear cut. The 
difference between a strong 3 and a weak 4 is unclear and a matter 
of opinion. Upgrading a few 3s to 4s for your favorite can make 
all the difference in the selection process for getting to a shortlist.
2. You only win the RFP if you wrote the original RFP . Take a step back and 
ask yourself how many RFPs you wrote for your customers this 
year. Twenty years ago, most vendors had a sample RFP they would 
supply to their internal coach as a basis for the eventual document. 
Today, the best you can do is suggest a few features or functions 3 
during an initial call or discovery, which may be incorporated into 
an eventual RFP document. 
  Think about this entire process from the customer’s viewpoint. 
The primary internal purpose of an RFP is to gather momentum and 
promote collaboration inside one or more customer departments to 
get a project started. It is just as much a political and psychological 
act, as it shows purchasing intent. It may be the first chance busi -
ness analysts have to document requirements gathered from users, 
middle management, and executives. There are now multiple web-
sites that provide incredibly professional standard RFP templates, 
so the project team doesn’t even worry about that. Once the docu-
ment is created, it is then a common set of requirements that every-
one can use to measure the capabilities of potential vendors. It is a 
concrete action to get legal, finance, purchasing, IT, business users, 
and everyone else on the same proverbial page. Submitting an RFP 
to vendors is a measurable milestone on a project plan and helps to 
satisfy someone’s management by objectives (MBOs).
3. Which means you should certainly have your set of half a dozen questions you 
could supply to a customer if and when requested.

---

## Page 48

26 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 27
  Go back several decades, and the RFP process tended to be a long 
and drawn-out process. Analysts were tasked with investigating 
the market and determining who might eventually respond. They 
had to rely on vendor literature, which was physically mailed to 
them, and then consult with research organizations like Aberdeen 
or Gartner to get their views on the winners and losers. When 
the RFP was eventually issued, the customer often had limited in-
formation about available technology and expected an education 
from each responding vendor. 
  Contrast that with today. Customers are far more educated and 
have information at their fingertips. They are often dangerously 
knowledgeable. Why dangerous? It is like waking up in the morn-
ing with a headache located behind one eye. You scour the inter -
net for diseases with that symptom and convince yourself that you 
are dying. But actually you have a headache that can be fixed with 
two aspirin. 
  The best way for you to write an RFP is to write it through social 
media and utilize Twitter, LinkedIn, Facebook, and even Instagram 
to promote both the material and yourself to become an influenc-
er. That is now a valuable role for an individual SE or a small SE 
team. You are still persuading, influencing, placing, and educating 
(PIPE), yet in a far more subtle and indirect way. When you dis -
cover the text in an RFP taken directly from your website or your 
blog—that’s a big win.
3. You must answer every RFP that you receive. Unfortunately, this is of-
ten the position of the salesperson, who rarely must invest a lot 
of their time into the process. Over the years, we have asked SE 
leaders across the globe the percentage of RFPs they receive and 
respond to that they know they will never win. That percentage has 
hovered between 30 and 35% since the first edition of this book 
was published in 2001. That is an incredible waste of time and has 
an impact on overall team morale.
  One of my favorite leadership lessons is “it is by saying no that I 
get all the yeses completed.” This approach certainly applies to RFP 
responses. Sales teams are gradually learning this lesson. Recent 
data4 shows that technology companies believe that they now only 
respond to 69% of the RFPs they receive. This is hard to match up 
with the “30% of the RFPs we complete we know we will never 
win” statement a few lines earlier. 
4. 2021 shows a 65% rate, compared with 69% in 2020 (Loopio Survey data).

---

## Page 49

28 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 29
The Go or No-Go Decision
Whenever an RFP arrives, usually now into the inbox of a sales or marketing 
rep, the sales team needs to decide whether they should respond and com -
pete for the opportunity or decline and say, “no thanks.” There are many 
factors to consider, such as:
• Is this an unsolicited or an expected RFP?
• Overall product fit;
• Competitors (including a do-nothing option);
• Required time frame;
• Budget and approval process;
• Involvement of partners, system integrators, and other third parties;
• Surplus or deficit of available personnel;
• Is there a dedicated internal RFP response team?
• Executive pressure;
• Is this a strategic account?
• Do we need to win or just get through to the next round?
• Can our automated RFP software handle this?
Since a professional RFP response consumes many resources, the go or 
no-go decision is important and should not be left to emotion but, instead, 
be data driven. That’s why the concept of scoring an RFP for win probability 
is so important, and we’ll examine that later in the chapter. But, first, we 
should study the economics of the opportunity and the overall RFP win 
rates.
CASE STUDY: A Lesson in Economics
One of our clients, a cybersecurity vendor, expanded rapidly because of natural 
growth and multiple acquisitions. As a result, the global SE team was over -
whelmed by RFPs, and the pace was increasing. Over 12 months, the SE opera-
tions director estimated that they had completed 185 RFPs globally, of which 
28 were wins, 77 were losses, and the remaining 80 were no decisions. Putting 
aside the low 15% win rate, those RFPs resulted in an annualized revenue run 
rate of US$6.9 million after currency conversion. That sounds like a good return, 
except when they calculated the opportunity cost of the SE time (discounting 
everyone else to zero), they discovered that it was $20 million. Thus, RFPs cost 
the company $13 million a year, which was in effect several pennies in earnings 
per share (EPS). After implementing a formalized RFP scoring system, the num-
bers improved significantly to reduce the losses and likely no-decision outcomes. 
The following year they won 32 out of 123 RFPs (26% win rate) with a much

---

## Page 50

28 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 29
higher average sales price and only lost 33, with the remainder being no deci -
sions. Now they are working on eliminating more of those no decisions. 
E E E E
Sales or presales operations rarely correlate RFP completion rates with 
overall win rates. It is not a number readily available to the chief revenue 
officer or the regional VP of sales engineering. One of our hardware cli -
ents has an overall opportunity win rate of 53%, yet their win rate (after 
research!) for unsolicited RFPs was 6% and for expected RFPs was 19%. A 
cybersecurity organization reported a win rate of 22% measured against 
hundreds of RFPs. An Indian consulting services company measured its rate 
at 8.8%. To put those numbers into perspective, every other activity within 
the sales process (such as demo, presentation, proof of concept) has a sig -
nificantly higher win rate. Why should that hardware company commit 
time and people to an RFP with a 19% chance of winning versus another 
deal with a 53% chance of revenue? They will only do that if:
a. There are available resources;
b. They are convinced that the win rate possibility is significantly 
higher than 19%;
c. They are forced into a response to maintain the relationship with 
the customer or an intermediary third party;
d. Or they don’t know any better.
Response Team Infrastructure
About a third of our customers have a dedicated response manager or a 
more extensive response team. That is usually dictated either by the vertical 
industry in which they operate or the company’s overall size. RFP comple-
tion has undoubtedly become one of the low-end parts of the SE job that is 
increasingly automated. That is a positive trend as it means that less SE time 
is spent writing responses now than five years ago. That time is switched to 
reviewing the output of an AI response generator rather than creating the 
response. If your company is still relying on a manual input process and 
boilerplate templates, you are missing out on an easy automation opportu-
nity and wasting precious SE cycles. The trade-off is that someone needs to 
feed the machine and keep the data and algorithms fresh. 
In conjunction with an internal bid team, I’ve also seen an increase 
in making the account rep the overall project manager for the RFP. This

---

## Page 51

30 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 31
responsibility secures a more positive commitment from the rep, who now 
has a stake in the process instead of just being concerned about the result. 
In addition, this skin in the game 5 ensures that the rep takes a more objec -
tive view about the RFP response, as it will also consume some of their time. 
Adding in responsibility for writing or reviewing the executive summary 
reinforces this view.  
Scoring the RFP
One dramatic difference over the past decade is that most companies now 
score their RFPs to decide whether they are worth pursuing. In 2014, less 
than 10% of companies scored RFPs; now, more than 70% keep a scorecard. 
The purpose of scoring is to provide a quantifiable measurement system to 
examine the relative merits of each incoming RFP. We are not (yet) at the 
point when we can say that any given RFP has a 34.5% chance of a win. We 
can say, via scoring on a scale of 0 to 100, that an RFP with a relative score 
of 72 is more likely to result in revenue than another RFP with a score of 44. 
That means prioritizing one RFP over another RFP and potentially deciding 
on a “no bid” response for the very low scoring RFPs.
Once you have created a scoring system, you can monitor its predictive 
ability against the end result of win/loss/no decision for the overall opportu-
nity. You should also retrofit RFPs from the past 6–12 months into the analy-
sis and fine-tune based upon that data. When correctly calibrated, using a 0 
to 100 scale, three bands will become apparent, as shown in Table 4.1.
Ideally, a score sheet should be a fill-in-blank sheet or form connected 
to your customer relationship management (CRM) system. Once they have 
read through the entire RFP, both the SE and the rep should complete the 
form as a team. This teaming eliminates gaming the process with biased 
answers, as the underlying score system will be transparent. Assign points, 
both positive and negative , on specific RFP attributes. For example, an RFP 
from an existing customer might be a +5, if they are a reference account, 
5. A delightful phrase from horse racing meaning incurring personal risk to achieve 
a result. The horse owners have the most riding on the outcome.
Table 4.1 
Correlation of RFP Score to Likelihood of a Win
Band 
Approximate 
Range Final Outcome
Low 0 to 40 Almost zero probability of success
Medium 40 to 70 20% of your RFP wins will come from this band
High 70 to 100 80% of your RFP wins will come from this band

---

## Page 52

30 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 31
then +8, while a new account might be a 0, and if that customer is high -
lighted on your competitor’s website, it is a -4. Create a series of standard 
questions based on the opportunity’s technical, business, and sales aspects. 
Note that you cannot cover every eventuality in the scorecard, so aim for 
the standard cases and apply the 80/20 rule.
You can find a sample one-pager scoresheet in the Resources section of 
the Mastering Technical Sales website.6
RFP Avoidance Strategies
Before you fully engage with the RFP and start the completion process, 
there are two technical avoidance techniques for the entire account team to 
consider. Bear in mind that your competitors may be thinking about these 
tactics as well. The two most prevalent techniques are the reference sell and 
the trial/proof close. Both may require additional work from the SE team and 
the prospect, yet can serve you well in larger or strategic opportunities. 
In return for this work, you gain better control of the sales cycle and may 
make life much harder for your competitors.
You can employ the technical reference close when you are confident 
that your product is a near-perfect 95%–plus match for the prospect, par -
ticularly if you have relevant and unique capabilities or are the major player 
in the market. The approach is to state, “We have many clients who had the 
same problem set as you now have. If we can show you specifically how 
we fixed them, is there a way to shortcut this process?”  That is the emo -
tional appeal to safety, followed by the rational statement of “by starting 
six months earlier, we can save you eight thousand a month in operating 
costs.” The worst that can happen is that the prospect says no, and at least 
you have planted a seed in the mind of some executive that may germinate 
later. The best is that if you get a yes, you can set up a few reference visits 
and rapidly advance through the sales cycle before your competition knows 
what is happening.
The trial/proof close involves even more work from the SE team, so it 
should be reserved for the more important deals. This technique consists of 
allowing the prospect to conduct a limited trial (or POC) either in their en-
vironment or (preferred) via a custom cloud-based configuration, subject to 
specific agreed-upon success criteria. Chapter 17 discusses these evaluation 
strategies in far more detail. An evaluation usually results in more effort 
than actually completing the RFP; however, it (a) can lock the competition 
out of the account, (b) gets you physically or virtually inside the building, 
6. www.masteringtechnicalsales.com/resources.

---

## Page 53

32 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 33
and (c) consumes valuable client time, which cannot be spent looking at 
other options. Even if a competitor manages to copy the strategy, you have 
reduced the playing field and improved your odds.  
The Alternate Response
Sometimes you will receive RFPs that are not a good fit for you. They score 
poorly because they are either outside of your core portfolio competencies 
or from companies that issue RFPs but never give you any business. You do 
not always have to “no bid,” as you can follow a more disruptive approach. 
As you will see, it is disruptive for the customer in a good way and disruptive 
to the competition in a bad way. 
The alternate response is helpful if you cannot satisfy the RFP as written 
but can provide the same or equivalent end result. For example, you politely 
and politically reply, “our portfolio is not a good fit for your desired business 
outcomes with respect to how you have asked us to solve it. However, if 
you would consider achieving those same outcomes this way instead (just as 
we have implemented at customers A and B), here is how we would satisfy 
your business requirements.” You then provide a brief description and visual 
of your proposed architecture to show that you have a plan and submit the 
response. Wait patiently. 
The customer may react in a multitude of ways, although you can be 
guaranteed that your response will elicit a serious conversation within 
the customer’s RFP team. The eventual reaction may range from a polite 
“thanks, but no thanks” to withdrawing the RFP, and you then have an op-
portunity to explain your proposal. 
CASE STUDY: The Alternate Response in Action 
Charlotte, an SE director in Western Europe, illustrates the power of the alter -
nate response. “We received an RFP for the creation of a massive data ware -
house. This project involves transferring and transforming humongous amounts 
of data and performing extremely complex analytics on the data. We felt it was 
an exercise in futility and absolutely the incorrect architecture for our client. 
The transfer and transformation aspects were a classic heat-seeking missile for 
complexity. It was also a terrible fit for our product set in terms of scalability and 
user management. Instead of responding to the RFP and losing to any of three 
other competitors, we proposed an alternate way of solving the problem. That 
problem was supplying same-day information to the business units instead of 
waiting a week or more. We offered a way to conduct analysis in place using 
real-time cloud-based copies of the primary data sources. Our solution came in 
at 60% of the cost and 50% of the effort. The RFP analysis and decision pro -
cesses were stopped dead in their tracks. The entire IT and business executive

---

## Page 54

32 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 33
team summoned us for a summit meeting. We engaged a partner, pitched our 
solution, and were ultimately awarded the business. The SE who designed the 
alternate response also won the Global SE of the Quarter award. Sounds like a 
fairy tale.”
E E E E
I have also seen an alternate response used by the sales team when 
dealing with a supposedly strategic but RFP-happy account that continually 
issues RFP documents but never awards your company the business. Use 
this tactic to reset and redefine exactly what a “strategic/partner-oriented” 
relationship truly means. Obviously, this is driven by the account represen-
tative and their management rather than the SE team. However, you can 
help by having a dataset of metrics at your disposal to back up this approach. 
Completing the RFP
Fortunately, this is an area of job responsibilities that has been dramatically 
automated over the past few years. Most organizations have transitioned 
from a library of boilerplate and standard responses to automated response 
software. It is still your responsibility to ensure that the machines are cor -
rect and that your answer is indeed memorable, interesting, and compel -
ling. If you are the project manager, you will also have the duties of assign-
ing tasks and following up on timelines. 
Hint: Use the Process
Ensure the RFP is entered into your CRM system and that the tasks are tracked within 
the system. It is far easier to lean on the sales stage process and timelines as a means to get 
things done rather than  constantly emailing, chatting, texting, and calling people. You 
also gain the benefit of tracking the activity for that RFP .
When reviewing the answers contained within your RFP, consider that 
there may be some measure of automation on the receiving side as well. 
That means whenever you can start your response with a yes, a no, or some 
number that is to your advantage, you should do so. Make life easier for the 
customer! Also, consider maximizing the use of tables, graphs, and bulleted 
lists rather than long textual descriptions. It is acceptable to refer to your 
technical documentation instead of including vast amounts of detail (e.g., 
“Yes. That capability has been in our product for the last two releases. Ref: 
System Guide Release 12.6i, page 266, www.mycompany.com”).
One area where you may wish to supplement any automated responses 
is to include and highlight competitive advantages. There is nothing wrong

---

## Page 55

34 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 35
with pointing out one of your strengths, or in contrast a competitor’s weak-
ness, in the text of an answer. 
Presenting the RFP
The cover letter and executive summary are as crucial as the actual contents. 
They are also far more likely to be read and distributed within the customer. 
It may not be your job to create these parts of the RFP response—however, 
that does not mean that you should ignore them. The summary should align 
with the technical proposal and highlight all major strengths identified with-
in your response. Again, the SE is often in a better position to uncover those 
competitive points than the salesperson. 
There should also be a review meeting with the customer set up shortly 
after final delivery. This meeting allows you to present your solution and 
gather real-time feedback—both verbal and nonverbal. It may also allow 
you to develop some additional relationships with the client if they are a 
new account. Once delivered, keep in contact with the client (subject to 
whatever limitations imposed upon you by purchasing) and provide a steady 
stream of follow-up articles, press releases, technical references, and so on. If 
you are fortunate enough to connect with a business owner or an executive, 
work extra hard at keeping that relationship—don’t turn it over to some -
one else at your company (see Chapters 9 “Customer Engagement” and 19 
“Trusted Advisor”). It is your responsibility to develop some rapport with 
the customer and not just sit back and await directions from the salesperson. 
Hint: Say “Thank You”
Contributing to an RFP is not something people consider their dream job; nor is it part of 
their primary job responsibilities, especially if they work outside sales. So once the RFP is 
completed, make sure you thank anyone involved in the process and keep them apprised 
of progress. Nothing is more infuriating for marketing, product, or engineering to contrib-
ute material to an RFP and then hear deafening silence. So say “thank you.” It will pay 
incredible dividends.
Summary
The best RFP is one that you never have to complete because the customer 
trusts you enough to give you the first shot at the business. Sadly, we are not 
always in that position, so RFPs are an unfortunate fact of life. The more you 
can create a repeatable process and leverage automation to do most of the

---

## Page 56

34 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The RFP Process 35
basic work for you, the more you can focus on the higher value aspects of 
the job. Apply some logic and metrics to the RFP process to remove emotion 
from the eventual decision. (If an account is “strategic,” or a salesperson 
inherits a new territory, then you will see that emotion in play.) 
Look for opportunities to short-circuit the process by offering technical 
references, a controlled pilot, or an alternate response. 7 Once you engage, 
use your internal systems to track tasks, responsibilities, and activity time 
for the RFP. Finally, always seek face time with the customer after deliver-
ing the RFP. Meet to clarify any answers and start the rapport building—
particularly if kept at a distance by their internal processes. 
Skill Building
7. This does assume that you have a suboptimal win rate and that your goal is to 
minimize RFP response time.
For the new SE: 
 1. Review (but do not question) the basic deal qualifications.
 2. Set time and resource priorities with your manager.
 3. Familiarize yourself with any automation tools and internal bid response teams.
 4. Don’t forget the client relationship after the paperwork is done.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Create a script you can use to ask for time extensions.
 2. Try writing a shadow executive summary and compare it with the final submission. This  
    is also initial input into business value discovery .
 3. Look for the salesperson to project manage the response. (Y ou can only hope.)
 4. Seek out business and executive relationships as well as technical ones.

Chapter Goals Technical Discovery
The real voyage of discovery consists not in seeking new 
landscapes, but in having new eyes.
               —Marcel Proust
D
iscovery is the process of uncovering as 
much as possible about the people, process-
es, politics, and needs of your potential custom -
er. Lawyers define discovery as “the compulsory 
disclosure, by a party to an action, of relevant 
documents referred to by the other party.”  Un-
fortunately, at least in the world of sales, there 
is nothing compulsory about disclosing informa-
tion. An elementary school teacher may define 
discovery as “the act of finding or learning some-
thing for the first time.” I find that a far more 
pleasant definition, as it encapsulates the fun and 
exciting part of the SE job.
Discovery encompasses many activities. For 
the purposes of explanation, we can divide them 
into two main categories. The first, covered in 
this chapter, is technical discovery. That covers the 
inputs, outputs, processes, system requirements, 
architecture, and standards in place (or desired) 
within the customer. The second, covered in the 
two following chapters, is business value discovery. 
It is just as crucial as technical discovery in that 
you learn about the business (monetary) drivers 
behind the technical requirements. Sometimes 
the two occur in parallel, sometimes in series, 
Understand the 
interrelationship between 
technical and business 
value discovery.
Learn how to avoid the 
three crimes of discovery.
Build your own 
personalized technical 
discovery framework.

## Page 59

38 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Technical Discovery 39
and often intertwined together like the double helix of DNA. Stretching that 
analogy a little further, discovery allows you to unlock the corporate ge -
nome of your customer and make the correct diagnosis to solve their prob -
lems and achieve their dreams.
During discovery, you act as a systems integrator on your customer’s 
behalf, eventually putting together a proposal for a cutting-edge solution 
centered on your company’s portfolio of products and services. This value-
added aspect is unique to the sales engineer and gives you the right to im -
pose your structure during this stage of the sales and buying process. As you 
review these chapters, include any steps or requirements that are specific 
and relevant to your customers (and omit those that are not). Pay atten -
tion to any dependencies in the steps, as you must factor those into your 
overall plan when scheduling customer and internal meetings. Note that a 
field-based SE with a handful of accounts will conduct deeper research and 
discovery than an inside or SMB SE who speaks with half a dozen different 
customers each day.
A last introductory note on discovery is the recognition that it is an area 
that generates significant friction between the SE and their sales counter -
part. Most SEs believe that there is no such thing as too much discovery 
and may feel that they need more information and one more meeting. On 
the other hand, most salespeople believe they are paid to progress a deal as 
quickly as possible, so yet another discovery meeting will get in the way of 
that velocity. So you need to strike a happy medium that is specific to each 
sales–sales engineer combination.
Initial Research—Before the Main Engagement
Before moving into technical discovery, you should gather as much custom-
er information as reasonably possible. This data allows you to be “danger -
ously knowledgeable” when you engage with the customer; plus, it shows 
that you have done your homework. Most customers, especially those in 
middle management and above, dislike being asked questions to which you 
can quickly find the answers beforehand with some research. For example, 
I once saw an SE ask a senior executive at Coca-Cola who were their main 
competitors! As well as being blatantly obvious,1 that information was read-
ily available in their annual report.
You can gather corporate information from various sources:
• An initial RFI or RFP if one was issued;
1. Actually, it varies by country. Typically, it is Pepsi, Red Bull, and Keurig.

---

## Page 60

38 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Technical Discovery 39
• Notes from the sales system made during earlier conversations;
• Data about existing installs/usage in other departments or affiliates;
• Knowledge from your partners;
• Annual reports and public financial information;
• Press releases and transcripts of investor calls;
• Social media;
• Third-party suppliers (like Dun and Bradstreet);
• Technology stack.2
Hint: Think Globally!
Do not limit your research to your particular geography. Always take some time to re -
search the country where global HQ is located and search through your internal systems 
for foreign affiliates. Then, connect with the SE who deals with that location. A preexist-
ing relationship, no matter how small, carries some weight as you go through discovery.
If you know who in particular you will be dealing with from the cus -
tomer organization as you journey through discovery, you should research 
them, too. We will look at this in more detail within the “Executive Con -
nection” chapter. In general, the higher your contact lives in the organiza-
tion chart, the more research you should conduct. Even a cursory 10- to 
15-minute search can yield some interesting and valuable data. Standard 
sources include:
• LinkedIn, Xing, or the local professional network platform;
• Twitter (people are often less guarded on this platform than any 
other);
• General Google search;
• Corporate website;
• Your internal systems.
As well as looking for a hook, some commonality you can use to con -
nect with the person, you may also discover where they have worked in 
the past, professional organizations, promotional path, and a host of other 
insights. Most customers now expect to be professionally researched, al -
though I’d recommend that you keep Facebook, Instagram, and similar 
platforms off the list. 
2. Utilizing tools such as Builtwith, Geekflare, or Wappalyzer.

Prelude: The Three Behavioral Crimes of Discovery
During discovery, your primary purpose is to gather as much information as 
possible and do that without judgment or engaging in premature solution -
eering. You are in the room to help the customer and not to sell the cus -
tomer. To help the customer, you need access to information to determine 
how you can help them. It is an exercise in patience, which is not necessarily 
a trait that most SEs (and salespeople) possess in abundance. As an SE, we 
are fixers and pleasers—we like to solve problems and make people happy. 
Unfortunately, sometimes we try to do that too quickly, and many discovery 
calls go off track because the sales team exhibits one of three behavioral 
crimes. Those crimes, using the simple TAG mnemonic, are tell, accept, and 
guess.
CASE STUDY: Origin Story: Creating Sales Engineer Games
TAG was created in the winter of 1993 while working at Sybase in New York 
City. Our technical sales kickoff meeting was rapidly approaching, and I was 
meeting with other SE leaders (big thanks to Bridget Piraino, Michael Shilling, 
and John Durant) to create an exercise designed to promote patience within 
our SEs and to stop an automatic dash to a demo or overhead technology slides. 
We initially looked at “ACGT” (the four building blocks of a DNA molecule) but 
couldn’t find anything suitable for the “C.” It seemed too geeky even for us. 
So, we ended up with TAG and ran an entertaining workshop later that year. It 
turned out that we all had a propensity to “tell” because we were so enamored 
of our speedy database. Listen to yourself and the salesperson during your next 
sales call and TAG each other afterward—it adds some fun to the debrief. 
E E E E
Tell
We see the same problems within our customers every day, and, within a 
few months, we become subject matter experts on how to solve many of 
those problems. It is then easy to assume that the customer immediately 
wants to hear that you can help them and that you have had the same ex -
perience with dozens of other clients. You might even view that rapid-fire 
diagnosis as a show of credibility. You listen to the customer for a few min -
utes, then interject to say, “It seems like you have a problem with x. We see 
that every day. Let me tell you how we can help.”
There are three immediate issues with moving into tell mode. The first is 
that you are cutting off the supply of information from the customer. There 
is still a lot more you can learn. The second is that you have not let the 
customer fully explain their issue—doesn’t it annoy you when you feel that

---

## Page 62

40 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Technical Discovery 41
someone is not actively listening to you but is instead deciding what to say 
next when you have finished speaking? Finally, something is not a problem 
until the customer admits that it is a problem. Depending upon your culture 
and country, telling the customer that they have a problem in front of oth-
ers is not a wise move. Even in more direct cultures, you are still calling the 
baby ugly, and you must be tactful.
An experienced SE will indeed rapidly diagnose pains and gains be -
cause they have seen it all before. And 95% of the time, their preliminary 
diagnosis after just 15–20 minutes is correct. However, your goal is not to 
impress the customer with how clever and insightful you are, but to build 
rapport and gather information. Patience is everything. People like to feel 
they are being heard and understood, as every customer also believes that 
they are different and unique. 
Accept
The second crime is that of accepting what the customer says without con-
firming the facts. When you hear something like “we need a better way to 
handle massive amounts of incoming data” and your company sells data 
analytics, machine learning, and business intelligence tools, it is natural to 
accept that statement as accurate because it confirms3 your reason for hav-
ing the meeting. You assume that the customer is correct and accept what 
they tell you at face value. The customer may indeed be right, be partially 
correct, or could be totally incorrect. Unless you dive into the reasoning 
behind these statements, you could spend a lot of sales time solving the 
wrong problem. 
CASE STUDY: The Pursuit of the Perfect Presentation
I must admit that accept is the crime that I still commit most often or find myself 
as a willing accomplice. Last year, I spoke with three regional SE VPs who were 
telling me about a common problem. “Our SEs are terrible in front of mid-level 
executives. They jump into an executive presentation or a short demo at once, 
and they do not make the connection. Can you help us improve our demos and 
presentations?” As that sounds like something that MTS can help them with, 
we started speaking about presentation workshops, a facilitated session with 
product marketing, and so on. After about 30 minutes, I realized that I had ac-
cepted their story. 
 “Wait. Tell me more about why the SEs are not making the connection?” 
 After more discussion, it transpired that we needed to divert the SEs away 
from their presentation decks and demos, and provide them with some addi -
tional techniques to personalize their executive engagements. By creative use 
of whiteboarding and storytelling, we completely redefined their engagement 
3. Known as confirmation bias, or the tendency to interpret new evidence as confir-
mation of your existing beliefs and theories.

---

## Page 63

42 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Technical Discovery 43
strategy—to the point that salespeople were asking to join the workshops. Yet, 
we were only one step away from barreling down the wrong road and devising 
an utterly ineffective solution. (Proving yet again—it is not always about the 
demo!)
E E E E
Remember that your purpose is to verify and validate what the custom-
er is telling you—it is not to challenge the customer. An indirect approach is 
often more effective. Instead of asking, “Why do you feel that way” or “Why 
is that happening?” you can say, “Hmm. Please tell me more.” It sounds like 
a subtle change, yet it is remarkably effective.
Guess
The third crime is that of guessing. You can never learn absolutely every -
thing that you need to know about the customer. There will always be gaps 
in your knowledge. Because we have been doing our job for years and trust 
our judgment, we automatically fill in those gaps. We call it wisdom and 
experience rather than a highly educated guess. If there is a piece of infor -
mation, a data point, or an answer you need, you must find a way to get 
it. Do not be afraid of asking the question. Do not guess. Guesswork is for 
amateurs and the competition.
Guessing also includes the offense of assuming. That may be assuming 
that you understand the customer’s problem, industry, or personal circum-
stances. Even when you feel that you have seen a situation before, you 
should still take the time to ask more confirmatory questions and conduct 
a little more research. This situation can sometimes be a source of friction 
between the SE and their sales partner, who may feel that they have enough 
information to proceed and do not need to ask any more questions. 
The Basic Methodology
Every client has a standard list of situational technical questions to ask their 
customers. Most of those questions revolve around the current architecture, 
process flow, and how much/how many types of style inquiries (think clas-
sic speeds and feeds). You should familiarize yourself with these questions 
and apply some form of order and framework to them that makes the ques-
tions sound like they come from you, rather than a standard sheet. You can 
help this personalization process by taking a minute or two upfront with the 
customer to explain exactly what will happen next and the purpose of your 
questions. Once customers understand why they may be asked a long set of

---

## Page 64

42 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Technical Discovery 43
questions and what will happen with their answers, they are far more will-
ing to cooperate. 
Regardless of whether you are selling hardware, software, services, or 
some amalgam of the three, there is still a basic framework you can run 
through in checkbox fashion. Most of this is fundamental systems analysis, 
and you should expand as needed. Figure 5.1 provides a high-level flow -
chart of the overall process.
Hint: Listen for the Numbers
Although opinions are helpful and assist with the emotional side of the sale, metrics rule. 
Whenever you can obtain measurable facts about time, people, money, inputs/outputs, 
or rates, that helps you and the customer define the problem. For example, “double our 
capacity” is better than “more capacity”—more on this in Chapter 7.
The Inputs
You should find the answers to questions such as the following:
1. What are the current inputs into the system? Inputs can be items 
such as data, documents, raw materials, and people. 
2. What are the sources of those inputs? Include physical or virtual 
location?
3. What is the input rate? Maximum and minimum rates?
4. What is the form of the inputs?
Figure 5.1 Summary flowchart of the basic methodology.

---

## Page 65

44 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Technical Discovery 45
5. Who owns the input source, and who is responsible for the trans -
mission? 
6. Does any preprocessing or intermediary processing occur? 
7. Are there any legal, compliance, or security concerns?
8. Will any new inputs be added or existing ones changed? 
The Middle Layer/Process
The middle layer is the process layer, or where most of the work occurs. You 
will need to ask questions such as the following:
1. Would you please tell me about any current technical standards 
you may have, such as database environment, development tools, 
preferred applications?
2. Likewise, are there any preferred vendors already in place that we 
need to accommodate?
3. What is the current staff allocation to support what is already in 
place?
4. How does it work now?
5. How would you like it to work?
6. What are the current issues with how it works?
7. What does your boss (your end users/customers) think about this?
8. What constraints (e.g., backup window, report deadlines, regula -
tory timing) are there? 
9. Any environmental or logistical constraints (e.g., power, water, 
sunlight)?
10. What happens in this layer to generate the necessary outputs we 
will discuss next?
Note that if you are proposing a brand-new solution, there may not be 
anything in existence right now. If this is the case, confine your questions to 
architectural standards and the future state.
The Outputs
You should find the answers to questions such as the following:
1. What are the current outputs from the system? Outputs can be 
items such as data, documents, raw materials, and people. 
2. What are the destinations of those outputs?
3. What is the output rate? Maximum and minimum rates?

---

## Page 66

44 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Technical Discovery 45
4. What is the form of the outputs?
5. Who owns the output stream, and who is responsible for the trans-
mission? 
6. Does any post-processing or intermediary processing occur? 
7. Are there any legal, compliance, or security concerns?
8. Will any new outputs be added or existing ones changed? 
9. What happens if one of those outputs fails?
The Visualization
Most of the data you have gathered so far is numerical and textual informa-
tion or verbal descriptions. If a picture is worth a thousand words, always 
try to get a visual of the current process and the desired operation. You 
should ask for any current process flow and design diagrams and then ask 
the client to sketch out a high-level version. Either collaborate with the 
client or allow them to sketch it by themselves, depending upon their artis-
tic ability and confidence. The visualization step during technical discovery 
generates an essential sales asset. You will return to the visual and use that 
as your overall map on many occasions during the sale. It will also evolve as 
you learn more about the customer and their environment. 
The (Optional) Magic Wand
Most of the customer staff you deal with will be “facts and figures”–type 
individuals, particularly those within the IT department. A gross categoriza-
tion of many people, but true. Sometimes it helps to get them away from 
the current state and problems and ask the magic wand 4 questions. You 
may need to do this individually rather than in a team setting to get a truly 
personal perspective.
• “If you had a magic wand and could wave it to change one thing 
about the current situation, what would that be?”
• “Suppose you still had that wand and could now use it to create the 
new, functional, and workable system (not perfection, just work -
able); what would change?”
• Then sit back and listen. 
4. Every culture is familiar with the Disney Princess persona. The magic wand 
question has never failed me in countries ranging from China to Russia to Brazil 
to Nigeria.

---

## Page 67

46 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
Summary
Technical discovery allows you to gain a complete understanding of the 
customer’s current technical and future technical states. You should sub -
sequently be able to explain to someone else how the system/process cur -
rently works (if one exists), what the desired solution looks like, and any 
technology constraints that may affect the future state. Technical discovery 
is not just about the technology, as the people who use that technology play 
an important part. To be effective, technical discovery needs to be personal-
ized and not feel to the customer like you are going through the motions and 
getting answers to a set of questions within a corporate spreadsheet (even 
though that may well be the result). The more of you that you can inject into 
the process, the easier it becomes to get the information you need. 
Hint: Use the Three Ps
Early in my career, a mentor told me about the three Ps of technical discovery. Patience—
every time you feel like offering an opinion, be quiet. Persistence—when you need a piece 
of information, don’t be afraid to ask for it. And the third P is for potential. Always look 
for the opportunity to make life easier and simpler for your customer. Hopefully, with the 
fourth P—your product.
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Discovery is a skill best learned by observation. Watch an experienced SE go through 
    their process.
 2. Then CASE their work. Copy and steal everything that you like.
 3. Inject some personalization into the standard questions.
 4. Beware of the three discovery crimes. Ask a colleague to TAG you.
For the experienced SE:
 1. Mentor a newer SE. Y ou’ll learn why you do what you do and not just the how .
 2. Look for opportunities to guide the customer toward a competitive advantage.
 3. Use the magic wand liberally .
 4. Update the discovery questionnaire and share it globally .

Chapter Goals Business Value Discovery 1: 
Pains and Gains
If you make listening and observation your occupation, 
you will gain more than you can by talk.
           —Robert Baden Powell
B
usiness value discovery (BVD) is the art and 
the science of understanding why your cus -
tomer is even thinking about buying some tech -
nology. Once you clearly understand those busi-
ness drivers, you are in a much better position to 
link your technical feature set to the satisfaction 
of those business drivers. Technical discovery will 
supply some of the basis for your requirements, 
if only because of the “that is the only way it 
will work” factor. Business discovery delivers the 
economic impetus to move the project forward. 
BVD is an essential skill set for any SE, yet it 
is an area where few companies provide much 
internal training. Instead, they prefer to train 
on the technology rather than on the use of the 
technology. Unfortunately, this approach means 
that the SE will revert to features and speeds and 
feeds at the first sign of resistance from the cus -
tomer. Now let us look at an entire BVD flow 
and use a simple and sales process–independent 
method to drill down into the business issues. 
Understand the three types 
of customer pain and how 
to apply them.
Learn the reversing tactic: 
how not to talk about your 
technology and maintain 
focus on the business.
Be able to gather an initial 
list of key business issues.

## Page 69

48 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 49
Hint: Discovery Is a Continual Process
Do not view any part of discovery as a one-time meeting or just an early step in the sales 
cycle. Discovery should be a continuous process throughout the sales cycle. You should view 
every meeting as an opportunity to learn something more about your customer and even 
seek out future possibilities. If your company operates in a consumption-based revenue 
model, it is hard to differentiate between where one opportunity stops and the next one 
starts. 
The Sales Engineer Concept of Pain
Every principal sales methodology implicitly or explicitly deals with the con-
cept of customer pain. That’s a business or technical situation causing a per-
sistent or sporadic problem that inconveniences and annoys the customer, 
often resulting in a loss of business. You may think that customers always 
want their pains to disappear. However, that’s not always the case. For the 
SE, it helps to divide these customer pains into three separate categories, 
each of which results in a different sales strategy and even a different ap -
proach to discovery.
Latent Pain
Latent pain is an issue that the customer either does not know that they 
have, or it is an issue they have lived with for so long that they are used to 
it and do not believe it is important enough to fix. Both versions—the un -
known and the known latent pain—are common. For example, we have all 
been in a meeting where the customer explains their current architecture. 
You take one look at it and are horrified because it has multiple single points 
of failures, duplicates data, leaves a path for hackers, and so on. Those are 
examples of unknown latent pains. A known latent pain occurs because a 
process works well (but inefficiently) or the customer is so used to taking 
corrective action that it no longer seems like a problem. Remember that it’s 
never a problem until the customer says it is a problem. 
Hint: Personalizing Pain
As an explanation of latent pain, here is my personal example. I have a slight pain behind 
my right knee. It’s been there for 30 years and really doesn’t bother me. No doctor will 
ever get me to spend money and submit to an MRI scan for further analysis. I live with it. 
It will be a classic known latent pain (until it isn’t one anymore). I use this example as a 
setup for the next type of pain.

---

## Page 70

48 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 49
You will also see latent pain manifest itself in various other ways early 
in the sales cycle. You sell into latent pain whenever a salesperson asks you 
to give a “general product suite overview.” You are hoping that something 
you show provokes a reaction from the customer and converts a latent 
pain into a current one. Latent pain is not necessarily bad, but you need 
to transform it into something more current to establish a real workable 
opportunity. 
Current Pain
Current pain is precisely what you would expect. It is the business equiva -
lent of “it hurts, and it’s bleeding—please help me!” Practically every use 
case or playbook example developed by marketing relates to solving a cur -
rent pain. For example, it is common for a sales team to create a slide that 
shows five or six of the most common pains they encounter within their 
customer base. That can serve as a prompter for further discussion. 
Also, remember not to fall into the discovery trap of accept. Just because 
the customer says it is a current pain does not necessarily mean that it is a 
current pain or even a business issue pain at all. You still need to verify it. 
CASE STUDY: Making the Pain Real
In our classes, I often ask the students what they might say or do to make my 
latent knee pain into a current pain that would cause me to take action. The 
responses fall into two categories. First, some gleefully try to scare me:
• “It might be a symptom of something worse.”
• “That happened to my friend, and he ended up breaking his kneecap.”
• “You’ll end up in a wheelchair and won’t be able to travel.”
Then others, more cheerfully, try to incentivize me:
• “Your granddaughter will start playing sports next year. Don’t you want to 
play with her and coach her?”
• “Your family is planning a Lord of the Rings hiking trip to New Zealand.”
 In both cases, they are trying to move me out of latent pain. One set of re -
sponses attempts to make the pain real and push me into current pain. The 
second set focuses on gains and pulls me into current pain.
 Which is more effective, and how would your customers respond? Personally 
(especially if the latent pain is minor), I react more positively to the pull of a 
gain than the push of a pain. Although it is not the job of a sales engineer to 
scare the customer, you certainly can explain what happens if they do nothing. 
E E E E

---

## Page 71

50 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 51
Vision Pain
Vision pain is created when the customer, either by themselves or with your 
help, looks ahead in time and decides they wish to act now (instead of in, 
say, six months). Common causes are as follows:
• Compliance;
• Governmental rules and regulations;
• Expansion into a new market;
• Proactively attacking a competitor;
• A pending audit;
• Mergers and acquisitions;
• Digital transformation.
You will usually hear this expressed by a senior executive who is en -
gaged in strategic versus tactical thinking. At least you now know there is 
some form of executive support behind the project.
Hint: Look for Pipeline Balance
Look at your pipeline of deals. Categorize each of them with an L, a C, or a V—sometimes, 
you may need to use two of the letters. Then, examine the balance between the three 
categories. If your pipe has a lot of Ls, you are in for a challenging year as you will need 
to convert the latent into current or vision to make a sale. The perfect pipeline contains a 
mixture of each type. If there are zero Ls, you are either in a hot market (congratulations!) 
or missing out on some opportunities.  
It does take some trust for a customer to open up and start talking about 
their pains with an outsider. The stronger the relationship and the percep -
tion of value the sales team supplies, the easier the process will become. 
Pain gathering is also a part of the sales process that requires considerable 
teamwork between the SE and the salesperson. It is just as important that 
they leave no stone unturned as not to step on top of each other’s questions. 
Cooperation and careful planning are essential. 
How Not to Talk About Y our Technology
I mentioned patience several times in the previous chapter. Combined with 
good active listening, it is one of the foundational skills for any sales engi -
neer. When working with a customer, there may be many occasions when 
that customer, a partner, or even the salesperson tries to get you talking 
about your technology portfolio and capabilities. As that is an area of com -

---

## Page 72

50 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 51
fort, knowledge, and pride, we are usually delighted to get into great de -
tail about our products and services. It is too early! If you start speaking 
about your technology before you understand why the customer asks you 
the question and determine their motivations, you are guessing. This situ -
ation is the most common way discovery crime #3 (guess) comes into play. 
Known as premature solutioneering, it will lead you into serious trouble. It 
is one of the behaviors that salespeople complain about the most to SE 
leadership.
The questions posed by the customer may be very tempting to answer, 
particularly if they fall into the sweet spot of your knowledge. An Italian SE 
once described it as the customer “offering me the keys to the Ferrari.” So, 
of course, it is very tempting to grab the keys and go for a drive, but there 
are probably a few questions you need to ask first.
In the business world, most decisions1 are driven either by pain, as not-
ed earlier, or by gain. Every sales process in the world teaches you how to 
uncover pain. Yet pain accounts for about 80% of the opportunities in the 
pipeline of the average tech company. The remaining 20% are driven by 
gain—the sense of something positive happening in the future. One of the 
differences between a good SE and a great world-class SE is that the great 
SE looks for gains as well as pains. The situation you are in—where the cus-
tomer is asking you technology questions when you are seeking business 
answers—is the perfect opportunity to pivot to that gain. 
The technique of staying on task and flipping back to pain or gain is 
known as reversing. This is best illustrated with a few examples. 
Your customer says: “Robin, we need to create a collection of comput-
ing power, storage, and network systems that serve resources to users on 
demand. That cloud infrastructure must be elastic and support tasks like 
big-batch processing or disaster recovery that burden our traditional, on-
premise data center resources.2 Can you help us?”
Robin may be tempted to start speaking about the impressive capa -
bilities of hybrid elastic cloud infrastructure—and that is exactly the wrong 
thing to do. So instead, Robin should reply: “Yes, we can. I’d love to learn 
a bit more about those burdens you mentioned and how they’re impacting 
your users.”
That is a reversal to pain or the current state. In effect, you are asking 
the customer to explain more about their current situation. For example, a 
second response might be: “Yes, absolutely, and we’ve helped thousands of 
1. And sometimes curiosity.
2. I did not make this up. I took that sentence directly from the website of a well-
known tech company.

---

## Page 73

52 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 53
customers do that. Looking ahead 12 months, if that kind of infrastructure 
was in place, what would you be able to do that you can’t do now?” 
That is a reversal to future gains. You are taking the customer out of the 
present and walking them into the future. (I call this puppy dogs, unicorns, 
sunsets, and walks on the beach.) So many people are stuck in their current 
problem set that they rarely think about what a pain-free life means in the 
future. Just linking your product set to something positive in the future cre-
ates an emotional bond and will help you out further down the sales cycle.
There are some critical guidelines around reversing:
1. There is a practical (and cultural) limit to the number of times you 
can reverse a client in a call. You are answering a question with a 
question, and that can become frustrating. A general guideline is 
three per hour, so choose wisely.
2. Where appropriate, start your answer with a yes or similar positive 
phrase. 
3. The response needs to sound natural, as if it were an offer to pro -
vide a more focused answer in a few minutes in return for getting 
information now. You should create a verbal library of your rever-
sals. One of my favorites is “I could talk  about that subject all day 
(and my salesperson says I often do), so if you could tell me a little 
bit more about [x] I can give you a far shorter and to-the-point an-
swer.” 
4. Beware of using the word but. Answering “Yes, but . . . ” psycho -
logically disconnects the first yes from the rest of your response and 
can make you sound negative. Substitute and instead.3 The only but 
a sales engineer should use is the one that they sit upon.  
You can reverse your sales partner as well. Every SE has been in a situ-
ation where the salesperson asks them to do something totally unexpected, 
and you have no idea why they ask. “Robin, this would be a great time 
to tell the customer about our reverse osmotic trans-warp hybrid transfer 
protocols.”
Robin can then use the same technique and reply: “Thanks, John. I was 
thinking the same thing and (turning to the customer) would first love to 
know how they handle those protocols today.”
How, when, and why you reverse your sales partner is a function of the 
trust between the two of you. If you are traveling down a path and have an 
idea of the eventual destination, it is your decision. You should debrief with 
3. This is wonderful conversational advice in all aspects of your life—personal and 
professional.

---

## Page 74

52 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 53
your sales partner immediately after the call is complete to understand the 
situation no matter what course you take. You may have missed something 
or the rep may have been over-eager to highlight competitive technology. 
Next, you need to agree on how to handle this in the future.
Hint: Help Me Out with This
Some sales methodologies promote the tactic of playing dumb and asking the customer to 
(re)explain something because you didn’t understand the topic or the scope of the ques -
tion. Be careful with this tactic as it’s a helpful technique for salespeople (“I must have 
missed that earlier, could you just explain why . . . ”) but should be used infrequently 
by the SE. Balance humility with the fact that you are supposed to be one of the more 
competent people in the meeting.
The Heart of the Process
The days of being able to walk into an executive’s office and have them 
patiently sit down with you and explain all their problems are now long 
gone. We have consigned the “what keeps you up at night?” strategy to the 
dustbin of history. You must have a solid grasp of the issues before you step 
foot into the executive office and arrive with ideas and solutions instead. 
That means that you run a lot of interviews and sessions with personnel 
lower down the organizational ladder and must collate and synthesize their 
responses. This process is intended for middle management, as most indi -
vidual contributors, especially within IT, won’t have access to any financial 
data.
The first part of the process is subdivided into four simple steps. Al -
though there may be a different terminology, every sales methodology that 
your sales partner may or may not follow has similar steps. The point is 
that it does not matter what you call these steps. It is the end result that is 
important. Have some fun, enjoy the process, and don’t take it too literally. 
If the customer gets ahead of you and provides valuable data in the middle 
of compiling a list of issues, accept it gratefully and write it down before get-
ting back on track. That flexibility and willingness to adapt will help to put 
the customer at ease. The four main steps are listed as follows:
1. Get a complete list of all the key business issues (KBIs).
2. Verify with the customer that it is a complete list.
3. Ask permission to add your own issues (if appropriate).
4. Prioritize the list.

---

## Page 75

54 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 55
Gather a Complete List
This step aims to get a written list of all the business issues that are facing the 
customer. Some of these issues may lie outside of the solution set for your 
portfolio—that is OK. You will gain an even clearer view of the business en -
vironment and may even engage a partner to help with those other issues. 
Some customers will be eager to share their problems (they are indeed in 
current pain), while others may be reluctant. The key is to get the conversa-
tion started and then to be quiet and listen. Remember the art of patience 
and reserve your opinions and judgment until later. Once the customer does 
start talking, your biggest issue may become time management and keeping 
the focus on the relevant issues. 
This step is a highly personalized part of the conversation, and unless 
you are a single product company that can solve a limited set of issues, there 
is no set script to follow. However, here are a few questions you can use to 
initiate the conversation.
• So why did you invite us in to meet with you today?
• What is it that your boss is concerned about?
• At your last staff meeting on this subject, what did you talk about?
• We’ve done [x] at other companies, is that of interest to you?
• Can you share with me some of your concerns about your current 
environment?
The most important process step is that you should not deep dive into 
any single issue before identifying all of the issues. Over the years and in our 
workshops, we see that the first issue the customer gives you is rarely the 
most important one. Sixty percent of the time in a safe SE-to-SE roleplay 
and 85% of the time in customer situations, the first issue discussed is not 
critical. It is a great illustration of why you need to list everything before div-
ing deeper into any issue. The rule is that #1 <> #1! 
CASE STUDY: Learning from the Physician 
Continuing the medical theme—every year, I visit Dr. Kitchen and have an an -
nual physical. The process starts with some lightweight social chat and a short 
interview, and then Dr. K gets out her tablet and asks, “John, what would you 
like to speak about today? What is bothering you?” 
 “I have this mild pain behind my right knee,” I reply.
 You would think that her next question would be “How long have you had 
that pain?” or “How much does it hurt on a scale of 1 to 10?” or even “Can you 
describe the pain in more detail?” Those are all tremendous diagnostic ques -
tions, and they are all the wrong types of questions. Instead, Dr. K. says, “Hmm, 
mild pain behind your right knee; what else is bothering you?”

---

## Page 76

54 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 55
 She does that because she is the subject matter medical expert, and I am not. 
She intends to gather as much data as possible before making any diagnosis or 
deciding upon her next set of questions. Maybe three or four items I mention 
are symptoms of a deeper issue? 
E E E E
Verify That the List Is Complete
This interaction should be the easiest and fastest of the steps. Once the cus-
tomer feels they have exhausted their list (“Guess that’s everything . . . ”) 
and your prompting has no other effect , it is time to stop and summarize. 
You have a written list and should display it and read it back to the custom-
er. Then, although it may sound a little sales-like, you can ask “If we fixed 
all these items, would you be happy and consider it a success?”
If the customer says yes, then you move on to step 3. If the customer 
says no, then you reply and ask, “What did I miss?” Gather that response 
and then revalidate the list again. 
You now have a complete list of the customers KBIs, as seen through 
the eyes of the person you have been interviewing. You should now make 
a mental first-pass estimate on which items your company can help with 
and which ones you cannot. 
Ask Permission to Add Y our Own
It is now time for you to show your expertise and suggest additional prob -
lems that the customer has not considered, or at least not yet mentioned. 
If your sales organization has embraced the concept of the challenger sale,4 
then this is the most straightforward entry point for the SE. It is a wonder-
ful opportunity to either expand the sale or reframe a problem and add a 
competitive differentiator.
The higher up the organization chart you travel, the more likely this 
technique is to work. Because of the trust and relationship dynamics, it is 
easier for the SE to perform this challenge or additive step than it is for the 
salesperson.5 The customer is far more likely to accept any input from you 
based on the helpful engineer aspect of your job title. In the absence of any 
long-term sales relationship, the customer may feel upsold when a salesper-
son suggests additional problems or issues. 
4. Adamson, B., and M. Dixon, The Challenger Side: Taking Control of the Customer 
Conversation, Portfolio Press, 2011.
5. Top salespeople already know this and incorporate the SE challenge into their 
account plans.

---

## Page 77

56 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 57
Executives tell us that one of the major values that an SE brings to the 
relationship is knowing, in sufficient detail, what other customers are doing 
with your products. That includes both the standard capabilities and some of 
the off-brand and unusual uses. You can leverage that value by referring to 
your other customers or the general marketplace. 
“Many of my customers [who are in this same vertical/who are the same 
size as you/who are facing these kinds of business issues] are also looking to 
[solve a problem that you can help them with]. Is that something that might 
be of interest to you?”
If the customers say “no,” you have made the effort and planted the 
thought in their minds. If the client accepts your suggestion, then get their 
permission to add it to the list. Don’t spend the next 15 minutes talking 
about it; just state that you’ll come back to the idea in the following steps. 
Prioritize the List
You now have a list of the key business issues facing that customer, typ -
ically from three to eight items in length. It is time to prioritize the list. 
Again, the straightforward approach is the best: “Which one of these is most 
important?”
About half the time, you will get a cooperative customer who has their 
priorities set out and understands the relative priorities. You review the list, 
and they will reply: “In priority order, that would be items three, four, and 
one. So those are the top three for me.”
The other half of the time, the customer will be uncertain and may re -
ply with either “All of them are important!” or “I’ve never really thought it 
through.”
Unless there is an undeniably obvious place to start, pass the initiative 
back to the customer: “I hear that a lot. So which one would you like to start 
with now?”
Your customer then makes a choice. It may indeed be the number one 
issue, it could be the issue they know the most about, or it could be the is -
sue their boss just yelled at them about right before your meeting. You will 
quickly determine the underlying reason as you go through the second part 
of the BVD process. Unless you have any indications to the contrary, it is 
sufficient to get the top three priorities and stop with those. 
I prefer to run the priority exercise on an individual basis to get as many 
inputs as possible. Working with a larger group, especially from multiple de-
partments, allows each group to set its priorities. You may get some interest-
ing insights about corporate politics. If a group has trouble agreeing, suggest 
that everyone gets three votes and tally the results that way. Let culture be 
your guide as to whether you allow voting.

---

## Page 78

56 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains 57
CASE STUDY: The Beginnings of Business Value Discovery 
The foundational steps of BVD were created way back in 1988 at Oracle. Un -
der the leadership of Tom Siebel, we created a brand-new set of vertical sales 
units. I was the SE manager for pharmaceuticals. We already had an excellent 
install base in the R&D divisions of Merck, Johnson and Johnson, and Glaxo. 
That caused a strange problem in that any time the salespeople or SEs heard 
of a customer issue with clinical trials, new drug applications, or adverse drug 
reactions, they pounced! It meant that although we generated a steady stream 
of business for the Oracle database we were missing out on many other oppor-
tunities in manufacturing, human resources, sales, and so on. So the very first 
version of the BVD worksheet was designed6 by me and two sales managers—
Sid Amster and Marc Schnabolk. The only purpose was to prompt our staff to 
ask, “And what else?” instead of diving into an immediate R&D issue.
 Fast forward more than three decades to a security company selling firewalls. 
The customer says, “We have a major problem handling the amount of encrypt-
ed traffic coming into our system.”  That’s a competitive strength of this com -
pany, and the salesperson jumps in to start explaining firewall options and pric-
ing. The SE says, “Just a minute, so that we can get a complete picture—what 
other issues are you seeing right now?” The customer continues to explain how 
they are overwhelmed with security logs, API metadata, and alerts. Three new 
business opportunities eventually opened up within that one account. Get all 
the issues out on the table first!
E E E E
Summary
You now have a complete list of the customer’s key business issues, have 
verified that it is a complete list (which includes capturing any nice-to-
haves) and potentially added an item or two of your own based on your 
market knowledge. Finally, you worked with the customer to prioritize the 
list. The result is one set of issues, from one individual or one group, to carry 
forward into the next stage of BVD, which involves a deeper dive into each 
of the top three issues.
Much like the earlier chapter on technical discovery, patience is the 
watchword. The less you talk and offer opinions, the more you will learn. 
The first stages of discovery allow the SE to establish credibility subtly and 
gently. Do not strive to be the most intelligent person in the room—even if 
you actually are!
6. Using WordPerfect and Lotus 1-2-3 under IBM DOS 4.0 for you historians.

Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Make a list of the common latent, current, and vision pains that you encounter.
 2. Ask your salesperson or SE manager to keep you on the business track.
 3. Build a set of reversing statements and strategize their use with your sales partner.
 4. Watch how other senior SEs use the challenge/add step in discovery .
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Build a KBI worksheet for your sales partner. Then, if they can conduct this initial 
    discovery , you don’t have to!
 2. Create a set of slides or a whiteboard with the most common set of pains.
 3. Build out some customer stories to support you in the challenge/add step.
