The BVD Quadrants
The customer now believes that they need help with one or more business 
problems and potentially a few business gains. We do not yet understand 
how the customer knows that it is a problem, what the future looks like 
when the problem disappears, and the monetary size of the problem. You 
are seeking the answers to four separate sets of questions. Start with priority 
number one and move through each successive key business issue. You may 
need to focus on just the top three issues.
How Do Y ou Know It Is a Problem (Evidence)?
You ask a series of questions to have the customer explain why they think 
it is a problem. These are situational questions and will revolve around the 
current state of affairs. You are seeking a description of how things work 
now (or don’t), including the good, bad, and ugly. It is acceptable to al -
low the customer to wallow in misery, mainly if you think they are more 
inclined to view things as latent rather than current pain. During this set of 
questions, you’ll hear a lot of descriptive phrases and some opinions. 
What Is the Size of the Problem (Impact)?
Based on the description and evidence you’ve just accumulated, what is the 
overall impact of the problem on the business? Ideally, you are looking for 
numerical data, which, as you’ll see later in this chapter, you can translate 
into time, money, or people.
How Will Y ou Know When It Is Fixed (Evidence)?
It is now time to move out of the current state and walk the customer into 
the future. You are asking for the customer’s subjective success criteria. 
You’ll often hear that they just want the pain to stop and the problem to 
go away. “The system stops crashing,” or “The users stop complaining,” or 
“We meet our deadlines/service-level agreements.” This is the point where 
you can link back to any potential gains or the solution of a vision pain and 
ask, “And what happens then?” Be prepared for silence as the customer 
processes this happy situation and be ready to suggest some ideas of what 
success looks like, once again based on your customer experience: “Many 
of my customers find that not only can they meet their deadlines, but that 
they can actually provide even better service and charge a premium for it.” 
What Is the Impact of the Problem Going Away?
This step is the future state counterpart to question number two. When the 
problem is fixed, what will be the monetary impact upon the business? It

---

## Page 82

60 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 2: Time, Money, and People 61
may well be the resolution of the current state issues, or it can also be addi-
tive with some gains you have uncovered through question number three.
As a guideline, if you take notes, evidence questions will result in tex -
tual answers, while impact questions should yield both text and numbers. 
So, what types of questions should you ask, and how should you phrase 
those questions?
Questions About Questions 
Did you know that there are three main types of sales questions? That is a 
closed-ended question. There are also open-ended questions and the mys -
teriously named nonquestion questions. They each play a role, not only in 
discovery, but in the entire sales cycle. 
The Closed-Ended Question
A closed-ended question usually results in a yes or a no. (Occasionally, you 
will get a maybe, or it depends, but those are the vagaries of SE life.) Sales -
people will tell you that closed-ended questions are terrible, as they do 
not promote conversation. In addition, closed-ended questions supply too 
many chances for a customer to say no. In English, closed-ended questions 
are often prefaced with a small verb such as can, do, or are. An example of 
a closed-ended question might be, “Do you have the capability to restore 
from an offsite backup?”
Closed-ended questions do have utility for the SE, and though they suf-
fer from a poor reputation, they are superb for closing out options, walking 
down a decision tree, or deliberately making the customer say “No, we can’t 
do that.” For example,
• “Do you have offices in Singapore?”
• “Are you subject to GDPR compliance in your U.K. and Ireland 
operations?”
• “Can you conduct a real-time daily profitability analysis for each 
retail location?”
While you don’t want to ask too many closed-ended questions, espe -
cially consecutively (as that can sound like an interrogation), they can dra-
matically shut down a range of options and serve a purpose in making a 
latent pain more current by provoking a series of negative responses from a 
customer. (Back to the knee—“John, could you run five miles?”)

---

## Page 83

62 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 2: Time, Money, and People 63
The Open-Ended Question
An open-ended question promotes a back-and-forth conversation. It re -
quires multiple words or sentences in response and is much beloved by 
salespeople and sales trainers alike. The vast majority of sales questions, and 
in fact questions in your everyday life, are open ended. If you are an experi-
enced SE with much situational fluency or consider yourself verbally adept, 
you do not need a BVD question framework. For the rest of us, I recommend 
the 5W+H framework. 5W+H covers who, what, when, where, how (many/
much), and which?
Hint: Why You Should Be Careful of Why?
You will notice that 5W+H does not include a why. That might seem a significant omis -
sion as everyone always wants to know about the why. However, in many cultures, why 
can be taken as confrontational and judgmental. A student once told me that in his part 
of the world, why had an implied “you idiot” on the end of the sentence, as in “why was 
it designed that way, you idiot?” You can rephrase every why question in discovery to 
something softer and gentler. Leave the why questions in this part of the sales cycle to your 
sales partner. 
For each 5W+H question type,1 build out your list of common business-
oriented nontechnical questions you can use with a client. You will not use 
all of them, so consider this a starter pack for your conversations. Some 
sample questions are shown in Table 7.1.
The Nonquestion Question
The nonquestion question (NQQ) is a stunningly underused yet amazing 
effective technique. It is the art of promoting and moving along a conversa-
tion without explicitly asking a question. Of course, you already do this in 
everyday life, without even thinking too much about it. Classic examples 
are as follows:
• Tell me more . . .
• Wow! I never thought about it that way.
• Please continue.
• I’d love to learn more about that.
• And even the simple “uh-huh” with an approving nod or waggle of 
the head. 
1. As you translate 5W+H outside of English you’ll see that you don’t always get a 
clean mnemonic. German is a perfect 6W, Portuguese works with a 5Q+0, French 
yields a 4Q +C+O, and Spanish gives you a 3C +2Q+D. It’s the framework that 
counts.

---

## Page 84

62 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 2: Time, Money, and People 63
Even with the most cooperative customer, you can reach a point where 
the number of questions just becomes overwhelming, and they will shut 
down or minimize their responses. Companies such as Gong.io and Chorus 
record sales calls, use AI to analyze the contents, and match them to the 
results. Their research shows that the “best” sales calls have between 8 and 
12 questions. Fewer than eight questions, and you probably don’t acquire 
enough customer information. More than 12 questions and the customer 
starts to feel as if it is an interrogation.
Nonquestion questions don’t count! They are neutral and are an abso-
lute gift to the SE profession.
Your overall questioning technique should use a mixture of the three 
question types. Open-ended 5W+H questions form the basis of the conver-
sation, supplemented with closed questions to gain some certainty about 
current or future issues or raise uncertainty about latent pain. Finally, 
sprinkle in the NQQs to show enthusiasm and to obtain more detail from 
the customer. 
CASE STUDY: The Amazing Conversationalist
My lovely wife Allison accompanies me on many of my trips and often attends 
team-building dinners held during a training session. She is five times the ex -
trovert I am, and, although she doesn’t know too much about sales and sales 
engineering, she can hold a conversation with anyone. As chance would have

it, we were separated at one dinner, and Allison sat next to a VP of sales. Back at 
our hotel later that evening, Allison said to me,
 “Wow, that guy can really talk. I barely got a word in edgeways!” 
 The following day the VP of sales approached me and said, “I enjoyed dinner 
last night. Your wife is a great conversationalist, and I learned so much about 
learning styles” (she’s a teacher). 
 I relayed that back to Allison, who commented, “You’re kidding! I just let him 
talk and then asked him a few questions.”
 Her point was that it’s all in the questions you ask and the appearance of 
listening. 
E E E E
Answers About Answers 
After all that focus on asking the right type and style of question, what 
happens next? What are you looking to extract from the answers to learn 
more about the business issues? There are at least two ways of processing 
the responses. The first is using 3WM+M. That is the three wonderful metrics 
+ mission approach.
The 3WM+M Approach 
The 3WM+M principle is that one of four high-level business drivers causes 
every technology purchase. So, how can you help your customer increase 
their revenues, how can you help decrease their costs, mitigate their risk, and/
or satisfy their mission? At the most basic level, revenue is incoming mon -
ey from sales or the equivalent of your paycheck. Cost is outgoing money 
spent, like expenses you incur for rent, mortgage, or cell phone. Risk is both 
corporate and professional risk, and mission satisfies a higher-level vision 
and purpose. So, to raise profits, you can either increase revenues, decrease 
costs, or do both! 
When you listen to your customers, you are looking for an opportunity 
to match their problem to one or more of the 3WM+M buckets. Therefore, 
you need a list of how your product portfolio can realistically do that. I’d 
encourage you to be realistic and remember you are in sales as opposed to 
marketing. 
There are two special cases for 3WM+M. The first involves government 
or local agencies. They often have a strange view of what revenue and costs 
mean. For many agencies, unless they are a taxing authority, revenue is

---

## Page 86

64 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 2: Time, Money, and People 65
what they appropriate from higher government in a budget. In many coun-
tries, the last thing a government minister or bureaucrat wants to do is 
shrink their staff and cut costs. Staff size is synonymous with power. Not 
everyone thinks like you.
Hint: Beware of Headcount Reductions
Your software may allow a customer to increase operational efficiencies and reduce the 
required headcount to complete a particular task. That’s usually a good thing for senior 
management and not good for the people involved. It is indeed not a benefit you want 
to highlight to those who may be automated out of a job. The code executives under -
stand is that they can deploy their personnel into other more effective positions within 
the company. 
The second special case is for mission. Always investigate the mission, 
mission statement, or values of a company. You can usually find this on 
their homepage. If you can then link your portfolio back to the satisfaction 
of a mission statement, you can win a deal, especially with government 
agencies, NGOs, nonprofits, and some larger F500 companies. An example 
is a local government that states, “Our mission is to serve our voters better.” 
That encompasses many opportunities. 
Most vendors have a sweet spot with two of the main 3WM categories 
and are weaker in the third. A software infrastructure vendor (affectionate-
ly known as an IT plumbing company) selling networking, backup, and re-
covery or a standard hardware company will have a stronger focus on cost 
reduction and risk mitigation and may struggle with revenue generation. 
Application companies tend to do better with revenue generation. When 
searching for revenue opportunities, look at the revenue generation depart-
ments of your clients. Looking internally at your company, those depart -
ments are sales (and sales engineering), support, consulting, and education. 
When you can increase the productivity of anyone in those groups, guar -
antee high availability of a customer-facing website, or even get products 
to market faster than before so sales can sell them—those are all potential 
revenue gains.
Hint: Revenue Trumps Everything
A few years ago, I had the pleasure of interviewing a panel of about 20 chief financial 
officers. I asked them if they would rather have 10 million in added revenue or 10 million 
in cost savings. They all said “Both!” When asked to choose one, 95% of them said rev -
enue. The number one reason was that revenue is harder to attain and rewarded better 
than cost reductions, which could be achieved at any point.

---

## Page 87

66 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 2: Time, Money, and People 67
CASE STUDY: The Programmer Productivity Software RFP (Part 2, contin-
ued from Chapter 4)
The five vendors each took about half a day to make their pitches. By the time 
we had listened to the final presentation, my brain was numb. However, one 
vendor (the one who took the time to conduct some extra discovery) seemed 
to do a better job than the others in linking our specific needs to their feature 
set. They had discovered that we were launching a new product, that IT was on 
the critical path for delivery, and that we’d delay the product launch by almost 
eight weeks. That would cost our company $8 million in revenue and untold 
missed market opportunities. It also put me, as the CIO, on the hot seat in every 
executive meeting.
 My favored vendor linked every capability in their product to speeding up the 
delivery and launch of a product, to the point where they would explicitly say, 
“and, John, this is how you can take another five days off the cycle.” I was confi-
dent they could deliver. However, when we scored the actual RFP responses and 
presentation content, my favorite vendor came second. They scored 70 points, 
trailing the number one vendor who scored 74 points. I exercised my executive 
authority and chose the vendor who came second, as they did a much better 
job of understanding my business (and my personal risk). I know that annoyed 
some of the developers, who saw some extra bells and whistles in the top-scor -
ing vendor, but ultimately my job was on the line.
 The coda is that my vendor fulfilled their promises. We did indeed cut weeks 
off the development time, and I could sit in the executive meeting and watch the 
board grill the VP of marketing instead of me.
E E E E
The Time, Money , and People Approach 
This approach is my personal favorite, as it is more likely to lead to hard 
numbers and data with which you can make a business case. You are look -
ing for any answer that has a reference to time (speed up by two days), 
money (avoid a $250,000 maintenance payment), or people (decrease from 
8 hours to 30 minutes). These three categories are what economists call 
fungible, in that they convert from one to the other. Thus, you can quickly 
move from one type to another with the proper conversion factors in place 
(for example, knowing € per hour). 
Hint: Using the First Law of Business Discovery
“Every business issue can be reduced to a single number. Either that number is too large, 
and someone wants to make it smaller, or the number is too small, and someone wants 
to make it larger.” The art of being a consultative sales engineer is to find that number, 
understand which direction it needs to move, by how much, who cares about it, and finally 
what that movement is worth to somebody important enough at the customer organization 
to have a budget.

---

## Page 88

66 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 2: Time, Money, and People 67
The tricky part of all this is to determine what it is—the number. It 
may be response time, percentage of abandoned e-shopping carts, customer 
churn, cost of goods sold, market share—they vary by job role and industry. 
Once you know what it is, you should ask five more questions, as summa -
rized in Table 7.2.
1. How do you define it? This question assures a standard definition. For 
example, the amount of time it takes to complete a task within IT 
might be tracked from the moment a ServiceNow ticket is opened 
until it is closed. 
2. What is it now? This is the current measurement. 
3. What would you realistically like it to be? This is the future state.
4. What is the short-term value of that change?  Although the short term 
is subjective, a good starting point is 12 × monthly, or an annual 
amount. 
5. What is the longer-term value of that change if it continues?  The long 
term may be as short as 24–36 months.
6. (Optional) How long would you expect to collect those gains?  This addi-
tional question gives you the lifetime of the project. Hardware and 
fixed assets may have a longer financial lifespan than software. 
So if the customer says: “We need to do a better job of standing up 
a new test and development system for incremental code releases to the 
cloud.”
Using the time-money-people conversion, 3,744 people hours is equiv-
alent to 1.9 people years2 would then multiply that by an annual salary cost 
to arrive at anticipated first-year savings. 
2. Using an approximation of 2,000 work hours per year.
Table 7.2  
Time, Money, People Conversion
Define “it” Tracked by ServiceNow from ticket entry to closure by project lead
Current state? It takes four people a week to get it set, and we do this twice a month
Future state? One person—a morning
Short-term value? (20 – 0.5) = 19.5 days per occurrence
19.5 × 2 / per month × 12 months × 8 hours/day = 3,744 hours 
Long-term value? Probably the same amount; it won’t decrease
Lifespan? Two years maximum

## Page 89

68 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 2: Time, Money, and People 69
Y ou Can Be the Expert
In a classroom environment and a perfect world, the customer not only has 
all the evidence and impact data but is willing to provide that data to you 
during discovery. Unfortunately, that is not the case, and you may not be 
able to complete the BVD process for various reasons. The two most com -
mon are that (a) the customer does not have the data or (b) the customer is 
unwilling to share the data for competitive or proprietary reasons.
Before you accept that the customer does not have the data, always ask, 
“Is there anyone else within your company who might have that informa -
tion? If so, can you introduce me to them?” 
For a large enough opportunity, after consultation with the entire ac -
count team, you could ask, “Can we (or one of our partners) help you get 
that data?”
In other instances, when the customer does not have a single data point 
or is unwilling to share their internal numbers, you can yet again play the 
subject matter expert card. Using the previous example, we arrived at a sav-
ing of 1.9 person-years. You ask the customer for the fully burdened cost 
(salary + overhead) of a typical worker in their company. If they do not 
know or won’t share the information, then it is up to you to provide it. 
“Well, a typical full cost for that kind of worker is usually about 150,000. 
Does that sound right?”
The customer will either respond that it is too high, too low, or a reason-
able estimate. If the 150,000 is acceptable, you multiply 150,000 by 1.9 and 
get 285,000 per year in productivity savings. It is a start, and we only need 
an estimate. 
The expert responsibility falls upon the SE as opposed to sales or mar -
keting. It is up to you (or the extended team) to have a set of valuable facts 
and figures at your disposal that allows you to have these conversations. For 
example, if you were unaware of the typical worker cost, that part of the 
conversation comes to an abrupt halt and may stall the sales cycle for a few 
days. These valuable facts and figures remove friction and delay from the 
sales process and enhance your credibility.
The Preliminary Financial Checkpoint—a Cost/Impact 
Comparison
We now know that the customer has a set of problems, the size, and at least 
an estimate of the impact of those problems and potential monetary gains

---

## Page 90

68 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Business Value Discovery 2: Time, Money, and People 69
or savings. The next step is to quickly compare your solution’s estimated 
cost to those gains and savings and then calibrate it with the customer. This 
step is not a complete return on investment/total cost of ownership (TCO)/
value engineering analysis; it is a literal back-of-the-napkin calculation to 
see if the project makes financial sense. I learned incredibly early in my 
career that the customer has a building full of staff whose sole purpose is 
to calculate project economics. That was not an area for the average SE to 
explore in any depth.3 Table 7.3 shows how it can be a practical qualify in/
out step. The eventual resolution of a mismatch in the matrix is usually the 
responsibility of the salesperson rather than the SE.
Summary
BVD is the setup for all future phases in the sales cycle. By understanding 
the business drivers, you can position the unique features of your product 
portfolio and directly relate them to any cost, revenue, or risk impact for 
your customer. BVD is a genuinely lightweight and adaptable process that 
can layer on top of any formal or informal sales methodology your sales -
force may use. The critical behavioral change is to hold back on your techni-
cal strengths and indulge your business curiosity. Apply that with patience 
and the right set of questions, and you can quickly become both a business 
and a technical sales engineer. You are now ready to directly link product 
features to the required business benefits and outcomes. 
3. Your company may have value engineers or business consultants who can do 
this for you.

Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Create a set of nonquestion questions for discovery .
 2. Match the anticipated business outcomes of your portfolio to the 3WM+M categories.
 3. Research an initial set of valuable facts and figures.
 4. Define your view of typical customer happiness. 
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Analyze your open/closed/nonquestion count and mix.
 2. Spend extra time on the mission part of 3WM+M.
 3. Build a library of time, money , and people examples. Share it! 
 4. Research existing customer examples of key business metrics (find the it!).

Chapter Goals The FABulous Sales Engineer
The greatest feature of every person is their kindness.
              —Ron Baratono
T
he chapter title may sound like clickbait from 
a dodgy website, yet being FABulous is a 
wonderful mnemonic and the backbone of the 
technology to business conversion performed by 
every sales engineer. 
Some Basic Definitions
A feature is a distinctive characteristic of a prod -
uct or a service. You can also think of it as a ca -
pability. As in “this device has 128 ports” or “this 
cream is pH balanced” or “real-time analytics.” 
Almost everything you read in a product speci -
fication sheet or see on most product marketing 
slides are features. 
An advantage is what the feature actually 
does and how that might help the user. Features 
directly create advantages. As in “this can sup -
port an entire department” or “it is gentle on 
your skin” or “immediately input and analyze 
your data.” The advantage should be something 
that can place your product or service in a favor-
able light for your customer.
A benefit is the result or outcome of an ad -
vantage and explicitly states what the feature 
Understand the difference 
between features, 
advantages, and benefits 
(FABs).
Relating benefits to solving 
business problems.
Learn how to apply FAB to 
your portfolio.

---

## Page 93

72 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The FABulous Sales Engineer 73
means to the customer. It will often have an emotional as well as a tangible 
financial viewpoint. To continue our explanation sequence, that means “you 
save money by only having to purchase and support a single box” or “no 
pain and no risk of skin damage” or “increase shopping cart value by making 
intelligent targeted upsell suggestions.”
The Sales Engineer Problem
Sales engineers love features. We absorb training about those features and 
are proud to understand the “bits and bytes” of those features and present 
and debate them in depth with our clients. It is a comfortable two-way love 
affair, and SEs tend to fall back into features when under stress and duress. 
We are usually strong with advantages as they are the abstract positives of 
the features we love to discuss. Unfortunately, that is where the process 
comes to a resounding halt, as most of us are useless at explicitly stating the 
benefits. In many cases, benefits are so blatantly obvious to us as engineers 
that we don’t bother to make the connection. (“It runs twice as fast as the 
earlier version; how can anyone not understand why that is good?”)
We then compound the benefits problem with the demonstration scripts 
and the slideware that we use. Either they are full of features with minimal 
advantages and benefits, or the FAB combination is mislabeled, and features 
masquerade as benefits. As a proof point, examine your technical product 
decks. Almost every slide with multiple bullet points is chock-full of fea -
tures, with minimal corresponding As and Bs. Try it!
A Classic Nontechnical Example
Think about a coffee cup. Imagine I wish to sell you “Coffee Container 2.0 
(CC2 ).”1 We first need to look at version 1.0 (CC1). Let us imagine an old-
style white styrofoam cup you commonly see in corporate cafeterias and 
the cheaper convenience stores. Now visualize that old cup—what are the 
problems (or pains) with CC1? You undoubtedly think that it is hard to hold, 
that you could burn your hand, spill the contents, and it is environmentally 
unfriendly. Note those are facts, while the impact of the styrofoam on the 
beverage taste is more subjectively emotional.
Now I will upsell on you on CC2. It is a ceramic mug that has both a 
handle and a lid. Very few of you will care about the type of clay used in 
the ceramic firing process, the kiln’s temperature, or even the exact thermal 
properties of the materials. Yet, those are features of the mug. Instead, I will 
use the FAB explanation in Table 8.1 to arrive at the benefit.
1. Because containers are big right now.

---

## Page 94

72 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The FABulous Sales Engineer 73
Use this progression from a feature to a benefit: is-does-means.
Expanding previous definitions, a feature is something that exists or a 
capability that a product has. The advantage is what it does. The benefit is 
what that advantage means to the customer. You can work through this by 
internally thinking, “What this means to you, Mr. Customer is . . . ” and 
whatever would come out of your mouth after that is indeed the benefit. 
Hint: Make It Personal
You will not burn your fingers applies to everyone. It is a powerful yet generic benefit. 
However, if you emphasize the “play tennis” benefit to someone who plays tennis or any 
other racquet sport, that becomes a personal benefit they will remember.
A More Functional Example
Try this. “This bag measures 22” × 9” × 14” (or 56 cm × 22 cm × 35 cm).” 
Those measurements are clearly features of the bag, as they fit into the is/
has definition of a feature. Moving to the advantages—what does having a 
bag of that size allow you to do? Think of airplane travel as a clue. It will 
enable you to carry the bag onto an aircraft in most parts of the world. So, 
it is a small carry-on bag. That means you don’t have to check the bag before 
your flight, and therefore:
• You save time at your departure and arrival airports.
• You don’t have to pay to check the bag.
• Your bag won’t get lost in transit.
• Your bag won’t get delayed because of a tight connection. 
• You can conveniently access whatever is in the bag on the plane.
Notice in this instance, we generated at least five benefits from a sin -
gle feature/advantage combination. It is your job as the SE to understand 
which are most important to the user. 
Table 8.1 
The FABulous Coffee 
Container Feature “This mug has a handle and a lid.”
Advantage “That will give you a better grip.”
“It will stop you from spilling the coffee.”
Benefit “Which means that you won’t burn your fingers.”
“You won’t have to get first aid or emergency treatment for a 
burned hand.”
“You can continue to code/play tennis with no impact on your 
life.”

---

## Page 95

74 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The FABulous Sales Engineer 75
Hint: Let Your Customers Help You 
Sometimes your customer can be the best source of benefits statements. For example, in the 
right situation, after explaining a feature and advantage combination, you can ask your 
customer, “And what do you think that capability would mean to you and your team?” 
Then be quiet and listen. If you do not get much of a response, then drive the conversation 
by asking, “Have you ever lost a bag?” or “What do those additional 30 minutes mean to 
your family?” 
The Fuzziness of Benefits
Sometimes a technical feature can be partly an advantage, and an advantage 
can sound like a benefit. There is not always a 100% clear-cut definition. 
One useful output of a benefit is that it will relate to either time, money, or 
people more than 90% of the time in the business world (see Chapter 7). 
In the carry-on bag example, the first four benefits relate to time or money. 
The final benefit is convenience related to efficient use of personal time and 
a potentially improved state of wellbeing.
If your benefit seems fuzzy or vague, ask the secondary question of “And 
then what happens?” This question is fundamental if your customer has told 
you that a key business issue is starting or stopping something. When they 
do finally stop or start—what happens as a result of that? That is a second-
level benefit and is frequently far more impactful than the initial top-level 
benefit. 
FAB in a Zero Discovery Setting
FAB works best in those settings when you have some idea about the cus -
tomer’s pains and gains, their hopes and dreams. But, unfortunately, that is 
not always the case, and sometimes you have to demo or present before dis-
covery. You can modify and even invert FAB using the introduce, verify, and 
resolve (IVR) technique (see Table 8.2). First, you introduce a problem likely 
to occur in the customer’s industry or environment. Then you verify the is-
Table 8.2 
IVR Technique
Introduce “Last month, my colleague spent an hour at the airport waiting for their 
bag to arrive.”
Verify “Has that ever happened to you?”
“How often?”
“And what happened? How did you feel?”
Resolve “I’ll show you this amazing TUMI travel bag. It will fit in the overhead 
compartment of any plane—even the small commuter flights. So, you’ll 
never have to worry about getting stuck in baggage claim again.”

---

## Page 96

74 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The FABulous Sales Engineer 75
sue to see if the customer experiences something similar. You may even get 
some additional information from the customer after they acknowledge the 
pain. Last, you demonstrate the capabilities and features that can resolve 
the pain. 
Back to Being FABulous
One of the major complaints I hear from sales leaders is, “my sales engi -
neers are awesome with the technology, yet they don’t always do the best 
job of linking that technology back to their customer’s business problems. 
In fact, when under stress, they always revert to technology and features.” 
Being FABulous means that for every feature that you speak about, dem -
onstrate, or present, you should always have a customer-specific example 
of the corresponding advantages and the benefits. I’d recommend that you 
write these As and Bs down either directly into your demonstration script 
or in the speaker notes of your presentation deck.
Summary 
This chapter is one of the shortest in the book, yet it can have the most im-
mediate impact upon your SE skill set. For every feature, no matter how ar-
cane or uncommon, always ask “What does that do?” to yield the advantage 
and then ask “What will that mean to my customer?” for the benefit. Please 
don’t assume that the benefit is so apparent that you don’t need to explicitly 
state it to the customer. If in doubt, be FABulous!
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Review your demos, decks, and whiteboards for features, advantages, and benefits. 
    Make sure they are correctly labeled.
 2. Note the As and Bs for any Fs you speak about with a customer.
 3. Record a customer meeting and calculate the F:A:B ratios. 
 4. Ensure that your benefits correspond with solving a KBI. Think time, money , or people as 
    a guide.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Take your everyday use cases and apply FAB to those cases.
 2. Generate some industry-specific or vertical FAB sheets.
 3. Review and critique every “What’s New in This Release” marketing communication 
    through the FAB lens. Map competitive intelligence reports against FAB.

## Page 99

78 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Successful Customer Engagement 79
toward the customer or the salesperson making the most noise (“the squeaky 
wheel gets the grease”).
It used to be the case that the customer engagement strategy was neatly 
divided between the account manager and the SE. The account manager 
met all the businesspeople plus any executives on the technical side, and the 
SE dealt with the individual techies and maybe their direct managers. Fortu-
nately, that is no longer the case, and there are no longer any hard and fast 
rules about who meets with and builds a relationship with the customer. The 
most successful strategy is that whoever is most likely to create that initial 
bond should engage with that person, regardless of title or position within 
their respective companies. Of course, there are cultural nuances to this ap-
proach. While it is perfectly acceptable for a 28-year-old SE to meet with a 
chief technology officer or chief information officer in the Americas, that 
may not work in certain parts of Asia or Europe based on a title mismatch. 
Regardless, the SE should proactively expand outside their comfort area and 
speak with more businesspeople and mid to senior-level management. 
The Technical Account Plan 
Most SE organizations now have a standard template for an SE-driven tech-
nical account plan. Rather than recreate your own from nothing, I recom -
mend you use the template or repurpose an outline that another more expe-
rienced (in terms of tenure at the company) SE has already created and road 
tested. There are also many plan templates in the public domain available 
through a basic Google search.1
Bear in mind that these templates are precisely that—templates. They do 
not necessarily help you with the thought processes and overall strategy be-
hind the plan. So, as you map out the engagement strategy, there are several 
places to start. Ultimately, everything comes down to the specific customers 
you need to meet with and how you might position and arrange the meet -
ing. As we are not yet at the point when their robots buy from your robots 
and displace the human touch, the person-to-person human interactions 
still drive the back end of the sales cycle. 
People: The Organizational Chart
If you have access to an organizational chart, possibly from previous cam -
paigns or sales calls, you can map out the people you know and the gaps 
1. Companies such as Salesforce, Atlassian, and Hubspot willingly provide free 
versions.

---

## Page 100

78 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Successful Customer Engagement 79
in that chart. That yields a set of candidates that the account team needs to 
contact. Naturally, some potential contacts will be friendlier than others. 
Installs: The Product Chart
You can also find potential landing sites within your customers based on the 
software or hardware they already own. You should be fully aware of your 
installed base and what they are using in which departments. You should 
also know where your competition is established. You can then create a 
whitespace map for opportunities and focus your efforts on expanding your 
install base or looking for areas with no presence from any vendor. Success 
stories from similar industries and verticals can be a great entry point for 
setting up an initial call. Selling into a competitor’s stronghold is always a 
possibility yet is arduous work and represents a major strategic decision on 
the part of the account team. 
Projects: The Initiative Chart
You can also uncover contacts from general projects and initiatives. These 
may be public initiatives that the company has already declared or general 
industry- or vertical-specific initiatives that you see others in their market 
space starting. You can then map those projects back onto the organization-
al chart to uncover who would care about the outcomes of those projects 
and who might be funding them. 
“Coolness”: The Technology Adoption Chart 
Geoffrey Moore of the Chasm Group created the technology adoption life -
cycle chart (Figure 9.1) decades ago. And it has stood the test of time. Fall-
ing into the chasm is an interesting concept, but not the topic of this chap -
Figure 9.1 The technology adoption curve. (Reproduced and adapted with per-
mission from the Chasm Group LLC.)

---

## Page 101

80 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Successful Customer Engagement 81
ter (but worth a read). 2 What does stand out is understanding where both 
your company and your clients lie on the chart. For instance, if you are a 
small company with a cutting-edge innovative product, you are at the far 
left of the chart. Attempting to sell into old-style manufacturing companies 
or companies who always upgrade 12 months after everyone else will not 
be successful. Instead, you need to look for those customers who genuinely 
value innovation and seek a competitive market edge. For larger companies, 
your portfolio may be scattered all across the chart, and you can save your-
self a lot of time by not trying to market and sell your innovative product to 
a late majority client and vice versa. 
Although the adoption chart was initially created to map products and 
companies, you can use it for specific customer personnel. It is possible to 
have an innovative or early adopter sponsoring executive within an early/
late majority company. Conversely, you may also discover that the chief 
financial officer within an innovative company is highly conservative and 
effectively maps to a late majority or laggard section.
First Contact
As you go through the planning process, you can broadly divide your cus -
tomer contacts into three categories. Those are strategic, tactical, and op -
portunistic. Strategic contacts are those you need to pursue to establish a 
long-term relationship, achieve multiple goals, and possibly close numerous 
opportunities. Tactical contacts are more transient and are formed to ac -
complish a shorter-term goal. They may develop into strategic contacts over 
time. Both strategic and tactical contacts will typically come from an account 
penetration plan. Finally, an opportunistic contact is a relationship that you 
fall into by chance. We have all had that experience where there is someone 
in the room with whom you instantly build a rapport, and it just makes 
sense to continue the relationship, planned or not. You will constantly meet 
all three types and a few special cases as well. 
You will not have the time, patience, personality, or even the inclination 
to develop a deep relationship with every customer you meet. However, it is 
the nature of the job, so do not feel bad about meeting a customer, achiev -
ing some goal, or getting a deal, and then never hearing from them again. 
It happens, and it is not necessarily bad behavior on your part or theirs. If 
your final interactions are friendly and professional, you can reestablish a 
relationship more than a decade later. 
2. Moore, Geoffrey, Crossing the Chasm, Third Edition, New York: Harper Collins, 
2014.

---

## Page 102

80 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Successful Customer Engagement 81
Where to Focus
Imagine yourself meeting the project team. You are in a room filled with a 
dozen people. Each represents a different customer group with a different 
point of view. Where do you start? There is no way you can make a connec-
tion with everyone, so how do you decide? 
• Use the plan . If there are people in the meeting you have already 
included within your technical account plan, then start with them. 
That is why you have a plan.
• Use your intuition. In a free-form group situation, observe the dynam-
ics of the room and observe style and title. In general, you should 
try to connect with the most senior people possible based upon their 
potential decision-making authority. Then watch out for those indi-
viduals who drive the meeting and push for decisions, especially if 
others defer to them. Use your judgment if that deference is due to 
authority, technical competence, or just being the loudest talker. 
• Use your brain. Within most customer organizations, there is a locus 
of political and economic power. You should also plan your interac-
tions based upon the decision-making process, who owns the bud -
get, and (for technical/architecture type transactions) who can veto 
a project. You will quickly learn that political power does not always 
follow an organizational chart or even a person’s title, so you must 
account for this. 
• Use the team. While you and your sales counterpart are planning an 
account strategy to influence the decision makers, it can be easy to 
ignore and insult the minor players. You can avoid this by engaging 
with the other team members in a group setting through follow-up/
briefing meetings, working lunches, and so on. The junior staffers 
are involved in the project for a reason, ranging from training to be-
ing an experienced lieutenant for an executive. As such, they need 
to feel positive about you and your team members, even if they do 
not have any significant input to the eventual decision. Managing 
these secondary players can present some challenges in terms of time 
management. You need to balance your available time against your 
other priorities. 
Hint: So Many People, So Little Time 
You may at first feel overwhelmed by the sheer number of people you need to meet and 
develop at least a primary business relationship. Seek some guidance from your manager 
and the salesperson about prioritization. Your guiding rule is always to remember the 
physician’s mantra: “First, do no harm.”

---

## Page 103

82 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Successful Customer Engagement 83
Find a Coach
The coach concept is prevalent throughout sales methodologies.3 The coach 
is a customer employee and is willing to provide you with information be -
yond what your competitors may have. The coach is predisposed towards 
your solution and will do whatever they ethically can to help you win the 
deal. Ideally, you need a coach within the inner circle of decision makers, 
although anyone with access to critical information is potentially a coach. 
It is possible, for more important strategic deals, to have multiple coach-
es in an account. They may reside in different departments or divisions or 
at different levels within the target organization. For example, you may see 
three levels of coach—an executive, line management, and technical coach. 
Each of these plays a different role within the sales process. 
The SE-coach relationship comes about in two main phases:
1. You develop a good working relationship with the individual.
2. Once that relationship is established, the future coach determines 
that they will personally benefit from having your solution set be 
selected. 
Take careful note of these two steps. Complete both steps before you 
start to cultivate the person as a coach. The coach needs to win when you 
win, and that classic win-win scenario is expected and implicitly or explicitly 
agreed by both sides. Ideally, you should select your coach rather than have 
the coach select you. Your coach must be connected, credible, and knowl -
edgeable. However, sometimes that volunteer coach, as the following story 
shows, can make things happen.
CASE STUDY: The Proactive Coach
The best coaches have a sense of urgency about getting you engaged and meet-
ing everyone within the organization. A proactive coach predicts and plans, 
while a reactive coach signals that they are not in the inner political circle and 
are far more likely to be a losing coach. Andrew, an SE manager from Europe, 
tells the story: “After our very first customer meeting, one of the IT directors 
pulled us aside and actually volunteered to be our inside coach. She even knew 
the sales terminology! She had decided that our solution would help her rapidly 
complete two other projects and put her at the front of the line for a new VP po-
sition opening up within the company. Over the next five weeks, she sat down 
with us, explained the internal purchasing process, introduced us to her boss, 
her peers, and most of the IT architecture committee. She also shared (within 
3. Coach and champion are often used interchangeably. Some sales methodologies 
such as MEDDIC differentiate between the two roles.

---

## Page 104

82 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Successful Customer Engagement 83
ethical boundaries) the approach and strategy of our number one competitor. 
This competitor also had a coach who was convinced that he had the inside 
track to the decision maker and didn’t take much time to introduce his selected 
vendor to others within the company. As a result, the final vote was 8–1 in our 
favor. This was a textbook case of an effective coach and a well-executed plan.”
E E E E
Hint: Why Take the Time to Find a Coach? 
By effectively using the coaching concept, you will reduce the amount of selling and busy-
work within an account. You now have internal boots on the ground to do that for you. 
The time you take upfront to find and develop a coach will pay off in the later stages of 
the sales cycle and future deals. Conversely, if your coach is absorbing time and resources, 
you may have to question whether they are genuinely a coach.
Now What?
Once you have found your coach(es), develop and support that relation -
ship. Follow the strategy outlined in Chapter 19 (“The Trusted Advisor”). 
The customer is already placing considerable trust in you and potentially 
putting themselves at risk by supporting you and your solution. 
One essential task is to keep up open communications with your coach. 
Suppose you let your relationship cool down (potentially because you be -
lieve you have already gained a technical or a business win). In that case, 
you may miss out on important updates and could find it harder to reestab-
lish that flow when needed. You do not want to seem “sales-like” and only 
contact the customer when you need something—it is a two-way relation -
ship. Establish a regular meeting cadence with the customer and be fully 
prepared for them to ask something back from you in return for whatever 
access they may be providing to you. Do not be shy about asking what you 
can do for them. 
Summary
“People buy from people.” Technology rarely sells itself, so it is the people 
who evaluate the options and make purchase decisions. The art of the deal 
is finding the right people—at all levels of the customer’s organization—and 
building a professional relationship with them. Sales engineers are often the 
best-placed individuals to start and to make those customer relationships.

---

## Page 105

84 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
You need a plan, and you need the commitment and attitude to engage 
proactively with the customer. You will discover that many of the stronger 
customer/coach relationships you make over the years will stay with you for 
your entire career. For example, a junior SE manager assigned to a learning 
and development (L&D)  project as the SE representative in 2008 was an 
executive vice president of the same company just a decade later. Take the 
long-term view.
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Search for an existing technical account plan template before trying to build your own.
 2. Seek a balance between technical and business contacts.
 3. Research potential contacts and look for common bonds and links to assist first contact.
 4. Observe how coaches within other accounts interact with the sales team.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Look for the win-win option within every coaching relationship.
 2. Maintain the relationships even when the deal is completed (for the next time!).
 3. Increase the proportion of executive contacts within your network.

## Page 107

86 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Structure 87
fears that people hold, applying some structure and some basic principles to 
the task can make it much less nerve-wracking and far more pleasant. 
There have been many studies (and TED talks) suggesting what it is that 
comprises the perfect pitch. Each analysis yields a slightly different answer, 
yet they all routinely indicate that the presenter is far more critical than the 
content. Although content may only account for 20% of the pure impact 
of a presentation, having effective and well-laid out content is a catalyst to 
improve the other delivery aspects. It serves as a solid foundation, especially 
for those moments when things start to go wrong. This chapter will lay out 
the structure of a perfect pitch, interleaved with some knowledge about how 
and why people pay attention. There is nothing wrong with manipulating 
the attention curve to ensure your message has maximum impact. 
Pay Attention to the Attention Curve
When considering the perfect pitch, default to the standard approach of 
“what works best for the customer?” First, spend some time thinking about 
how and why the customer pays attention to you and what they are most 
likely to remember. Then match that to what you want them to remember 
and to your content. 
We can begin by taking a typical example of a 60-minute sales call to -
ward the start of a sales cycle. In a transactional environment, this may even 
be the only interaction you have with the customer. Based on watching 
thousands of calls, we can lay out a typical timeline. Once you complete 
pleasantries, small talk, “can you see my screen?” logistics, and a quick re -
view of an agenda, you have already consumed 10% of the allotted time. 
The salesperson will then launch a corporate overview, complete with a 
market overview containing a few facts to scare 2 the customer, and a buzz-
word-rich positioning statement. The audience members, particularly those 
of a technical orientation, are becoming impatient and want to see the prod-
uct. They may even vocalize that need. Once that initial ordeal is finished, 
the salesperson hands the room over to the SE, who then occupies center 
stage for the rest of the meeting until the clock runs out, or there is a call for 
any questions. Finally, the salesperson closes with a summary, next steps, 
and profuse thanks. 
So yes, that preceding paragraph is a little tongue-in-cheek and quite 
cynical—yet it represents a significant proportion of sales calls that are 
2. Cybersecurity vendors excel at this technique, intended to convince the customer 
that there is a high possibility that they have already been hacked, breached, and 
compromised.

---

## Page 108

86 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Structure 87
happening right now! This standard routine is a highly ineffective and inef-
ficient way to run a meeting when viewed through the lens of the customer 
attention curve (see Figure 10.1). Once settled, the customer will pay the 
most attention to you at the start and the end of the meeting. You will re -
ceive 5–7 minutes of close attention at the beginning because of curiosity, 
politeness, and simple human nature. You will receive 5–7 minutes of close 
attention at the end because you use triggering phrases like “in summary,” 
“to conclude,” “my final point is,” or just because the customer looks at the 
clock. Ultimately, the customer falls into the trough of despair, paying about 
35% of their attention for the remaining time—unless you lift them out of 
that trough. This leads to three immediate conclusions:
1. You have 10–14 golden minutes when you know the customer is 
naturally paying attention. Use that time for something important 
to the customer (solving problems) rather than something impor -
tant to you (corporate overview and pricing).
2. The salesperson is typically on stage for most of those golden min-
utes.
3. Customer attention is naturally at the lowest ebb when the SE is 
attempting to perform their magic. 
The proposal is that you turn the call inside out and cater to the cus -
tomer’s needs rather than your own (Figure 10.2). That means: 
1. Starting and ending with a topic that is of interest to the custom -
er (and helps you). Typical examples would be a summary of the 
Figure 10.1 The standard customer attention curve.

---

## Page 109

88 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Structure 89
problem statements, explaining that you can solve them, and an 
overview of benefits once the call is completed.
2. Moving the all-about-us corporate marketing overviews and even 
Q&A out of the start and end of the call into the middle sections (if 
they are even needed).
3. Injecting a heartbeat into the middle sections of the call to con -
sciously keep the overall attention level at a much higher level in -
stead of sitting in the trough. 
The heartbeat is the most exciting and different concept. You are looking 
to cater to today’s audiences’ more limited attention span by intentionally 
switching your pitch’s focus, style, and/or media every 10–12 minutes. You 
can trigger that heartbeat through many actions, such as having another 
speaker, switching from a demo to slides, telling a story (Chapter 16), show-
ing a short customer video, or firing up a whiteboard (Chapter 15). The 
larger your audience, the more critical the heartbeat becomes. You can easily 
judge the attention of a one- or two-person audience, less so with a half-
dozen customers, a diverse virtual audience, or a physical conference room 
full of people.
These factors supply the overall outline in which to place the perfect 
pitch. By following these guidelines, you can double the average attention 
rate from 35% to 70%. Increasing customer attention means that you have 
a stickier message. The stickier the message, the more likely it will trans -
late into revenue as both you and your company will stand out from the 
competition. 
Figure 10.2 The revised customer attention curve.

## Page 110

88 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Structure 89
Hint: Selling Sales
You’ll discover that one of the primary obstacles in implementing some of the pitch chang-
es will be convincing your sales partners to make these changes. I’d suggest that you 
interactively sketch out your version of the attention curve and connect it to some real 
experiences. You are asking the salesperson to give up some of their traditional control 
points, so look for a few small wins at first as it absolutely needs to be a positive and col -
laborative process. 
Developing a Focused Message
Many methods are available for developing presentation content, and what 
follows is a synthesis of the best of the best from those methods. So, select 
an upcoming presentation, grab a stack of old-fashioned index cards, large 
sticky notes, or your favorite electronic organizer, and begin drafting your 
presentation. 
Start by answering these questions:
• What is the main issue?
• What is the purpose of this presentation?
• What needs to be presented?
• How should you explain that?
• How much do you know about the topic?
• How much does your audience know about the topic?
• Which ideas will need support (verbal or factual), and where will 
you find that support?
• How much time do you have?
Then specify your objectives:
• What do you want to accomplish?
• What points do you need to make to your audience?
• Do they need to take action?
• What decisions need to be made (during or after the meeting)?
• How will you know the presentation was successful?
Start with Structure: Success = RM + 3KP
The structure equation starts with success. You achieve success by staying 
on message and not straying into peripheral and potentially damaging top-

---

## Page 111

90 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Structure 91
ics. The two components are the residual message (RM) and the three key 
points (3KP). The RM is defined as “Three days from now, if there is just 
one thing you want the client to remember, what would that be?” It is the 
sticky part of your message and can range from the general, such as “this is 
the safest solution,” to the more specific, such as “a 50% efficiency gain in 
the first 50 days.”
Underneath the RM are the 3KP. These are up to three key items, facts, 
topics, references, and the like, which support the delivery of the RM. The 
critical concept is to have no more than three items—it is acceptable to have 
two or even just one key point. Utilizing the Power of Three makes life easier 
for your customer and easier for yourself. It can:
1. Establish logic and simplicity, allowing both you and the audience 
to stay on track.
2. Provide balance and order. There is a clear expectation of how 
much they need to listen and how you are progressing through 
your pitch. 
3. Keep the audience connected. They stay engaged because they 
know where they are, like using a road map. 
You provide a simple structure where everything hooks onto one of the 
3KP, and three is easy to remember. The memory retention difference be -
tween recalling a list of three and a list of four is surprisingly large. 
CASE STUDY: The Shopping List
As a nontechnical example, when I am driving home, my lovely wife will often 
call me and ask me to stop at the local store and pick up a few items. “John—
can you get some dog food, oranges, and grapes?” That is three items, and I can 
easily remember them. Then she will add, “oh—and while you are there, please 
buy some peanut butter, apples, and toilet paper.” We’re now up to six items. 
I, like your clients, am easily distracted—and the chances that I remember all 
six items when walking into the grocery store are minimal. It’s like a slide with 
too many bullet points. Instead, I remember the first two items: dog food and 
oranges and the last item of toilet paper. The middle of the list disappears. That 
is the Power of Three.
 (And yes—we now apply high tech to this situation, and I get a text message 
with a shopping list or a link to a shared document!)
E E E E

---

## Page 112

90 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Structure 91
As a more practical example, refer back to the section in this chapter 
about the attention curve, and you will see the RM+3KP structure as out -
lined in Table 10.1. 
Drill Down on Solution and Proof Points
For each key point, before you even think about slides or a demonstration, 
make a list of ideas to support the KP. Generate as many ideas as possible, so 
do not write complete or grammatically perfect sentences. Instead, aim for 
short phrases and trigger words to capture your ideas. Include any relevant 
facts or figures, customer stories, and dates, and don’t edit any ideas on the 
fly—just let the thoughts flow.
Remember that this is the part of the presentation where you are ex -
pected to deliver the most value as the subject matter expert. When creating 
proof points, it is easy to default to “show the product.” Yet, real customer 
stories and fact-based experiences often represent a more compelling way 
to summarize the benefits you can provide.
Should you have an idea that doesn’t fall under one of your three key 
points, you need to ask yourself if that idea is truly relevant or something 
you just want to talk about because you can? Avoid the temptation to create 
a fourth key point or to combine two different items (like A+B: simple and 
inexpensive) into a single KP. 
Organize the Ideas
Now that you have a collection of ideas within each KP, move the ideas/
concepts around to see if they form a natural grouping. A visual method 
such as physical or virtual sticky notes can really help. Once you have a 
collection of these subpoints, prioritize them based upon what you know 
about the customer or a typical customer audience. For example, in a stan-
dard corporate presentation, these items would become bullet points.
Table 10.1 
A Self-Referential RM+3KP Example
Residual Message Restructure Your Sales Call to Double Customer Attention 
Key Point 1 Maximize the golden minutes by talking about something 
important to the customer.
Key Point 2 Move the less valuable components like traditional marketing/
corporate introductions and Q&A away from the start and end, 
and place them in the middle (if needed). 
Key Point 3 Inject a 10- to 12-minute heartbeat into your pitch.

## Page 113

92 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Structure 93
Once more, aim for a maximum of three subpoints or supporting points 
and ideas for each key point. Depending on your time, you can create a 3x3 
or a 3x3x3 detail-level presentation.3
Focus the Key Points
The fear that the audience will not understand what we are trying to explain 
leads the inexperienced or less confident presenter to keep talking and div -
ing into unnecessary detail until the audience must make sense of a series 
of randomly linked points and opinions. Decide which ideas and concepts 
you can eliminate from your pitch and be savage in your editing. If in doubt, 
leave it out!
Hint: Time Flies
One early piece of presentation advice a more senior SE gave me was “the number one 
cause of a poor 30-minute presentation is that it contains 45 minutes of material.” You 
should have a plan to complete that 30-minute presentation in 20 minutes when needed.
The best presentations hold just enough material to cover the topic and 
drive home the RM+3KP. Whenever you supply additional material, “just 
in case,” you dilute your message and potentially complicate your delivery.
Transfer to Y our Delivery Mechanism
Now is the time to transfer your RM +3KP and 3x3 outlines into your elec -
tronic delivery mechanism; for most, that is programs such as PowerPoint 
or Google Slides. If you plan to whiteboard at least part of your pitch, pat 
yourself on the back and read Chapter 15. Imagine what will be on each 
slide and mentally confirm that it will meet the objectives of the presenta -
tion. At this point, you effectively have a storyboard outline or screenplay 
and can fill out the details. 
Note that this technique runs in reverse to the traditional SE presenta -
tion methodology, which takes an existing deck and retrofits it back to the 
client’s needs. Decide what you will present, and then go through your ex-
isting content, rather than go through your existing content and edit for the 
client.4 The rationale is that by starting with client requirements and objec -
3. See Atkinson, C., Beyond Bullet Points, Redmond, WA: Microsoft Press, 2018, for a 
full 350 pages on this technique.
4. The law of common sense applies here. The amount of effort applied to a throw-
away low-value pitch or selling into the SMB market is way less than creating 
something for a strategic meeting with a large client.

---

## Page 114

92 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Structure 93
tives, you will have a more open mind rather than being mentally locked 
into current content availability.
Hint: Bullets Can Be Lethal
Although bullets can be powerful for idea creation, the bullet feature of slide programs 
is one of the most overused and clichéd forms of delivering powerful points. Bullets have 
their place in educational material (like this book) or as a simple way of presenting a list. 
Still, they should not be used as the standard display mechanism for delivering technical 
sales information. Think in bullets but don’t deliver in bullets. When revising a bullet-
laden marketing slide, cut and paste the bullets into the speaker notes. Select up to the 
three most important bullet points, condense them and place them (in larger text) on the 
slide. Support those points with an image where possible. Although counter-intuitive, it is 
easier for the audience to relate to a summary slide plus three slides with three points on 
each slide rather than one massive text-based slide with nine bullet points. 
The research behind the overuse of PowerPoint and simplifying slides is 
compelling. Dr. Richard Mayer5 cites an example of removing bullet points 
that resulted in a 28% increase in audience retention and a 79% increase in 
audience transfer (explaining what they just saw to someone else not in the 
room). U.K. researcher Chris Atherton reports that sparse slides with image 
and text instead of dense text result in a doubling of memory retention. Fi-
nally, cognitive scientist Dr. Carmen Simon6 summarizes what people really 
remember from PowerPoint—and it is less than you expect! This data has 
been available for more than a decade, yet we (and our customers) are still 
subjected to truly horrible slide decks. 
CASE STUDY: A Misfire with My Bullets
Stefan, a solutions consultant in the Nordics, tells this story: “I was asked to put 
together a 30-minute CIO-level presentation on our updated product portfolio, 
which could be used in executive briefings and corporate visits by my fellow 
SEs. I used the Power of Three concept to design slides and then compromised 
after the communications and product marketing team provided feedback. That 
resulted in some slides having between 7 and 10 bullet points. I changed the 
slides to emphasize the key points using colors and thought that would be suf-
ficient. However, it was a disaster for two reasons. Firstly, many SEs were ner-
vous presenting to the customer and our executives and resorted to reading 
every bullet point on each slide. Secondly, just having an unimportant bullet 
point on the slide often prompted unwanted and awkward questions. As a re -
sult, no one got through the deck in the 30 minutes.”
E E E E
5. Mayer, R., Multimedia Learning, Second Edition, Cambridge: Cambridge University 
Press, 2008. Also see https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hw2hi7D1ALE. 
6. See https://blog.polleverywhere.com/what-people-remember-from-powerpoint- 
presentations.

---

## Page 115

94 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
Although it isn’t fair to ask a sales engineer to present “Steve Jobs” style 
with a single large image and a few words on every slide, there is a lot 
to be said for the decomplexification of the fundamental structure of what 
and how we present. This is no book of slide design techniques, so do your 
research and then apply the science—it does work. You are not dumbing 
down7 the presentation; you are providing clarity and a logical structure.
Summary
A solid foundational structure is essential for a successful presentation. Al -
though there are those among us who can wing it in an impromptu ad hoc 
fashion and still hit the target, they are few and far between. The RM+3KP 
format promotes discipline in the creation and delivery process and serves as 
an anchor for you and the customer. It can also get you out of trouble when 
nerves, the customer, or the salesperson takes you off course. 
As a final thought, it is fine to say, “And the three main takeaways I’d 
like you to have in your mind when we finish today are KP1, KP2, and KP3. 
Which, of course, results in RM.” 
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Create your version of the attention curve. Discuss with your sales team and your SE 
    manager. 
 2. Y ou are in control of the technical sections of a presentation. Consciously design a 
    heartbeat.
 3. Examine your current corporate decks. Extract a reasonable RM+3KP .
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Standardize the use of the attention curve by reformatting sales calls.
 2. Look for virtual versus physical differences in the attention curve.
 3. Think in bullets, but don’t use (too many) bullets when presenting.
 4. Include sales and product marketing in a team heartbeat exercise.
7. Dumbing down is the deliberate oversimplification of intellectual content in 
education, literature, cinema, news, video games, and culture. It was originally 
coined by a U.S. movie mogul in 1933.

speak.” It is a false statistic,2 and even Dr. Mehabrian backed away from the 
research3 in later years. However, at least 50% of your message is delivered 
outside of the words you say, and this chapter focuses on the verbal and 
nonverbal techniques you can use to command the room and make sure 
that the rest of your body reinforces your actual words. Although Chapter 
14 focuses exclusively on virtual demonstrations and presentations, we will 
also cover some aspects of virtual communications in this chapter. Body lan-
guage is vital in all media, as it will amplify or diminish the power of your 
message and is part of what we consider presence.
Nonverbal Delivery Skills
The next time you see a dynamic and engaging speaker, perhaps an execu -
tive from your own company or a gripping TED talk presenter, pay attention 
to what makes them seem so vibrant. Of course, such speakers do not always 
have great content, but they have extraordinary nonverbal delivery skills, 
which seem to reach out and force the audience to pay attention. There are 
six major components of nonverbal delivery skills for the SE: physical ap -
pearance, posture, eye contact, gestures, movement, and facial expressions.
Physical Appearance
You never get a second chance to make a first impression. Dress appropri -
ately for your listeners, the company, the location, and the purpose of your 
presentation. Although technical personnel have some latitude on how they 
dress, it is usually better to aim higher (you can always remove a tie, unbut-
ton a collar, or roll up your sleeves) and not outdress the salesperson. Elimi-
nate any distractions, such as extreme fashions, big jewelry, and anything 
that glitters or reflects light. If you have a nametag draped around your 
neck, remove that before you go on stage. Remember that high-contrast col-
ors are authoritative for clothing, whereas lower-contrast colors are friendly. 
In a virtual environment, double-check your camera focus, lighting, 
background, and general appearance. For example, a striped shirt or blouse 
2. At the other extreme, if someone runs into your office stating, “Leave the build-
ing; it is on fire!” the messages of (a) there is a fire and (b) get out! are fully 
conveyed by the words. You don’t have to be an expert in body language to com-
prehend the meaning.
3. The actual experiment was conducted with 37 psychology undergraduates where 
there was a mismatch between words and facial expression. The equivalent of 
saying “I love you” with a total look of disgust on your face.

---

## Page 118

96 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 97
can cause video transmission problems, and beware of any books or photos 
in the location that might be viewed as controversial. 
 Hint: That First Impression
Do give some thought to that first customer impression. Do you really want the first thing 
they see to be you on your hands and knees under the table  plugging in a laptop or HDMI 
cable, or live video of you frantically typing and distracted as you set up a demo environ-
ment for a virtual meeting?
Posture
After an initial appraisal of how you look, posture is the next micro-area of 
nonverbal judgment. People not only listen to you, but they also watch you 
quite intensely. Slouching (or being out of frame) tells the audience that 
you are not particularly interested in them, whereas displaying a good pos-
ture shows that you are prepared and confident. All of this happens before 
you even utter a single word. When standing, use a neutral position—place 
your feet about hip-distance apart and relax your knees, with weight even-
ly balanced and your arms at your side. When sitting, have your hands in 
view and open on the table in front of you. Keep your upper body straight 
but not rigid. That posture also helps your voice resonate better by decom-
pressing the diaphragm. 
Hint: Your Mom Was Right!
The worst possible posture is sitting at a desk, crouching forward with your fingers on the 
keyboard. Yet, that is the standard operating procedure for most demonstrations and slide 
shows. Instead, lean backward and press your shoulders against the back of the chair, or 
get a standing desk. Although this may feel strange, it will open the diaphragm and give 
you 20–25 percent better vocal projection. Also, consider using a remote clicker instead of 
using keys to advance slides. 
Eye Contact
Another key communications component is eye contact. Attempt to have 
a series of random one-on-one conversations with the audience, includ -
ing those at the sides and the back of the room. Aim for up to five seconds 
of eye contact with each person before moving on to someone else. Avoid 
darting your eyes around, scanning the audience, or continually looking 
down at your notes, inspecting your shoes, or staring at the ceiling. Con -
versely, do not become too fixated on one individual and use excessive eye 
contact, even if they are the most important person in the room.
The amount of eye contact is a cultural variable. In some East Asian 
cultures, more than 3 seconds becomes uncomfortable, while in the Middle

---

## Page 119

98 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 99
East, prolonged eye contact between two people of the same sex signifies 
“I’m truthful.” Note that eye metaphors across many cultures portray the 
importance of eye contact for social communication. For example, we have 
“Eyes are the windows to the soul” in the West and “Eyes are as eloquent as 
the tongue” in Japan. 
Eye contact is of particular importance in a virtual environment as it 
most definitely shows interest and engagement. The issue is making sure 
that you look at your camera (and therefore the other participants) instead 
of the display screen showing the other attendees or your presentation. 
Hint: The Camera Is Your Lens on the World
When operating in a virtual environment, drag the collaboration’s tool window, which 
shows who is speaking as close to your camera lens as possible. Then, as you watch the 
video of the other speakers, it will seem as if you are looking directly at them. 
Gestures
Hand gestures are one of the best ways to inject energy and passion into your 
presentation. They are an intrinsic part of our nonverbal arsenal. University 
of Chicago psychologist Susan Goldin-Meadow notes that blind people, even 
those blind from birth, gesture in accordance with their native language just 
as sighted people do.4 Use various gestures with a single hand (asymmetric) 
and with both hands (symmetric). You should avoid making too many sym-
metric gestures where both hands make a similar movement (think to pull 
apart or put together). Extend your arms outward from the shoulders with 
elbows slightly away from the sides. Unless you are speaking for emphasis, 
your hands should mostly remain in front of your body (TV newscasters) 
instead of being outside it (TV weather forecasters). 
When working in the virtual environment, gestures are just as impor -
tant, so you should make sure that your entire body is on camera so that the 
audience sees the motion. If you are at a point in your demonstration when 
the focus is on you instead of your screen, consider pausing the presentation 
so that your video gains center stage. 
CASE STUDY: The Spiral Staircase
Adding gestures to your speaking repertoire is a quick way to power up your 
presentation. It’s worth practicing in front of a mirror or recording yourself for 
practice. Having been educated in the British school system, I had a good grasp 
of the skills required for speaking and an overly formal but effective way of 
delivering a presentation. However, my nonverbal communication skills left a 
4. https://www.aaas.org/susan-goldin-meadow-what-gesture-says-about-how-our-
minds-work.
