## Page 120

98 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 99
lot to be desired. What worked in the United Kingdom did not translate (in 
all senses of the word) to the United States. After my first presentation, my 
manager told me that I was the first person he had ever met to describe a spiral 
staircase without using his hands! To cure this awkward habit, he gave me a 
list of a dozen topics to prepare. Every week I had to present to the entire team 
for 3 minutes how to throw a ball, make pottery, learn karate, and so on. Any-
thing that would make me move my body and hands. It took me 2 months to 
kick the habit, and today, the spiral staircase example stays with me. Did you 
know that medieval spiral staircases always ran clockwise to make it harder for 
right-handed attackers to use their swords ascending the stairs? (Picture John 
waving his hands!)
E E E E
Movement
Body movement amplifies eye contact and gestures. Standing or sitting in 
one location, for example behind a podium or in a chair, restricts your natu-
ral energy and makes it harder to give a high-energy pitch if that is your 
goal. Always move with a purpose, especially as it is the number one way 
to control a physical or virtual reality conference room psychologically. If 
you need to move across the room or the stage, then make that move, and 
when you get to your destination—stop! Don’t move backward and for -
ward (known as the Texas Two-Step) or repetitively pace around the front 
of the room (known as Duck Arcade). At all times, attempt to keep your 
body open to the audience and avoid showing your back unless you abso -
lutely have to do that.
It is fine to move across the front of a projector if you need to transit the 
room. I recently watched an SE explain the current state on one side of the 
room and then consciously cross to the better-lit window side to describe 
the future state. Subtle yet powerful reinforcement. 
You can use movement to control the audience via some psychological 
cues. For example, if you suspect that someone in the room is not paying 
attention, is sleeping, or reading their email, you can move toward them. If 
another person has asked you four questions in a row and monopolized the 
conversation, you can turn away from them as you finish your answer and 
make eye contact with the most senior person in the room. 
Hint: Get Up and Move
Don’t feel that you have to remain behind the keyboard or pinned to the side of a room 
when you present. I’d recommend that at least every 15 minutes, you find an opportu -
nity to get up out of your seat to point to something on the screen or sketch out a quick 
diagram. Movement captures the eyes and the attention of the audience. In fact, the brain

---

## Page 121

100 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 101
predicts movement, such as watching a ball’s flight and estimating where it will land. It is 
a great wake-up technique for our neurons. 
Facial Expressions
Your facial expressions put the final touches on your nonverbal content and 
are especially important whenever your image is projected—either to a large 
audience or via a web camera. Unless you are delivering bad news, smiling 
is the most potent cue to transmit feelings of happiness, warmth, and em -
pathy. A smile spurs a chemical reaction in the brain, releasing certain hor-
mones, including dopamine and serotonin, making us feel good! Conversely, 
a blank or neutral expression often signifies a monotone/flat delivery. Make 
sure that your face is visible—large eyeglasses or over-the-face hair detract 
from your expressions. You should also be aware of your expressions when 
someone else is speaking or taking questions to ensure you do not acciden -
tally show negativity.
Combining facial expressions with a gesture and changing vocal pitch is 
guaranteed to grab people’s attention. 
Hint: Video Capture
The next time that you are presenting, capture a small part of your pitch on video. Then 
play the video back twice. The first time looking for your broad expressions—how much 
are you smiling, frowning, and so on? The second time, stop and start the video on mul -
tiple occasions and trap yourself in a freeze-frame. “Your face can make over 10,000 dif -
ferent expressions and over 50 kinds of smiles,” says Patti Wood, body language expert. 
“What you consider as your neutral resting face can appear like a smile of contempt.”5
Verbal Delivery Skills
Verbal communication is far more than the words that come out of your 
mouth. Examine the phrase “this new product is going to be very popular” 
and practice saying it with as many variations and emotions as you can. 
First, emphasize a different word, and then use anger, excitement, or sur -
prise to color the meaning. Nine simple words can convey dozens of other 
purposes! To quote the great Bard, “All the world’s a stage, and all the men 
and women merely players,”6 and you are preparing for opening night. Five 
5. “4 Little-Known Realities of ‘Resting B Face’ That a Body-Language Expert Wants 
You to Know,” https://www.wellandgood.com/what-does-rbf-mean.

## Page 122

100 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 101
primary vocal qualities are required to deliver a performance worthy of a 
standing ovation.
Pace
Pace is a measure of how long a sound lasts. In effect, it is your vocal speed. 
Speaking too quickly causes words and syllables to be cut short, leaving 
your audience breathless and struggling to follow your message. On the 
other hand, speaking too slowly can sap the energy out of a presentation 
and make it seem dull. Varying the pace helps to keep interest simply by 
injecting some change. The optimum speaking (and listening) pace varies 
internationally and regionally but averages around 150 words per minute. 
Your variation does not need to be dramatic and need only vary 5%. Use a 
faster pace to show passion, excitement, and urgency. Use a slower pace to 
indicate importance, sadness, or the introduction of new ideas. 
Hint: Help Yourself to Slow Down
One common bad habit of technical presenters is to speak too quickly. Over 80% of SEs 
say this is a presentation fault they need to fix in our workshops. Nerves or time pressure 
will drive your speed. So first, determine your actual live speaking speed. You can do this 
by turning captions on in a virtual meet or just by counting words against the elapsed 
time. Most presentation software programs now have a capture and playback mode that 
will give you this data. Then create a visual cue to slow down. Typical examples are writ-
ing “slow down” on a sticky note and posting that on your keyboard or adding “pause” 
to a demo script. I place a small stop sign at the bottom of a few slides in my keynotes to 
remind myself to pause and take a breath. 
Pitch
Pitch measures how high or how low a note (syllable) is—as heard by your 
audience. In general, higher-pitched words generate excitement and enthu-
siasm, while lower-pitched words convey authority and conviction. There 
is not much you can do to modify your natural pitch, although adopting an 
erect posture for clarity and using a good microphone for virtual calls can 
undoubtedly help. You will find that slowing down the pace of your words 
often causes a lower pitch. 
Tone
Vocal tone conveys your emotional state, both implicit and explicit. For 
example, a trembling voice signifies fear and nervousness, which can dis -
turb the audience. A positive tone shows confidence, which can relax the 
audience and even make them smile. Nerves (see the section later in this 
chapter) are the primary contributor to poor tone.

---

## Page 123

102 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 103
Volume
Volume is the measure of how quietly or how loudly the audience perceives 
you. Even with a microphone, the audience must clearly hear you without 
effort. If you have a naturally quiet voice or are operating with poor acous-
tics, you ask the audience to expend mental energy to listen to your words. 
That brain processing time is then spent gathering the words, and less is 
spent understanding the words. Practice a technique kindergarten teachers 
use and gradually lower your voice to draw speakers in and then raise your 
volume to make a significant point. 
Hint: Make Some Noise!
If you would like to exercise the engineer part of your job title, measure your voice level. 
There are many decibel/noise apps you can run on a tablet. Aim for a speaking level of 
between 62 and 70 dB as measured by the audience. The normal person-to-person conver-
sation is about 60 dB. 
Articulation
The final vocal point is to speak clearly and not run words together or drop 
your volume at the end of a sentence. Breathe to keep yourself relaxed and 
to maintain strength and power in your voice. Eliminate filler words such 
as umm, so, right, OK, and you guys; replace them with pauses. Realistically, it 
is impossible to cut all filler words and phrases from your speech, especially 
in ad hoc conversations. However, you are seeking to reduce them to a level 
where they are no longer a distraction. Slowing yourself down by just 5% 
and giving your brain and mouth time to resynchronize will help you. 
CASE STUDY: Right!
Tina was a nervous presenter, even though she was the strongest technical SE in 
the group. Large audiences seemed to make her nerves even worse, which pro-
moted her use of filler words as her brain tried to synchronize with her mouth. 
Her two fillers added “right?” at the end of sentences for confirmation and “you 
guys” when addressing the audience. Tina recorded one of her presentations and 
conducted some self-discovery on her filler-word usage. She wanted to under -
stand precisely when she used the words and in which situations. The recording 
revealed that she used you guys when transitioning from one topic to another 
(usually at the start of a slide) and right? when making an important point. Tina 
decided to replace you guys with real names and right with silence. After some 
practice, she bravely presented to a group of her peers and gave them poster -
boards with her filler words written on them. She figured that her competitive 
nature would kick in as she did not want them to wave the boards during her 
presentation. It worked! Her initial run-through cut down her fillers by two-
thirds, and she kept on improving after that. 
E E E E

---

## Page 124

102 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 103
Pauses give you and your audience time to think and process new in -
formation. For example, a comedian will naturally pause before the punch 
line to set up the audience and create a little suspense. You can pause either 
before or after a key point to give it maximum impact. 
Hint: Exercise Is Good for You
If you are looking to fine-tune your voice, or if your voice becomes raw from too much 
speaking during the day—try some exercises. In the same manner that physical exercise 
builds muscle tone and stamina, voice exercises can do the same for your vocal cords. Be -
fore a major speech, I always hum a tune (currently “If you’re happy and you know it, 
clap your hands,” borrowed from my granddaughter), and I try some breathing exercises. 
Search the internet for something that appeals to you! It helps to make a difference.
Start with a Bright and Beautiful Beginning
Every presentation course you will ever attend recommends that you start 
with an “energizer”—a story, fact, or statistic that makes your audience sit 
up and take notice. You gain an upward beat in the attention curve because 
you are a new and different speaker—it is now your job to maximize that 
heartbeat. Use the PUNCH mnemonic in Table 11.1 to remember the sig -
nificant energizer categories. Examples in a technical sales setting would 
include telling a brief customer story (refer to Chapter 16), showing a short 
video, or delivering a bold claim about your solution, which leads the cus -
tomer to think, “wow, I did not know that!”
Hint: The Baked Cake Philosophy
“Start with the end” or “do the last thing first” is a popular methodology in the product 
demonstrations that we cover in some of the following chapters. Imagine you are watch -
ing a cooking show on TV or online. The chef is showing you how to bake an absolutely 
Table 11.1  
PUNCH (Courtesy Garr Reynolds)
Category Example
Personal Tell a story about yourself or your audience. Share a common experience.
Unexpected Do, show, or say something unexpected to “shock” the audience.
Novel Show or teach the audience something new.
Challenge Challenge the status quo or the current way of thinking. “I can back up 
your entire manufacturing system in less than 10 minutes!”
Humor Make your point stand out and come to life. However, your company is 
not paying you to be a comedian, so be careful. It usually is best to make 
the humor about yourself or the salesperson.

---

## Page 125

104 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 105
fantastic and delicious cake. What is the first thing the baker shows you? It is a delightful, 
finished product. It is not a list of ingredients nor a description of the flour, butter, and 
breed of hens that laid the eggs. Why does the chef do that? It is to light up your taste buds 
and to stimulate your curiosity so that you watch the rest of the show. Sometimes you need 
to let the customer eat their dessert before you serve the vegetables. 
Make a Fantastic Finish
Finish with power, finish with enthusiasm, and finish on your terms. This is 
one of the times when the customer is genuinely paying attention, so make 
it count. Your final minutes should be highly relevant to the customer and 
lead to a compelling conclusion such as “And that is how we can solve your 
latency problem and speed up your branch transactions by 50%. Your staff 
will no longer have to apologize to their customers for the slow speed of 
your computer systems.” As you are wrapping up, I recommend you have 
visual support—a slide or a screen that summarizes the benefits, gains, and 
pain relief you provide.
Do not finish with a “Thank you,” “Any questions?” or a weak transition 
like “well, I guess, that’s it . . . now back to the salesperson.” When you have 
finished, the customer should be in no doubt about the benefits that you 
(that is, both you personally and your company) can provide. The last thing 
the customer sees, especially in a virtual call, should be summarization. You 
can handle the important thanks elsewhere, and we have already learned in 
Chapter 10 that you should not finish with Q&A. Any questions? 
Using Y our Nervous Energy
Everyone gets nervous before their presentation. If you do not feel any 
nerves, you should question your mental mindset as it is likely that you 
will come across as dull and flat. Accept nerves as part of the job, and both 
incorporate and enjoy the resulting adrenaline rush, knowing you will end 
up with the satisfaction of a job well done! 
The main culprit for those nerves is stress. Stress causes mental and 
physical tension, which ruins your posture, voice, and spontaneity. Your 
pitch becomes higher as your throat constricts, your shoulder and back mus-
cles tighten up, and your legs may shake. Your brain senses this and releases 
more chemicals to compensate, and it becomes a self-defeating cycle. The re-
sult is either a canned and scripted presentation (when a presenter may read 
the slides or a demo script) or a random collection of disconnected thoughts.

---

## Page 126

104 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 105
Your primary response should be to welcome nerves and not to fight 
them. Actors and politicians recognize the value of nerves because they can 
sharpen a performance. This value is due to the adrenaline release and our 
ancestral cave dwellers’ classic fight or flight reflexes. If the nerves win, you 
go into flight mode, withdraw from audience interaction, and overly focus 
on the pitch. You are effectively in fight mode by accepting the nerves, 
ready to stand your ground and make that perfect pitch. You do this by 
channeling and redirecting the nervous energy into a positive good. Some 
techniques that have proved effective are as follows:
• Mentally visualize yourself giving the presentation beforehand—es -
pecially the first and last five minutes.
• Relax your body. I wiggle my toes, flex my knees, shake my hands, 
and take some deep breaths to get oxygen into my lungs and brain.
• Drink water. Avoid alcohol for obvious reasons. Caffeine is a stimu-
lant that only amplifies the adrenaline response. 
• Understand that you are far more sensitized to your nerves than 
your audience is. 
• Visit the bathroom. Wash your face and hands. Try a power pose 7  
(think superhero)!
• Case the joint. Become familiar with your surroundings, lighting, 
projector switches, TV cabling, and so on. 
Even the best presenters make mistakes, accidentally skip a slide, or hit 
the wrong button in a demo. That has happened to everyone in the audi -
ence, and it just proves that you are one of them. If it is something minor, 
then it is likely no one will even notice, and you should just continue. It is 
not your job to point out every typo error in a slide!
Presenting to Large Groups
When presenting to a large audience—more than a few hundred—you are 
often placed on stage behind a podium, bright lights shining down on you. 
You are also on camera with a larger-than-life image of you projected on 
multiple screens around the room. You frequently cannot see the audience 
because of the lighting (but you can hear them!). A situation like this can 
7. Watch Amy Cuddy’s 2012 TED talk about body language: https://www.ted.com/
talks/amy_cuddy_your_body_language_may_shape_who_you_are.

---

## Page 127

106 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 107
be terrifying the first time you encounter it, and it is why you should always 
familiarize yourself with the location beforehand. 
Some of the basic nonverbal rules no longer apply. Eye contact is made 
with a camera instead of individuals, and it is hard to get a read of interest 
within the room. Continue with all your gestures, movements, tonal variet-
ies, and especially the smile. I would also recommend that you use a wire -
less microphone instead of being tied to a fixed microphone—most of us feel 
better when we have the freedom to move around. If there is a podium, it is 
best used as a stand for your reference notes and water bottle, rather than as 
a shield for you to stand behind. 
For large virtual presentations, you have the advantage of being com -
fortable with your surroundings (it is your home or business office, after all) 
and having notes and prompts available to you—but off camera. Of course, 
you still have issues with reading the room, yet using virtual technology 
such as chat, Q&A boards, and interactive polls can get active audience en -
gagement. In addition, it is a less stressful situation than being live and on 
stage.
CASE STUDY: Ask for Help
The best source of guidance and advice for working with large groups often 
comes from the crews and technicians who handle stage lighting, sound, and 
logistics. A few years ago, I had to give a keynote speech at a large conference in 
Las Vegas. I discovered that Jerry Seinfeld would be opening that evening and 
that his road crew had already set up their systems and would be using me as 
their test subject. I spent 2 hours with the staff asking questions and watching 
how they set up the stage for Jerry. It was an amazing experience. The lesson is 
to always ask the local crew about feedback issues, lighting glare, camera angles, 
and so on.
E E E E
Summary
The depth and breadth of your technical knowledge cannot overcome poor 
presentation techniques. Your job is to make the experience as painless and 
straightforward as possible for the audience. A strong beginning and a fan -
tastic finish, wrapped around memorable and compelling content, are the 
ideal structure for the perfect delivery. Think of your verbal and nonverbal 
signals as the amplifier of your sales message. Take the time to practice and

---

## Page 128

106 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Perfect Pitch: Delivery 107
familiarize yourself with your content, as that cuts down on nerves and 
helps to promote you as the capable and informative SE. 
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Video is the best teacher. Record yourself presenting and then review .
 2. Practice facial expressions (especially smiling) and gestures in front of a mirror.
 3. Rehearse your presentations both physically and mentally .
 4. Accept your nervous energy and redirect it. Learn from others.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Video is still the best teacher. Record yourself presenting and then review .
 2. Build up a library of beautiful beginnings and fantastic finishes.
 3. Give feedback to less experienced team members.
 4. Accept speaking opportunities outside of work.

Chapter Goals The Dash to Demo
Nature does not hurry, yet everything is accomplished.
                —Lao Tzu
T
he overeager rush to demonstrate your prod-
uct, which I refer to in this chapter as the 
dash to demo , is often the signal for the end of 
any sales opportunity, whether you are selling a 
small item of utility software or a complex piece 
of hardware. It signals the total abandonment of 
any disciplined sales strategy because the sales 
team believes that the product will sell itself. In -
experienced salespeople, or occasionally even an 
accomplished sales team, become convinced that 
a world-class demo of the product will be enough 
to get them the deal. Unfortunately, that is not 
always the case, and there is usually far more to 
the sales cycle than a great demonstration. 
Jim Cathcart, the author  of Relationship 
Selling,1 says, “In selling, just as in medicine, pre-
scription before diagnosis is malpractice.” This 
chapter will look at the causes of the dash to 
demo and how predictable and consistent plan -
ning can make your life easier and less stressful. 
1. Cathcart, J., Relationship Selling,  New York: Pen -
guin Publishing, 1998. Since 1984, Jim has written 
more than two dozen books on the subject of sales 
and relationship selling. I consider him one of the 
pioneers of the entire sales process industry.
Appreciate why and how 
the dash to demo occurs.
Build a checklist for the 
planning and logistics of 
demonstrations.
Learn how to handle a zero-
discovery demonstration.
Understand the 
implications of different 
audience types and mixes.

## Page 131

110 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 111
Why Does the Dash to Demo Occur?
There are two quite distinct variations of the dash, each of which has a dif -
ferent set of surrounding logistical and client issues. Note that I use demo 
to cover the classic product demonstration and a standard product presen -
tation—not every product can be shown and demonstrated in a tangible/
visual manner.
The first is a zero-discovery demo. Zero discovery means that you have had 
zero (or at least only minimal) opportunity to conduct discovery with the 
client before the meeting. This situation is driven as much by the prospect 
as it is by the salesperson. To overcome objections early in the sales cycle, 
the sales team’s use of the phrase “we will show you that in the demo” 
convinces the customer to see the product. The rationale is that seeing is 
believing and that wonderful things will happen once the demo is done. 
This setup leads to higher and higher expectations for the eventual unveil -
ing of the product in front of the customer. The higher that bar is raised, the 
greater the expectations, and resultant stress placed on the technical team 
to deliver whatever has been explicitly or implicitly promised beforehand. 
There is now an intense focus on a single event that can make or break the 
opportunity. 
You will note that the prospect is often a willing accomplice of the dash. 
“Just come in and show us the product. We just need a general idea of your 
capabilities.” You are breaking the guess component of TAG (see Chapter 
5), as no matter how much independent client research you conduct be -
forehand, you are still guessing as to what their exact needs and business 
requirements may be. That is not a fun position to place yourself in—espe -
cially for the more complex portfolios. 
CASE STUDY: The Role of the Demo in the Sales Cycle
“We just hired a brand-new chief revenue officer, and they decided to imple -
ment yet another sales process. It allowed us to map our presales activities di -
rectly to the sales stages and monitor the deal outcomes based on adherence to 
the process. We measured everything for over 15 months to allow for seasonal-
ity, product upgrades, and the new process’s uptake. It was a fantastic opportu-
nity to get some real sales metrics around our demonstration capabilities and 
effectiveness. As measured by win percentage, we learnt that our best success 
rate occurred when we gave the main demo after exiting stage 1 (discovery/
needs analysis) and mid-stage 2 (solution design). However, we discovered that 
two-thirds of our demos happened too early in the sales cycle. Even allowing 
for vision generation and special cases, we could safely say that at least 50% 
of our demos were mistimed. We created a partial fix by supplying some short 
(2-minute) simple automated [unattended] demos to the salesforce, allowing us 
to satisfy some of the client’s curiosity and defer the demo until it was the right

---

## Page 132

110 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 111
time. As a result, the overall win rate rose by nearly 10%. Our CRO looked like 
a star.”—Monika, SE director
E E E E
The second variation is a zero-day demo. Zero day means that you have 
had no opportunity to conduct discovery before the meeting with the cli -
ents— and that is by design . There are perfectly valid reasons for this to oc -
cur—such as selling in a transactional/SMB environment or when you are 
selling a single narrowly focused product set. However, you have one op -
portunity to meet the client and pitch your product through a demo, so ev-
erything must happen simultaneously. More on this later—it is not as bad as 
it sounds and is the standard operating procedure at numerous companies.
Failing to Plan Is Planning to Fail 
Whether totally ad hoc or calendared weeks in advance, the best internal 
risk mitigation strategy is a good, predictable, and consistent plan in practi-
cally every demo situation. Consider that the two primary objectives of a 
demo2 are: 
1. Risk mitigation for the client. If you think of the art of selling as mov-
ing a client from their current state toward their desired state, then 
the demo is the reinforcement and support of the sales bridge that 
will carry them into the future. It is not the bridge itself! The demo 
must support your message and your capabilities in a positive and 
relatable way for the client.
2. Proof. You are seeking to prove that you can solve the customer’s 
business problems (pains) and help them achieve future gains and 
that your company is uniquely qualified to do that. The demo cre-
ates the idea and imagery inside the customer’s brain to see them-
selves pain-free in the near future. 
Those two objectives may sound rather philosophical and psychologi -
cal, yet many people buy emotionally and then defend that decision with 
logic afterward. You win their hearts first! Although the remainder of the 
chapter deals with the “hard” items like logistics and agendas, never lose 
sight of the softer emotional sell.
2. Yes—of course you want to win the deal.

---

## Page 133

112 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 113
It pays to carefully plan out the demonstration, with a laser-sharp focus 
on how to solve the client’s business problems in an economical and expedi-
tious way. Every action during the demo should either lead back to one of 
these solutions or reinforce competitive advantage. 
In terms of logistics, you should always have a checklist3 for some of the 
basic facts surrounding the event. Most SEs have two checklists—one for 
physical and one for virtual events. At a minimum, you should know the 
following:
• Number of people attending;
• Attendee location (physical/virtual/mix);
• Names, titles, and contact information of attendees;
• Organizational hierarchy/relationships;
• Third parties (partners) in attendance;
• Partial attendees (i.e., executives who may only be present for 10–15 
minutes);
• Amount of time allotted;
• Projection and sound facilities;
• Internet or network access;
• Security/firewall protocols;
• Virtual transmission preferences (for web-based demos);
• Power supply/lighting;
• Breaks/food;
• Whiteboard access.
Note that practically none of this logistical information, except for orga-
nizational chart data, is something you gather in a traditional discovery call.
Hint: Create a Checklist
After just a few months in the role, you will have a mental checklist that you run through 
before every demo. Nevertheless, I would still advise that you create a formal checklist for 
those occasions when you are working with a new sales team or are depending upon a 
customer or partner to set things up on your behalf. 
When presenting face to face to a large group of people, especially for an 
important meeting, it is often worth visiting the facilities beforehand, which 
can be arranged by your internal coach or sponsor. This visit allows you to 
3. You can find several comprehensive checklists in the book resources section of 
the MTS website.

---

## Page 134

112 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 113
check out all the requirements. In addition, it makes for a marvelous op -
portunity to informally meet some of the participants beforehand (“I just 
happened to be in the building”). If travel makes this impossible, ask for a 
quick video tour.
The Agenda
The use of an agenda has gone in and out of fashion over the years. I am 
a big fan of a written agenda, distributed beforehand, to prevent surprises 
and set some boundaries on what to cover during the meeting. It does not 
have to be lengthy or wordy—it should just handle the basics. My first SE 
manager told me that “whoever controls the agenda starts with control of 
the meeting.” An agenda can explicitly gain buy-in from the prospect about 
the length and general contents of the meeting. It is one less unknown for 
you to worry about in case of a demo dash. 
Even for a single slide agenda, it should not be casually created the 
morning of the demo and haphazardly added to the front of the deck. 
Adopting the mantra of consistent and repeatable processes, having a set 
of standard agendas (adapted by client/industry, size, product, and so on) 
gives you some control. Distribute the agenda at least 24 hours beforehand, 
and feel free to include other items such as the demo GPS roadmap I’ll cover 
in the next chapter, plus short bios on the sales team. Those bios are espe -
cially important if you have invited any subject matter experts or personnel 
from outside of sales (such as product management, support, and so on).
Before Y ou Even Start 
An 11 am demo (especially when virtual) does not start at 11:00. It techni-
cally starts at 10:45 am, when you sign onto the web transmission system 
or connect your laptop to a physical projector. The demo actually starts 
between 11 am, and 11:10 am once the majority of the audience is in place. 
Those 15–25 minutes between startup and go time can be put to good use. 
That time is when the rotational slide deck (RSD) comes into play. The RSD 
is a grouping of about six slides that you play in rotation (like an unattend-
ed trade show deck) before the demo starts. It should contain the following:
1. Your overall intro slide (title, date, logo, and so on);
2. Pictures of the sales team, names, and a short bio;
3. Agenda for the meeting;

---

## Page 135

114 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 115
4. Corporate facts and figures (size, employees, languages, number of 
customers);
5. Promotional marketing slide or customer references;
6. Your choice of support slide. Possible “common pains.”
Anyone who signs on early or walks into the conference room before 
you start will now see these slides while setting up or conducting small talk 
before the actual start of the meeting. It is a great way to get some free mar-
keting and messaging. You also benefit from everyone knowing that they 
are in the right (physical or virtual) room and a guarantee that they can see 
your screen! 
Preparing the Way
All too often, the demonstration consists of one SE sitting behind their lap -
top projecting onto a screen in a slightly darkened conference room. Before 
we get to content, there is more to logistics and planning. Here are a series 
of tips gathered from our workshops and personal experience related to in-
person meetings. Chapter 14 (“Remote Demonstrations and Presentations”) 
covers virtual-specific ideas. 
1. Can everyone see and read the screen? Sit at the back of the room 
and try to read your most complicated or text-heavy screen or slide. 
Not everyone has perfect 20/20 vision! You may need to increase 
the font size or magnify your browser window to ensure visibility. 
Red, purple, light green, and light blue are notoriously poor display 
colors (regardless of your corporate color palette). 
2. Apply the 50-ft (15m) rule when preparing your presentations and 
demos. Everything looks good on a big screen right in front of you 
or the product manager—but can it be seen from a distance? 
3. Arrange the seating so that you have eye contact with the sales -
person. This activity means the rep may have to sit on the opposite 
side of the table. When in the same room, the only way the team 
communicates small items will be nonverbal—so the visual aspect 
is crucial. Should trouble develop, or if the rep needs to tell you to 
speed up or slow down, some small gesture is all that is required. 
(This is one area when virtual presentations and peer-to-peer mes-
saging are so much better!)
4. Have an emergency phrase or gesture prepared. For example, in 
case of a catastrophic equipment meltdown, severe nerves, or a

---

## Page 136

114 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 115
software crash, the rep knows to step in and take control and re -
move the focus of attention from you and your screen. (Remember 
to hit pause if screen sharing.) 
5. Never, ever be alone in the room. In a team-selling situation, it 
may be tempting for the rep to step out and make some calls, read 
emails, or follow up on some item raised earlier during the call. 
However, Murphy’s Law4 suggests this is the prime time for a prob-
lem to occur. A presenter always needs assistance in the event of 
an equipment failure or even an awkward pause. The reverse also 
applies—never abandon your salesperson when they are present -
ing for precisely the same reasons.
6. Have a backup prepared. Make a copy of your presentation on a 
USB stick and in the cloud. Have screenshots of your demo that 
you can walk through with the client.
7. Always have your own auxiliary equipment available, such as a 
power strip, marker pens, HDMI converters, mini-Ethernet hub, 
power/plug converters, Wi-Fi hotspot, and so on.
8. Print out the essential copies of your slides or screens (including 
the demo GPS roadmap) and have them available as handouts. 
9. Clear the way. You should neatly tuck away all cabling. This habit 
ensures that you (or the customer) do not trip over a stray lead, 
and it also looks more professional. Bring some Velcro ties just for 
this purpose.
10. Turn off or place on vibrate all cell phones, tablets, and other de -
vices. After three decades, you would think this becomes second 
nature in the business world, yet at least twice a week, I watch a 
call being digitally and noisily interrupted. I usually make a big 
show of turning my device off to encourage others to do the same 
and avoid embarrassment.
11. Ask your meeting sponsor to announce that laptops should be 
closed for maximum attention.5 
12. Make sure you know how to control lighting, blinds, display units, 
and all in-room electronics. If part of your audience is virtual, un-
derstand the camera angles and sound restrictions. 
4. “Anything that can go wrong will go wrong—at the worst possible time” 
(paraphrased).
5. I feel this is now a losing proposition. A decade ago, you could get 80% compli -
ance on the request, now it is less than 20% because different generations work 
in different ways.

Engaging the Audience 
Nothing is guaranteed to put the audience to sleep more than a 3-hour 
monotone delivery showing every possible feature, function, and menu op-
tion within the product. Creating the agenda should help you craft the demo 
flow, bearing in mind the heartbeat function of the attention curve (Chapter 
10). Just as variety is the spice of life, it is also the essence of a good demon-
stration. I am not suggesting a Las Vegas–style multimedia presentation, but 
sometimes you need to entertain the eyes and the ears to win over the brain. 
Customer participation is a wonderful way to increase interaction and 
differentiate your product. I can recall numerous presentations where the 
customer was made part of the show. For example, I have had to supply a 
blood sample to a medical device maker and contact information for a real-
time social media demo. I still remember those demonstrations! Building 
participation into your demo at any level helps interaction and engagement. 
It is not necessarily having the customer use the keyboard (although I have 
seen that). It can be via a survey, poll, or just collecting some simple data 
from them. Use the entertainment factor to highlight a vital capability to an 
executive or to show a strong competitive differentiator. 
Presenting a demo to a mixed audience of technical and businesspeople 
can be one of the most challenging aspects of the job. Everyone wants some-
thing different and uniquely views their personal needs. Even with superb 
up-front discovery, you are still asked to cover a lot of ground and not leave 
anyone behind. Executives require information at a higher level—usually 
with a financial focus, while the technicians are more interested in speeds, 
feeds, and compatibility issues. Answers given to a technical analyst could be 
irrelevant to a senior manager and vice versa.
The solution is to divide and conquer (which is why job titles are es -
sential before any sales meeting). Provide, or at least make the offer of, a 
separate session for the technical staff to cover any complex issues and make 
available any relevant documentation and links for specifications and refer-
ence architectures. In return, get permission to defer any questions that may 
be way over the head of middle management or executives. You are work -
ing on the basis that these managers and executives are more important to 
the decision process than the technicians. Yet, you cannot afford to ignore 
the technicians as that will hurt you at some point in the buying process. If 
the technicians truly drive the buying process, which happens more often 
than you might expect, then adjust your strategy and reverse your timing 
and emphasis.
Similarly, break out some specific part of the agenda for a high-level 
overview timed to the presence of those executives. This overview may be 
two or three slides but could also involve some extensive whiteboarding or 
general conversation. Dealing with executives as an SE is an art (see Chapter

---

## Page 138

116 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 117
20). When in the middle of a demo, remember that you can often tell an 
executive how something works rather than showing them how it works, 
especially if you have a short customer story to back it up.
Hint: Three Great Questions to Ask Yourself (and the Sales Team)
Once you have your pitch put together, whether in outline or finished format, it is worth-
while to ask three questions about every screen, slide, or capability you plan to show.
1. So what? Why are you showing this item, and what purpose does it serve?
2. Who cares? Do you know who in the audience will care about this specific thing and  
 why that is?
3. Says who? When making a statement or claim about your product or citing a  
 statistic, who is the authority? If it is your marketing department, be prepared to  
 back up the claim with hard data. 
If the answer to any question is “I don’t know,” then you have a candidate for elimination. 
Back to the Dash to Demo
When placed in the position of having to demo with zero to minimal knowl-
edge about your potential customer, you can follow multiple actions and 
strategies. Do not immediately start presenting or giving a demo. The sales 
team is now in the position of having to perform some discovery on the fly. 
This tactic is going to require some flexibility on your part, so consider it a 
challenge. Here are some techniques I have found helpful to encourage the 
customer to disclose useful information.
1. Trade your story for theirs. The rep may have a strong desire to start 
with a corporate overview. Although I am not a big fan of broad -
casting who we are and what we do, you can offer to exchange 
your corporate story for their version of what brought them into 
the room today. A corporate overview slide can also go into the 
rotational slide deck.
2. The pain and gain sheet. Think of this as a slide with some questions 
on it. These are important questions that you know your solution 
can answer. Examples vary depending upon what you are selling, 
but they are along the lines of “How can we reduce our backup 
window?” or “Do you know what your exception rate is on in -
voices and how much that costs you?” You can include this sheet 
as part of your rotational slide deck for maximum impact.
3. The “A Customer Like You” story. Start with two or three stories about 
customers just like them. For example, if you are selling to a small-

---

## Page 139

118 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 119
medium community bank, make it another small financial custom-
er, another small-medium customer, and another customer in the 
same city/location. You are looking for either a “That sounds just 
like us!” or a “We don’t have that problem!” kind of response.
4. The staff meeting conversation.  When working with a manager and 
a group of her direct reports, try the “last staff meeting” question. 
I draw a picture of a table and a couple of stick figures and ask, 
“when you talked about [what you do] at your last staff meeting—
what were the items that came up for action?” This visual works 
particularly well if you sell “IT plumbing” solutions or equipment 
varying from servers to heat pumps to medical devices. 
5. The demo/GPS roadmap.  When called in to perform a generic (out-
of-the-box) demo, give the customer some control over what you 
show. The demo GPS roadmap (detailed in the next chapter) graph-
ically shows the customer what they are going to see step by step. 
Hand this out to customers and ask for feedback regarding what 
would be important to them and what wouldn’t be. Choose the one 
or two items they say are essential and ask, “How do you do that 
now?” with as many follow-up questions as you can before they 
start to get impatient. Try to have them draw something for you to 
explain their current state.
6. Just ask! Play the technical/doctor card. “Listen, I can talk for hours 
about this subject and all the great things that my solution can do, 
but I’d like to narrow it down so I can focus on those things that 
are important to you. Can I ask you a couple of questions before 
I start?” If you get some resistance, use the doctor analogy about 
understanding all the symptoms so you can get a complete picture.
Checkpoint Charlie
There is an old maxim in public speaking: “Tell them what you are going 
to tell them, then tell them, and then tell them what you told them.” This 
statement should serve as a reminder to continually check in with the cli -
ent throughout the demonstration to ensure that you are still on target. It 
can be very annoying for one person, either the rep or SE, to be continually 
asking, “how are we doing?” so divide this task up beforehand and develop 
some variations. Next, refer to the agenda to confirm that the topics have 
been satisfactorily covered. Finally, use the call’s final 5–7 minutes to sum-
marize the overall benefits and delineate the next steps. For example, if you

---

## Page 140

118 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Dash to Demo 119
use the heartbeat approach from Chapter 10, pause briefly after each beat 
to summarize and gain agreement. 
Hint: What Is Your Brand?
Just as your product or service may have an overall brand, so should your demo. Decide 
beforehand on a few keywords or phrases which you will repeat multiple (6–7) times 
during the demo. Examples might be low-risk, large customer base, scalable, cost-effective, 
compliant, and so on. Award yourself bonus points if the customer repeats one of those 
phrases during the demo!
CASE STUDY: The Pain Slide: Take T wo Aspirin and Call Me in the 
Morning
Andrew, presales operations manager at a startup software company, tells us: 
“Seventy percent of our sales calls were one-and-done online meets. We had 
one opportunity to meet with and impress the prospect to get the sale. A busi -
ness development rep would make either an outbound or accept an inbound 
call to gather some very rudimentary data and then pass the appointment over 
to the sales team. We launched a new product and worked with marketing to 
build a set of common use cases. We called this the pain sheet. A single Power-
Point slide held seven questions about IT infrastructure designed to make the 
customer say, “No, I don’t know the answer, but I would like to find out!” We 
would show that slide, ask the customer to choose their top three priorities, and 
then continue into a segmented demo flow designed to highlight those three 
items. We had over a 90% customer hit rate just with those seven questions, 
and it helped each sales team handle the zero-day (and even the zero-discov -
ery) demo we learned about in class.”
E E E E
Summary
Planning and preparation are everything. When you benefit from a sys -
tematic discovery process, you can target your demo to those uncovered 
pains and gains. When faced with the dash to demo, for whatever reason, 
the repeatable demo process will deliver excellent results and lower your 
everyday stress about the unknown. Never perform a blind demo, as you 
should always feel confident that you can set up and ask some questions 
beforehand. Connect your product features to the customer business re -
quirements, be FABulous (Chapter 8), and enjoy the day.

Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Be prepared. Start with a standard set of agendas. 
 2. Create a logistics planning checklist—even if you think you don’t need it!
 3. Work with a salesperson to build your first rotational slide deck.
 4. Construct (or repurpose) a pain sheet for on-the-fly discovery .
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Seek out opportunities for audience interaction during the demo.
 2. Pay attention to all the small setup details.
 3. Incorporate multimedia as part of a heartbeat strategy .
 4. Refine the pain sheet by adding in some gains.

Chapter Goals Building the Demo
There are those who follow maps and those who make 
them.
            —Alberto Villoldo
E
ver given one of those demos when the cus-
tomer is confused, perplexed, and ultimately 
completely lost? You find yourself saying, “What 
we’re now going to show is . . . ” and “Next, you’ll 
see . . . ” but you hear “Wait, back up, what did 
you just do?” or “Didn’t we see that ten minutes 
ago?” These are all signs that your customer is 
conceptually challenged by what they are seeing. 
Imagine that your demo is a journey—and the 
customer is spending so much time figuring out 
if you are turning left or turning right, or do they 
take this exit or the next one, that they are miss-
ing all the beautiful scenery on the way!
This chapter deals with the construction and 
design of a demo using the heartbeat method 
and attention theory. As a result, your customers 
spend more time admiring that beautiful scen -
ery (the business and technical benefits) and less 
time understanding where you are and what 
you, and your mouse, are doing. 
Look at the demo from the 
customer’s viewpoint.
Learn how to construct a 
demonstration roadmap.
Understand the practical 
uses of the roadmap.

## Page 143

122 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Building the Demo 123
The Pain of the Demo (Customer Perspective)
We usually associate that pain word with the business problem that the 
customer hopes you can help them solve. However, the other side of pain 
linked to a demo is the actual customer pain of watching a poor demo. Con-
sider these three fundamental problems of watching a demo or presentation 
from the viewpoint of a potential customer.
1. The curse of knowledge.  The presenter (that is you!) often has the 
curse of knowledge. 1 You have presented this demo 50 times be -
fore, and you know and understand your solution and the resultant 
flow by heart. Yet this is the first time the customer has seen your 
demo. You make far too many assumptions about what the cus -
tomer really “knows” and what is so evident that you do not need 
to mention it. What may be an intuitive user interface or feature 
name to you is a complete mystery to your audience.
2. Attention span.  The customer’s attention wanders momentarily—
meanwhile, a new screen pops up—and they are too embarrassed 
to admit that they are lost.
3. The murder mystery. The customer is highly comfortable with the fa-
miliar, so a murder-mystery demo (“guess what happens next?”) 
makes them uncomfortable. They have no idea of where you are 
going, and even if that is relevant to them. Customers want to be 
part of the journey and part of the plan.
CASE STUDY: Lost and Bemused—Part 1
Our client engaged us to interview prospects after a sales call to judge various at-
tributes of the call, including the clarity of the demo. We (alarmingly) discovered 
that 78% of clients in a virtual environment and 54% in a face-to-face setting 
admitted that they became lost and confused at one point during the demo. That 
made them 50% more likely to tune out and pay minimal attention to whatever 
followed as it reinforced a view that the software was complicated. About 30% 
of demos were repeated for the same audience.
 We conducted a similar study with a second client and received corresponding 
lost and confused numbers of 74% and 55% in a virtual/physical setting. Other 
anecdotal evidence supports this data. There is much room for improvement. 
E E E E
1. First used in 1989, it is the cognitive bias when an individual communicating with 
others unknowingly assumes that others have the background to understand. 
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curse_of_knowledge.

---

## Page 144

122 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Building the Demo 123
Introducing the Demo GPS Roadmap
Using the analogy of your demo/presentation as a journey, the custom -
er needs to know, before you even start, answers to the following three 
questions:
1. Where am I? That is the starting point. Next, get a confirmation 
that the current state you are showing (based on your business and 
technical discovery) is their current state.
2. Where am I going? What is the final destination, and why do I 
want to travel there?
3. What is the route? How long will I be traveling, is it dangerous, 
and what are the landmarks along the way? Stretching the anal -
ogy, they will also want to know if they can drive themselves, hire 
other drivers, or take another form of transportation.
Think of the GPS in your car, tablet, or other mobile device. Plug in a 
starting and ending point, and it generates a suggested route. Use Apple or 
Google Maps, and you can even get a nice old-fashioned hardcopy of the 
streets with all turns and sights listed. It is a tangible affirmation of your 
journey. 
Once you are underway, you can look at the GPS map and know ex -
actly where you are at any given time. If you are a passenger in the back 
seat, with no access to a map of any kind, you are placing total trust (and 
responsibility) upon the driver. You assume that the driver knows where 
they are going, takes the most efficient route, and has checked for traffic 
and accidents. A competent, insightful driver might also ask if there is any-
thing special that you would like to see. Your customers are not always that 
trusting, but you expect that trust if you demo the old way and take them 
on a magical mystery tour. There is a better way.
Building the Demo GPS Roadmap
On the principle of first showing them the baked cake (from Chapter 12), 
Figure 13.1 shows a completed demo roadmap. There are three major steps 
in building this—the three Cs of chunks, content, and clicks. This process 
works equally well for both a new demo and retrofitting an existing demo 
into a map. For a current in-use demo, it helps to have a script or a video 
recording of the demo available for analysis.

---

## Page 145

124 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Building the Demo 125
1. Create the chunks. Divide your demo up into logical chunks or seg -
ments. Ideally, you should have between three and eight chunks 
in a demo, and each chunk should take less than 12 minutes to 
complete. Group similar capabilities together into a chunk, based 
upon either capability or user (create a new chunk whenever you 
switch roles). Label each chunk with a summary such as current 
state, reporting, or interfaces.
2. Select the content. Within each chunk, you can show up to three main 
capabilities or features. If you need to show more than three items, 
you can divide the chunk into two sections. Each piece of content 
should specifically relate to driving one of the benefits you uncov -
ered during discovery. Next, annotate each chunk with the three 
main items.
3. Count the clicks.  For each chunk, count or estimate the amount of 
time (in minutes) it takes to deliver, the number of discrete screens 
you will show, and the number of clicks you need to make to drive 
the demo. Record the number of minutes, screens, and clicks in the 
small boxes to the right of the chunk label. 
Congratulations! You have now built your first demo GPS roadmap. You 
can fine-tune it as follows:
Figure 13.1 An illustrative demo GPS roadmap.

---

## Page 146

124 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Building the Demo 125
1. Number each chunk. 
2. It is a journey, so connect each chunk box with arrows to move 
between the parts.
3. Use your judgment as to whether the map is one page or two pag-
es. That is typically dictated by font size.
4. Examine the minutes-screens-clicks (MSC) ratios. Although there 
are no hard-and-fast rules, seeing 16 screens in five minutes or 
more than two or three clicks per minute signifies a busy demo. 
Your goal should be to minimize the MSC numbers.
5. Adapt your terminology to your customer. For example, phrasing 
for a business audience may be different than for a technical 
audience. 
Hint: Apply Common Sense
One cybersecurity client created a roadmap during one of our workshops for their stan -
dard demo. After outlining the flow, they realized they were trying to show 19 distinct 
features within 35 minutes and didn’t even have a use case for the flow. No wonder 
their clients were confused. Stepping back, losing the curse of knowledge, and applying 
common sense can tell you much about the impact of your demo. On closer examination, 
one of the chunks in Figure 13.1 warrants further exploration and simplification—what 
would you do? 
Using the Demo GPS Roadmap
You can use the roadmap before, during, and after a demo. 
Send the roadmap to the primary customer contact several days before 
the demo and ask them to print out several copies (if in a conference room 
setting) and distribute them to attendees. This step can confirm what you 
are going to show and may help with some demo flow customization.
Use the roadmap during the demo as a constant reference point. For 
example, “We just completed part 1, which examined the way things work 
now, the failure analysis, and the history of failures.” You can also refer to 
the map if anyone arrives late or loses their place in the demo. Finally, it 
can keep you on track whenever the customer (or the salesperson) asks you 
about some feature or capability you plan to show later. Instead, you can 
respond, “Yes, and we’ll show that reporting function in part 4.” I have seen 
SEs quickly sketch the roadmap on a large whiteboard in a conference room 
to provide that consistent anchor point for larger groups.

---

## Page 147

126 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Building the Demo 127
Once you complete the demo, you can resend an updated version of the 
map that might have some specific customer product or systems nomencla-
ture and any additions or detours you made.  
The SE organization can collect the demo GPS roadmaps and create a 
reusable library at a more strategic level. This library, coupled with some of 
the available demo automation tools, can drastically reduce the setup and 
delivery times of demonstrations early in a sales cycle. To use a technique we 
discussed in Chapter 6 about visualizing gains, imagine if a salesperson asked 
you, “Can we deliver demo scenario number 19 from the library?” 
CASE STUDY: Lost and Bemused—Part 2
The demo GPS roadmap produces actual results. A customer ended up creating a 
library of demo maps to the point where a salesperson asked for “Demo number 
five, please.” The two customers we initially worked with reported an average 
19% increase in customer satisfaction with their demos, a 75% reduction in 
“Show me that again” re-demos,2 and a 24% increase in conversion rate (using 
raw $ and £) for revenue generation. 
E E E E
Hint: You Will Need Multiple Maps
You cannot build a map for every conceivable demo. Instead, concentrate on the standard, 
repeatable demos, which usually comprise 80% of your demo activity, and create a map 
for each of those. 
Summary
A roadmap will not solve all your demo flow and customer engagement is -
sues, but it will solve many. By providing a visual point of reference, your 
customers will feel more engaged, less likely to become lost, and less likely 
to pull you off track with questions you will cover later in the meeting. The 
benefits for you are that you will defeat the curse of knowledge, your demo 
message will stick, and your conversion rates will increase. I will close out 
this chapter with a final thought from your tour guide: “A roadmap always 
tells you everything except how to refold it.”
2. Their internal term was a “mulligan”—a phrase borrowed from golf, which refers 
to a free shot given to a golfer after playing a previous poor shot.

Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. First, build a roadmap for a standard, existing demo.
 2. Use the roadmap with customers to confirm a demo agenda.
 3. Work with your sales counterpart to make it part of your standard operating procedure 
    during a demo. Sales should also become familiar with the map.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Use the MSC boxes to fine-tune the demo. Show only the minimum.
 2. Experiment with alternative (or conditional) routes through the map.
 3. Build (and share) a standard library of roadmaps.

If you always do what you always did, you will always 
get what you always got.
              —Albert Einstein
T
he confluence of high-speed internet access, 
work from home, and an amazing advance 
in demo platform capabilities has brought about 
significant changes in how we interact with cus-
tomers. Nowhere has this impact been more 
noteworthy than for the basic product demon -
stration or presentation. The demo has long been 
viewed as the central role of the SE (aka the 
demo jockey). Anything that affects the how or 
the why of the demo makes a material difference 
in our daily work habits. 
Even before the Covid-19 pandemic, an in -
creasing proportion of sales calls were conducted 
remotely through the web instead of being face-
to-face meetings. In North America, even heavily 
field-based strategic SE teams remotely handled 
as much as 50% of customer meetings. That per-
centage was 30% in most of EMEA and just un -
der 20% in the Asia-Pacific region. From 2014 
to 2019, the virtual to physical ratio shifted by a 
few points in favor of virtual meets. Of course, 
Understand the critical 
differences between 
virtual and physical sales 
demonstrations.
Stress the importance of 
fully utilizing the demo 
platform.
Cover the dos and 
don’ts of effective virtual 
demonstrations.

## Page 151

130 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Remote Demonstrations and Presentations 131
2020 and 2021 radically accelerated the shift 1 and caused a much-needed 
improvement in the base technology. 
The shift is also noticeable for two outward effects. The first is that ev -
ery SE (and salesperson) has embraced and learned the new technologies. 
Second, at a slower pace, so have the clients. So, for example, you no longer 
must explain how to use chat or share a screen.
In this chapter, we will examine the basic premise of remote demonstra-
tions (I will use the all-encompassing term of remote demo for simplicity), the 
positives and negatives of remote versus physical interactions, and the vir -
tual SE mantra of “Know your customer. Know your technology and know 
your product/demo.”
The Basic Premise
The rationale behind using a webcast is twofold. The first is that it offers con-
siderable savings in both time and money compared to jumping on a plane, 
train, or automobile and physically meeting your client. The second is that it 
allows greater reach, as you can easily connect with an audience in multiple 
time zones and countries. Corporate best practices may state that webcasts 
are now the primary customer interaction and that face-to-face meetings 
become the exception. Practically every SE organization has some form of 
inside SE team that handles at least the initial sessions, the front-end client 
interactions, plus services the SMB market. Other business models run more 
than 90% of all customer interactions virtually.
However, just like every other tool you have at your disposal, just be -
cause you have it in your tool kit  does not mean that you must always use 
it. There are many occasions when a face-to-face meeting is superior to a 
virtual meeting, even though it may be harder to arrange. Remember the 
craftsman’s adage, “When all you have is a hammer, everything looks like 
a nail.” 
Advantages and Disadvantages
Aside from financial concerns and productivity gains, the main advantage of 
a remote demo is that it can speed up the sales cycle by allowing high-touch, 
low-cost engagement, and more touches per day. In addition, you no longer 
need to find a space in your calendar for travel, and it is usually more con -
1. Case in point, the quarterly revenues of Zoom Communications grew from US$60 
million to $956 million in the space of 3 years (https://investors.zoom.us/).

---

## Page 152

130 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Remote Demonstrations and Presentations 131
venient for the customer to get everyone to attend a virtual meeting rather 
than a physical meeting. When dealing with multiple time zones, this is a 
significant advantage. You can also, with agreement from both sides, re -
schedule at short notice. 
The other significant advantage for an SE is having your reference and 
backup material readily available without anyone knowing. For example, 
you can have a demo script or speaker notes for a presentation on your 
desk, a technical spec sheet loaded up in a separate browser tab, a pre -
planned set of questions, and even some competitive positioning notes. 
This accessibility is a frequently overlooked benefit compared to running a 
face-to-face meeting—although it is something that inside SEs have known 
about for many years.  
The primary disadvantage is that you are not in the same room as 
the client. Even with video on, you only have limited ability to read the 
room. You lose the benefit of many of the nonverbal techniques covered 
in Chapter 11. It becomes much harder to judge if the customer is paying 
attention or taking notes, and it stifles the interplay and communication 
between the participants. Many SE practitioners find the virtual environ -
ment more mentally tiring and taxing because you must consciously inject 
energy throughout the proceedings without the benefit of continuous body 
language feedback. The undesirable practice of the back-to-back scheduling 
of webcasts further exacerbates this energy drain.
Hint: The Power of the Webcam
All virtual communication tools now include a webcam option, although not everyone 
uses it. A January 2020 Forbes Insight report studied the impact of webcams. The results 
speak for themselves. When the webcam was turned on, as compared to when partici -
pants were only using audio and screen sharing, survey participants found that overall 
quality of communication was 62% higher, engagement was 73% higher, and levels of 
understanding were 50% better. So, turn the webcam on and encourage your customers 
to do the same!
Although you approach each virtual interaction with unbridled op -
timism and passion, you should assume that you are competing against 
email, smartphones, side conversations with the mute on and video off, and 
even feet up on the desk behind closed office doors. The burden then falls 
upon you and your sales partner to actively engage with the audience and 
deliver your message. Our unscientific estimate is that you start at about 
60% efficiency compared to a physical meeting.

---

## Page 153

132 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Remote Demonstrations and Presentations 133
Know Y our Customer
The sales team should treat every virtual call with the same preparation and 
professionalism they do for a physical call, if not more due to the challenges. 
Preparation means conducting complete discovery (if that is your sales mo -
tion), understanding who is on the call, and what all parties need to get 
to make it successful. Preparation also includes setting clear meeting goals 
(see Chapter 20 for information about MARS-BARS) and going through the 
complete planning cycle laid out in Chapters 10 through 12.
For an inside SE, time constraints may dictate that you do not always 
get the full degree of preparation that you need. For example, it is common 
to have five or six 1-hour demo blocks2 booked in a single day. This type of 
schedule is where the handoff between the salesperson, or business develop-
ment rep, to the SE is critical. The more information you can get, the better 
you can prepare, even if you only make verbal adjustments to your materi-
als instead of physical ones like customizing slides or demo data.
Hint: Verbal Adjustments and Making the Team Look Good
One unwritten purpose of everyone on the sales team is always to make your teammates 
look good. One example of this is stating, “I carefully reviewed the comprehensive notes 
that Robin made in your previous discussion and have a few follow-on questions,” rather 
than saying, “Robin just covered the basics,” or “There were a few gaps in your discussion 
with Robin.” Of course, Robin’s notes may well be terrible or nonexistent, but the customer 
does not need to know that!
You also need to carefully consider the agenda and the overall timing of 
the meeting. Just because you have 60 or 90 minutes does not mean that 
you must use all that time. Think of your reaction when a 60-minute ses -
sion is only 50 minutes, and you get 10 minutes back in your day. Customers 
remember! I’d suggest that you set the overall meeting length based upon 
the realistic attention span of the customer rather than how much content 
you feel you have to cover. I asked one of my early mentors what the perfect 
length for a sales call was. She smiled wryly and replied, “just long enough.” 
Table 14.1 shows recommendations for virtual meeting length by purpose. 
You should also apply the virtual tax concept from the following case study 
to these recommendations.
CASE STUDY: The Virtual Meeting Tax 
Sean, an SE leader from Australia explains the tax. “Last year, we instituted 
a new customer meeting policy and decided that all initial calls and standard 
2. This is a great opportunity for demo automation and creating either standalone or 
SE-assisted demos that can replace the “six-a-day” routine.

---

## Page 154

132 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Remote Demonstrations and Presentations 133
demos should be 50 minutes in length. We also took account of what our COO 
called the virtual meeting tax—which was that every meeting started five min -
utes late and that you would lose 50% of your audience in the final five min -
utes. We decided that we would plan for a more realistic 45 minutes of custom-
er interaction and that if things were going well, then the originally planned 
50 minutes (say, 11:00 to 11:50) would stretch to 60 minutes. This took a lot 
of the rush out of our demos, always the last major agenda item, and gave the 
SE more time to cover material at a more moderate pace. Everyone was happy! 
The SEs found the calls less stressful; the reps didn’t have to schedule so many 
follow-up calls (to cover material we didn’t have time for in the first call), and 
the customers were happy because we did not jam yet another full 60 minutes 
into their busy calendars. We also noted a 6–7% overall improvement in con -
version rate in moving from the first call to a second call.” 
E E E E
Know Y our Technology
Each webcast platform is different. The way Zoom works is different 
(enough) from Meet, Teams, or WebEx that you need to take the time to 
explore every available menu option and add-on. In addition, you should 
ensure that your basic infrastructure setup is functional. That covers sound, 
webcam, and environment/location.
Starting with the webcam, the basic web camera built into a laptop is 
rarely of sufficient quality and limits your placement and viewing options. 
I would highly recommend using a separate web camera (at least 1080p 
at time of writing) and clipping it onto the top of a device or monitor. The 
camera then requires sufficient lighting so that people can see your face and 
that your skin tones appear natural. Setting up your desk with natural light 
behind you, such as a window, leads to backlighting, and you seem to live 
in the shadows. Instead, the light should come from the front or a ring light 
that illuminates your face. Also, aim for a professional-looking and clean

background such as a bookcase 3 or use a virtual background (make sure it 
works well—poorly done is worse than not using virtual backgrounds at all), 
possibly with your corporate logo discretely located in that background.
Sound and volume are especially important in the virtual environment. 
As noted in Chapter 11, if the customer has difficulty hearing you, they will 
rapidly tune you out. Therefore, only use the built-in microphone as a last 
resort. Otherwise, use a headset or a stand-alone professional microphone 
(Blue Yeti is an excellent choice). Check the audio settings to reduce and 
automatically compensate for background noise. I use a David Clark Pilot 
headset for multihour workshops, which is incredibly comfortable 4 and of-
fers built-in sound reduction. 
Each tool has a discrete set of features and functionalities. Table 14.2 
shows a sample representation. Learn how to use and incorporate each of 
these into your customer interactions. 
To supplement the webcast tools, software add-ons can make your cus-
tomer engagements far more memorable, interesting, and compelling. These 
range from visual interaction products that go far beyond simple polls to 
allow in-call creation of graphs, word clouds, and list responses up to mini–
broadcast studio software. These studios can create scenes, supply multiple 
virtual rooms, and integrate whiteboards, slides, and speakers into one inte-
grated format. The list of such products grows every month, and they now 
form a wrapper around classic webcast tools. 
Hint: Read the (Online) Manual
Each tool has either an online manual or a comprehensive set of help screens. Read every 
one of them. You will find, particularly in admin/setup mode, some truly useful microfea-
tures that can help you out. For example, I recently discovered how to watermark sensitive 
materials if an attendee used a screenshot capture! In addition, seek out professional train-
ing from someone who has utilized the capabilities in the field and provides an environ -
ment for you to learn, experiment, and get feedback.   
The final piece of technology is the humble mouse or pointing device. 
The background of most software products and slides is white. The default 
mouse icon is white and ridiculously small. It is the classic white cat in a 
snowstorm effect. If the customer spends brainpower trying to find your 
mouse, they are not listening to your message. There are two immediate 
steps you should take. The first is to change your mouse. Turn the mouse 
dark/inverse and enlarge the icon (this is typically in mouse settings). The 
3. I intentionally place items of interest, such as a cricket ball, a wooden elephant, 
a copy of this book, and an old coffee mug on the bookcase shelves. They often 
serve as icebreakers to start a conversation.
4. They may make me look a little geekish, but no one ever says that they cannot

second step is to use the webcast tool annotations or other settings to high-
light the mouse or invest in mouse enhancement software or hardware.  On 
the hardware side, this allows your pointing device to spotlight or magnify 
part of the screen. Alternatively, some webcast tools or software let you 
turn your mouse into a laser pointer or fuzzy red/blue ball (like a GPS map). 
You can then directly annotate and highlight a section of the screen using 
your mouse. All should be essential parts of the SE toolkit and worth fur -
ther investigation and investment.
Hint: Beware “Zippy Mouse Syndrome”
My colleague and demo guru Peter Cohan coined the phrase “zippy mouse syndrome.” It 
describes the SE who wildly moves the mouse all over the screen and circles text items on 
slides like a shark hunting its prey. A secondary offense is when the SE physically points 
at their screen while saying, “if you look over here.”  The mouse is in constant motion, 
which becomes almost dizzying and overwhelming for the customer.

Webcast Functionality to Explore and Use
Function Description
Screen sharing The standard capability to share your screen, an application, or even 
just a browser tab. The general rule is to share as little as necessary 
(i.e., one browser tab versus an entire screen).
Chat The ability to communicate with other sales team members during a 
call. Plus, the ability to share links to reference materials or get input 
from participants, especially when more than six people are on the 
call.
Participants A list of participants on the call. Review to check for raised hands or 
emoji reactions.
Q&A A location to store questions when dealing with a large audience 
instead of continually taking questions.
Polls Polling the audience with a question; suitable for larger audiences, 
icebreakers, or even a quiz.

and annotations
Being able to explain a concept or respond to a question visually or 
highlight key points on slides. (See Chapter 15 for technique details.) 
Breakout rooms Used for splitting the audience into two or more subsets. Excellent 
when covering technical and business items or doing a deep dive 
with admins (and no one else cares about that . . . ).
Live transcript Voice to text conversion. Incredibly useful for keeping a record of the 
discussion, for Q&A, and for multilingual audiences.
Translation The next step after the transcript. Converts your speech into captions 
and then (loosely) translates into closed captioning or a second 
language. 
Pause The ability to pause or freeze what you are sharing. Useful when 
something goes wrong or you need to make a change behind the 
scenes.

East.

Know Y our Product and Know Y our Demo 
Although the SE may be at the mercy of the underlying webcast software, 
the actual demo environment is far more controlled in a virtual room than 
in a physical one. You have additional intrateam communications options, 
on-desk reference materials, screen pauses, and so on. These all make your 
life significantly easier to help counteract the lack of real-time physical in -
teraction. As a result, there is no excuse for a demo failure or a series of “I 
don’t know” responses. 
The counter-intuitive advice is to let go of your product knowledge and 
instead place yourself in the position of your customer. An SE can fall so in 
love with their product and demo that they talk too much (“cool—nice to 
have” is not necessarily relevant to your audience). It is time to pause and 
create interaction or validation whenever you have been speaking and dem-
oing for more than a few minutes. Referring to the demo GPS roadmap of 
Chapter 13, you might want to consciously count the number of pauses for 
feedback within each demo chunk.  
Record your demo 5—live with a customer (with their permission) or 
during a dry run. Look for the following outputs:
1. Use a monitoring tool  to note when you speak and for how long, 
and how often the customer speaks. The output can be incredibly 
insightful and give you some clues on modifying the flow and the 
talk track of your demo. 
2. Also, note how often you use your company name and product 
names. Most customers do not care what you call your product or 
even what release number you have—all they care about is what 
the product does and how that can help them. 
3. How often do you go navigational? That means talking about what 
your mouse is doing (click, drag, button) instead of the outcomes 
and results of the mouse action.
4. Use of customer names.
5. Which actions generate the most questions, interactions, or positive 
reactions? 
6. Webcam use. Are you looking at the web camera, and therefore the 
customer, or at your screen, and thus seem to be looking away from 
them?
5. You can use the base webcast functionality or else a specially designed coaching 
and prompting tool.
