## Page 158

136 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Remote Demonstrations and Presentations 137
Summary
Webcast technology replaces a large portion of your physical presence in 
the virtual demo world, so embrace and improve your use of that technol -
ogy and incorporate it into your demos and presentations. Utilize all the 
assets discussed in earlier chapters—the rotational slide deck, the logistical 
worksheet, the agenda, the demo GPS roadmap, and the heartbeat theory 
of the attention curve. They each play an essential part in the delivery of 
the perfect webcast. Yet do not lose sight that even surrounded by all the 
technology and techniques, ultimately everything still depends upon you!
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Learn all the features of your webcast tool. Read the online manual.
 2. Record some of your demos and analyze them afterward.
 3. Have an independent third party (not a work colleague) review your home office setup.
 4. Utilize the webcast tool capabilities, install mouse enhancement software, or buy a 
    spotlight-style presentation tool. 
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Create a list of “demo crimes. ” These are heinous SE acts that cause the customer to lose 
    focus or create unnecessary questions.
 2. Incorporate add-on tools to enhance your virtual deliveries.
 3. Run a demo competition and capture best practices in small video snippets.

If the first thing you do in a sales call is to show Power-

  —Scott Lillis, former VP of Sales Engineering,  
   Veeam Software 
L
ast year I overheard a customer telling a client 
of mine that “I see a thousand slides a month. 
Why should I pay attention to your slides?” It is 
a wonderful and thought-provoking question! 
Although slides can be a beneficial sales and 
educational tool, they can, like most tools, be 
overused and become dull and blunt over time. 
In today’s corporate world, there is unquestion-
able PowerPoint fatigue. “Death by PowerPoint” 
is no joke: How many slides have you seen this 
month? How many slides have you delivered? 
How many can you remember? There is a better 
way.
The Power of Whiteboarding
For a definition, I classify any visual form of 
drawing as whiteboarding and the finished prod-
uct as a board. That includes using the classic 
whiteboard, a poster board, a sheet of paper, the 
proverbial back of a napkin, a tablecloth, glass 
windows, and a tablet or touchscreen-based

of whiteboarding and how it

drawing app. Yet collating the data from more than 20,000 sales and sales 
engineering professionals that we have trained over the past decade shows 
that whiteboarding is a lost art. Less than 15% of the students used any form 
of visual selling techniques before taking the class. Within a virtual environ-
ment, that number dropped to under 5%.
Some apparent advantages of whiteboarding are that it is interactive, 
customizable, personal, collaborative, and highly adaptable. The personal 
credibility that an SE can gain by sketching out their solution architecture 
instead of relying on a prebuilt slide created by a corporate product line 
manager is fantastic. Referring to the attention curve in Chapter 10, a white-
board is a perfect fit for one heartbeat of a presentation. It generates an at -
tention surge merely by being different!
CASE STUDY: The ROI of Virtual Whiteboarding 
A mid-range security company was looking to up its game and differentiate it -
self from its two closest competitors. The SE team decided to minimize their use 
of some exceptionally beautiful, professionally designed Google Slides. Instead, 
it focused on a visual method to present their (1) customer success stories, (2) 
business value benefits plus pain-gain chains, and (3) competitive differentia -
tors. We conducted a series of workshops to enable their global SE team, who 
then ran an internal competition to create and deliver the “best of the best” 
virtual vignettes. The SE team then used those vignettes to create a series of 
standard whiteboard packages that served as the foundation for a variety of ad 
hoc visualizations. Over the following 200 days, they noted the results seen in 
Table 15.1.
The Drawbacks of Whiteboarding
To be fair and balanced, there are some disadvantages to the visual approach: 
It can be challenging to draw complex solutions due to limited space (even

handwriting and artistic ability, although these are mitigated with tablet

with maintaining the customer’s attention and the overall professionalism 
of personal sketching versus professional slides, especially in an executive 
or governmental setting.
Whiteboarding Within the Sales Cycle
The impact of whiteboarding is that it fits practically everywhere within a 
sales cycle, from basic qualification and initial technical/business discovery 
through to executive presentations and closing. Here are just some of the 
areas I have experienced effective whiteboarding:
• Discovery
• Drawing the customers’ current architecture;
• Confirming and prioritizing current business issues;
• Initial view of obstacles and gain/pain.
• Solution design 
• Current state versus future state comparison;
• Visualization of removing obstacles;
• Collaboration with customer.
• Technical review 
• Specific illustration of how a feature or system works;
• Competitive comparison;
• Summary tabulation of speed and feeds.
• Executive meetings
• Summarization of current issues, business drivers, and proposed 
solution;
• Summary financial/ROI analysis.
• Ad hoc

• Traditional on-demand back of the napkin;
• Supplemental drawing on top of slides.
Getting Started
You may think of a whiteboard as an ad hoc tool, yet the best whiteboards 
are those that you have previously designed and practiced—they just look 
ad hoc to the customer! I recommend starting with a small set of 8–10-min-
ute whiteboard vignettes that you can use in multiple situations. To create 
one of these vignettes, you focus on two parallel design streams.
The Overall Content
Use the same residual message + three key points (RM+3KP) formula de -
scribed in Chapter 10. Write these items down and spend some time mak -
ing sure that they are memorable, interesting, and compelling (MIC). 2 For 
example, when putting together an initial pitch for Acme Solar Panels, Table 
15.2 illustrates the RM+3KP inputs.
Note the use of prescriptive words such as minimal and reduce and the 
threefold repetition of 20 for making the message sticky and memorable. 
Sales engineers can take a few pointers from the marketing folks in these 
activities. 
The Visuals
The visual component is a series of frames, much like a slide build, each of 
which contains about 15–20 seconds of actual pen-on-canvas drawing. By 
sketching out your board frame by frame, you can immediately create the 
overall flow and determine what goes where on the canvas sheet, rather 
than making it up as you go along. If you’ve ever sketched out a board and 
either ran out of drawing room at the edge or ended up placing ugly arrows 
across the board from one end to the other, you have experienced a lack of 
preparatory framing.
2. Also known as dropping the MIC!
Table 15.2 
RM+3KP Example for Acme Solar Panels
Residual Message Acme Solar—The Clear 20/20 Difference
Key Point 1 A simple installation in only 20 minutes
Key Point 2 Minimal maintenance; 20-month service interval
Key Point 3 Reduce utility costs: a 20% saving!

Whiteboarding Mechanics 101
Before you even pick up a pen, it is worthwhile thinking about some of 
the basics that can make or break your visual presentation. This is because 
both SEs and salespeople become so focused on what they will draw that 
they forget about how they will draw it! Some of these best practices ap -
ply equally to the physical and virtual WB configurations, while others are 
more important in one environment as opposed to the other.
Stance
When using a whiteboard, it is very easy to turn your back on the audience. 
That is a poor practice for many reasons:
• You lose eye contact with the audience;
• Your voice projects off the board rather than going directly to the 
ears of the audience;
• In many cultures, showing your back is rude and inconsiderate.
The secret to a good stance is the 90-degree or laser-beam view. At 
no time should your feet be at less than a 90-degree angle to the board. 
Imagine a laser beam shooting out from the board heading toward the back 
of the room—your feet, especially the one closest to the audience, should 
never cross that beam. Otherwise, that laser beam will zap your foot to re -
mind you that you have a closed stance. It does take much practice to get 
this right, so you will need to persevere and even watch video footage. 
An important byproduct of the stance guideline is that you should nev-
er be speaking and drawing simultaneously. Otherwise, you are looking 
at the board and not your audience. This situation leads to a basic rule of 
whiteboarding: “The board has no money; the customers do—so look at the 
money!” If you can draw on a classic whiteboard and simultaneously look 
at the audience, you are wasting your talents as an SE.
Hint: Move the Board!
When you have a moveable whiteboard or a poster board, position it off a little to one side 
depending on if you are left or right-handed. This way, you maximize the open area of 
the board to the audience. Don’t stress about people in the front corners—they chose to sit 
there! For a poster board, stand to its side and wedge your front hip against it. That way, 
the board itself will prevent you from stepping in front, especially as most people’s arms 
are long enough to reach the far side of the board.
When presenting virtually, I’d suggest (in contrast to giving a demo) 
that you keep your video turned on to help with engagement. That also 
enables you to wave your stylus around and highlight any other props you

---

## Page 165

144 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Whiteboarding and Visual Selling 145
may use (I’ve been known to use a classic Hollywood director’s clapboard to 
get things started). You can also periodically make eye contact and check on 
your audience. Also, lean back in your chair, or stand up, instead of crouch-
ing over your devices. Now your diaphragm is not compressed, and you 
have better vocal control and pitch. 
Timing and the Chunk
The chunk is a new SE unit of time for drawing on the board. It represents 
an elapsed time of no more than 10 seconds and is the most extended time 
you should be drawing and not speaking (because (physically) you are ei -
ther drawing or speaking—never both). A chunk is long enough to allow 
you to place many icons or some text on the board, but not too long that the 
audience loses attention and focus. 
SEs tend to rush their writing and drawing because of a lack of confi -
dence or a faulty internal clock. Rushing leads to mistakes and an untidy 
and illegible board. It also self-fulfills the bad handwriting belief in most 
SEs, which is exacerbated by trying to multitask in speaking and drawing 
simultaneously.
In a virtual environment, the concept of a chunk is a little more fluid. 
It is possible to draw and look at the audience with the correct equipment 
setup, although I still would not recommend excessive multitasking. You 
can certainly draw some simple shapes and a few arrows while speaking. 
However, once the visuals get more complex, you should focus on them and 
maximize your use of the silence chunk to draw neatly and efficiently. 
Hint: Explain What You Are Drawing!
One exception to the multitasking guideline is that it is fine to explain what you are draw-
ing as you draw it, as in “We’ll place a firewall here on the perimeter and that’s how 
we’ll handle the encrypted network traffic coming from . . . ” This technique is also highly 
effective when using an icon or a symbol to express a concept, such as drawing a robot for 
artificial intelligence or machine learning. 
Cadence and Rhythm 
As part of their training, teachers are taught by their instructors how to use 
a whiteboard (or a smart electronic board) in their classroom. The primary 
concept they learn, shown in Table 15.3, is touch–turn–talk. This concept is di-
rectly applicable to the sales engineer, as it promotes a rhythm to the session. 
Here is a quick illustration of the concept. As the presenter, you touch 
the board (with an open hand or palm instead of pointing) to supply con -
text. You will usually draw focus onto the objects you have just drawn. You 
then turn to face the audience, talk about what you have just diagrammed, 
and then transition into a sentence or two describing what you are about

---

## Page 166

144 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Whiteboarding and Visual Selling 145
to draw. You then draw your next chunk of the WB and repeat the pro -
cess. This rhythm prevents you from speaking into the board or drawing for 
30–40 seconds without speaking.
The modifications you should make in a virtual environment are that  
turn is interpreted as looking into the camera, and touch is driven either by 
the mouse pointer or by a temporary highlighter pen.
Speed and Handwriting
The number one reason for poor handwriting and messy diagrams is that 
SEs try to write and sketch too quickly. This is primarily driven by the desire 
to eliminate any dead air and to cover too much material in a short amount 
of time. Try the following exercise.
Draw a vertical line that divides your board in half. Write the follow -
ing on one side of the board: your name, title, company name, and a few 
simple shapes. Have someone else observe your stance and time (this works 
virtually as well). Write normally. Now write precisely the same items but 
take a little extra time to make them neat and legible. Capital letters or 
block print is perfectly acceptable as opposed to cursive lettering. Compare 
the two times.
You will find that the difference between before and after (or quick ver-
sus neat) is usually about 10%. Those one or two extra seconds can make a 
difference between having a professional-looking and legible board or hav-
ing a bunch of illegible scrawls. Many electronic whiteboards do provide the 
benefit of text recognition and auto-assist drawing, which can rescue even 
the least talented SEs when using a tablet.
As a general guideline, your lettering on a classic whiteboard should be 
about 2 in (5 cm) in height to be legible. On a tablet, you have the options 
of shrink and zoom or scale to fit. If you are concerned about general leg -
ibility, try writing from the shoulder instead of pivoting from the elbow or 
the wrist. Of course, the exact opposite is true on an electronic device, and 
you should brace your drawing hand to eliminate shake. 
Table 15.3 
Touch–Turn–Talk in Action
Draw I draw the interface between the solar panel grid and the customer’s electrical 
distribution system. This takes a maximum of 10 seconds.
Touch I point back to the interface while still at about a 90-degree angle to the 
audience.
Turn I face the audience and fully engage them with hand gestures and eye contact, 
ensuring I am not obscuring the diagrams.
Talk This is where the interface takes place (e.g., “Most installations are 20 minutes 
or less . . . and now let us look at why this is so easy to maintain.”).
Draw Repeat the process.

---

## Page 167

146 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Whiteboarding and Visual Selling 147
Artistic Ability and Iconization
One of the best reference books for visual selling is The Back of the Napkin by 
Dan Roam.3 Dan makes the case that if you can draw the 12 basic shapes, 
the letters of the alphabet, numbers, and a monetary symbol, then you can 
sketch enough of a problem on the board so that people can understand it 
(see Figure 15.1). Then, of course, we must add a different shape, which is 
“the cloud.” There are also solution-specific symbols that you may need to 
practice (e.g., a simple lock and key are helpful when dealing with security). 
The critical point is that anyone can draw boxes, fill them with words, 
and draw connecting lines. However, you need to aspire to something higher 
and more memorable. The principle of iconization is that if you draw some-
thing that is only about 20% correct and lifelike and then tell the audience 
what it is, they will make the mental leap and fill in the rest. Remember that 
it needs to be conceptual and iconic rather than artistic. Nothing you draw is 
ever going to hang in the Louvre!4
Hint: Create an Iconic Library
In our customer workshops, we create an iconic library—a collection of standard icons that 
the SE team can share when creating their whiteboards. Most teams have between 80 and 
150 of these icons, which makes life easier for the less creative members of the group. You 
can categorize the library in whatever manner you wish. We usually recommend dividing 
the library by standard questions such as who/what, where, when, how many, why, and 
how? Many companies also have standard icons stored as JPG or SVG file libraries that 
you can access and utilize on tablets. 
The Implications of Color
The use of colors (Table 15.4) reflects what you are drawing and has a psy -
chological impact. Your standard color should be black. Use black for what 
is now the current situation. Next, use red to highlight what in sales termi -
3. Roam, D., Back of the Napkin,  New York: Penguin, 2009. See also www.napki -
nacademy.com.
4. That said, experiment with drawing auto-assist features. I discovered how to 
draw a kangaroo and a meteorite with Jamboard and managed to include both in 
a customer presentation.
Figure 15.1 The twelve basic shapes. (Courtesy and copyright Dan Roam.)

---

## Page 168

146 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Whiteboarding and Visual Selling 147
nology we call the pain. You use red to show the challenges and difficulties 
the customer faces. Then you use green to draw your solution (the desired 
state), showing how it uniquely addresses the red issues. Finally, use blue 
for authoritative statements, analyst and customer quotes, and any facts or 
figures to make your case.
In more than 95% of your boards, these four colors are sufficient. How-
ever, you should be aware that a small proportion of the male population 5 
has some form of red-green color blindness (in which case you omit one of 
those colors). Two notable additions are using yellow on a tablet for high -
lighting purposes and orange when dealing with the Dutch. 
Hint: Mind Your Colors!
Red is considered a lucky color in some Asian countries (as is yellow), so don’t use red for 
the competition in these cases. Yellow is associated with mourning in some parts of Latin 
America. Green can be associated with bad news in Israel. Use one color with a simple 
solution and multiple colors when comparing to a complex multivendor or multiproduct 
solution. Brown and purple are preferable to yellow or orange just for visibility purposes. 
Never reinforce a brand color—so if competing against IBM, you would never write IBM 
in blue. 
Some Shortcuts (Cheats) from Customers
Many of our great ideas come directly from our students in our workshops. 
Here are a few suggested ways to make life easier for the inexperienced or 
not-so-confident SE.
• When drawing on a poster board, you can sketch out a diagram 
beforehand in pencil or green pen. No one else can see it, and you’ll 
5. Red-green color vision defects are the most common form of color vision de -
ficiency. This condition affects males much more often than females. Among 
populations with Northern European ancestry, it occurs in about 1 in 12 males 
and 1 in 200 females.
Table 15.4  
The Psychological Use of Colors
Pen 
Color Situations in Which to Use the Color
Black Current state. The now. This is the default color.
Red Problems and pain the customer/industry faces. Use for competition or to 
highlight a very important point. 
Green The desired state. How your solution works. Also used for money (in the 
U.S.) and anything eco-friendly. (This is often is what you are selling!)
Blue Authority. May be customer quotes, analyst statements, ROI numbers, 
expense reductions, and so on.

## Page 169

look like a fantastic artist. You can start with a standard shape (like 
concentric circles or interconnected clouds) with an electronic board. 
• Use sticky notes (both physically and virtually) for memorable num-
bers.
• You can also use sticky notes for animation purposes. It is a great way 
to move files and data (or viruses) around an architecture diagram.
• Write any key numbers right at the top of the board, under the fold 
in the shadow, so only you can see them!
• On an electronic board, create a final page that contains frequent -
ly used icons and predrawn shapes you can drag into your working 
board.
• Use a slide (or JPG image of a slide) as a background. It’s a great way 
to incorporate a complex reference architecture into an electronic 
board.
• If your virtual transmission technology allows for direct annotation 
of slides when presented, use that feature.
• Remember that you can collaborate just as easily with a virtual 
shared board as you can with a physical board (and multiple pens!). 
• As a starting point for timing, most boards follow a 1:3 ratio. That 
is, for every minute that you draw, you will speak for about three 
minutes. So, an eight-minute delivery is about two minutes of actual 
drawing and six minutes of talk track. 
CASE STUDY: I See What You Mean
We worked with a group of first line SE managers from Northern Europe dur -
ing one of our electronic whiteboard training sessions. They intended to replace 
a series of slides with an interactive visual palette that they could use in their 
executive briefing workshops and a design session they named “Art of the Pos -
sible.” Over a day and a half, they eliminated 122 slides and shortened their 
session length by 45 minutes. They reported fabulous results after the very first 
pilot customer-facing session. The SE director said, “We created 12 million euros 
of solid pipeline in a two-hour session with a single customer executive team. 
The instant their chief technology officer said, ‘I see what you mean,’ I knew this 
was going to be a big success!”
E E E E

## Page 170

148 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Whiteboarding and Visual Selling 149
Using the Whiteboard as a Closing Tool
If you use these techniques, you will communicate effectively and sell your 
products and services. It is an instantly materializable skill in that you can 
practice it on a Tuesday and use it on a Wednesday—and amaze the sales -
people, the customer, and your manager. The ideal outcome is that you get 
real-time feedback from the customer, and you can diagram and modify 
the desired solution to fit their needs and win the deal. You can then, with 
permission, take a photo (if a physical whiteboard) to convert into a slide 
or share an electronic whiteboard to get confirmation that the diagram ac -
curately reflects the solution to their problem. If the customer agrees to that 
course of action, you have accomplished multiple goals. First, you have 
completed needs analysis and business value discovery. Second, the cus -
tomer understands your value proposition. Finally, you have buy-in from a 
gatekeeper or decision maker.
Hint: Clean Up Afterward
After the meeting, it is acceptable to clean up your board by replacing handwritten notes 
with typed text, straightening lines and boxes, and adding a few common logos and icons. 
You can then share this live with the customer—just don’t make it seem too robotic and 
correct everything. Part of the joy of visual selling lies in the imperfection of the content! 
Perhaps most usefully when selling to IT and business, you have an 
agreed-upon solution that can be labeled and attributed to the design team 
(e.g., “Solution crafted by Sonia Smith and her amazing cloud architecture 
team”). It is also personalized with customer-specific locations, products, or 
departments. You can now reuse this diagram with the business side of the 
customer and in other departments. A picture is worth a thousand words 
and a few references.
Summary
The effective use of a whiteboard and the entire concept of visual selling is a 
highly visible and valuable skill for an SE. It can instantly differentiate you 
from your peers and the competition by providing an alternate communica-
tions mechanism with your customers. You do not need to have neat hand-
writing and abundant artistic talent; you just need to structure and simplify 
your thoughts. It is an area where the creative SE can step up and make 
a difference in the sales process, and you will also discover that it will also 
prove to be an advantage in other nonwork aspects of your life. Being able 
to simply explain a complex subject is one of the most sought-after SE skills.

---

## Page 171

150 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Select three subject areas to build out initial whiteboards.
 2. Create your RM+3KP and frame out the whiteboard.
 3. Familiarize yourself with both virtual and physical whiteboard technology .
 4. Practice the delivery and video/record yourself for feedback.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Create a library for finished whiteboard frames and videos.
 2. Publish and extend an icon library and copy any corporate assets.
 3. Share best practices and collaborate with the product and marketing teams.
 4. Keep track of how many slides you retire.
 5. Hand the pen/stylus to the customer and let them draw .

Chapter Goals Storytelling
Marketing is no longer about the stuff that you make, 
but about the stories that you tell.
               —Seth Godin
S
torytelling is about as old as human lan -
guage. Our prehistoric ancestors used stories 
to make sense of their lives and nature, as well as 
their surroundings. Their stories were a way to 
share knowledge and preserve history. You can 
see visual stories in the ancient caves of Lascaux 
and Sulawesi dating back some 20,000 years. We 
live in the story.
Think back to when you were a small child, 
sitting on the lap of one of your parents or your 
grandparents. They told you stories to keep you 
entertained and to teach you about the world. 
(They also told you stories to make you fall 
asleep—but that is not a positive outcome for 
a book about sales!) From the earliest stages of 
your life, you used stories to communicate. As 
adults, we continue to tell stories, even though 
we don’t realize it. Some studies1 show that 65% 
of our daily conversations are based on the use 
of stories. 
Yet most sales engineers tell me that “Stories 
are for sales and marketing people. We deal in

facts, figures, and the reality of what the product or service can do. That is 
the environment where we are most comfortable.” Balance that with (ironi-
cally) the statistic from one customer that the average sales presentation 
contains 1.5 numbers every minute. How can you possibly expect the cus -
tomer to remember any of that and make coherent sense of your pitch?
That is where the effective use of storytelling comes into play. You are 
not writing a best-selling novel or a Hollywood hit. Instead, you are being 
asked to look at yet another way to make your message stick. Storytelling 
allows you to literally drop the MIC. Remember, that is memorable, interest-
ing, and compelling! 
The Good and the Bad of Stories and Sales
Every story has a beginning. We can start by looking at the positives and the 
negatives of using stories in a sales situation—as shown in Table 16.1. 
The items on the negative side of the table can seem daunting, especially 
to the logical process-oriented SE who is uncomfortable with the softer side 
of selling. Yet that same SE tells multiple stories every day about how their 
application crashed, how they fixed a bug, or how they MacGyvered 2 two 
products to work together. Extend that to the story about the idiot who cut 
them off in traffic or the rude Uber driver—we all use stories to communicate. 
It turns out that the positives greatly outweigh the negatives.
2. From both the MacGyver television shows of the mid-80s and the current day: 
to make or repair (an object) in an improvised or inventive way, making use of 
whatever items are at hand.
Table 16.1  
The Good and the Bad of Stories
The Positives of Stories The Negatives of Stories
Captures the attention of the audience Can seem lightweight
Motivates people to take action You can miss the mark and not connect
Builds trust and rapport A bad story is a bad story

What Makes a Good Story—Structure and Syntax
We will apply our engineering discipline and examine what makes a good 
story.
1. A story has a beginning, a middle, and an end. You don’t always 
have to go in that order, but we do expect those three components.
2. A story has a main character we think of as the hero or the hero -
ine. You (your company or your product) are never the main char-
acter. The customer is always the hero. 
3. A story contains at least one conflict or a challenge/problem for 
the main character to overcome. That is analogous to business pain 
(see Chapter 6).
4. A story usually has a villain. The villain may or may not be physi-
cally real (hacker, bug, downtime, outage, competition, regula -
tions, lawyers, government) and frequently drives the pain.
5. A story contains a resolution or a fix to the conflict, often linked to 
your solution. That resolution is the “happily ever after” phase.
6. The story may also hold a moral as the heroine overcomes the 
challenge. 
This outline looks amazingly like most movies, books, and TV shows. 
There is a formula that screenwriters and authors follow to build a compel-
ling plot. We can follow the same process to harness your inner creativity. 
Pixar, Inc., a subsidiary of Walt Disney Studios, has a multidecade history of 
creating highly successful animated movies such as Toy Story, Cars, Monsters, 
Inc., and Finding Nemo. Each has generated untold millions of revenues and 
kept the attention of millions of parents and children alike. It is a beloved 
and successful franchise. It uses a story structure called the Pixar Seven-Step 
Story Spine when crafting the outline of any new project.  Pixar animator 
Austin Madison creatively tells the story3 of the story spine in an incredibly 
amusing YouTube training video. A general outline spine with some added 
detail is shown in Table 16.2.
Hint: Transitions Matter
Notice the use of “because of that . . .” as a transition phrase rather than “and then . 
. .” Because of that implies causality and a logical chain of events. “And then . . .” is a 
common phrase uttered by an SE during a demo when they show a set of disconnected 
features. 
3. See https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aLVi0hjNrig.

One purpose of storytelling is to make the abstract more concrete, so 
Table 16.3 is an example you should recognize.
There is a lot more to  Star Wars Episode 4 (A New Hope ). However, way 
back in 1977, it did launch a massive franchise that continues to feature in 
popular culture throughout the world.  First, there is the setup and start of 
the story, which all changes with the inciting incident (until one day . . . ). 
 Table 16.2 
The Pixar Story Spine (Austin Madison, Lead Animator)
Step Phrasing Detail
1 Once there was a . . . A hero/heroine with a goal.
2 Every day he/she . . . More about the hero/heroine (usually good things).
3 Until one day . . . They face a challenge or a conflict—the inciting incident.
4 Because of that . . . Critical for a longer story. A compelling narrative is 
not random scenes (features/products) but has a vital 
element that causes the next event.
5 Because of that . . . We are building up the pain or tension.
6 Until finally . . . The heroine overcomes the challenge. Good triumphs 
over evil.
7 Ever since then . . . Results and outcomes. The moral of the story.

This acts as the catalyst to propel the rest of the story. In the Wizard of Oz, it 
is the tornado; in The Hobbit, Gandalf arrives at Bilbo Baggins’ door, and in 
the Indian Panchatantra, it is the treachery of the crocodile’s wife. 
Hint: Apply the Spine to a Movie You Love
Before you apply this seven-step process to your technology portfolio, try it with a well-
known book or movie. It’s a great team exercise to see how far you get into the steps before 
someone guesses your choice and completes the plot. 
Fine-Tuning the Story—Grammar and Numbers
The Seven-Step Spine is a wonderful way to construct an entire narrative 
for a story. Yet, a story does not always have to be a multiple-sentence 
structure; it can be a few simple words. This is a situation to harness the im-
mense power of language and make it work to your advantage. 
The first way to accomplish this is by using similes, analogies, and meta-
phors (SAM).4 They provide a method to make the complex simple and to 
turn esoteric into an everyday occurrence. Table 16.4 illustrates some of 
those typical examples. 
4. You are certainly not being paid to be a grammar expert, so although academics 
may fight over the different analogical language devices, it doesn’t matter how 
you label them—they all have their uses.
 Table 16.4 
The Use of Figures of Speech
Figure of 
Speech Definition Example
Simile Usually uses “as” in a 
direct comparison.
Sly as a fox.
Blind as a bat.
Stubborn as a mule.
Your explanation is as clear as mud.
Analogy How two things are 
alike. Often uses “it’s 
like.”
Technology is like a steamroller. Either you are 
driving, or you are part of the road.
That’s as useful as rearranging the deckchairs on

what you’re going to get.

comparison. Doesn’t

He’s one icon short on his toolbar.

Note that these devices are highly language and culture dependent—so 
what works in one country does not necessarily work in others. There is a 
famous story of a sales VP visiting Asia for a business review. He reminded 
the sales team to be in constant contact with their prospects and said, “Out 
of sight, out of mind.” Unfortunately, that phrase was translated into “Invis-
ible idiot”!
Sports metaphors also do not play well across different countries. For 
example, American baseball or football idioms such as “quarterback,” “call 
an audible, or “in your wheelhouse” are just as incomprehensible to most 
of the world as “hit for six” or “on a sticky wicket” to noncricket playing 
nations. 
CASE STUDY: A Visit to the Veterinarian 
Sometimes a simple analogy can be the easiest way to form the beginnings of 
an explanation. For example, when our dog Yardley, a wonderful Golden Re -
triever, was getting on in years, we took her to the vet, as she had mobility prob-
lems. The vet told us about a drug with a complicated name (like Penta-oxy-
cyclo-metacarb) that might provide relief. She explained that it used an NSAID 
vector to attack chronic swelling and worked by inhibiting some inflammatory 
enzymes—although the canine metabolic system did not always tolerate it. My 
wife and I looked at each other in confusion, as we were lost.
 We said, “So, it’s basically doggy Advil?”5
 “Yes,” replied the vet. 
 “Why not say that so it’s easier for us to understand?”
 The vet, a little shocked, said, “I thought I did!”
 You get the point. Sometimes, the meaning comes across loud and clear by 
relating a complex topic to something a little more straightforward and better 
understood. 
E E E E
The second technique for fine tuning the story involves humanizing num-
bers. If the average sales presentation contains 1.5 numbers per minute—
how can you make the significant numbers stand out? The answer is to 
make them relevant and personal. Numbers have feelings too. All too often, 
we present a batch of numbers to support a point, but, without context, it is 
challenging for 90% of us to internalize and truly comprehend the meaning 
of these numbers. This context particularly applies to very large numbers, 
very small numbers, percentages, and multiples. For example, you might tell 
a busy world-traveling executive that you can speed up a process by a factor 
of 7×. Follow that with “imagine if you could travel from London to New 
5. Also known as Motrin, Nurofen, or more generically ibuprofen.

---

## Page 178

156 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Storytelling 157
York City or Sydney to Singapore in about an hour—what difference would 
that make to your life?” Now that 7× becomes concrete and real, and is far 
more likely to be remembered. 
You cannot humanize every number in a deck or a demo, so you need 
to be selective. You also need to be prepared to translate that number into 
something more meaningful and stickier. 
CASE STUDY: That is a lot of data!
Bill, a Technical Consultant at a data storage company, illustrates the point. 
“We had a statistic at the start of our standard deck that said that the amount of 
data the typical customer would need to handle would multiply by a factor of 
35x over the next 4 years. It cited an impressive piece of analyst research, yet it 
always missed the mark with the customer and left them underwhelmed. We 
needed a way to humanize the 35-times number and make it painful. The next 
time I presented the deck, I pointed to a whiteboard mounted on the wall. ‘If 
that is the current amount of data you are handling, then it will be the size of a 
football field in 4 years. American or real football [it was a mixed audience]—it 
doesn’t matter. Whiteboard, football field. That’s a big change.’ We never made 
it to the next slide!”
E E E E
A Special Case—The Conversational Customer Reference 
Story 
Telling a brief story about how another customer solved a problem and 
succeeded with your products is a potent sales tool. Unfortunately, it is also 
a vastly underused tactic by SEs and greatly abused by marketing depart -
ments. Don’t think of this as the classic reference slide with a logo, a quote 
from a happy customer, and a short tag line. Instead, imagine you are sitting 
down with the prospective customer and having a cup of tea or coffee in an 
informal setting. 
You will create a short story, 60 seconds at most in length (160 words in 
English), which sounds natural and unrehearsed following a shortened and 
modified Seven-Step Spine (see Table 16.5). 
The chief controller of GE Capital in Connecticut had a problem. He 
couldn’t close his accounting books and cash balances fast enough at 
the end of the day. That meant he often had to guess where to send 
money. Last year, they paid $6 million in fines and overdraft fees. He 
approached us because of a previous business relationship and because 
IT said they could fix it by throwing $10 million of equipment at it.

---

## Page 179

158 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Storytelling 159
He said he needed to close out his books by 5:30 pm each day. So we 
provided him with a way to shrink the size of his production database 
and speed up the daily processing to close his books out at 5 pm. Because 
of that, he saved $6 million in fines, generated an added $11 million 
in interest, and optimized cash distribution across the GE affiliates. 
Everyone was happy!
That is 146 words and takes just over 50 seconds to tell if you resist the 
temptation to editorialize or include product names. How could you use that 
story? Imagine an executive asking you, “What kind of results should we 
expect?” or “Have other finance departments used this technology?” You 
gain credibility and reduce any perceived risk of moving forward with your 
proposal by telling the reference story. The higher up the corporate ladder 
you sell, the more often you can tell, and you don’t have to show (demo) 
an outcome. 
CASE STUDY: A Final Story About Stories 
As an SE director at Business Objects in the early 2000s, I discovered some fan-
tastic reference accounts, yet we rarely mentioned them in our sales motion. At 
the time, our massive number of references was an unused competitive advan -
tage against competitors MicroStrategy and Cognos. As part of a quarterly MBO 
program, I asked each SE within the region to submit two personal reference 
stories using the 60-second format. One of my managers jokingly called the 
resultant collection of stories “Tales from the Book of John.” We kept the stories 
as a regional SE asset, and our business continued to grow. One day I received 
a call from Jim Tolonen, our CFO. Jim had a problem in that his team was re -
ceiving poor reviews for their quarterly Wall Street earnings broadcasts, and he 
wanted to spice up the meetings by speaking about more customers. He had 
heard about our reference book and asked to “borrow” it for some anonymous 
stories. You can hardly refuse the CFO, so we sent him the book. After the next 
quarterly earnings call, Jim’s analyst satisfaction score went up about a point, 
and he kindly sent a lovely gift to my entire team. Everyone is in sales—it is the 
power of story.
E E E E

toward your solution.

solve the problem. “They told me they needed a way to . . . ”

Summary
Do not be afraid of stories, and actively use those stories in a sales situation. 
Stories are for everyone, not just sales and marketing people. Even in a 
pure speeds and feeds–type conversation, a story can still make the overall 
concept and any specific numbers sing. 
They do, however, need practice so that they sound natural rather than 
something learned in a class or from a book. That is the personalization 
aspect of the story. 
Take the seven-step spine structure and develop a set of product or 
feature stories that you can use to make your presentations and demonstra-
tions more memorable, interesting, and compelling. 
Once there was a sales engineer who loved technology and loved solving 
technical problems. Every day he gave technical presentations and long 
custom demos but dreamed of doing more. Until one day, when he was 
out in the field, a salesperson told him to “get out of the weeds” and 
clearly explain how his technology could help his customers instead 
of talking about boring bits and bytes. Because of that, he decided to 
explain the portfolio in simple terms that nontechies could understand. 
Because of that, he built many stories and FABulous explanations. Until 
one day, he became one of the most popular, well-paid, and successful 
SEs in the entire geography. (Ever since then, he has been on the path 
to becoming a Jedi Knight.)
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Listen to the stories of the other SEs and experienced salespeople.
 2. Repurpose those stories using the seven-step spine to make them your own.
 3. Create your personal stories once you have some initial wins.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Develop a human explanation of all the important numbers in your presentation.
 2. Build a set of conversational customer reference stories.
 3. Look for complicated and complex concepts—apply SAM.

CHAPTER

Chapter Goals Evaluation Strategies
What can be asserted without proof can be dismissed 
without proof.
          —Christopher Hitchins
D
uring the sales cycle, you arrive at a stage 
when you need to prove that your solution 
works and meets the customer’s requirements. 
That proof may vary from the simple “We trust 
you, it’s a proven product, and we’ll buy it ” 
through a range of SaaS configurations to a com-
plex multiperson, multiweek on-premises install 
and test plan. In between those two extremes lie 
demonstrations, custom demonstrations, trials, 
evaluations, and proofs of concept.
This chapter examines the scale of evalua -
tions, how to negotiate the success criteria, deter-
mine the least amount of work for an evaluation, 
and successfully execute the evaluation plan.
The Effort Scale (How Did We Get Here?)
SE organizations automatically associate evalua -
tions with a proof of concept (POC). Salespeople 
frequently push an opportunity by offering a free 
trial or evaluation—which primarily translates 
into a POC. The terminology becomes synony -
Creation of an evaluation 
scale linked to the amount 
of work.
Determination of success 
criteria and creating a 
project plan.
Execution of the project 
plan to gain the technical 
and the business win.

---

## Page 183

162 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 163
mous. I’ll use POC1 to encompass all trials, evaluations, and POCs as a gener-
ic term. However, suppose you accept that one of your primary work goals 
is to help the salespeople achieve the maximum revenue with the minimum 
necessary effort. In that case, you should not automatically default to a POC. 
There are other evaluation strategies to investigate, some of which are listed 
next.
1. References. Find references from two or more existing customers 
who operate in a similar scale and configuration. Set up both tech-
nical and business conference calls. The sales team may or may not 
be present and active on the call.
2. Reference visits. Have your customer physically visit another custom-
er who operates at a similar scale and configuration. The sales team 
may or may not be present and active on the call.
3. Lightly customized demonstration.  Set up a relatively standard prod -
uct demonstration, customized with appropriate process/industry 
terminology and some data. This is loosely defined as less than 2 
hours spent in modifications. You may have a set of similar demos 
prerecorded and stored in a demo library for future use.
4. Moderately customized demonstration. You can also set up a lightly cus-
tomized demonstration expanded with client-specific data, process-
es, or UI modifications. This is loosely defined as taking between 
2 and 8 hours of work and is potentially reusable for clients in a 
similar vertical/industry.
5. Heavily customized demonstration. This demonstration is specifically 
created and scripted for a single client with purpose-designed data 
and inputs/outputs. It is loosely defined as more than eight hours 
of work and unlikely to be re-used. You may also be demonstrating 
interfaces with other products and systems in multiple locations.
6. Proof of concept (trials).  Differentiated from previous options, the 
customer operates autonomously (at times) and offsite from your 
location (on-premises or cloud). A POC may last from a day to a 
month and is differentiated from a trial by some vendors in terms 
of increased engagement and overall planning.
7. Pilot. This is a complete installation into a production-style environ-
ment. It is limited by deployment or usage parameters for testing 
and acceptance purposes. 
1. You will also hear about proof of value as an alternative phrasing to proof of 
concept.

---

## Page 184

162 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 163
Determine where each of these options, plus any others you devise, 
land on the effort scale for your portfolio and organization. The strategy 
is to select the approach that involves minimal SE effort in return for the 
maximum chance of success. You may also be driven upscale by your com-
petition. If they offer a POC, then it is likely that you will have to provide 
the same just to remain active in the opportunity.
Customers typically ask for a POC for one of three main reasons. The 
first reason is that they have been unable to determine whether a claim you 
made during the sales process is accurate. This uncertainty may be due to 
situations such as lack of references, zero documentation, or your product’s 
newness. There may also be general issues with integration or compatibil -
ity. The second reason is that the customer wishes to play around with the 
product2 before buying it. This often shows that the customer does not re -
ally know what they want but will recognize it when they see it. In these 
cases, it is an unknown unknown.3 The third reason is that there may be le-
gal or compliance requirements to evaluate all technology before purchase. 
Each of these situations leads to the need for a definition of success and 
creation of a project plan. The depth of the plan depends on the amount 
of time, money, or people consumed by the project and is usually directly 
proportional to dedicated resources. 
At the absolute other end of the scale is the pilot. A pilot is the proper 
installation of a product or service into a production environment. A pilot 
may be limited by other parameters such as the number of deployed sites or 
the number of servers/devices/users involved. A pilot is delivered because 
the solution will stay in place if you meet acceptance criteria. In most cases, 
a pilot is a chargeable event and usually primed by either internal profes -
sional services or a partner organization. Pilots and SEs do not mix well, 
as the role of an SE is to make something work without much regard for 
eventual production deployment. There may also be legal liability issues 
around an SE touching a client’s production system—so you should always 
check that. 
Creating the Project Plan
When a client requests a POC, the account team should consider this as an 
initial negotiation point. A POC is an exchange or barter arrangement in 
2. Such as the infamous “architecture review committee” much beloved by many 
Fortune 500 companies.
3. For more on this, read about the Johari window: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Johari_window.

---

## Page 185

164 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 165
that you agree to commit assets in return for a consideration from the cus -
tomer. To use the negotiating terminology, that consideration may be any -
where from being allowed to continue in the bid process to winning the deal 
and obtaining revenue. This step is a chance to match your selling process 
to the customer’s buying process. If the amount of work required from you, 
your company, or third parties is significant, you should create a project plan 
containing a statement of work with clearly defined success criteria.
You should then work with your sales partner to present this plan to the 
customer. The plan can have the effect of qualifying out some of the casual 
tire kickers.4 It demonstrates to the serious customer that you have a plan 
and have done this before and mean business. Some technology companies 
charge for POCs, with the customer being credited for services once a con -
tract is signed. Although this approach was quite popular a few decades ago, 
especially when sizeable pieces of hardware were shipped around, it has 
largely fallen out of favor except for a few niche services areas or a codevel-
opment project.
Even for a lightweight POC (where you anticipate minimal engagement 
on your part), it still helps to present a suggested project plan and user expe-
rience guide to help your customer traverse the initial setup, administration, 
and testing of your solution. The project plan should undoubtedly include 
a reference timeline, roles and responsibilities, key milestones, and overall 
success criteria. In addition, many companies now supply online fill-in-the-
blanks guides and microtraining videos to help their customers get started 
and lessen the load on the SE and support teams. 
How Do Y ou Define Success?
Your ability to negotiate success criteria is probably the most critical step 
in the entire evaluation process. You need to find an intersection between 
what your customer needs to be convinced about, the value you can pro -
vide, and the required amount of time. Think of that as the intersection of 
a three-circle Venn diagram. If that intersection does not exist, then you 
are wasting time better spent on another opportunity. You may be able to 
improve your chances of success by negotiating a smaller POC. If you face a 
competitor with more significant resources or a more feature-rich product, 
you will benefit if that competitor cannot bring those extra resources to bear 
on the POC. Conversely, if you work for a larger company competing against 
a smaller company, you may wish to broaden the scope and overwhelm 
them.
4. The term comes from salespeople at car dealerships. Tire kickers would come 
around frequently, kick the tires a few times on the cars that they liked, but never 
make a solid purchasing decision on any particular car or trim.

---

## Page 186

164 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 165
How to Win: Determining the Success Criteria
Begin by asking the customer why they want to engage in a POC. (“Please 
tell me more about the internal process behind this POC.”) Then, you 
should be able to answer the following questions:
1. Do we need to prove specific points or give the customer a general level of 
comfort? Be wary of situations where you need to provide an un -
defined level of comfort. This can lead to a situation where the 
customer wants to play with your solution to get a feel for it. The 
apparent ease of lighting up a cloud instance will lead the customer 
down this path. It is perfectly acceptable to ask, “Could you give 
me a bit more definition around what a good feeling or a level of 
comfort means?” 
2. Has the customer provided objective success criteria? Could a third party 
with limited knowledge of your solution judge whether the POC 
can meet the requirements? If not, you are in danger of having 
political alignment or other unknown factors interfere with the 
POC. Your company may already have a template for success cri -
teria. If not, you can create your own using any standard goal-
setting method such as specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, 
and time-bound (SMART). See the example in Table 17.1.
3. Are external consultants or advisors involved? For example, a customer 
may hire consultants to help them evaluate products. These con -
sultants may have their agenda (aside from continuing their rev -
enue stream), especially if they may be involved in the eventual 
rollout of the technology or the overall project. You and the rest of 
the account team will need to discover what defines success for the 
consultants as that may differ from the actual customer’s definition 
of success.
Table 17.1 
Sample SMART Success Criteria
Specific Load daily individual store sales item data for the Europe, Middle East, 
and Africa geographies to be ready for morning processing.
Measurable Use a 10% sample of data for process validation. Require less than 1% 
data errors and duplicate the current validation report.
Achievable What is the top success criteria? This solves timely decision-making issues.
Relevant First analysts must access the system at 7 am EST.
Time-bound This will be achieved by day seven of the POC.

166 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 167
CASE STUDY: It’s Not the Software—It’s the Implementation!
Tim, an SE manager from DACH (Germany, Austria, Switzerland), shares his 
story: “We were invited to participate in a big data query/warehouse opportuni-
ty with a local media company. They had hired a consulting company to conduct 
the overall evaluation on their behalf and a subsequent POC. We were in direct 
head-to-head competition with the other large vendor in our space and felt that 
we were the better fit. As we entered the POC phase, we had a set of mutually 
agreed success criteria—about 60% objective and 40% subjective. Progressing 
through the 10-day POC, we outscored our competitor by 10%–15% on most 
measurable items such as load time, response times, and so on. We felt we were 
in a good position to win what was a sizeable deal. We lost! Badly. The con -
sulting company had a sister organization that had a delivery practice centered 
around our competitor. Every single subjective factor (ease of setup, graphical 
user interface, ability to customize) went in our opponent’s favor, and our wins 
were minimized as trivial (“Who cares if it takes a report 4 or 4.3 seconds to ren-
der?”). It was hard to argue against, even with an apparent conflict of interest 
and the German sense of detail and accuracy. We learned an expensive lesson 
about the value of having 100% objective criteria and spending more time un -
derstanding the personal wins of external agents.”  
E E E E
4. Can you make proof points that your competitors cannot? Are those points 
part of the documented success criteria? A no to either of those questions 
indicates your POC success criteria are out of alignment with your 
win-the-deal criteria. Even if you do not have a direct competi -
tor, you still need to prove better than “Do Nothing, Incorporated” 
(DNI) (see Chapter 25). 
Once you have answered these questions, you can develop your en -
gagement strategy. The key is to make sure that everyone on the sales team 
understands their role in winning. 
Evaluating the Success Criteria
After initially documenting the success criteria, you must make sure you can 
achieve them to prove your point. Although you may have set some criteria, 
your customer and even a competitor can influence what to include. For 
each item, ask the following:
1. Are you better than the competition at this?
2. Can you objectively prove it?
3. Can you prove it with the time, money, and people available?

---

## Page 188

166 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 167
Once you have honestly answered these questions for each item on the 
success criteria list, you must answer one more obvious question—will you 
now score higher than your competitor? This is tricky as you must account 
for the competitor’s internal and political connections and their influence 
on the buying process. However, there is nothing more painful than being 
beaten by a competitor on an item or feature you consider a strength. 
Intellectually Closing the Deal
Now that you have identified the success criteria, you or your salesper -
son should get the customer to commit to their willingness to do the deal 
when you complete the POC. For example, present your estimated staffing, 
elapsed time, and equipment costs, and ensure the customer realizes that 
their company will be making similar investments. If you cannot get this 
commitment, do not agree to the POC.
One good practice is to bring in a sales or SE manager at this point to 
help you confirm the customer’s readiness to proceed. If you do not com -
plete this step, you risk winning the evaluation and then having the cus -
tomer not go forward. This is commonly the case when the review is run by 
the IT staff, who may not have the authority to move the project forward. A 
pre-POC executive validation is a good buying qualification signal.
Running a Proof of Concept
When placed in charge of a POC, ensure you are ready for the responsibil -
ity that comes with it. Evaluations are costly, high risk, and high visibility. 
If your POC melts down because you did a poor job, it can taint you for 
the rest of your career with the company. You need experience in project 
management plus a deep understanding of your solution. Without these 
two strengths, you will have a difficult time succeeding. If you feel weak in 
either area, consider asking your manager to bring in a more experienced 
person as a mentor from whom you could learn the appropriate skills. Table 
17.2 shows the high-level steps/phases for a POC. Some of these phases may 
be redundant or even overkill for a lightweight POC, so use your judgment.
Phase 1: Document the Success Criteria
Collaborate with the customer to establish what the expected deliverable is 
for each item. Ideally, you can prototype the system with the customer and 
then work backward to identify necessary engineering tasks.
Although much of the focus around a POC centers on the hardware 
and software aspects of the process, it is essential to devote considerable 
time to the “peopleware.” This time involves building relationships with all

---

## Page 189

168 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 169
customer personnel concerned with the POC. Highlighting and discussing 
the success criteria with all involved will help in gaining their buy-in and 
overall assistance. Dropping a checklist on someone’s desk or email inbox at 
the end of the week is not the best way to start a business relationship. 
Phase 2: Mini-Discovery
Once you have documented the success criteria, ask for access to the peo -
ple responsible for using the functionality. Spend the time reviewing your 
documented success criteria to ensure that your goals accurately reflect their 
business needs. Pay particular attention to whether there are other quantifi-
able business benefits.
Conclude this phase by having the customer sign off on the require -
ments. Once again, this is to get their commitment that your document de -
scribes the result they need to keep the purchase process moving. Again, 
consider this more of a personal agreement, as you are very unlikely to 
receive anything formal or legally binding at this point. 
Phase 3: Development
The development phase is when the real work starts. Of course, your com -
pany will have its working style, but the following suggestions are valuable 
for any SE team. First, pad the schedule by no less than 30% because the 
unexpected will happen. Second, if necessary, negotiate your way out of 
some of the requirements.
Third, compartmentalize the critical development phases. Most develop-
ers are used to having the flexibility to spend extra time on particularly com-
plex problems. By “time-boxing” key deliverables, you can help ensure that 
a single setback does not affect multiple downstream deliverables. This ap -
proach is not intuitive for traditional developers, who will frequently claim 
that a given deliverable can be completed “in a few more hours.” Be firm 
when this happens; tell the person the requirement will be addressed once 
all other requirements are satisfied.
Table 17.2  
The Seven Phases of a Proof 
of Concept
1 Document the success criteria
2 Conduct mini-discovery
3 Development
4 Test (and test again) 
5 Deployment (if necessary)
6 Demonstration and validation 
7 Presentation of results

---

## Page 190

168 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 169
Hint: Prepare for the Worst
Before starting the process, notify your corporate support department that you will be con-
ducting a POC and provide them with as much technical information about the prospect’s 
environment as possible. Ideally, for a complex or beta-product POC, ask for a dedicated 
support engineer to call and a backup for that individual. Of course, you do not need to 
do this for every POC, but certainly for those where you are onsite or may be unavailable 
for significant periods. 
Phase 4: Test
Budget time as necessary for testing. If your deliverable is simply a dem -
onstration, you may need to perform minimal testing. If usage testing is 
necessary, then you should allocate significant time for testing. Actual use 
always unearths unforeseen problems. You are better off being seen as the 
stable solution, even if you miss a few of the less critical success points based 
on available time.
Hint: Automate the POC Process
Some companies are now in the early stages of applying remote monitoring/AI to the POC 
process. A bot oversees the POC and tracks workloads, general activity, errors, and the 
like, so it can notify the SE when anything important occurs. It’s an excellent investment 
to free up valuable SE time.
As a best practice, always take screenshots of particular events, success-
es (and failures), test case results, and so on, for documentation purposes. 
Phase 5: Deployment
If you deploy your system onto customer equipment or sites, book a pre -
liminary deployment time when your initial development is complete. 
Problems will frequently arise during deployment that you can work on 
in parallel with development. When operating in the cloud, your instance 
should be primed and ready for operation.
Hint: Always Check Out the Interfaces
In my experience, the most common problems with POC setup and logistics have to do 
with accessing the customer’s applications. If your system relies on interconnectivity with 
any other systems, be sure to have your access and passwords confirmed and tested well 
in advance.
Phase 6: Demonstration and Validation
Once you have completed the requirements, present the implementation 
to the customer. Depending on the nature of the audience, you may wish

---

## Page 191

170 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 171
to use a formal approach as described next for phase 7. In many cases, the 
validation of the results will be an informal discussion with the customer’s 
technologists. In either case, present the customer with your documents and 
ask them to sign off on your success criteria.
Phase 7: Presentation of Results
This payoff presentation may be with a broad group/committee or only to 
the decision makers. If it is just the executives, the salesperson will probably 
deliver the presentation, and you will have to help put together the content. 
We recommend an agenda that includes the following:
• Overview of requirements;
• Discussion of project approach; 
• Project results;
• Unique business benefits and reiteration of acknowledged customer 
pains and gains.
The focus should be on the unique benefits of your solution. Have the 
stack of signed-off success documents handy as well. It will make for an im-
pressive prop when you are dealing with the executives.
Don’t Forget Training and Documentation
When your customers want to use your product, they will first need train -
ing. Training may range from personal one-on-one coaching to a set of on -
line video tutorials. Encourage your customer to watch the videos before 
laying hands on the product. Easier said than done, but do not underesti -
mate the difficulty of developing and delivering a POC and simultaneously 
training the customer. Your temptation may be to speed along and just get it 
done, yet you need to let the customer feel that they can easily accomplish 
all of the required tasks.
Document any work performed during the POC. Work includes formal 
and informal meetings, issue resolution, and the multiple “can you just . . 
. ?” activities that invariably arise. If you write any customer or POC-spe -
cific code, then document it extensively along with comments. The few ex -
tra minutes you take for documentation will save you hours at some later 
point. The most common tracking mechanism is a shared drive with live 
documents, including the success criteria status. For a complex POC, you 
might want to keep some internal notes, which can later be handed over to 
a post–sales/implementation team, covering both complex deliverables and 
soft expectations.

---

## Page 192

170 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 171
Don’t Forget the Competition!
By now, you should be well prepared. You have put together a compre -
hensive and professional success plan to meet the customer requirements. 
However, do not lose sight of the competition, whether a standard cor -
porate competitor or DNI. Always expect some competitive pressure and 
maneuvering.
If, as a result of answering Q4 of the “How to Win” section, you can 
find some low-risk competitive differentiator that the customer did not nec-
essarily ask for yet supports one of the success criteria, then you should 
include it. This tactic is analogous to the “Add” step discussed in Chapter 6 
(“Business Value Discovery”). By proposing and then demonstrating some-
thing unique, you place any competitor in a difficult position where they 
are unlikely to have the time to formulate a response. You may even be able 
to destabilize the competitor’s core value proposition. Consider the follow-
ing classic case study. 
CASE STUDY: Changing the Rules
In the late 1980s, both Oracle and Sybase were vying to become the standard 
RDBMS for the entire global research and development organization at pharma-
ceutical giant Eli Lilly. Everything came down to a head-to-head POC installed 
at their corporate headquarters in Indianapolis. Oracle (my employer) had an 
advantage in having a larger stable of third-party clinical trial applications. We 
ran most tasks faster than Sybase on multiprocessor machines—admittedly due 
to a sprawling, untidy architecture. Sybase ran about twice as fast as us on 
single- and dual-processor machines and had already incorporated referential 
integrity and basic data validity checks, which the developers and database ad-
ministrations (DBAs) absolutely loved. There was little difference between us. 
Except—Oracle had just released a Version 1.0 system on the Apple Macintosh, 
allowing data access through cards and spreadsheets. Every researcher used a 
Mac. We held an impromptu brown-bag lunch session and demonstrated the 
system to a few dozen R&D personnel. We then engaged a young Oracle prod-
uct manager named Marc Benioff during a subsequent corporate visit—and 
won the deal! A $99 add-on generated an eventual multimillion-dollar revenue 
stream over the next 10 years.
E E E E
And Don’t Forget the People
I noted the importance of peopleware earlier in the chapter. You will rarely 
win the deal just because your product works. You win the contract when 
you win over the people. Chapter 19 (“The Trusted Advisor Sales Engi -
neer”) lays out a framework for building these relationships. Whether you 
have an occasional phone check-in, more frequent email/Slack communi -

---

## Page 193

172 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 173
cations, or are onsite for multiple days, you always have an opportunity to 
build the relationship and help the customer. Just because you have a plan, 
that does not mean that the POC will run itself. You cannot solely focus on 
getting the POC done, as you will miss many other opportunities for interac-
tions around you. Part of the earlier advice to pad the schedule by 30% is 
to account for an impromptu lunch meeting or a coffee with the boss. It is 
all part of selling, even though you may want to be 100% engineer during 
a POC. 
The Technical Win
Finally, a few thoughts about the technical win (TW). What is the technical 
win? How do you define it, and is it even necessary? Has the technical win 
become redundant in the modern world of solution selling? Ask any group 
of sales engineers to define their job, and they’ll likely mention the phrase 
“We’re responsible for winning the technical sale.” Many SE organizations 
measure and publish their technical win rate for various tasks like RFPs, 
POCs, and demonstrations. Achieve the technical win  is now a required out -
come to exit an intermediate stage in some sales processes.
A VP of sales engineering at a large software company told me their 
POC technical win rate was 82%, yet the business win (BW) rate (defined as 
generating revenue) was only 69%. I asked if his team was in any way com-
pensated for a technical win. They are not. No one is paid on the technical 
win. Never! But you can learn something from the statistics.
Let’s take a step back and look at a working definition. The technical 
win is when someone with authority (a recommender or approver in sales 
terminology) says that your solution is the best fit for their needs. That may 
be winning a competitive evaluation, a benchmark, or simply a verbal or 
written statement. But what is that worth other than a checkbox in your 
CRM system? 
It is an unpleasant fact (small startups aside) that the TW rate will al -
ways be higher than the BW rate. That is the case in more than 200 of 
my customers. Companies can decide to do nothing; lose or move funding; 
make an acquisition; change market strategy; fire your internal champion; 
or base their decision upon boardroom or golf course politics. The BW rate 
can sometimes underperform the TW rate by 20 or 30 percentage points. 
CASE STUDY: The Worthless Win
Christopher, an SE director in the U.K. and Ireland region, tells this story: “My 
team was onsite at a large financial services company here in the U.K. So was

---

## Page 194

172 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Evaluation Strategies 173
our competition. We were going head to head for 2 weeks. At the end of the 
fortnight, we had outperformed our competition and won an 11–0 vote from 
the evaluation committee. That’s a technical win! But no purchase order ap -
peared. A month later, we discovered our competitor had won the business 
with inferior technology by promising to move all their U.S. employee retire -
ment assets over to the financial company. Again, the technical win was worth-
less. We were all clearly outsold.”
E E E E
Whenever an SE team externally advertises its technical win rate in any 
part of the sales process, they effectively taunt the sales team. That usually 
ends in tears. So, what is the value? It is in the personal and internal orga -
nizational improvement of the SE team. By closely looking at why you win 
and why you lose, you can get better in all aspects of customers interaction. 
The most significant return is usually in the area of POC metrics. The lesson 
to be learned from the technical win is that it is not the point at which SE 
engagement halts. The SE team is responsible for continuing and partner -
ing with the sales team to ensure that the business win follows. Fame and 
fortune then follow the business win. 
Summary
POCs are challenging to control and time consuming. Unfortunately, cus -
tomers still insist on them as every customer believes (rightly so!) that their 
needs and their IT environment are unique. There are time, money, and 
people costs to perform a POC on the buying and selling sides of the equa -
tion. A POC should be positioned as a material step toward a significant 
partnership rather than a throw-away activity because the account team 
runs out of ideas.
Like most activities in the life of an SE, a few extra minutes spent in 
the planning and discovery stages of a POC can go a long way to making 
life easier later in the process as you are approaching decision or end-of-
month/quarter time. Tony Matos, author of The Essential Guide to Navigate 
Your Proof of Concept, summarizes everything with his “Seven Habits for Proof 
of Concepts” (Table 17.3).5 He shows the seven habits that help improve the 
human interactions of a POC with a customer while also improving your 
technical win rate.
5. Matos, T., The Essential Guide to Navigate Your Proof of Concept, Tony Matos, 2020.

---

## Page 195

174 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Shadow a more experienced colleague as a junior project manager on a complex POC.
 2. Build a list of potential differentiators for competitive disruption.
 3. Plan for the hardware, software, and peopleware components of the POC.
 4. Work hard and plan even harder! Y ou are in charge of a significant corporate investment.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Collaborate with the account manager on the value proposition. 
 2. Mentor and oversee your less experienced colleagues.
 3. Automate as much as possible. Maintain all the relationships after the POC is complete. 
    Expand your network.
Table 17.3  
The Seven SE Habits for Successful Proof of Concepts
1 Take time to document the POC. There is value in capturing the what, who, 
when, where, and how.
2 Understand why you consent to a POC. Take the time to qualify on both 
technical and business criteria.
3 Deliver business value. Take the focus off the technology and place the customer at 
the center of the POC. 
4 Focus on the essentials that lead to a successful POC. Know and complete the 
required elements for success. Avoid a partial implementation. 
5 Understand the outcomes of a POC. Grasp the bigger (strategic) picture of the 
customer’s requirements.
6 Conduct thorough discovery before starting a POC. Just because you can do it 
does not mean that you should.
7 Build the customer relationship. Nurture trust. Invest time to understand them, 
walk in their shoes and see life through their eyes.
