CHAPTER
18
Chapter Goals Answering Questions
We thought that we had the answers—it was the 
questions we had wrong.
                —Bono
M
illions of articles exist about objection 
handling,1 also known as answering ques -
tions. It is usually thought of as a sales-oriented 
activity yet is an essential skill for a sales engineer. 
In earlier editions, this chapter was titled “Objec-
tion Handling,” which later became “Managing 
Questions,” and I’ve finally settled on “Answer-
ing Questions.” The distinction is important.
Questions are good and positive activities 
and should be encouraged throughout the sales 
cycle as a sign of interest and curiosity. They are 
mostly neither adversarial nor confrontational 
but are a natural means to progress the oppor -
tunity and relationship through the sales cycle. 
The inclination of an SE is to fix things and help 
people, as we are inherently pleasers. Therefore, 
we answer questions when asked. I prefer to 
think that salespeople handle objections, while 
sales engineers answer questions. 
Throughout this chapter and the book, I free-
ly interchange between objection and question. 
1. A quick Google search yields over 33 million re -
sults for “sales objection handling.”
Learn a simple four-step 
process to answer most 
questions.
Be able to categorize 
questions by type quickly.
Differentiate the different 
personalities behind the 
questions.

---

## Page 197

176 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 177
The difference between the two is that one is a step removed from the other. 
A question is direct, as in “Do you have the ability to monitor encrypted 
network traffic?” Objections are commonly in the form of a statement—the 
two sales classics being “Your price is too high,” and “We have no budget.” 
The technical equivalent of these statements is “That user interface looks 
very complicated,” and “You don’t have the functionality we need.” You can 
then translate these into related questions, which become “Can you explain 
to me the design principles of your user interface?” and “How can you do 
this set of tasks?”
One foundational rule of sales engineering is that your intent matters 
more than your ability and your knowledge. Merely showing a willing at -
titude to help the customer can help cover up a few gaps in your technical 
knowledge base. That positive attitude comes through in your verbal and 
nonverbal communications and can make an amazing difference in custom-
er receptivity. 
Listen, Accept, Clarify , and Execute—The LACE Strategy
Answering questions can be one of the more stressful parts of the SE job. 
First, it helps to have a foundational methodology in place, so most mental 
gymnastics that occur when you are asked a question occur by habit rather 
than by 100% conscious thought. Otherwise, you can overthink every situ-
ation, and that invariably ends in tears as you second-guess yourself into 
disaster.
The foundational methodology we’ll use here is listen, accept, clarify, 
and execute (LACE). Table 18.1 provides a quick summary of each step, and 
Table 18.2 provides an illustrative answer process.
Now to break down each step into more detail.
Table 18.1 
The Basics of LACE
Stage Detail 
Listen Show the customer you are actively listening and understand.
Accept Emotionally accept the question and demonstrate empathy.
Clarify Make sure you understand the questions. 
Who answers?
First get more information.
Execute Communicate the answer.
Confirm understanding.

---

## Page 198

176 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 177
Listen to the Customer
Most of us believe that we are awesome at listening. In truth, we are not. 2 
We spend time listening to our inner voice of “What am I going to say when 
this person finishes speaking?” instead of genuinely listening to the other 
person. Effective listening is further complicated by the fact that not only do 
you have to actively listen,3 but you also need to convince the speaker that 
you are indeed listening. 
Before answering a question, the first important step is listening very 
carefully and observing the individual asking the question. Next, look at the 
speaker as they pose the question and then prove that you are listening by 
taking some form of action. This action can be taking notes, nodding your 
head, or even quietly muttering “OK” or “Uh huh.” The outcome is that the 
speaker feels, through your body language, that you are paying attention. 
You may even need to overemphasize some of these reactions in a remote 
video interaction to ensure the customer feels their message is being re -
ceived. Everyone wants to be heard!
The second step is to pause before responding to the question. A com -
mon mistake for inexperienced SEs is recognizing the question and then 
immediately launching into their answer. Sometimes they may even inter-
rupt the customer in their eagerness to supply the solution. Younger SEs 
(and salespeople, to be fair) are so keen to demonstrate their knowledge 
2. Ask your spouse, partner, children, or a close friend. Just try it!
3. Active listening isn’t only applicable to sales; nor is it a new thing. Dr. Carl Rog-
ers, one of the founding fathers of psychotherapy research, originated the con -
cept of reflective listening in the 1940s. In the following years, Richard Farson, a 
student of his, renamed it active listening.
Table 18.2 
LACE in Action 
Question: Can Your Product Do Reverse Polymerase  
Network Transmission (RPNT)?*
Stage Detail 
Listen [Nod head. Smile. Pause.]
Accept “Do RPNT? Absolutely.”
Clarify “I expect that’s probably something you need to get done many 
times a day?” [Look at rep to confirm that you should continue 
to answer.]
Execute [Wait to see if customer provides more information.]
“Yes, we can do RPNT. Many of my customers view that as a 
significant and unique feature of our product.”
[Pause to see if the customer wants more information or 
supplies additional context.]
*This will undoubtedly be supported in the next release of your product.

---

## Page 199

178 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 179
and look smart that any pretense of active listening is tossed aside. There are 
many advantages4 to a brief pause:
• It shows that you are seriously considering the question.
• Someone else (from the customer side) may handle the question on 
your behalf.
• Should the question lie outside of your area of expertise, another 
team member has an opportunity to answer it in the accept phase.
Accept the Question
Once the question is asked and you have paused, it is time to accept (or 
acknowledge) it. This acceptance does not mean that you agree with the 
question, and it is not the time to be defensive or argumentative. Instead, 
you can think of this as an emotional acceptance. For a concise and straight-
forward nonthreatening question that you can answer in a sentence, the 
accept phase is noticeably short and may be a simple head nod or verbal 
“OK.” However, for other instances where you feel there may be emotion 
or judgment behind the question, this phase is an absolute necessity even 
though you may get through it in just a few seconds.
You show acceptance both verbally and nonverbally. Nonverbal accep -
tance may consist of a smile and the use of your arms to gather in the ques-
tion (imagine someone has gently tossed a ball at your chest). Verbal accep-
tance should be no more than a sentence or two. Tables 18.3 shows some 
examples.
A few words of warning about the accept phase: do not overdo it. Your 
response needs to sound authentic and like something you would say and 
4. Revenue intelligence from gong.io shows that exceptional sales teams pause 
for 1.5 seconds, while average performers only pause for 0.3 seconds. Silence is 
golden!
Table 18.3 
Accept/Response Examples
Customer Accept Phase Response by SE 
“That seems like a lot of steps to 
accomplish a simple task.”
“Many customers who see this for the first 
time think that.”
“I’m frustrated that we didn’t get the 
project started last year.”
“Wow—I can see how that would be 
frustrating.”
“Will we have to change our current 
processes to accommodate your product?”
“That’s a valid concern. It seems like you 
are worried about . . . ” 
“If you don’t have this ‘x’ feature, then 
the demo is a waste of time.”
“Understood.”

---

## Page 200

178 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 179
mean. This takes practice. If it feels formulaic and artificial, it will hurt the 
relationship and introduce some friction into the Q&A process.
Clarify Everything
At this point, the question has been asked, and you have processed and 
acknowledged it. We now must clarify both the inputs and the outputs. 
Clarifying the inputs means that you have a 100% understanding of the 
question and why it was asked. Clarifying the outputs means that you and 
the rest of the sales team know who will answer the question and how to 
answer it. 
CASE STUDY: A Sad and Painful Mistake from My Past
We were giving the first demonstration of our application system to an IT team. 
Although they were not the final decision makers, we needed their technical 
approval aligned with a win from the business side of the customer to close the 
opportunity. All had gone well, and we were near the end of the meeting. One 
of the IT leaders asked a final question:
 “Will our base users be able to customize their screens and menus?”
 “Of course,” we responded. “They will be able to customize any of those 
areas!”
 That got their attention. “Oh, that’s bad. It makes life difficult for the help 
desk if there is a nonstandard UI. We don’t want that.” The smiles turned into 
frowns. It was a terrible answer.
 We had been so eager to point out what we believed was a competitive ad -
vantage that we did not take the time to understand the context of the ques -
tion. A better response might have been: “Is that an option you’d like the busi-
ness users to have available to them?”
E E E E
In terms of understanding the inputs, it is essential that you have cer -
tainty about the question’s meaning and why it was asked. Later in this 
chapter, we will look at several ways of categorizing a question. It boils 
down to avoiding the three destructive behaviors of discovery: tell, accept, 
and guess (see Chapter 5). In a Chapter 5 case study, we guessed and as -
sumed that what we thought was a positive feature of our product would 
be viewed the same way by the customer. 
Don’t overthink this phase of Q&A, as you don’t always have to clarify. 
The question may be simple, or it may not need an answer. However, if you 
have any doubt in your mind about the context of a question, then you 
should clarify it—at least until you have a better and more trusted relation -
ship with your client. There are numerous ways to clarify the question. 
Three of the most common are repeat, restate, and rephrase.

---

## Page 201

180 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 181
Many sales methodologies recommend that you should always repeat 
a question back to the audience. I’d suggest a more situational approach. 
Certainly, if you work with a large audience (physical or virtual) or micro -
phones are involved, you should repeat the question. With a smaller audi -
ence, it may not be necessary and can be viewed as annoying. Remember 
that the art of this technique is to make Q&A sound just like a conversation. 
You can include a simple question in your answer as a form of repetition.
• “Yes, we do have offices in Stockholm and Singapore.”
• “No, there is no requirement to install agents on every desktop.” 
Restate is synonymous with paraphrase. You can use this technique for 
a long and complex question or a poorly worded and vague question. The 
intent is to seek confirmation about the question and precisely what is being 
requested. You are not changing your customer’s words or their emotions. 
Frequently, you will start your response with phrases such as:
• “So that I understand you . . . ”
• “If I got that right, your key requirement is . . . ”
• “I want to make sure I provide the most accurate answer, so could 
you . . . ”
You are looking for either a yes/no response from the customer, or, in 
some situations, more information to frame your answer more accurately. 
The psychology behind this approach is that it (a) once again demonstrates 
active listening and (b) offers an exchange to the customer in terms of a bet-
ter answer in return for more information. 
Rephrase is different from restate in that you are consciously changing 
your customer’s words and even their intent to soften or redirect their emo-
tions and feelings. It is fair to say that this is not always a strength of SEs, 
particularly those with more engineer than sales in their background.
You should now have a clear understanding of the question and an idea 
of how you should respond. That “you” may be the entire sales team in the 
meeting, rather than you individually. Answering questions is a team sport, 
so the final step before supplying the answer is to ensure that the team is 
clear on who will answer the question.
The safest strategy is that whoever is on stage when the question is asked 
gets the first opportunity to answer the question. So, if the SE is midway 
through a demo, the SE should respond to any questions. If the salesperson 
provides a corporate overview or leads a conversation around licensing, the

---

## Page 202

180 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 181
rep gets to react first. Note that the initial response might be a redirection to 
someone else on the team—as in “ Michelle, that sounds like a pricing question; 
would you like to answer that?”
Hint: Brief The Outsiders
Outsiders are anyone not on the immediate sales team. That includes product manage -
ment, marketing, support, and classic third-party partners. Unless the outsider is far 
more senior/experienced than you, the default instruction should be “Don’t answer any 
questions unless we [the rep/SE combination] pass them across to you.” In this situation, 
I’d recommend that the salesperson take on the role of MC, or master of ceremonies. 
CASE STUDY: Stay in Your Lanes 
The customer has certain expectations about the roles and responsibilities of 
the sales engineer and the salesperson. Therefore, it can be confusing when -
ever you cross over into the other’s lanes—both for the customer and the sales 
partner. 
 Martin was a 20-year SE veteran with the same large hardware company. 
He was now on the first call with Salvatore, his eleventh new salesperson for 
the territory, and was resigned to “breaking in” yet another new sales partner. 
Toward the end of the meeting, the customer had a question about a particular 
product configuration. Martin expertly answered that question and then dis -
cussed the licensing and packing implications around the various configuration 
options. Every time a pricing/option conversation arose, Martin took the lead, 
even though he seemed to be jumping in front of Salvatore. The call concluded 
satisfactorily, and the customer agreed to make a small initial purchase. Martin 
was happy. Salvatore was not and expressed his dissatisfaction with Martin.
 “Martin, if you handle classic salesperson areas such as pricing and packag -
ing, what is my value? Why would the customer even want me on the call or 
want to speak with me next time? It’s wonderful that you understand our pric-
ing so well, and I’m sure I’ll need that expertise in the future—but next time, 
you need to give me the first shot. In return, I promise never to answer ques -
tions about throughput and latency.”
 Martin apologized, the relationship bloomed, and Sal and Martin were the 
number one team in Central Europe 2 years later.
E E E E
Execute the Answer
If you are now thinking that there has been much preprocessing before you 
get to answer the question, then you are absolutely correct. All the prepa -
ration work makes the actual response much easier to position, formulate, 
and deliver. When answering the customer’s question, you have two re -

---

## Page 203

182 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 183
sponsibilities. The first is (obviously) to supply the answer; the second is to 
ensure that the answer lands—which means that it is both satisfactory and 
deemed acceptable by the customer. Looked at in reverse, it is no good giv -
ing a great answer if the customer either does not understand it or they feel 
that you missed their point. 
The number one guideline for answering is to use the fewest words pos-
sible to convey sufficient information to feel that their question was an -
swered. That is a delicate balance. I usually recommend that, if possible, you 
start with a yes or a no before launching into your complete response. This 
response helps set the tone if that first yes or no is net positive to the sales 
effort rather than negative. To put that concept into perspective, what hap-
pens to your attention span when you ask a question, and the answer gets 
beyond 30–40 seconds?
There are several techniques to help a more complex response. The first 
is to draw a picture (see Chapter 15, “Whiteboarding and Visual Selling”). 
This tactic is the proverbial a picture is worth a thousand words 5 and works 
wonderfully well in both a physical and a virtual setting. The customer may 
well end up with a visual souvenir of your response, so it is far more likely 
to be internalized and memorized. The second is to tell a brief customer story 
(see Chapter 16, “Storytelling”) about how a customer in a similar situation 
used your solution or solved their problem. 
This stage may also be when you realize that you do not know the an -
swer to the question. In most cases, it is fine to state that you don’t know 
the answer and that you will find out and respond in the next 24 hours or 
sooner. But if you have to say “I don’t know” more than a couple of times 
in a sales call, then someone—either you or the customer—is in the wrong 
room. An exception in these situations is if you are the subject matter expert 
in the customer meeting. Saying “I don’t know,” especially in some cultures, 
can lose you and the salesperson a lot of credibility.
Once you complete your answer, you should assess any nonverbal cues 
(nodding heads and the like) and confirm that the answer was acceptable. 
Of course, if the customer verbally expresses a sentiment like “That sounds 
great,” your job is done. Typical confirmation phrases are:
• Did that answer your question?
• Does that address your concern?
• Do you see how that would work in your environment?
5. It actually takes the average SE about 6 minutes to get through a 1,000-word 
answer—so a picture is certainly the way to go in that extreme situation!

---

## Page 204

182 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 183
• Is there anything else I can add?
I’d usually recommend against using “Does that make sense?” or “Is 
that clear?” as they can both imply some judgment about the mindset of 
the customer and the SE.
Once you answer the question, then it is time to move on. There is noth-
ing to be gained by piling on and offering an additional explanation or more 
facts—whether they come from you or someone else on the team. Lawyers 
like to say “Asked and answered,” to shut down a line of questioning.
CASE STUDY: What She Really Meant . . . 
Once a question has been answered, then it is time to move on. Sara, a solution 
architect for a large cloud provider, tells a story of what can happen if you do 
not move on: “We were giving a technical architecture presentation to the cli -
ent’s evaluation team. They were quite a knowledgeable group, so we arranged 
for Karl, our principal subject matter expert, to be on the call. I had never 
worked with Karl before and knew he had a reputation for being direct and 
to the point. Part of the way through our presentation, I was asked a question 
and responded with what I thought was a perfectly acceptable answer. The cus-
tomer liked the response and said so. However, Karl jumped in and said, ‘What 
Sara meant to say was . . .‘ and proceeded to tell the customer some inside 
information that contradicted what I said and caused considerable confusion 
with the customer. On at least two other occasions, Karl jumped in and talked 
over me or the salesperson (we’re both women) and killed the trust we had 
developed with the customer. I left the call feeling very aggrieved that another 
SE had shown this behavior, and the issues eventually delayed the deal. Karl 
did not understand that his role was to support us, not contradict and correct 
us in front of the customer.”
E E E E
We’ve now covered the foundational strategy of LACE. Now, we must 
fine tune our responses based on the type and category of question. That 
requires more experience and situational fluency.
Categorizing the Questions
Categorizing the question runs in parallel with the listen and accept phases 
of LACE. You can categorize the question in two ways. The first is by the 
type of question, and the second is by who is asking the question.

---

## Page 205

184 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 185
Categorizing by question type can be done with a simple traffic light 6 
concept, as shown in Table 18.4.
Red lights are those questions that may highlight issues such as a miss -
ing capability, a competitive disadvantage, or a mismatch in business terms. 
Unless you deal with a red light, it will haunt you for the rest of the opportu-
nity and will likely act as a brake on anything being closed. A red light ques-
tion may be deliberately asked, or it may arise by accident—harmful intent 
is not always behind it. Your job is the negate the red and to turn it yellow 
or even green. That may not happen in one meeting, so to stretch the color 
analogy, sometimes the red turns pink before it becomes yellow. 
Green lights are those questions that are positive and highlight your 
capabilities, breadth of portfolio, number of references, and the like. There-
fore, it is essential to treat a green question just as you would any other and 
give it the time and respect it deserves. 
If a question is not red or green, then by default, it is yellow. Most ques-
tions are yellow until you get to know your customer and develop a better 
relationship. Yellow7 means “proceed with caution.” The purpose of coloring 
up a question is to help you assess your response’s relative advantages and 
disadvantages and to ensure that you don’t get into trouble by guessing or 
assuming intent. It pays to be cautious.
Categorizing the people who ask the question can further guide your 
response. Remember that these are guidelines rather than rigid rules. Al -
though you may often feel that you are part psychologist and are excellent 
at reading people or the room, don’t base your entire response on an as -
sumed category. 
We can broadly divide 99% of questions into six categories, as shown 
in Table 18.5.
6. J.P. Knight, a railway signaling engineer, invented the first traffic signal. It was 
installed outside the British Houses of Parliament in 1868. (https://en.wikipedia.
org/wiki/J._P._Knight.)
7. Yellow = amber in some parts of the world.
Table 18.4 
Traffic Light Question Types
Color Definition Typical Examples
Red Negative. Known as a blocker or 
a showstopper.
Can be asked by the coach of a 
competitor, a DNI supporter, or a 
techie under threat.
Yellow Neutral or unknown. Something 
doesn’t quite feel right about it. 
Can be asked by anyone. Treat with 
care and respect. Don’t assume.
Green Positive. It helps the sale move 
forward.
Often asked by your coach or sponsor.

---

## Page 206

184 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 185
The Standard Question
One stunning fact I’ve learned over the years is that 81.9% of questions 
are asked . . . simply because someone wants an answer to a question that 
they have! There is nothing more profound or more complicated occur -
ring. That is also one of the other reasons I dislike the objection handling 
label. Contrary to first impressions, these are the best types of questions to 
have raised. It is a legitimate chance to remove a doubt or concern from the 
customer’s thought process. You are removing the unknown and educating 
the customer. The question may be difficult; it may be easy; it could be any 
color. There is, however, no intent behind it except to get an answer.  
The Coach Question
This is a question, hopefully green, that your internal coach or sponsor 
poses. You can prime the pump by suggesting several pertinent questions to 
the coach before the meeting. A variant of this is common in public webi -
nars where the host may ask canned questions of their guests to put a posi-
tive spin on things. This is the same strategy. When responding to a coach 
question, you must treat it like any other question in that you pause, give 
it some thought, and do not seem too eager to answer. Once you answer, 
take a moment to let the response sit with the audience to understand the 
significance of the answer. 
The Competitive Question
The competitor’s coach asks these questions, usually red or yellow. They 
are the exact reverse of the coach question in that they have been placed 
to make you look bad. However, it is usually worth checking, especially if 
physically in the room, whether it was read from a document or email. That 
means there are probably more to come. The usual advice of treating it like 
any other question applies, and you need to remain calm and composed 
even if you feel under pressure and attacked. Supply just enough informa-
Table 18.5 
Broad Categories of Questions
Type Description
Standard A regular, typical question or statement.
Coach A leading and positive question from your internal sponsor or 
coach.
Competitive A leading and negative question from your competitor’s coach 
or sponsor.
Consultant A question asked by an outside person paid to look smart and 
assist the decision-making process.
Seymour A question from an internal techie who wants to see more of 
your demonstration, white papers, specifications, and manuals.
Hostile A question from an individual who just wants to be difficult.

---

## Page 207

186 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 187
tion to answer the question (and no more). Seek confirmation about the 
answer, and if that is not provided, then offer to post the question to the 
parking lot/action item list for follow up.
The Consultants Question
Consultants are in the room to look good in front of their clients and to give 
impartial advice to that same client. They may have some expertise either 
in your product portfolio or at least the marketing segment you occupy. Re-
member that the consultants have a job to do, just like you, and are continu-
ally selling their services in front of the client. If you know that a consulting 
organization is involved in the evaluation or decision stages, make every 
effort to prebrief the consultants and turn them into either coaches or neu -
trals. By briefing the consultants and giving them competitive sound bites, 
you may get them reinforced in the meeting and receive green questions.
Unless the consultant is being wildly inaccurate and misstating capabili-
ties, it is never worth directly confronting them. Instead, it is better to speak 
about what your clients are doing with your products. That information is 
difficult for any consultant to contradict. 
The Seymour Question
A Seymour is a technical individual who always wants to see more of your 
slides, demonstrations, technical specifications, and documentation. Typi -
cally, they ask technical and elaborate complex questions about topics that 
are not interesting for most other people in the room. Those questions are 
usually driven by intellectual curiosity or the desire to be the smartest per -
son in the room. The best strategy with a Seymour is to promise them as 
much information as possible after you finish the call—and then follow up. 
Seymour questions can be any color, so ignore them at your peril.
The Hostile Question
Rarely will there be an individual in the meeting who is confrontational and 
asking very pointed and outwardly hostile questions. This situation does 
not happen as often as sales folklore would suggest. Sometimes that person 
doesn’t want to be in the room and takes it out on you; other times, they 
want to play stump the chump and just bury you with red questions. Even 
though it may feel like a personal attack (and it may be), the best approach 
is a combination of:
• Compliment the questioner on their knowledge;
• Engage the salesperson as a buffer;

---

## Page 208

186 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Answering Questions 187
• Seek help from other customers in the meeting;
• Offer a separate session to cover their specific questions.
Hint: I Want More Questions
If you feel you are not getting sufficient interaction with your clients, try this technique 
directly from the education 8 profession. Do not finish with “Any questions?” That is a 
closed-ended question and often results in a no or just plain silence. Instead, say, “What 
questions might you have for me?” That simple change yields a 40% increase in ques -
tions. Why? Because it is both an open-ended question and it implies that questions are 
expected and encouraged. If it works with teenagers, it works with anyone!
Summary
Answering questions is a complex topic, and you need to be aware of many 
moving parts and nuances. Even though you now know that more than 
80% of questions happen just because someone wants an answer, you still 
need to apply your attention to these questions before seeking out the other 
20%. Listen and observe. You can learn much by how a question is asked 
and by what is not asked. 
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Practice the LACE framework and experiment with it in low-risk situations.
 2. Build a top 10 or 20 list of common questions. Then, create a LACE response for each.
 3. Use the pause and thoughtful silence. Patience is key .
 4. Smile and slow down!
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Y ellow lights are an opportunity to learn more about a client. Practice these responses.
 2. Mirroring (repeating/rephrasing the last words spoken) is an excellent skill in discovery 
    and negotiations. Extend the skill.
 3. Disarm and befriend the Seymours.
 4. Keep smiling!
8. Thank you to Mrs. Allison Care.

rusted advisor. That is fourteen letters, five 
syllables, and two words that encompass 
thousands of personal definitions. It is a classic “I 
know it when I see it” vague and fuzzy phrase. 
Yet, becoming the trusted advisor to your cus -
tomers, partners, peers, and salespeople should 
be the desired end goal of every SE and salesper-

This chapter looks at the overall benefits of 
becoming a TA, takes the first pass at a defini -

way to measure and then use trust in the buying 
cycle. The next chapter (“The Executive Connec-

We are engineers. We like being able to measure 
and quantify stuff. However, trust is such a com-

the overall impact on the sales cycle. One place to start is by looking at mea-

at an industry conference, one of our clients decided to move ahead and 
implement a full-on TA program within sales, sales engineering, technical 
support, and services. The intent was to train all areas of the company that 
regularly touched the end customer. The COO insisted on running the pro -
gram with both a subject and a control group to measure the differences and 
set a time span of 12 months. It felt like an actual scientific experiment. A 
successful one! They reported the following results, and as you read through 
the list in Table 19.1, take a minute to imagine the impact on your organiza-

the engine of sales, data like this should convince even the most skeptical 
among us that there is value in the approach. Many other customers who 
have implemented this program solely within an SE team have reported 
four- and five-digit ROIs. That ROI comes from the fact that so many more 
actions and processes can happen in parallel rather than sequentially—be -
cause everyone trusts everyone else to do their part—and that speeds up the 
buying process.

Defining the Trusted Advisor
The essential part of the label is that TA is not a label or title you can give 
to yourself or put in your email signature. Only the customer can award 
that title.1 That is an important point, as the core of a TA means looking at 
almost everything from your customer’s viewpoint. 
We can start by breaking the words apart. First, we have the trust. What 
makes a customer trust you? It is much more than your technical knowl -
edge and capabilities, as those are the essential table stakes those customers 
expect of any SE. For the SE, it is a combination of honoring your commit-
ments, speaking the truth, and acting in the best long-term interests of both 
the customer and your employer—even if that may conflict with the short-
term best interests of your own company. 
Now we move on to advisor. We have all been in situations where we 
have given people personal or professional advice, and they have ignored 
us. Your customers must be willing to listen to you before you can even 
provide advice. What makes them ready to listen to you? At least some part 
of trust. Some personal examples:
1. Margaret, the copy manager at Staples, my local office supplies 
store. Over the past years, she has saved me a small fortune on 
printer supplies and binders by offering coupons, multipacks, and 
special offers. Less money for Staples, more for me. Except I just 
bought a new laptop and printer at Staples instead of Best Buy. 
Trust!
2. Tom and Nick, my business partners in Asia. We have conducted 
millions of dollars of business for more than 10 years based on a 
handshake and a three-page written agreement. Trust!
3. Chris, the sales manager at a local Audi car dealership. (I know 
it is a cliché, but read on as names have been changed to pro -
tect the guilty.) We leased a new car, agreed on terms and a price, 
and then needed to wait a few days for delivery. When we ar -
rived to complete the paperwork, the monthly payment rate had 
increased dramatically. In addition, a bunch of warranty, service, 
and maintenance contracts were included without our knowledge 
and presented as “If you want the car, then take these options.” We 
refused, found another car, and the dealership has since lost out on 
multiple referrals from us because of that experience—no trust.
1. Yet a cursory search of LinkedIn shows more than 2 million people with some 
form of TA in their job title or description.

---

## Page 213

192 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer 193
Try answering these three questions to get a better understanding of the 
relationship between trust and advice:
• Question 1: What can you do to prove to your customer that you 
act in their best interest? (It does not have to involve saving money.)
• Question 2: Think about a recent situation when you provided (good) 
advice—and it was ignored. Why did that happen? (The other person 
was a complete idiot is not the answer you should be looking for!)
• Question 3: Think about a recent situation when you provided (good) 
advice—and it was accepted. Why did that happen? 
The best working definition we have found (without using either trust 
or advice) is “A knowledgeable person who listens to me without judgment, 
honestly shares their thoughts, and acts in my best interest.”
Hint: Remember Who Ultimately Signs Your Paycheck
Although you should always be acting in your customer’s best interests, that doesn’t mean 
that you should constantly take an adversarial position against your company on behalf 
of the customer. If you start taking actions that may be ethically, morally, or financially 
detrimental to your customer, you may want to reevaluate your position. Ultimately your 
paycheck comes from your employer, and it is rarely in the interest of the SE, or the cus -
tomer, for that type of relationship to develop. 
An Introduction to the Trust Equation
 + + =   
C R IT PS  
A good starting point is a definition used by Charles Green in The Trusted 
Advisor.2 He defined trust as measured by credibility, reliability, intimacy, and 
self-orientation. The initial work was mainly directed toward the services and 
consulting industry. It is worth defining these as they relate to an SE. Mathe-
matically, you want high scores in credibility, reliability, and intimacy, and a 
low score in self-orientation! With permission, I adapted his equation, added 
positivity, and got an exciting mnemonic—CRISP. My customers speak of be -
2. Maister, D. H., R. M. Galford, and C. H. Green, The Trusted Advisor, Twentieth Edi-
tion, New York: Free Press, 2021.

---

## Page 214

192 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer 193
ing “CRISPy.” You can find even more practical detail about this topic in The 
Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer.3
Credibility (C)
The great thing about being an SE is that you automatically bring some 
credibility to sales situations. When you and the salesperson are together, 
then you are the one who is likely to be considered as more credible because 
you are not “that sales guy.” You can further break this down by evaluating 
if you are believable when you speak/act, and are you ultimately proven 
“right”? One of the most significant credibility traps is the feeling that you 
must prove your value every minute you are in a meeting. Sometimes the 
best credibility comes from listening very hard and then speaking.
Reliability (R)
Years ago, I went to a management class in England and was taught about 
do as you say  (DAYS). That acronym has stayed with me ever since. If you 
are serious about becoming a TA, it is not too hard for you to live up to your 
commitments and honor your word. What proves difficult is when other 
people in your organization affect the reliability measure. You can promise 
an answer in 24 hours, yet you are stranded if product management does 
not get back to you. 
Intimacy (I)
Intimacy measures how well you know and understand the customer, both 
personally and professionally. It does not necessarily mean that you know 
the name of their spouse, children, and favorite coffee drink (though that 
may help), but that you understand their personal wins and feelings about 
any situation/sale. It is all about putting yourself in the customer’s shoes 
and listening for topics of personal significance. The intimacy comes from 
understanding why they wake up in the morning and understanding their 
motivations. A high “I” means that you can predict, or at least anticipate, 
someone else’s reactions.  
Self-Orientation (S)
S is in the denominator of the equation. Therefore, a low score is good, and 
a high score is terrible. This factor measures how much you are thinking 
about yourself and your company versus the customer’s wins. When you 
find yourself thinking about selling instead of solving, of quota instead of 
discovery questions, or of transactions instead of long-term strategy—then 
3. Care, J., The Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer,  self-published, 2020. Available on 
Amazon.

---

## Page 215

194 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer 195
that is a high (and therefore a bad) S. This is the most problematic charac -
teristic for an SE to adopt in a sales-driven culture given the pressures they 
will be under to help sales in hitting their numbers every month or quar -
ter. Another customer defined a high S as putting a commission before the 
customer. 
Positivity (P)
We all know those who spend their time looking for problems and issues 
instead of finding a healthy balance, including opportunities and innova -
tion. You can be a TA with a low P score—many finance, legal, and medical 
professionals fit into that category. So, unfortunately, do many SEs—mainly 
when speaking with their sales counterparts. When you point out the nega-
tives in an opportunity, highlight the corresponding positives as well. The 
same applies to working with your clients.
The Automatic Trust Advantage of the Sales Engineer 
One of the positive aspects of the role of the sales engineer is that you rarely 
start from the lowest possible score in terms of credibility or reliability. For 
example, in the absence of any other factors, when a salesperson and a sales 
engineer walk into a customer conference room together, the SE is auto -
matically trusted to a far greater degree than the salesperson. That is because 
the salesperson is expected to be selling explicitly, whereas your default be-
havior is that you are there to help the customer.4
As noted elsewhere, the customer, particularly at lower levels of the 
organization, will share information with you that they would never dream 
of telling a salesperson. It is your job to collect this information and share 
it with the sales team without violating the customer’s trust. Above all, re -
member that your technical credibility must remain pure and unquestioned. 
Once the customer catches you misleading them, either directly or indirect-
ly, by “forgetting” to tell them something, it is almost impossible to regain 
their trust. It is better to say, “I don’t know,” or “That is obviously something 
vital to you, and I’d rather go research it so I can give you a 100% accurate 
answer.”
4. Some SEs explicitly use alternative customer-facing titles that omit the sales part 
of their job, such as “consulting engineer” or “security engineer.” My belief is that 
most customers do understand that you are part of a sales organization, no matter 
what your job title may be.

---

## Page 216

194 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer 195
Creating Y our Own Trust Scale
To calculate your trust score (T-score) with an individual customer, you 
need an agreed-upon scale. Note that it’s much easier to perform this on 
an individual basis than a corporate one. This is because a company cannot 
trust you; an individual can. 
For each characteristic, you should develop a scoring system that con -
forms to these rules, using the guidelines shown in Table 19.2. Note that S 
and P have different scales.
Then, for each characteristic, complete Table 19.3. You need to look 
at two separate components, which are the two primary columns, to the 
right. The first is based on how you behave when you act as a 1 through 5 
for that characteristic. Seek out as many behaviors and specific examples as 
you can. The second column shows how the customer behaves when you 
are a 1 through 5 for that characteristic. For instance, if reliability = 1, you 
may miss, or be late to most meetings, fail to follow up, and are rarely fully 
prepared (in short, you will be looking for another job soon). The customer 
may react by complaining, escalating, or asking that you be removed from 
the account. (You can download sample sheets from the Trusted Advisor 
section of the MTS website.)
Hint: Some Scoring Suggestions
1. Most organizations will typically set a 3 as expected and professional behavior. Some 
will set the bar higher or lower, depending upon their culture, so it is essential to get 
multiple inputs when creating a corporate T-chart.
2. P has an unusual scale of 0.5 to 1.33 and acts as a multiplying factor. Set P = 
1.00 for most relationships and only adjust up or down based on specific circum -
stances. Otherwise, it is too easy to boost a score by claiming an exceptionally positive 
relationship.
Calculating the T Score
Choose an individual in one of your accounts. Examine their behaviors and 
select a score for each characteristic. There are two guiding principles:
Table 19.2 
Guidelines for the Trust Score Rubric
Characteristic

Low (Bad) 
Score

1. Use integers for C, R, I, and S. If in doubt, round up or down to the 
“worst” number. (So a 3½ in I becomes a 3, and a 3½ in S becomes 
a 4.)
2. Use the customer behavior score rather than your score if there is a 
difference between the two. (If you believe you act as if C = 4, but 
the customer behaves as if C = 3, then C = 3.)
 Then fill in the blanks and calculate a score.

By going through the math, you will discover that the lowest score is 
0.3, and the highest possible score is 20. Most initial scores will fall in the 2 
to 4 range—as long as you are realistic about the relationship. If you choose 
to set P = 1.00, then the range is limited at 0.6 to 15. The T-score is a non -
linear approximation. There is little difference between a 2 and a 2.5, but a 
world of difference between a 5 and a 7. The key to improving a T-score is 
examining each characteristic and building a plan (usually a set of tasks or 
events) to increase each of the five distinct factors in the equation.

Hint: Where to Focus
SEs have strong credibility and reliability, weak intimacy, and average self-orientation 
and positivity scores. For the mathematically inclined, the fastest short-term way to im -
prove is obviously to decrease your S score.
Using Trust in the Buying Process
Most opportunity and account planning processes will at some point in -
clude a list of the customer personnel, a sales definition of their roles such 
as approver, recommender, influencer, or coach, and a multitude of other 
attributes. Then, add two additional columns—one that shows your T-score 
with that individual and another that shows the salesperson’s T-score (if 
they decide to embrace the system). You can gain some fascinating insight 
from this:
1. Seeing if there are trust gaps in the customer organization;
2. Identifying areas of overlap (both the SE and the rep don’t need to 
have high T-scores with every customer);
3. Selecting who can approach the customer for certain activities.
Once you have implemented the T-scores for a few months, you will 
identify specific barriers and hurdles in your client base. Collating data (see 
Table 19.4) from 20-plus technology sales organizations reveals the follow-
ing data points.
CASE STUDY: Being CRISPy
Here is a practical T-score application and testimonial from Mika, a 20-year 
SE veteran from a large hardware company: “Our business was stagnant. We 
were selling the same products and services to the same companies in the same 
way—and had been doing that for years. It was getting boring and certainly 
lacked any real challenges. As we were planning for yet another fiscal year, I 
sat with my salesperson, and we decided to get CRISPy. We added an additional 
preparation layer and mapped out our customer interactions based on explic -
itly improving all of the trust scores. We included CRISPy-ness as part of our 
debrief, too. What a new and refreshing way to look at relationships. The job 
became fun again! We beat every single monthly and quarterly target and were 
into commission accelerators by the start of Q4. After 20 years I thought I knew 
everything, and I’m so glad that I was proved wrong. Everyone on my team 
now talks about being CRISPy.”
E E E E

Some SE Specifics
Just as we must link features to business benefits, we must also link trusted 
advisor theory and scores to more practical applications. Review all of your 
direct and indirect customer-facing activities (such as written communica -
tions, discovery, demos, and presentations). For each of those, consider how 
you can make them extra CRISPy. Be as specific and prescriptive as you can. 
It is a different and valuable mindset. For more on that process, you can re-
fer to The Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer companion book. 
As noted earlier, SEs pride themselves on their credibility and reliability. 
Some 58% of SEs rate credibility and reliability as their top two attributes 
(in either order). I classify those two attributes as the hard pair. They are 
easier to influence and measure. There is a lot to be said for building on your 
strengths, but when it comes to trust, you also need to work on the two soft 
attributes of intimacy and self-orientation, coupled with positivity. To see 
how you compare to almost 10,000 other SEs, refer to Table 19.5.
Summary 
This chapter is an overview of setting up a trusted advisor sales engineer 
program and the metrics to measure, monitor, and report on it. We’ve dis -
covered that just the act of defining what a trusted advisor means to your 
company (and your customers) and then attempting to measure that trust 
can make a significant difference to your sales efforts. The number one bar-
rier to successfully implementing such a program is not the SEs and not the 
customers, but the quota-carrying salespeople. They have a fear that this 
will somehow slow down some deals. That may well happen the first quar -
ter you run the program, yet it is a classic case of minor short-term pain in 
return for some significant long-term gains.

Range/T-Score Standard Results and Outcomes
Less than 2 Poor—minimal trust and no relationship.
2–4 Formal business relationship. Friendly but not deep.
4–5 Customers will start to sell and promote you internally (at 
low risk).
5–7 Good solid relationship. Customers will take positive action 
and do things for you that have some risk.
7–10 Trusted advisor I. Your target!
Greater than 10 Trusted advisor II. Professionally, you usually have two or 
three of these at most.

Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Rate yourself on the four primary factors from strongest to weakest.
 2. Note down specific examples of being CRISPy in the various customer -facing tasks and 
    activities you perform.
 3. Write down your definition(s) of a trusted advisor SE.
 4. Why do you trust others in your personal and professional life?
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Create a quantifiable trust scale for the five factors.
 2. Integrate the T -score into your account planning.
 3. Expand the use of the T -score to the salespeople and partners. (How much do they trust 
    you, and vice versa?)
 4. For managers—embrace the use of T -scores and be CRISPy . Regularly inspect and 
    provide feedback on T -score assessments.

Chapter Goals The Executive Connection
An executive is someone who has no time to listen to 
you but has all day to talk with you.
      —Taylor Rhodes, Technology CEO
M
eeting with higher-level executives can 
make many of the more technical SEs very 
nervous. Yet once you realize that a different set 
of priorities drives executives, these meetings be-
come easier to manage. This chapter covers the 
fundamentals of dealing with executives, both 
inside and outside of your company, and their 
decision-making processes. I’ll also interweave 
practical aspects of the trust equation from the 
previous chapter.
An Executive Definition
I use the term executive loosely in this chapter. Do 
not exclusively focus on the highest-level execu-
tives within your customer organization—those 
labeled as CxOs (chief information officer, chief 
financial officer, chief technology officer, and so 
on) or the xVPs (executive or senior vice presi -
dent)—as that is an unrealistic expectation. Over 
the past few years, we have randomly polled our 
workshop attendees about the actual percentage 
of true high-level executive meetings they attend 
every quarter. Typically, in the enterprise and 
Understand what 
executives want and expect 
from you.

plan the executive meeting.

and then follow up after a 
meeting.

## Page 223

202 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Executive Connection 203
public sector environments, the answer is less than 2% and less than 8% 
within SMB. For the purpose of this chapter, an executive is anyone from a 
technical or a line of business division of your target customer organization 
who has significant authority over personnel, budget, or strategy. In a large 
Fortune 500–type organization, that may be one or two levels down from 
the CIO (referred to as CIO-1 or CIO-2).
What Do They Want from Y ou?
We surveyed to ask that exact question in partnership with an MTS cus -
tomer and one of their consulting partners.1 We polled 2,179 mid- to senior-
level executives from large companies in 22 countries, broadly represent -
ing every major technology market globally, except China. The question we 
posed was “What are the major skills you expect from a vendor’s technical 
presales team?” The answers are in Table 20.1.
A deeper look at the responses shows that the top four responses were 
uniform, in that over 85% of the participants gave those answers. Deep 
technical knowledge came in fifth place with about a 40% score, and then 
there was a long tail of other expectations. This ranking means that the skill 
that most SEs pride themselves on—having technical knowledge—is viewed 
as table stakes by many executives. The expectation is that the more consul-
tative skills listed as numbers one through four are an absolute requirement. 
This is quite a radical shift from the days of the dot-com boom or before 
when deep technical knowledge was undoubtedly front and center at num-
ber one. It is a sign of how much the role has changed over the past 20 years, 
yet the role of the salesperson has barely changed at all. 
1. As a historical note, EMC (now Dell-EMC) originally commissioned McKinsey 
to run a smaller survey back in 2011 focused on that same question. The data 
seemed so valuable that we recycled the concept a decade later.
Table 20.1 
The Major Skills Executives Expect from You
Q: “What are the major skills you expect from a vendor’s technical 
presales team?”
Rank Professional Skill
1 Someone who understands my business.
2 Someone I can trust to do the right thing for me and my company.
3 Someone who can clearly and effectively communicate with me.
4 Someone who can design innovative solutions with my team.
5 Someone who can provide deep technical knowledge.

---

## Page 224

202 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Executive Connection 203
Harsh reality does not always align with those hopes and wishes. Al -
though a little stereotypical, the executive thinks of a technician as some -
one likely to get into far more detail than they want to hear (as that is what 
they see internally—imagine a network engineer meeting with the CIO!). 
The SE is also not expected to be as polished as the salesperson in dress2 or 
presentation skills and to be more introverted than extraverted. 
An executive meeting occurs either because your own sales team, or 
someone from the customer, believes that you have valuable informa -
tion or insights that should be provided to that executive. The executive 
hopes that you are prepared, well briefed, and can impart your wisdom in 
a concise, professional manner. Your mission is to meet and then beat those 
expectations.
Hint: Executives Are People, Too!
Although the prospect of meeting with a senior leader may seem daunting, remember that 
they are as human as you are. Without resorting to clichés about knowing that “they put 
their socks on one at a time just like you,” focus on the person and not the position. Show 
the appropriate and culturally correct amount of respect and humility, but remember that 
you are in the room for a reason. 
Most executives are profit and investment oriented, and money is the 
universal currency of their business drivers. Part of their job is to invest 
their corporate assets in a manner they believe will generate a solid return. 
If a salesperson called on you at home claiming to be able to reduce your 
monthly utility expenses by 35%, just as they did for your neighbor, you 
would want all the financial details.
Plan the Meeting
Start with the strategy that the magic number for executives is one plus 
three. That strategy means that ideally, you should focus on one main mes-
sage in any executive interaction. Then, you can reinforce that message 
with up to three key points. (Review the RM+3KP section in Chapter 10.) 
The executive rule of one applies to all forms of interaction—emails, texts, the 
spoken word, and especially slides and demos. Any slide with more than 
three bullet points on it is a candidate for immediate rejection. Executives 
are paid to make risk-appropriate choices by distilling sizable amounts of 
data into meaningful and insightful decisions. By summarizing key points 
2. Don Smith, former global VP of sales engineering at Clarify used to have a ba -
sic set of SE fundamental rules, one of which was: “Never outdress your sales 
partner.”

---

## Page 225

204 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Executive Connection 205
and eliminating distractions and noise, you are more likely to get their full 
attention.
Setting the Stage
Your internal coach or a third party will often have sponsored the meet -
ing. Presumably, the meeting would not have been calendared if the execu-
tive had nothing to gain from it, but some advance press never hurts. Ask 
your sponsor to talk you up without setting unreasonably high expectations. 
Looking at the trust equation, they are setting you up for a strong start on 
the credibility and positivity factors. If this is a second or third meeting with 
the same executive, something good must have happened beforehand. As 
a former IT executive, most people could get into my office once, but a re -
peat visit meant that I had learned something or you had helped me make 
a decision. 
Research the people with whom you will be meeting. Look for any pub-
lished papers, presentations, or speeches and then read them thoroughly. 
In addition to Googling the executive, review any professional sites such 
as LinkedIn and Xing. Depending upon their generational profile, you may 
also learn something from personal or corporate blogs and apps like Twitter. 
Reviewing more personal apps like Facebook and Instagram seems a little 
creepy and like stalking, so be careful of how you proceed in those areas. 
Good solid research, both electronically and physically (don’t forget to ask 
“so tell me a little more about him/her” to those who know them), can give 
you a hook. A hook is a commonality or a shared interest that you may use 
to open the relationship or interject as part of a story. That also helps drive 
the intimacy factor of the trust equation. 
CASE STUDY: The Power of Research
Juan and Robin’s sales/SE team had managed to set up an introductory call 
with the new chief security officer at a major athletics equipment supplier. As 
part of their call preparation, Robin researched the background of the new CSO. 
She discovered that this was the new CSO’s first commercial position. All of her 
previous experience had been as a managing partner of the security division of a 
consulting company. Further online research showed that she was well known 
within the industry for her expertise in the forensic analysis of data breaches. 
It just so happened that Juan and Robin’s cybersecurity portfolio included a 
little-known product that could help with that kind of analysis. Robin quickly 
familiarized herself with the product, and Juan uncovered a reference customer. 
 Twenty minutes into the initial call, all was going well, and the CSO asked, 
“I don’t suppose you have anything that deals with data breaches?” Robin pro-
vided her with a quick high-level overview of the product and its capabilities 
which, in her own words, made her “look like a rockstar and created instant 
credibility.” Juan name dropped the reference customer and what had been a 
good call turned into an amazing sales call. The relationship was now firmly

---

## Page 226

204 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Executive Connection 205
established, and over the next few years, a robust strategic partnership was cre-
ated between the two companies—all based on some simple research.
E E E E
Be sensitive to language, culture, and geographic location. For example, 
a financial services executive in New York City is likely to be short, sharp, 
and to the point with a minimal amount of time spent on pleasantries and 
introductions (so credibility and reliability outweigh the intimacy). By con-
trast, in Asian cultures, the first 20 or 30 minutes may be simple introduc -
tions, exchanging business cards, and a series of polite compliments. A U.S. 
executive may be all about “bottom line up front” (BLUF), while a German 
executive still wants the same key points but won’t allow you to cut cor -
ners to get there. You will also learn that executive attention spans can vary 
widely by position. As a broad generalization, CFOs, operations, and securi-
ty executives can remain on-point and focused for longer than SVPs of sales 
or marketing. Observe the habits of executives within your own company.
Not only is it essential to have your primary discussion points well pre-
pared, but the executive needs to understand why you are meeting. Just 
like any other meeting, confirm the expected length and subject matter to 
be discussed. You can certainly start the business part of the meeting with 
a statement such as “By the end of our 15 minutes together, I thought we 
could . . . ” You may also wish to leave that task to the salesperson/manager 
or your manager if present. 
Hint: Time Is Money!
The most valuable resource an executive has is their own time. Just because you have 
30 minutes on the calendar does not mean you will get the full 30 minutes. In the best 
possible case, assume you have five minutes for introductions and five minutes for the 
wrap-up, leaving 20 minutes of content. In the worst-case, your meeting time will be cut 
in half. Plan for this and do not leave the most critical item to the end of the agenda. You 
may not get there!
Follow the Money
Many of the executives you meet will have some form of budgetary or 
overall profit and loss (P&L) responsibility. Although not taught as part of a 
traditional SE professional skills curriculum, I’d strongly recommend some 
basic financial acumen training so you can read a balance sheet and an in -
come statement, especially if you are a major accounts SE dealing with the 
same small set of companies every year. There is no expectation that you 
generate some complex total cost of ownership (TCO) spreadsheet. Still, it 
helps to be financially literate when dealing with people who care about

---

## Page 227

206 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Executive Connection 207
those financials. You should know if the company is making a profit, its 
margins are increasing or decreasing, and if any vital financial ratios are 
markedly different from the norm. 
In the United States, financial data for public companies is readily avail-
able from an annual report or 10-K and 10-Q filings from the SEC EDGAR3 
database. Similar sites exist for all major international stock markets. Anoth-
er good step is to read the annual report and the last few earnings releases 
or to read the transcript of an analyst’s meeting. Earnings announcements 
often highlight the key strategic initiatives for a company. So, if your solu -
tion aligns with those initiatives, you have a clear advantage. 
The second aspect of following the money is learning how the executive 
is compensated for performance. Compensation drives behavior, so under -
standing the performance measurements, whether they be revenue, gross 
profit, or stock price can assist in linking the impact of your portfolio to their 
success. Unless you are in a trusted advisor position or at least have a high 
intimacy, this might be a better topic for one of your executives to raise dur-
ing a later meeting. It is unlikely that a CxO will share the details of their 
performance metrics with a lowly SE during a first meeting. 
Setting the Goals of the Meeting
Despite having our one main message and all the preparation in the world, 
the meeting does need to have a purpose and at least one overall goal. Write 
down the goals and share them for agreement with your sales partner. I use 
the mnemonic MARS-BARS (Table 20.2) to help me remember.
The minimum acceptable result of sales call (MARS) means that if this 
goal is achieved, the account team will consider the call a success. A default 
MARS may be to get the next meeting, and nothing terrible happens. The 
best achievable result of sales call (BARS) is like a stretch goal. If this hap -
pens, you may do backflips in the parking lot, and the salesperson owes you 
lunch. It is, forgive the pun, a sweet acronym to remember.
Goals may revolve around gaining agreement or approval for a future 
course of action. Others involve simply getting the required information to 
refine your solution and proposal. Still others depend on removing a road -
block or objections from the boardroom or other executives. 
3. See https://www.sec.gov/edgar.shtml. You can also see insider buying and selling 
of stock.

Using MARS-BARS to Set Goals
MARS Minimum acceptable result of sales call
BARS Best achievable result of sales call

Hint: Be Realistic!
If you follow the SMART methodology of goal setting, then R is both relevant and realistic. 
A salesperson telling you that their BARS is to leave the meeting with a purchase order 
is not realistic. In 30-plus years of experience, I have only twice left an exec call with a 
physical or digitally signed PO. It just doesn’t happen. You may, however, gain an agree-
ment to approve a PO.
Y ou Are the Expert
Be confident in your approach and delivery. You are the subject matter ex-
pert that the sales team (or customer) has decided is the right person for the 
meeting. This meeting is your opportunity to demonstrate that high cred -
ibility and raise it even further. Run a thought experiment. Who are you 
more likely to listen to and believe, a nervous SE who can barely put two 
coherent sentences together, or a steady, quietly confident SE who believes 
in what they are saying? 
If you have the time and opportunity, try a dress rehearsal and role 
play the presentation/demo/discussion with your manager or director be -
forehand to practice your delivery. As a matter of career advancement and 
personal branding, it pays (well!) to be known as an SE who is comfortable 
with the executive connection. 
Execute the Meeting
As part of the planning process, you need to decide if your role will be 
education/consultative or sales oriented. My experience is that an SE will 
accomplish more, achieve a higher success rate, and lower their self-orien-
tation by being consultative 4 versus sales focused. By educating and even 
gently challenging the executive, you reinforce the general aura of being in 
the room to help. Leave the direct selling to sales management. 
 Hint: The Magic Number Revisited 
As a sports coach working with young children, one of the keys you learn early is to keep 
the parents (the economic buyers) happy. One technique is to ensure that your players 
have a simple answer to the parental question of “What did you learn today?” Every 
4. CIOs and other technology leaders have found themselves in greater positions 
of leadership, taking a critical role in facilitating the shift to remote work, re -
configuring supply chains, automating factories, and so on. Rob Alexander, CIO 
of Capital One Financial, says, “We’ve seen years worth of adoption jammed 
into just a few months. We need to be well-rounded business leaders with deep 
expertise across multiple disciplines and a growth mindset.” Sounds like a sales 
engineer! (Wall Street Journal interview, September 21, 2021).

practice would finish with a huddle where we would quickly summarize the one skill we 
worked upon. Without stretching the analogy too far, the executive should leave the meet-
ing with a very clear idea of the lesson they learned. 
Presentation Ideas
Most executives are visual and tactile learners. This learning style means 
a picture or an action, instead of the traditional one thousand words on a 
slide, best communicates a technical message. One exception to this is when 
demonstrating your product. Often you can tell an executive how some -
thing functions in response to a question rather than physically showing 
them. This tell versus show is a perfect example of employing the conver -
sation reference story discussed in Chapter 16 (“Storytelling”). Although I 
have no subjective data to back up this assertion, I firmly believe that the 
later you demo to an executive in a qualified sales cycle, the better it goes.  
As a supplement to the “what skills” survey shown in Table 20.1, we 
asked a subset of those executives if they preferred a formal electronic 
presentation or an informal conversation. Some 81% voted for the casual 
conversation. Termed shoulder-to-shoulder selling in some countries, this 
presentation method recommends sitting next to your customer and mak -
ing your point using a piece of paper, brochure, whiteboard, or tablet. As a 
corollary, executives within our customer base recommend 5 this approach 
for their employees.
Hint: John’s Favorite Tactic 
When meeting with sales, presales, or marketing executives, my favorite line is to pull out 
a set of pens and say, “I left my laptop in the car. How about a conversation, and then 
we sketch out a picture of what we’re thinking about?” Imagine how many thousands 
of slides these poor executives have to see every month. It is a different approach, and if 
executed with even a small amount of skill, it can help both you and your company seem 
memorable and different. One of the most significant transactions in MTS history was 
sealed on the back of a napkin in the Boomerang Bar at Circular Quay, Singapore. 
Should you be required to give a slide-based presentation because of 
culture, custom, or setting, then apply the maxim “less is more.” The perfect 
presentation is one that you can destroy if you remove just one more thing. 
You need enough to make your point and convince the audience to accept 
your MARS-BARS and nothing else. I’m not suggesting that you channel 
your minimalist inner Steve Jobs and have one image and a single word on 
each slide; just don’t add even more content “just in case.” If you apply the 
5. I learned a new phrase—“avoiding the YAPPs,”  meaning yet another PowerPoint!

50% of time construct, you will never get to that content anyway. Although 
reference and supplemental material may be needed after the fact, don’t 
incorporate that into the visible body of your presentation. Instead, take 
your cue from the senior executive in the room about speed and whether 
to dwell on a slide or move on. 
CASE STUDY: “Decomplexification” in Action
I once worked for a multibillion-dollar company that sold hundreds of different 
enterprise software products and wasn’t particularly well known as a market 
leader in any area. They still aren’t. The corporate sales portfolio covered solu-
tions in IT infrastructure management, security, and overall governance. It was 
unbelievably difficult to explain all that to my friends, never mind a busy line 
of business executive. Finally, after several confusing attempts at explaining, I 
settled on the analogy of a house—certainly a familiar object. While drawing a 
picture of a house, I explained that our management products monitored the 
use of utilities like water, gas, and electricity and made sure, through the ap -
propriate use of fuses, thermostats, and the like, that the house continued to 
function as the owners expected, even if they were off the property. Security 
products locked the door and windows and provided an alarm system, CO2, 
and smoke detectors to keep the occupants safe and sound. Our governance 
products ensured that bills were paid, maintenance was performed, permits 
were applied for, and taxes were paid. The analogy was an instant hit, especially 
when the customer grabbed the pen and extended the story by adding security 
gates, dogs, closed-circuit cameras, and so on. 
E E E E
Answering Questions
Executives like to be clear and direct, and most of them expect to drive the 
agenda and ask a lot of questions. An executive is likely to ask you just as 
many questions, if not more, than a highly technical audience. Those ques-
tions will have a distinct theme and are a mixture of curiosity, challenge, 
and clarification. The best thing you can usually do in front of that senior 
executive is to answer the question. 
Dave Kellogg, serial CxO and board member, cites the following 
example.6
• Question: On a scale of 1–10, how is the team doing?
6. See https://kellblog.com/2012/01/17/the-one-key-to-dealing-with-senior-exec-
utives-answer-the-question.

---

## Page 231

210 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Executive Connection 211
• Bad answer: Well, you know, the team have been trying really hard. 
Things haven’t been perfect, but they’ve really been pulling together 
lately, and I think things are improving. We’ve filled the open head-
count and have made progress.
• Good answer: 7.
• Best answer: 7, although we have one or two problems to resolve.
You should answer the question because the executive wants it an -
swered. You should answer it briefly because there is a high likelihood of 
having a line of questions they want to follow. The best answer is the best 
as it both answers the question and gives the executive the option to “pull 
the thread” to learn more if they wish. It is their decision. Although you can 
still employ much of the strategy of Chapter 18 (“Answering Questions”), 
especially to make sure you understand why the question is being asked, 
you have to take a few shortcuts to preserve the patience of the executive 
and keep the meeting flowing.
Follow-Up After the Meeting
As part of the post-call debrief, you should discuss follow-up items. It is no 
longer the case that everything is channeled through the salesperson, espe-
cially if you made a personal or technical connection between yourself and 
that executive. Now is your opportunity to enhance your credibility and 
prove your reliability. Over the years, you can build up a contact list full of 
executives with whom you have had meetings. There is no reason you can-
not offer to make yourself personally available later should the executive 
need more data or have more questions. Perhaps 20% of the time, you may 
receive a follow-up contact request, or an incoming LinkedIn connection, 
allowing you to build a relationship that can help both your immediate and 
future career. 
On a more strategic level—play the long-term game. Remember that all 
the senior managers and directors that you meet with will be promoted. I 
now know several CxOs who were individual contributors when I first met 
them as a junior SE many years ago. Those relationships are still in place and 
can prove very useful, and not just for sales.
On a tactical level, one of the hardest things to accept is that, although 
you may have won the technical battle and the evaluation team tells you 
that you are their number one pick, you can still lose the deal. Some execu-
tive decisions are still stereotypically made on an exclusive golf course or 
over an expensive dinner. This type of outcome is far outside the realm of

---

## Page 232

210 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Executive Connection 211
the typical SE and can be very frustrating to observe. The deal is not com -
plete, despite the technical win, until the paperwork is signed. Be vigilant, 
and don’t disengage from the customer until the business win happens.
The Informal Executive Connection
Be prepared to encounter executives (and everyone else) from all compa -
nies in various everyday settings, such as airport terminals, sports events, in 
line at the coffee shop, and even at local parties. A social conversation may 
at some point include “Who do you work for?” or “What line of business 
are you in?” and even in countries where there is a clear line between the 
personal and the business, you can nonetheless gently engage with them. 
Whether you work for a large Fortune 100 name brand company or a tiny 
startup, always respond with your company name and a short one or two-
sentence tagline. Do not launch into what sales trainers endearingly call the 
elevator pitch,7 as it is the wrong setting. The subsequent conversation may 
go anywhere and usually results in purely social interaction. If you get a 
response like “How do you do that?” or “Tell me more,” then answer briefly 
and make sure you are running a conversation and not a presentation. End 
with a question for your new executive friend, and see what happens. 
Summary
More than any other SE activity, an executive session needs to be highly 
focused and stay on message and target. It is high risk and high reward. Use 
the executive rule of one to keep your presentation or conversation simple, 
and remember your maximum of three talking points to supplement the 
message. Respect the person as much as the position and value the execu -
tive’s time. Avoid long demonstrations or presentations and, in most cases, 
spend time on the business benefits and the return on investment they will 
receive by purchasing from your company. Finally, be truly FABulous (see 
Chapter 6).
Prepare, plan, and prepare again for the meeting, keeping in mind your 
MARS-BARS goals for the meeting. I’d also suggest that you prepare for 
7. This is a short description of an idea, product, or company that explains the con-
cept in a way such that any listener can understand it in a short period of time. 
It takes no longer than the typical 60 seconds you spend in an elevator with 
someone. I believe that you should give the elevator pitch the shaft.

and subsequently debrief after the meeting using the trust equation and see 
how you may have impacted any of the five T factors.
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Be prepared. Practice your presentation piece in half the allotted time. 
 2. Apply the executive rule of one.
 3. Keep it simple and on message. Don’t ramble.
 4. Set your MARS-BARS together with the account team.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Examine the corporate mission and financials.
 2. Map your product benefits directly back to revenue, cost, and risk.
 3. Reduce your reliance on formal presentation materials.
 4. Build the relationship and apply the trust equation.
