Business Value
Price is what you pay. Value is what you get.
          —Warren Buffett
T
he purchase of any sizeable amount of 
technology will usually require a business 
case. Even if an executive has a budget fully 
appropriated,1 they will need to show that the 
purchase makes business sense. To use the lan -
guage of business value discovery (Chapters 6 
and 7), the outputs they need will be savings of 
time, people, and money coupled with either a 
decrease in costs, increase in revenue, satisfac -
tion of mission, or mitigation of risk.
We know that our solutions have zero inher-
ent value unless they are used to solve a prob -
lem. They only become valuable when we help 
our customers put solutions into use to solve a 
problem. In the world of everything-as-a-service, 
customers want us to help them create value in 
use. For most customers, the business problems 
contain an explicit or implicit requirement for 
measurable value creation.

214 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 215
This chapter covers the importance of taking the final value step, the rise 
of value engineering and cloud financial management (FinOps), and the im-
pact of the emotional ROI. These are definitely some of the more advanced 
topics covered in the book. 
Proving the Value
It is essential that the customer feels comfortable with the monetary benefits 
of the solution. Do not automatically assume that they get it because the eco-
nomics seem so apparent to you. Liza Minnelli, starring as Sally Bowles in 
Cabaret,2 sings, “Money makes the world go around, the world go around.” 
Money is the universal currency of business, so the sales team needs to em-
phasize the economics in the closing stages of the buying process. 
Once we qualify a customer opportunity or business engagement, we 
need to provide the technical validation and then map the successful deliv -
ery of capabilities to evidence of measurable customer value. That means 
that we need to answer the somewhat philosophical question of what is 
value? How do we define value? To design value for our customers, what do 
you need to know and do? 
We must begin to think of ourselves more as value engineers. Practically 
that means we need to focus on being business problem-solvers rather than 
only thinking of ourselves as sales engineers. This is especially true if you are 
proposing as-a-service or consumption-related capabilities (e.g., cloud ser -
vices). We’ll expand on this in upcoming sections of this chapter, but here 
are some things to think about related to customer value:
Organizations create value by:
• Managing costs down;
• Expanding volume and velocity;
• Increasing productivity;
• Improving margins;
• Or a combination of all of these.
Practically, that means they can choose to do one or more of the 
following:
2. Cabaret, 1972 musical drama film, directed by Bob Fosse.

---

## Page 236

214 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 215
1. Deliver unique services and capabilities;
2. Deliver exceptional customer and employee experiences;
3. Repair and streamline internal processes;
4. Digitalize the customer journey as a seamless experience;
5. Drive operational excellence and ongoing innovation.
As we explore the opportunities to do the math and create measurable 
value for customers, here is something (controversial) to consider related 
to value: the products, solutions, services, and capabilities that we provide 
to our customers have zero inherent or implicit value. Think about that for 
a moment. Our solutions only become valuable when they are put to the 
proper use. That use is when they create value by delivering positive busi -
ness outcomes with and for our customers. There is your moment of Zen.
How Do Customers Assess Value?
As sales engineers, we need to understand and provide input into custom -
ers’ processes to assess value. For many of us, our primary contacts tend to 
be on the technical or operational side when we are working with custom-
ers. We probably have a good sense of the types of value assessments our 
technical contacts use in evaluations, proofs of concept, or when comparing 
proposals. Important metrics like time savings, productivity gains, the total 
cost of ownership (TCO), and simple ROI calculations are standard in tech-
nical teams up to, and often including, the CIO, CISO, or CTO. However, do 
we understand and influence the business assessment of whether or not our 
proposals create value? Any substantial investment will make its way to the 
head of procurement, the CFO, and (potentially) other C-level executives 
in the organization.
At this point, you might be saying, “Hold on. I don’t have good contacts 
with the business side of the house. My sales rep even struggles with that. 
There’s no way I can influence someone like the CFO.” Even if we don’t 
have direct access to the CFO and other business executives, we must com-
municate our value creation potential using language and numbers that 
they will find credible and valuable. We can arm our key contacts in the 
technical team with the information they need to influence the business. 
It is a knock-on effect as our contacts speak with people within the CFO’s 
circle of influence.

---

## Page 237

216 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 217
Typical Assessment Methods
The good news is that most customers use a relatively short list of value as -
sessment methods. The most common measurements3 in organizations of all 
types (including government and nonprofits) and sizes are:
• Total cost of ownership (TCO);
• Payback period (PP);
• Return on investment (ROI);
• Net present value (NPV);
• Internal rate of return (IRR).
The other bit of good news is that all of these measurements attempt to 
answer the same question: “Is this a good investment of my money?” But, 
more specifically, we must be able to answer this question (with evidence): 
“What are the total costs and benefits of this solution to the customer’s orga-
nization and over what period?” 
Technical organizations tend to use TCO, PP, and ROI. On the other 
hand, business functions and organizations, like finance, are more likely to 
use NPV and IRR. 
Technical teams like TCO, PP, and ROI because they are relatively 
straightforward. Those measures do an excellent job approximating the val-
ue created by deploying the right solutions against the right problems. For 
example, TCO measures the purchase price plus the total costs to operate 
that solution. If we compare the full benefits to the TCO, we get a straight -
forward cost-benefit or ROI analysis. Additionally, suppose we do a simple 
calculation of when the total benefits will exceed the total costs of the solu-
tion. In that case, we get a simple payback analysis and breakeven point for 
the investment. However, even though TCO and the PP are straightforward, 
they miss some critical variables for business decision makers. This is where 
NPV and IRR come in.
The reason that business teams, like finance, use additional tools like 
NPV and IRR can be summed up in one word: time. The CFO, for example, is 
not just interested in the total cost of a solution compared to the full benefit. 
Instead, the CFO wants to know how much time that investment will take 
to pay off. In this sense, time is money for the CFO because an investment 
3. You may also see some more complex measurements such as return on invested 
capital (ROIC) and cost-volume profit (CVP).

---

## Page 238

216 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 217
that nets a quick payback is much more valuable than a payback that takes 
years to provide positive returns.
The Importance of Time to Value
What is the value of lowering costs (especially upfront costs) and acceler -
ating the benefits of an investment? Naturally, the time to value (TTV) is 
quicker. This TTV is one of the attractions to cloud-related or consumption-
oriented investments in the brave new world of everything-as-a-service—
lower upfront costs and accelerated deployments and benefits, leading to 
quicker time to value.
It is here that the evaluation of time becomes a critical aspect of doing 
the math. For CFOs, as an example, assessments such as NPV and IRR are 
seen as much more conservative ways to look at costs versus benefits than 
simple TCO, PP, or ROI analyses. This is because NPV and IRR help CFOs 
take a very critical look at just how long it takes to generate those expected 
benefits. An old saying helps to illustrate this, “Gold that I have in my hand 
today is more valuable than the promise of any gold in the future.” 4 What 
does that mean for us? Any expectations of benefits or value creation that 
we are proposing or demonstrating for the customer are just that—expecta-
tions. In other words, there is always the risk that those benefits won’t fully 
materialize since the future contains many unknowns. CFOs will discount 
any future benefits to account for risk and practical variables like inflation, 
opportunity costs, the cost of capital, and other factors. Any future benefits 
that we share with our customers are discounted lower each successive year 
into the future because the money that the customer has access to, and the 
immediate use of today, is more valuable than the benefits you promise in 
the future. This explains why quicker benefits are more attractive to your 
customer than benefits over a more extended time.
CASE STUDY: Budgets and Beer
As a bonus, this is a beautiful example of humanizing numbers, as discussed in 
Chapter 16 (“Storytelling”). 
 Anders is an engineering executive at a media company in Europe. The CFO 
at the company gave Anders the objective to optimize their cloud spending 
by 10% to improve the margins of the company’s on-demand video content 
services. When Anders shared this news with his team, the reaction from the 
front-line engineers was, “Okay. So what? What’s 10%? Big deal.”
4. An alternative version is “A bird in the hand is worth two in the bush.”

---

## Page 239

218 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 219
 Anders explained that the January cloud spending had been €375,000. So, 
simple math meant 10% optimization would net €37,500—enough to fund 
some much-needed hiring. Again, however, the engineers looked around at 
each other and, again, collectively shrugged.
 Anders had an idea. He then explained to the engineers that €37,500 per 
month was the equivalent of more than 10,000 beers (in Anders’ town any -
way)! More than 400 cases of beer! Now Anders had the team’s attention! Be -
cause €37,500 per month is a lot of beer—uhhhh—money!
E E E E
Here is a way to summarize all of this without any financial jargon. 
Whether your customer is a corporation, government body, or nonprofit:
• When money is invested, it needs to accomplish (return) a good outcome.
• Executives and managers need to measure those returns as costs ver-
sus benefits.
• Time—the time it takes to generate positive returns—is a crucial vari -
able.
• Costs (or budget or capital) are being paid.
• Benefits (or results or value) are being generated.
• If returns are above the customer’s hurdle rate, we are creating value.
• If returns are below the customer’s hurdle rate, we are destroying 
value.
The purpose of this chapter is not to make you an expert on things like 
net present value or internal rate of return. However, you should be aware 
that your customer is using these methods to assess your investment. Also, 
you should be aware that although the math might seem complicated, the 
concept for us is “How can I show my customer the quickest and most reli-
able way possible to achieve the benefits of my solution that exceed the cost 
of the initial investment?”
Closing the Loop 
Many sales transactions go astray because the customer has a different in -
ternal view of your solution’s benefits. It is your responsibility to fill this gap 
of understanding and ensure that you and the customer share the same set of 
measurable outcomes. To illustrate this, I’ll continue with Table 21.1, which 
is also the example from Table 7.2 in Chapter 7.

---

## Page 240

218 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 219
For simplicity and more straightforward math, let’s directly convert the 
3,744 hours into two full working years or two full-time equivalent (FTE) 
headcount. We will also assume that an individual’s fully burdened cost 
(FBC), salary plus overhead/benefits, is €125,000. 
In the most extreme case, a sponsoring executive could terminate these 
two individuals and save €250,000. Of course, it is never the role of an SE 
to suggest to a customer that they can fire people, but you can certainly 
suggest that “You could then deploy these two people onto more productive 
and impactful projects.” Typically, the time is redirected onto other projects, 
and you can claim the productivity gains. I have yet to meet a manager who 
does not believe that they would get their money’s worth out of the equiva-
lent of two new staff. However, the critical statement is that someone from 
the sales team must say, “So that is a productivity gain of about €250,000. 
Does that sound correct?” and then get customer agreement. 
Hint: Full and Complete Savings 
Help the customer to see the total savings. In our example, you should write or type 
the €250,000 to visually reinforce your statement. For psychological impact, write the 
amount as 250,000 and not as 250K. 
There is further to go with this example. We have sped up the devel -
opment process by four days. That means that the final product or service 
is ready for sale four days earlier than before. Now we can investigate the 
results of quantifying faster time to market, as shown in Table 21.2.
The key statement becomes, “It seems that by accelerating the start of 
the development process, your IT teams would get these products to market 
four days faster, so your salespeople have four extra days to sell those prod-
ucts. Based on your figures, that is a revenue acceleration of €26,667 every 
month, or €320,000 in a full year. Does that sound about right?”
We now have a combination of a €250,000 productivity gain and a 
€320,000 revenue gain. So that is one side of the equation. The other side

The Customer Does Have to Spend Money , Too
To realize the gains, the customer will need to spend money. Although it is 
not (usually) the job of the SE to calculate exact contractual pricing, you’ll 
usually have an idea of an approximate spend amount. Depending upon 
your pricing model, that spend may be monthly or a one-time upfront 
amount. These examples ignore any one-time startup or shutdown costs, 
maintenance charges, and so on.

shows a breakeven at the end of project life, so it is an unlikely candidate 
for investment. Line 4 has zero payback. Every customer has their payback

criteria,6 which rarely extends beyond three years. The critical statement, 
for line 1, here is “Based on these rough numbers, we are seeing a payback 
at about six months. Does that seem reasonable?” This yields the answer 
to the eventual CFO question of “How quickly will I realize the benefits?”
The monthly payback calculations of Table 21.4 are much simpler. If 
your customer saves/makes more money than they spend with you in an 
average month, the payback is positive. In our example, the monthly gain 
is (570,000/12) = 47,500. The two important factors then become the abso-
lute and the percentage gain.
In these examples, line 1 is not a workable economic case, and line 2 is 
positive but not compelling. Both lines 3 and 4 seem like excellent returns. 
The warning is that everything depends on scale, as a 35,000 per month 
gain may be trivial compared to a budget of hundreds of millions. Our criti-
cal statement for line 4 is “Using your data, this project gives an extremely 
positive return of about 35,000 per month, or 280%.” Once again, that is 
the CFO soundbite. 
The point of emphasis is that you are looking for a broad customer 
agreement on approximate numbers. If your business case hinges on 
whether personnel costs are 125,000 versus 115,000, it is unlikely to get 
approval. The desired outcome is that when you present your proposed so-
lution, you can directly link the technical features to the business outcomes 
and the economics.
How Do Customers Create Budget?
If we have shown a customer evidence that we can somehow create value 
for them, the response might sound like this: “Okay, I see the opportunity, 
but I can’t just create a budget for this.” But, of course, that’s not entirely 
accurate. Indeed, customers may have specific budgeting periods or time

more advanced financial concepts.

222 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 223
constraints on when and how to ask for a new budget. Still, new funding is 
being allocated for valuable investments all the time.
When creating a budget for new investments, customers have at least 
three paths they can take, or a combination of these three paths. 
First, they can use capital budget (CapEx) that often comes from cash 
that the organization holds as an asset on its balance sheet. Investments that 
use CapEx will be owned assets and shown as short- or long-term assets on 
the balance sheet. Thus, capital budget essentially exchanges one asset—
cash—for another asset—your solution, for example. 
Second, customers can use their operating budget or operating expenses 
(OpEx) to pay for an investment. OpEx is paid as an ongoing expense re -
flected on the organization’s P&L or income statement. These investments, 
common in as-a-service or cloud-related investments, are not owned and are 
therefore not reflected on the balance sheet. However, as monthly, quar -
terly, or annual expenses, these are reflected on the income statement. 
Finally, customers can use financing to fund either CapEx, OpEx, or a 
combination of the two. There are many positives to financing because cus-
tomers can use financing as either a source of capital or purchasing power. 
For many years, financing for well-qualified borrowers has been reasonably 
cheap since interest rates after the global financial crisis have remained at 
all-time lows. Using financing, customers can also unlock value by cleaning 
up old assets on their balance sheet. They sell those assets back to a financing 
company for a lump sum cash payment or by having the financial services 
firm lease those assets back to the organization as a way to conserve or im -
prove cash flow.
Here are just a few points to summarize how customers consider if, 
when, and how to create budget for investments they want to make in pur-
suit of value creation.
Customers who favor using CapEx for technology tend to:
• Have high liquidity or lots of cash on their balance sheets;
• Operate at relatively high levels of profitability;
• Have high levels of purchasing power or access to funds.
Customers who favor using OpEx for technology tend to:
• Run leaner balance sheets but have high short-term cash flows;
• Need flexibility, speed, innovation, and agility;
• Have relatively predictable profits and cash flows.
Customers who favor using financing tend to:

---

## Page 244

222 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 223
• Need to increase purchasing power and working capital;
• Have a desire to preserve cash or liquidity (or leverage low-cost capi-
tal);
• Want to unlock value on their balance sheet by selling or converting 
assets.
Value Engineering and FinOps 
Value engineering is critical, as it helps to prioritize your transaction above 
others. One of the major reasons that Do Nothing, Inc., or its cousin al -
ternate use of capital (AUC) wins so many deals is because the customer 
decides to spend their budget elsewhere. A budget-owning executive may 
have 50–60 potential projects on their to-do list. Each project has an as -
sociated cost of time, money, people, and some inherent risk. A team then 
ranks the projects, and ROI becomes one of the criteria. Even if all 60 proj-
ects show a positive return, not all of them will be funded. There is a cut 
line. If the project is above the line, it receives the financial go ahead. If it 
falls below the line, then it is deferred. 
At its simplest, it is the job of the entire sales team to make sure that 
your project is above the line and makes the cut. In the days of significant 
IT CapEx allocations, you could still gain the technical win and then lose 
to DNI or AUC. Once a company transitions to a cloud OpEx model, the 
initial financial barrier to entry is lower. So, more projects are started based 
on “fail faster,” and the cut line goes deeper into the project list. Perhaps 35 
projects start instead of 20. There is now a correspondingly greater pressure 
to succeed faster. 
There is usually not much of a requirement for a complex financial cal-
culation for smaller transactions, and the simple back-of-the-napkin calcu-
lation or a basic spreadsheet is sufficient. However, for the more significant 
or multiyear transactions, complex calculations come into play. This is not 
the domain of the typical SE and has given rise to a new subtype of SE, the 
value engineer (VE). These are SEs who have a financial/MBA background 
or a natural inclination for the area.
A value engineer is typically engaged to work with the client to formu-
late a first pass economic justification and then assist in the internal justi -
fication process. Some sample key responsibilities for a VE are as follows:
• Interview potential customers in later stages of a deal to build a joint 
business case. Works in partnership with salesperson and lead SE.

---

## Page 245

224 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 225
• Helps support a difficult customer renewal with a value realized esti-
mate (how much was saved over the prior period).
• Presents business case to an economic buyer.
• Provides guidance on how to structure the commercials of a complex 
transaction to align with the business case.
• Work with marketing teams to build out the value components of 
customer case studies. May use customer or third-party data.
• Help the account team with deal strategy and identify the need for 
specific business cases based upon the pipeline.
The VE plays a crucial role in the largest transactions. They are effec -
tively the subject matter expert in linking the technology portfolio to the 
customer outcomes and can allay customer fears about their investment. 
The pay as you go cloud model leads to a rise in shadow IT and the overall 
variability of spending. Shadow IT refers to projects that are managed out -
side of and without the IT department’s knowledge. Most commonly, this is 
technology such as SaaS applications, cloud storage, or messaging apps. A 
line of business organization purchases technology because it is faster than 
waiting for IT approval. That becomes a major problem for finance, purchas-
ing, and IT, as they cannot estimate monthly costs. And so was born FinOps.
Cloud Financial Operations
FinOps is shorthand for cloud financial management. It is the practice of 
bringing financial accountability to the variable spend model of the cloud, 
enabling distributed teams to make business tradeoffs between speed, cost, 
and quality. Like the emergence of DevOps in the early 2000s, when devel-
opers and technology operators became more aligned using agile manage -
ment principles, FinOps is an emerging discipline for vendors, partners, and 
customers who are delivering and using more XaaS (anything as a service) 
subscriptions and/or consumption models.
Most finance initiatives revolve around saving money, however. FinOps 
is about making money. 7 Cloud can drive more revenue, signal customer 
base growth, enable more product and feature release velocity, or even help 
shut down a data center. FinOps is all about removing blockers; empowering 
engineering teams to deliver better features, apps, and migrations faster; and 
enabling a cross-functional conversation about where to invest and when. 
Sometimes a business will decide to tighten the belt; sometimes, it will de -
cide to invest more. But now teams know why they are making those deci-
sions. By linking your proposal to your customer’s FinOps practice, if they 
7. See www.finops.org.

---

## Page 246

224 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 225
have one, you maximize your chance of getting above the cut line and 
receiving the all clear for your sale.
An analogy frequently used in discussions about correcting inefficien -
cies in cloud operations through FinOps practices is friction. Waste in cloud 
spending is like friction in a machine that produces heat—wasted energy. 
Friction in the physical world is not only simply energy that is wasted. That 
friction can result in unnecessary expenditures, downtime, and losses if not 
mitigated and managed. In a FinOps context, friction prevents customers 
from achieving their desired business outcomes because of inefficient or 
wasteful spending on cloud services. This type of friction is wasted energy 
because resources (usually money in the form of an organization’s operat-
ing expenses) are spent, but this spending fails to generate any value.
We all know that customer expectations of ownership, service, and ac-
cess have evolved. With an everything-as-a-service mindset, individual cus-
tomers and their organizations are embracing new consumption and opera-
tional models (many powered by FinOps) in which little or no friction exists 
between desired outcomes and the satisfaction of a business need. Today, 
customers want as little friction as possible between their desired outcomes 
and the timing of when they realize value.
Time is money. In the world of everything-as-a-service in the cloud, 
this has never been more true or more critical. Long-term planning based 
on relatively fixed quarterly or annual budgets no longer works. Instead, 
we measure cloud spending in hours, minutes, and (in some cases) seconds. 
Traditional financial processes that assume long and/or slow budgeting and 
spending cycles in business will not cut it in the cloud. Now that cloud is 
a material part of most of our customers’ spending, many CIOs have been 
caught off guard by the rapid shift of long-term investments recorded as 
capital expenditures on the balance sheet to business-driven spending re -
corded as operating expenses on the income statement (i.e., the P&L).
The move to the cloud has made traditional financial processes, which 
worked well for more than a century, seem slow and outdated. Tradition -
ally, procurement was primarily the gatekeeper on any material IT spend 
because they had to approve all large equipment purchases. However, as 
organizations move into the cloud, the pay-as-you-go model—also known 
as the variable spend model—allows engineers and business users to bypass 
this procurement approval process. Now that cloud spending has reached 
material levels, organizations are left to allocate, explain, and control these 
costs. Unfortunately, with over 300,000 SKUs offered by the major cloud 
service providers, cloud financial management is a problem that will not 
solve itself.
A common problem with customer cloud spending is that costs creep 
up until some tipping point (or cost-related incident). The CFO then panics,

---

## Page 247

226 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 227
and suddenly the full attention of leadership is on this “out of control cloud 
spending situation.” This “spend panic” leads to an immediate push for ac -
countability and controls. Finance and procurement express frustration with 
the complexity of the cloud bill and a resultant strong desire to reduce costs. 
Even—and this has really happened—a push to go back toward a data center 
strategy and abandon cloud altogether.
None of this is comfortable. All of these areas are still new to both cus -
tomers and technology vendors. That is why becoming comfortable and con-
fident in this type of discussion and customer engagement is valuable for the 
SE. The world of cloud services is not just about raw capability and perfor -
mance. Increasingly, it is much more about resource optimization, manage-
ment, unit economics, and business outcomes. As sales engineers, we have 
the benefit of perceived credibility in many areas of expertise. As a result, 
our customers now expect us to have the ability to engage them in the in -
creasingly important challenge of making the economics of cloud work in 
support of positive business outcomes.
The Emotional Return on Investment
In contrast to Sally Bowles, the Beatles tell us that “All you need is love, love 
is all you need.” Although John, Paul, George, and Ringo weren’t speaking 
about complex technology, they have a point. As sales engineers, we uncov-
er the customer’s key business issues, translate those into the universal cur-
rency of money, and uniquely match our technical solution to those needs 
to maximize returns for everyone. We do this because we have learned that 
only talking about technology will usually cost us the sale and annoy the 
salespeople. We know that our customers must justify the purchase to some-
one in the organization. Yet  every veteran SE can tell you stories about a 
deal where:
• You make a compelling economic case to replace an incumbent ven-
dor.
• Your solution is 20% cheaper than the competition.
• The payback for your proposal is measured in weeks instead of 
months.
• And nothing happens!
That is because buyers don’t always make logical decisions, especial -
ly when risk is involved. There are entire books dedicated to this subject

---

## Page 248

226 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Doing the Math and Proving Business Value 227
(like Dan Ariely’s Predictably Irrational).8 I’m sure that you can think of some 
personal and business decisions of your own that were not purely 100% 
driven by economics and numbers in a spreadsheet. Your customer needs a 
rational business case to make a purchase decision (money makes the world 
go around), but they need something else as well. They need to remember 
what they’re going to buy from you and why they will buy it from you 
(that’s the love). 
Customers do not buy solutions; customers buy results. The classic ex -
ample is that home improvement consumers do not really want quarter-
inch drill bits. They want quarter-inch holes. This means you must under -
stand the specific and required results and how the customer will get them. 
We talk about creating a bridge from the current state to the future state, 
or from “A” to “B.” Extending that bridge analogy, a solid financial return 
is the foundation and infrastructure of the bridge. However, to get the cus-
tomer to start the journey, they need to know:
1. It is safe to step on the bridge.
2. Others have crossed it before them (unless they are seeking a lead-
ing-edge advantage).
3. It’s a great view!
4. What is the pain and gain of getting to the other end?
5. They can economically afford (pay the toll/fare) to cross the bridge.
6. You will be their guide.
CASE STUDY: Love and Money
As an IT executive, I once had a $3 million proposal that would pay back in 79 
days. (I still remember it!) But I couldn’t execute on it, despite all the common 
sense and economic reasoning, because we had no more money to spend at the 
end of the year, and I could not create a new budget. The CFO wouldn’t finance 
it, and the additional spend would have hit earnings by a penny per share and 
hurt the stock. Do Nothing happens for many reasons. They don’t teach you 
that in business school or value selling class. I was, however, in love with the 
proposal enough that I executed the deal on the first day of the next financial 
year on almost similar terms. My company sprinted across the bridge.
E E E E
8. Ariely, D., Predictably Irrational, Perennial Press, 2010.

---

## Page 249

228 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
 Do not spend so much time on the spreadsheet that you forget about 
the people. Buying from you could be a career-changing decision for the 
customer. 
Summary
Just as we transition from features to benefits in our demonstrations, we 
need to transition from benefits to explicit and expected financial outcomes 
to make the entire business case. Even though your proposal may generate 
an attractive positive return, that return is not guaranteed to obtain funding. 
You must ensure that your customer agrees to the financial outcomes and 
can fit those into their internal approval process. That internal process runs 
much smoother when your customer generates the numbers for themselves 
or in collaboration with you, rather than simply accepting your version. Fi -
nally, don’t get so wrapped up in the economics that you neglect the human 
side of the sale. People still buy from people that they trust and respect. 
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Take the final step and explicitly state the outcomes.
 2. Mention those outcomes in your technical presentation. 
 3. Research industry-standard metrics/measures that help in project justification.
 4. Remember the people. 
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Shadow a value engineer or business consultant practitioner. 
 2. Learn more about FinOps and your customer’s internal practices. 
 3. Y our trusted advisor status can help you gather data that no one else in your company  
    can get.

The Ramp Process
Ramping refers to the time it takes for an em -
ployee to acquire the necessary skills to perform 
their job effectively. Ramp time is an important 
metric for sales organizations because it directly 
impacts the required lead time to obtain revenue 
growth. It is challenging to execute a plan to

double revenue in a year 1 if you don’t hire enough salespeople and SEs to 
handle that growth. Over the past decade, it has taken an average of three 
months to hire a new SE, and then a further eight months for them to ramp 
up (more on that in Chapter 31, “The Interview and Hiring Process”). If 
your average sales cycle is seven months, a company needs to hire an SE 18 
months before revenue expectations. That is a highly conservative number, 
and most companies will instead plan for 10–12 months. The timing is a con-
cern for sales operations as they conduct annual planning. A small company 
may be taking out loans or selling equity to finance the investment (that 
is, you) in future sales. That initial ramp time is a sunk cost for larger com -
panies, with little prospect of any sales occurring. So, whatever you can do 
to accelerate your ramp time can only make you and your hiring manager 
look professionally competent. You are, in effect, solving a current pain by 
providing a better ROI on the decision to hire you.
This situation means that you need to take charge of your onboarding 
and ramping experience and be active rather than passive. Previewing the 
success plan, whatever you can materially do to impact a deal, either by di -
rectly working on it or offloading tasks from a more senior SE, is a definite 
plus.
Setting Goals with Y our Manager
Once hired, immediately meet with your manager and discuss what you 
need to be successful. Success criteria are a standard part of every SEs life, 
both internally and externally. Your company should have a set of expecta-
tions for your abilities at certain key milestones. Table 22.1 presents a sample 
planning process that can be the basis for the first discussion and later one on 
ones. You must be clear about those expectations. 
Hint: Document Your Experiences
If you are one of the first SEs to join your company or are a new hire in a new geography 
or business unit, document your experiences. Keep a running diary of the positives and 
negatives and share it with your manager and the onboarding or enablement team. It can 
serve as a guide for the next hire and potentially improve their ramping experience. In ad-
dition, it is a great way to show some added value!
Request that you have a one-on-one meeting with your manager at 
least every two weeks. Devote a portion of this meeting to cover your plan 
and what is happening in the next two to four weeks. Even if you see your 
manager every day, set some time aside on the calendar for a progress re -
view. If you are remote, these meetings are essential, even if one of the 
1. Theoretically you could double your prices, although that is not a sustainable 
year-over-year business model.

expectations is that you be a highly motivated self-starter. These regular 
reviews will allow you to gather feedback, request assistance, and look at 
any minor course corrections you may need to reach a level of total SE pro-
ductivity. You can be confident that your manager is regularly asked about 
your progress by their manager.
The Need to Succeed
Now that you have set your goals, the next step is to discover what you need 
to know to achieve them. Most of this information will come from the man-
ager and the onboarding team but be prepared for an iterative approach. 
Key information areas will include some combination of the following:
• Product knowledge (technical know-how, demos, presentations);

Sample Planning Outline with Your Manager
1. How do I define success?
 a. Quota or revenue requirements, including timing, bonuses, accelerators
 b. “Club” achievement trips and other benefits
 c. Job description for my role and next level
2. What do I need to be successful?
 a. Product expertise
 b. Presentation and other outbound skills (e.g., demos, whiteboards)
 c. General technical skills (such as coding)
 d. Meeting key support groups and individuals at corporate HQ
 e. Understanding my role in the sales process
 f. Knowing the informal processes (how things really work around here)
 g. Learning how to make my manager (and usually the sales manager) successful

 h. Who are the external leaders?
4. Training policy

 b. Corporate reimbursement for external training (industry associations, MBA, 
certifications)
 c. Equipment—how do I replace/gain more?

• Organizational structure and processes (who does what and where 
to go to find things);
• Corporate overview (basic details about your company and market 
positioning);
• The sales process (the steps you must complete to close a deal, plus 
roles and responsibilities during the sale);
• Business- or industry-specific knowledge (vertical or subject matter 
expertise).
Identify the critical skills in each of these five areas (plus any other areas 
you feel are relevant) and determine when you are expected to have these 
skills and when you will be allowed to get them. You will have gaps in your 
background, so discuss how you can improve in those areas. Frequently, 
new SEs will lack either technical experience or sales experience. If your 
training assumes a basic level of competence in either area, make sure you 
are on track to meet or exceed those requirements. Attending a training ses-
sion too early in your onboarding process, when you do not have the neces-
sary base level of competence, will waste your time. You’ll probably miss out 
on the more advanced material.
Boot Camp
A sales training/enablement group may host a boot camp session2 to get you 
up to speed on the products you will be selling. Unfortunately, many SE 
organizations focus 90% of their time on pure technical training and only 
10% on other areas, such as giving effective product presentations, handling 
Q&A, and competitive differentiation. Before attending the training, create a 
personal list of objectives. Build your own need-to-know knowledge list and 
check off the items as the introductory training covers them. Review that 
list with your manager and a few other more senior SEs and look for gaps, 
especially as your onboarding comes to a close.
One significant advantage of boot camp is that it creates a social group of 
newcomers all in the same situation. It is a powerful bond, and I’d encour-
age you to interact with as many of your classmates as possible and keep 
the connections once you finish camp. You’ll find that your peers will solve 
many of your “How do I?” questions, and you’ll do the same for them. In 
particular, try to build relationships with SEs from other geographies/coun-
tries and offer to share materials with them.
2. Endearingly known as “geek weeks” in some companies.

---

## Page 254

232 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The New SE: Getting Started 233
Develop a 30-/90-/180-Day Plan
The next step is to move beyond looking at your general ramp process and 
examine the specifics on how to succeed. My recommendation is to break 
the goals into manageable components for completion in 30, 90, and 180 
days. (I proudly note that the book you are reading is part of the SE on -
boarding process at many companies.) This approach allows you to opti -
mize your ramp time by completing concrete milestones. 
Work with your manager, salespeople, and peers to build out your defi-
nition of success. The primary and most measurable criteria are to achieve 
your revenue targets. Those targets may be monthly, quarterly, and annual 
numbers, split between total and incremental revenue. By now, you should 
have a good idea of precisely what you need to do to successfully close deals 
to make those targets. Next, observe the more experienced members of the 
sales team (both sales and SEs) and incorporate your version of their best 
practices into your desired daily behavior. 
Hint: Show That You CARE
Every experienced SE will freely admit that over their career, they have liberally taken 
and reused material from other SEs. Therefore, the number one development rule for the 
SE is to CARE. That self-serving acronym stands for copy and repurpose everything. Any 
time you see or hear another SE do something memorable, interesting, or compelling, 
borrow it—and make it your own. 
Even if it is not required, goal setting is an integral part of your success 
plan. If you don’t know where you are going, then anywhere will serve as 
your destination. Your definition of success should enable you to meet your 
corporate goals as well as any personal objectives. You can write this in the 
form of a mission statement:
Achieve 120% of first-year targets by utilizing superior sales skills and 
in-depth product knowledge and providing excellent customer service.
Although superior and excellent are subjective, the above is a good 
start. As an SE, if you can hit the number, build a good network, and de -
velop product expertise—all wrapped with solid professional skills, your 
career is off to a flying start. 
The Overall 30-90-180 Structure
The structure provides a simple mechanism to organize your early prog -
ress. First, take any goals and training plans and map them to the 30-, 90-, 
and 180-day periods. In the earlier example in this chapter, we looked at 
five key information areas (product, organization, corporate, sales, specif -
ic skills). You can cross-reference your development plan by time and by

---

## Page 255

234 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The New SE: Getting Started 235
those five factors. Next, consider the dependencies between the goals and 
opportunities provided by travel. For example, if you spend one of your first 
weeks at corporate HQ, you can begin working on organization and sales. 
A good plan should be clear and actionable. Do not place items in the 
plan that you don’t understand or know how to execute. Also, (gently) 
question why certain items might be included by default into your plan if 
you see no utility. Those items are typically salesperson related, like pricing 
or contracts. The plan should always include dates and place relationship-
building opportunities in parallel with activities like training when meeting 
with new people from support, corporate marketing, or product manage -
ment. You then get a two for one. Regularly review your task list to assign 
dates if they are not on the calendar. The six months will pass much faster 
than you expect. 
The First Six Months
Business leader and success mentor Darren Hardy says, “Starting is not most 
people’s problem. Staying, continuing, and finishing is.”3 You will undoubt-
edly bring great enthusiasm and commitment to the new job; the follow-
through during your first six months will set you up for continued success. 
Here are some ideas and recommendations collected by other SEs to help 
you make the first six months a solid foundation for that success.
Find a Mentor 
Finding a mentor can be difficult. Yet there are many documented advantag-
es to finding someone willing to help you navigate your professional career. 
Various studies have shown that mentees are five times more likely to be 
promoted, that mentor programs increase diversity by anywhere from 9% to 
24%, and that 89% of those mentored go on to mentor others later in their 
career. The Association of Talent Development notes that more than 70% of 
Fortune 500 companies have a formal mentor program in place. 
Many companies will assign you a buddy to help you through the ramp-
ing process. That buddy will not be your manager but a more experienced 
SE looking to give something back to the community. A buddy may or not 
be a mentor. Note that many organizations now require senior SEs to help 
more junior SEs in order to gain promotion to a master or principal SE level. 
There is a definite two-way benefit at stake. My general advice is to be ag -
gressive in seeking out a mentor based on “If you don’t ask, then you don’t 
get.” Look for people with whom you share common interests or who have 
3. https://darrenhardy.com/

---

## Page 256

234 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The New SE: Getting Started 235
held a position similar to yours. Most people are flattered if you ask them 
for advice (who does not like to give advice?) on improving your skills and 
advancing your career. Such questions show that you hold them in high 
regard. That doesn’t mean that you lock yourself into the SE organization. 
Some of my best mentors have come from the ranks of sales and marketing. 
Read the Manual
Really! Reading the manual is essential if you are dealing with your corpo-
rate engineering or marketing teams. They have developed training mate -
rials, marketing materials, and product documentation precisely to avoid 
dealing with a continuous flow of one-off questions. If you bother these 
people with questions already in the book, you will lose credibility, and 
they will lose patience. But, on the other hand, if you bring them customer 
questions, product feedback, and constructive ideas, you will have a ready 
and willing audience.
Understanding the content of all documentation (including video) is 
essential when dealing with highly technical customer contacts. There is 
always someone who wants to know what every option does and the effect 
of checking/unchecking every configuration box. They will ask you about 
some obscure setting or a flag mentioned in a release note. It also pays to be 
up to date on bug fixes, recent upgrades, and recent activity in user group 
forums.
Watch the Movies
A massive amount of training is available through on-demand (in the flow 
of work) video. In addition to standard corporate videos, you’ll also discover 
recordings of unique demos and presentations by the masters of the trade 
within your company. If your company utilizes a prepackaged video library, 
you should be very familiar with the contents of those demos. Subscribe to 
any regular weekly or monthly training videos or podcasts.
Join the Club
Every major city now has an SE club. This club offers an opportunity for 
SEs from all companies to gather and share their toys. They are an excellent 
networking event as well as a genuine learning opportunity. There is also a 
constant stream of offline/virtual program events that you can watch. It is a 
way to learn from the best and show how much you CARE. 
Master the Product
Balance your first six months between technical and professional skills. 
Once you master the basics of what your product does and how to demo or 
present it to the best effect, you need to speak about how other customers

---

## Page 257

236 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The New SE: Getting Started 237
put it to use. Don’t get so wrapped up in features that you forget about the 
outcomes and results obtained by your customer base. 
Work in the Factory
If you have the choice of working at headquarters instead of at home or 
in a remote office for a few months, take it! In addition to the networking 
advantages, you may be able to participate in some special projects in areas 
such as beta testing, demo development, or competitive analysis. These can 
build up your core skills and provide you with unique knowledge you can 
take back to the field. 
Practice, Practice, Practice
My German grandmother would always say, “Übung macht den meister,” 
which means practice makes the master. Become a “demo jockey” 4 and 
know how to use, present, and speak about your product under all circum-
stances. If you have a broad portfolio, then apply the 80/20 rule and focus 
on the critical 80% to be the expert. Also, practice for worst-case scenarios 
and know how to recover from crashes, connection losses, equipment fail -
ures, and the dozens of other disasters that might befall you. 
Know Y our Customer Stories
Until you have your own reference stories, apply the CARE principle, and 
use corporate and local references (see Chapter 16, “Storytelling”). Early in 
your career, customer stories can be vital in helping you understand your 
value proposition. In addition, learning about specific use cases and speak -
ing of the derived benefits enhances your technical and business credibility.
Use Y our Product (If  Y ou Can)
If you can use your product in your everyday work life, then do so. This 
technique primarily applies to application products that salespeople and pro-
fessionals use. You will then learn how your products work. Depending on 
the quality of your product, this is known as “eating your own dogfood” or 
the far more elegant “drinking your champagne.” For example, I gained no-
toriety at Oracle by building my own PC-based frequent flier mile tracking 
4. This has become a disparaging phrase. In certain circumstances there is nothing 
wrong with knowing and showing the minutiae about a product demo. It’s only 
wrong when you do that multiple times a day.

---

## Page 258

236 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The New SE: Getting Started 237
system. I then demonstrated SQL*Flier to many CIOs and line-of-business 
customers rather than showing a boring clinical trial adverse drug reaction 
system.
Get Feedback—from Everyone
Whenever someone has an opportunity to see you in action, ask for feed -
back. Customer feedback is precious. You can ask a customer if you met 
their needs, what they found most relevant, and if anything missed the 
mark or was superfluous. By engaging in this form of discussion, you may 
learn what you did and did not effectively communicate. Don’t feel bad if 
you get constructive feedback focusing on what went wrong rather than 
what went right. People tell you this because they want to help you im -
prove. If you like more structure in the feedback process, try using the T3-
B3-N3 approach (see Table 22.2). This structure works particularly well 
with salespeople. It may seem daunting asking for nine items of feedback, 
so rest assured that most people will struggle to get past four or five things.
Summary
You have much to accomplish, and your ramp time will fly past much faster 
than you may expect. Start with your goals, and then develop and refine 
the plan to achieve those goals. The ramp time is a bit of a honeymoon pe-
riod in that people will be more forgiving of mistakes. (They are expected!) 
Plus, you’ll have the opportunity to try and learn many new things. Focus 
on building both the skills and the relationships you will need to be success-
ful in the future. Share and constantly review your plan with your manager 
and your mentor, and don’t be afraid to ask for help.
Table 22.2 
The T3-B3-N3 Feedback Structure
T3 Top Three What are the top three things you saw (I did) 
that you liked?
B3 Bottom Three What were the bottom three things I should 
never do again (and why)?
N3 Next Three What are three things I should try next time 
I do this?

For the new SE: 
 1. Focus on the definition of success during and after onboarding.
 2. Beware of saying yes to too many projects and activities. Y ou have a plan!
 3. Find a mentor.
 4. Constantly review and reevaluate your plan.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Don’t forget the relationships. Meet as many people as possible.
 2. Think about your brand (see Chapter 23) and differentiation.
 3. Assist in the mentor -mentee selection process.
 4. Support your new hires. Don’t assume that silence is a positive.

Brand
All of us need to understand the importance of brand-
ing. We are all CEOs of our own companies. Me Inc.
               —Tom Peters
T
he wonderful thing about being in technical 
sales is that no day is ever the same. Cus -
tomers, and salespeople, always want something 
new and different. There are always new prob -
lems to solve and different ways to explain your 
technology. You have a great deal of flexibility 
to perform your job as you see fit, which means 
that you can inject much of your personality into 
the role. The constant challenges and changes 
can be exhilarating and cause an SE to become so 
wrapped up in the day-to-day tactical activities 
that they lose sight of the longer-term career ad-
vancement strategy and personal improvement. 
Branding sounds like a marketing function. 
Yet creating and improving your brand is an es -
sential part of being a well-respected and in-de -

having a personal brand, creating that brand, and 
some tips for growing that brand. I also discuss 
ethics and reasons why those personal ethics are 
foundational to your long-term success in sales.

## Page 261

Defining Personal Brand
Adam Ritchie, principal of Brand Direction, says, “The first word in personal 
brand is personal. Now add an ity to it, drop the brand, and that’s what it 
really means. Personality. That is the secret.” 1 My more functional defini -
tion of a personal SE brand is “What do you want people to say about you 
when you are not in the room?” Think of this as a short one or two-sentence 
phrase that accurately says what you do and believe. It is your unique value 
proposition (just like your products have a unique value proposition) and 
competitive advantage rolled into one statement. The best statements, much 
like a great demo, are short, memorable, interesting, and compelling! 
To construct your brand statement, start with a collection of adjectives, 
descriptors, and short phrases which serve as the foundation to fill out your 
brand. That brand is the first thing people will associate with you, often 
before meeting you. Although marketing purists will object, I am mingling 
the classic definitions of brand and reputation to create your personal value 
proposition for SE branding. 
Hint: Brand versus Reputation
If you are a marketing purist, then reputation is what a brand earns. The brand is how 
you implicitly and explicitly present yourself to the world. Reputation is the legitimacy of 
your brand and the sum total of your track record.
Building the Foundation
It is fair to say that sales engineers do not always have tremendous respect 
for marketing. It seems like too much of a “touchy-feely” profession with 
hard-to-measure outcomes.  So let us take the abstract concepts of brand, 
reputation, and value and turn them into something concrete and usable. 
Along the way, we’ll refer to a subset of a brand creation process that I went 
through some years ago to serve as an example. This process will get a little 
personal and may even drive up some of my trust factors. See Figure 23.1 
for reference.
Gather the collection of adjectives, phrases, and descriptors that you 
think apply to you. Write down your contributions and then ask for input 
from others. The more people you can ask, in different roles, the better. You 
may also want to ask your immediate family for their thoughts. Make sure 
that you are in active listening mode and accept all the input without judg -
1. “Brand Direction” with https://aritchbrand.com/.

---

## Page 262

240 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Creating Y our Personal Brand 241
ment. Only question responses when you don’t understand them, rather 
than if you disagree. It is free and precious feedback for you. 
This process yields a collection of fragments covering:
1. What you believe;
2. What other people say;
3. Dreams and plans;
4. The harsh reality and “needs improvement” items.
Then map these fragments using your favorite visual organization tool. 
For example, I use a mixture of good old-fashioned sticky notes and Jam -
board, a graphic/whiteboard program, to segment the fragments into five 
boxes. 
1. Core values. I list family, future, customer, and success as the key ele -
ments of my belief structure. To expand upon that, anyone who 
has met me for more than 30 minutes knows how proud I am of 
my family and that given the slightest opportunity, I will show a 
picture of at least one grandchild. I’m far more interested in look-
ing forward than examining the past, and I believe that customers 
contribute 100 percent of my revenues. Together with my family, 
that leads me to celebrate success. 
Figure 23.1 A sample brand template.

---

## Page 263

242 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Creating Y our Personal Brand 243
2. Driver/behaviors. These include being fair and consistent , works hard, 
and being creative. As background, my DISC2 behavioral profile is a 
“Yellow” or an “I.” That means I am an Influencer and get things 
done by persuading others. However, it also characterizes me as a 
mild extravert and not at all detail oriented.
3. Leadership qualities. As a VP, I wanted my team to believe that I had 
their back (I always publicly supported them), I believed in them, 
and I was genuinely interested in them and their careers. Everyone 
has leadership qualities, even if you do not currently have the title 
to go with them.
4. Personality traits. You will have an extensive list in this box. My own 
top three are likes to win (aka competitive); if you want help, you prob-
ably have to ask me; and an early-riser morning person.
5. Needs improvement. You should also have a long list in this box. You 
need to be aware of the pluses as well as the minuses of your brand. 
My examples are not very good at details; it can take some time to “know 
him”; and thinks he would be a good VP of sales (he wouldn’t!). 
You now have enough data points to start thinking about your brand 
statement. You should notice a lot of overlap between the descriptors and 
the top-scoring behaviors and actions in your trusted advisor scorecard. 
Creating the Statement
So far, we have been collecting data and gathering opinions. That is the 
discovery phase of branding. Now we have to propose a solution—which 
means building the statements. This area is where you will need to apply 
some creativity and harness the ideas of others. A quick internet search 
shows you that more than 75 percent of the personal brand statements be -
long to people in sales or marketing. Very little exists, except for boring job 
descriptions, for a sales engineer. You are breaking new ground!
Try writing some sentences that capture the spirit of your brand. You 
may need to link two or three of them together. Here are some generic ex -
amples—the specifics are up to you, as they are very personal.
2. For more, see https://discinsights.com/disc-theory. DISC was originally created 
over 100 years ago at Harvard University as a way to broadly characterize the 
personality traits of human behavior.

## Page 264

242 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Creating Y our Personal Brand 243
• “I help customers to enable and accelerate their digital transforma -
tion journey.”
• “I work with the sales teams to overachieve our business objectives.”
• “I bring technical magic to solving business problems.”
• “I help my customers apply the best of [my company name] to their 
organization.”
• “I get the technical win.”
• “My customers view me as their trusted advisor.”
• “I help my SMB customers become unicorns in a sea of donkeys.” 
The key is introducing a part of you—your personality—into your 
definition. 
CASE STUDY: T wo Family Sports Examples
Back in England, I played field hockey at my university. I was usually the least 
skilled player on the field, surrounded by various regional and national squad 
members from the United Kingdom, Ireland, and the Netherlands. It was often 
a humbling experience—so why was I on the team?  Well, my on-field brand 
was “I work hard, I encourage my teammates, and I score goals.” The first two 
parts of that brand were not that special, although I’d claim that I had to work 
harder than most to make up for my lack of skill. The third part, “I score goals,” 
was the critical differentiator and interested the rest of the team.  
 Contrast that with my son, Matt. He played Division One soccer at the col -
lege level in the United States for four years. When asked to describe Matt, his 
teammates would say, “He works hard, leads us by example, and makes us all 
better players.” My brand was transient, tactical, and results oriented. Matt’s 
brand will last a lifetime.
E E E E
Using and Living the Brand
For comparison purposes, note how the salespeople and your manager in -
troduce you to others, especially the customers. Although your title can be 
intriguing—my role varied from Sales Support Engineer III to Chief Techni-
cal Dude—the exciting part is how they describe what you do and why that 
is important to the customer.
As an immediate exercise, pause for a few minutes and write down a 
standard two- or three-sentence bio that salespeople can use to introduce

---

## Page 265

244 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Creating Y our Personal Brand 245
you. It is always better to have someone introduce you and say nice things 
about you rather than introduce yourself. Here is an example I overheard 
the day before writing this chapter. 
Hello, I’d like to introduce my more technical partner, Morgan. Their 
actual title is solution architect. Morgan brings ten years of industry 
experience and started as a [IT position] just like some of you. Morgan 
is now responsible for the digital cloud transformation of several of our 
largest accounts, and I know they are more than happy to discuss that 
with you. So over to you, Morgan.
Note how the salesperson laid the table for Morgan and enhanced the 
brand.
1. Morgan is their partner, with a technical orientation.
2. Morgan designs big solutions.
3. Morgan has ten years of experience and has walked in your shoes.
4. Morgan works with some large accounts, just like you.
Much as the trusted advisor concept encourages you to be CRISPy, the 
personal brand enables you to live the brand. I keep a visual of my current 
personal and corporate brand by my desk. Then, whenever I doubt a course 
of action—for example, I am tempted to write a brusque or sarcastic email, 
or I feel frustrated by a customer—I read the brand sheet, take a deep breath, 
and move on. Years ago, as a CIO in a high-pressure job, I nicknamed that a 
WAB moment—walk around building.
The final thought is around the focus. You cannot be everything to all 
people. An SE cannot be a subject matter expert in all the products within 
a portfolio or speak the language of multiple verticals. Especially as a new 
hire, you need to find a niche and center your brand around that niche, and 
then expand outward. You are better off starting as “The number one SE for 
the banking industry within the U.K.,” or “The best SE to speak with CIOs,” 
rather than aiming for “Delivers the best demo in the company.”
For the more experienced SE with 20-plus years under their belt, start 
thinking about your legacy and the shadow you cast. A personal brand is a 
lifelong project that constantly evolves and changes. How are you impact -
ing others, and are you improving the overall global profession of the sales 
engineer? Deep thoughts.

---

## Page 266

244 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Creating Y our Personal Brand 245
Honesty and Ethics
You may be surprised by the total lack of references to honesty and integrity 
within the branding or behavioral statements. That is because, to use the 
words of one of my former managers, it is like paying people to consume 
oxygen. When it comes to the customer, you are the technical conscience of 
the salesforce. It is your job to explain and demonstrate what will eventually 
be sold. There should never be any doubt, as your credibility as an engineer 
is at stake. Therefore, you must keep the highest level of integrity when 
dealing with salespeople, customers, partners, and, yes, even competitors. 
Working rule #1 of the SE is never lie. That lie can be a lie of commis -
sion, in that you intentionally say something incorrectly. Or that can be a 
lie of omission, in that you neglect to say something that you should have 
said. Once caught in this kind of situation, your credibility falls to zero, 
and the customer (and their organization) will never trust you again. Your 
peers and your manager will have doubts about you as well. No one would 
choose to deal with a dishonest professional.
What constitutes a lie? This usually occurs around product functional -
ity or availability.3 For example, you told the customer that a feature was 
available, then, later, you discovered that it was not. Or the product is being 
dropped. In this case, you should make a point of notifying the customer 
that you made an incorrect statement. Sometimes you obscure a negative by 
telling a customer about a feature, but that may depend on another action 
or product. Another common situation is when your sales partner makes 
a false technical statement. The moral question is to determine which of 
these situations are trivial and which are significant enough to impact your 
customer’s success. These situations are not uncommon. What is important 
is how you deal with them.
CASE STUDY: Saving the Salesperson
A story from one of our students: “We were presenting the technical details 
of our hardware. The senior manager in the room asked if we could support 
128 users on the box. The eager young salesperson verbally jumped in front 
of me and replied, ‘Yes, of course, we can.’ We couldn’t! The maximum limit 
was 64, so we needed two boxes. It was important. I responded, ‘Actually, we 
had some additional training on that just last night, and the maximum limit is 
now 64 users. You will need to run two boxes in parallel. That will give you 
some load balancing and failover options you wouldn’t get with a single box.’ 
3. Most organizations now expressly forbid SEs from speaking about futures and 
release dates. Slide decks will have all forms of legal disclaimers about forward-
looking statements. Do not sell futures.

---

## Page 267

246 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Creating Y our Personal Brand 247
I immediately corrected a misstatement and saved the salesperson and the deal, 
leaving everyone’s credibility intact. A win for everybody.”
E E E E
In general, if the situation will materially affect your customer’s time, 
people, or money, you should raise the issue. If the problem is an extra $50 
from an overall monthly $50,000 expenditure, it is not worth mentioning. 
On the other hand, if it will delay a go-live date by a few weeks or require 
more equipment or personnel, then your credibility is at stake. A good heu-
ristic is that if your company would be willing to pay for the difference in 
cost (without penalizing your revenue contribution), it may not be neces -
sary to raise the issue with the customer. In all cases, you should discuss the 
situation with your manager and the rest of the sales team. Your company, 
industry, culture, and position will likely have other unique ethical ques -
tions. Your customers also have rules. For example, some customers insist 
that you cannot even buy them a cup of coffee in the corporate cafeteria to 
avoid any sense of undue influence. For ethical concerns not related to your 
products, you should apply working rule #2 to treat the people you sell to today 
as if they are the people you will sell to tomorrow.  
For even broader concerns, consider working rule #3: Would you be com-
fortable if your friends and family were to read about this on the front page of the 
newspaper tomorrow?
Summary
Your brand affects your career, it impacts your happiness, and it influences 
those around you. 
Think about “What do I want people saying about me when I am not in 
the room?” That applies to your management chain, your sales team, your 
customers, and your partners. It is a powerful way to extend your capabili -
ties, reinforced by your words and actions. Couple that with a solid ethical 
foundation, and your customers will always hold you in high regard and 
consider you to be that trusted advisor. 
We’ll finish with the words of Goldie Chan, 4 a digital strategist and 
LinkedIn expert. “Creating the right personal brand will not only help you 
be known in your field and consistently land work, but it could be the dif -
ference between ‘Who are you?’ and ‘Thank you for being here’ in your 
career.” 
4. See https://www.goldiechan.com/. Absolutely worth the read.

---

## Page 268

246 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Creating Y our Personal Brand 247
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Create the initial descriptor list.
 2. Write down a brand statement (or several of them).
 3. Build up your strengths.
 4. Seek feedback (and listen to it).
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Fine-tune your brand. Times change.
 2. Look at potential differentiators 12–18 months from now .
 3. Revisit your apparent strengths. We all get lazy .
 4. Think legacy . How are you improving the lives of other SEs?
For all:
 1. Live your brand. Every day .
 2. Follow the three working rules.

              —Helen Keller
P
artners are one of the most influential and 
force-multiplying tools in the SE arsenal. 
When you are looking to advance an opportu -
nity through a sales cycle, what could be better 
than having someone else take that responsibil -
ity and do it on your behalf? It is a classic ROI 
example. Some short-term investment on your 
part yields an ever-growing longer-term gain. 
This chapter covers a high-level view of the dif -
ferent forms of partnership you might encounter 
and provides an overview of typical relationship 
and infrastructure issues. It then finishes with a 
simple enablement strategy to make your part -
ner investment even more worthwhile.
The Different Forms of Partnership
A sales partnership is loosely defined as a func -
tional relationship between two or more corpo -
rate entities working toward a common good. 
The common good is a happy customer and a 
source of revenue. There are many different 
forms of partnership. These range from a third 
party who occasionally resells your product or 
Discuss the different forms 
of partnership.
Learn how to work 
the relationship and 
infrastructure.
Outline a partner 
enablement strategy.
