## Page 271

250 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Selling with and Through Partners 251
introduces you into an account to a 100% partner-led internal organization 
(you may hear the term indirect) staffed by partner engineers rather than 
classic sales engineers. Regardless of the internal and external business ar -
rangements, the burden still mainly falls upon the internal technical team to 
enable and support the partners. 
The partnership is also more than just working the opportunity together. 
A third party may resell your products directly, build their product or service 
on top of yours, or only recommend your products to prospects. There is no 
question that partners supply additional leverage to your salesforce. Instead 
of just having your salesforce sell, you now benefit from having another, po-
tentially larger organization also selling on your behalf. The potential down-
side is another stakeholder at the table, and decisions, strategy, and even 
revenue allocation become more complicated.
For the SE, having a partner engaged in your opportunity opens a world 
of possibilities. For example, you now have access to demonstration centers 
(many of the larger third parties have better centers of excellence than the 
average vendor), hardware, software, and other people/resources that were 
previously unattainable. 
At the top end of the scale is a technology vendor which is entirely 
partner focused, as 100% of opportunities are allocated to the partner and 
driven through partners. The field team rarely engages directly with the end 
customer, as the external partners are the sole channel into the ultimate 
customer. In this environment, opportunities are sourced in multiple ways. 
The partner may be incented to generate and pursue their opportunities, 
registering them with your company, or else every opportunity is distributed 
outward to the partners. Many smaller companies start with this strategy 
and then slowly move their top 10% strategic accounts into a direct model 
to assert better control over their key customers. Other companies stay faith-
ful to the 100% partner-led philosophy, and both structures have proved 
successful.
The next step down the scale is the hybrid strategy. There are complete 
field sales and SE teams focused on all the accounts. There is a parallel set 
of partners who are also actively selling into those accounts. The business 
interface between the partners and host vendor is at the business manager 
or development manager level. Their job is to strategize and coordinate joint 
resources to pursue the opportunities. Typically, there is only a small 1 part-
ner SE organization tasked with marketing and support functions. 
At the lowest end of the scale, with the smaller companies, is the ad hoc 
partner strategy. There is no formal sales arrangement (although a business/
commission type agreement is in place), and the initial opportunity pursuits 
1. Small meaning less than 5% of total SE headcount.

---

## Page 272

250 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Selling with and Through Partners 251
are “make it up as we go along.” However, once the volume of partner op-
portunities becomes large enough, this arrangement quickly morphs into 
something formal, at least for the top-producing partners. And thus a part-
ner team is born. 
Hint: Follow the Money
Sales VPs will tell you that “Compensation drives behavior,” and nowhere is that more 
relevant than in partner dynamics. Who gets paid for what will drive partner engage -
ment far more than any organizational chart. This may sound a little cynical, yet except 
in 100% indirect-driven organizations, there is usually a gap between how partner en -
gagement should work and how it actually does. 
There are many intervening organizational structures, and every com -
pany has different dynamics and priorities. Like snowflakes, no two partner 
organizations are identical. 
Working the Relationship and the Infrastructure
Every SE needs to interact with partners, even if that is not a specific part 
of your stated roles and responsibilities. That interaction may be direct in 
that you take part in sales calls with the partner. On the other hand, that 
interaction may be indirect and behind the scenes as you help the partner 
prepare for a call, or it may be support oriented in general enablement and 
training. In any situation, you are working with the partner and preparing 
them to meet the customer and conduct business on your behalf. They are, 
in effect, a proxy SE.
Defining Account Ownership
Salespeople spend considerable time debating who “owns” an account. 
Ownership is determined by who sets the overall strategy, leads the sale, 
and ultimately has the final say on account decisions. Ownership is tradi -
tionally decided by who brings whom into the deal. For example, if you 
are working on an opportunity and determine your cause would be better 
served by engaging a partner with some unique expertise, you are bringing 
them into the deal. When a large systems integrator (SI) is bidding on a deal 
and asks you to participate, they bring you into the deal. 
I always liked the softer concept of being the “prime” on an account 
rather than an owner. If you are the prime SE, you set the schedule, dis -
tribute tasks, and ask for more help and resources from your partners. Do 
not be afraid to ask for help or advice from the partner—it is why you are 
working together. Note that you may spend a sizable amount of time coor-

---

## Page 273

252 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Selling with and Through Partners 253
dinating people and schedules for substantial transactions instead of selling. 
You may run the risk of becoming a glorified project manager. 
You should also set expectations with the customer. The customer will 
expect you to be the principal technical face of the account team and their 
primary nonsales communication point. That is your decision to make, and 
you can delegate that communications point–person position. Running 
these opportunities gives you tremendous leverage, and you can stockpile 
favors for the future. Although the marketing and business development 
teams may release a stream of press releases and blog posts announcing 
cooperative agreements, it is at the field level that this PR turns into reality. 
No amount of marketing can build up the massive goodwill generated by a 
successful win-win transaction.
Particularly in smaller companies, a disparity may exist between part -
ners the salesperson chooses for an opportunity compared to your selection. 
Given the freedom to make the decision, a rep may select a partner based on 
account access (the partner can make an executive introduction), while the 
SE selects partners based upon technical and business fit. When you choose, 
jointly make the partner selection at the start of the sales cycle to maximize 
the possibility of the win. That takes precedence over everything else. 
Hint: There Are Partners Within Partners
When working with a systems integrator or a big-name consultancy firm, pay careful 
attention to their power structure. Make sure you are dealing with the correct internal 
partner/managing director. Focus on the senior managers and managing partners onsite 
who are dealing with the customer and less on corporate relationships. 
You may sometimes have a partner thrust upon you because of politics. 
Your VP meets their VP, and they decide to partner on the next opportunity 
as a sign of good faith and “partnership”—no matter what. This happens 
more often than you would expect and is a fait accompli.2 In this case, make 
the most of the opportunity—at least you can count on some executive sup-
port for the deal!
Rules of Engagement 
The primary and moral rule of engagement (ROE) is to be faithful to your 
partner. Once the partnership is declared (and the customer sees it in ac -
tion), you should not work with other competitors or even independently. 
A colleague once described the process as the equivalent of a marriage, ex -
cept that sometimes the worse comes before the better. Practically speaking, 
the process holds until the customer picks one partner but not the other. 
2. Meaning a done deal. So deal with it.

---

## Page 274

252 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Selling with and Through Partners 253
Pivoting back to the trusted advisor concept, you lose all trust when you 
abandon a partner, as all five factors of credibility, reliability, intimacy, self-
orientation, and positivity take a long-term hit. Word will spread to the 
detriment of your future sales career. You will see an impact even if you are 
only part of a team that makes this decision and not the leader. 
For the classic field SE, you need to balance the needs of the local part-
ners against the direct sales opportunities you are actively pursuing. My 
recommendation is that unless one partner stands out from others, you 
should be opportunistic. Look at your target market, discuss likely partners 
with your sales counterpart, and then proactively engage with them before 
any opportunities arise. Remember that, especially for consulting compa -
nies, their focus is billable hours, so you may need to build the relationship 
through breakfasts, lunches, and dinners.3
You should also agree upon the level of support and response time with 
the partner. You have a full calendar and will be out of the office for long 
periods and balancing other opportunities or partners. It is unreasonable to 
expect your response time to be equivalent to a technical support hotline, 
so it is vital to set the basic rules upfront. You will notice other partners do-
ing the same with you—it is an accepted business protocol. The ROE should 
also cover what services you can supply to the partner. For example, some 
partners may expect you to consult on a proposed design; others may ex -
pect you to create an entire solution design.
From a managerial aspect, the requirements of partner management 
superimposed upon the daily schedule can be overwhelming for a small SE 
team. It is hard for a field SE to see the benefits of a partner engagement 
when faced with their own sales team’s monthly or quarterly revenue pres-
sures. The SE manager needs to plan, together with the sales manager, the 
quarterly (and next quarter) partner approach ahead of time. Shutting out 
partners until you need them will restrict long-term growth and limit the 
sales pipeline even in the short term. On the other hand, too heavy a part-
ner focus can starve current deals of SE time and damage quota attainment. 
For the young organization, it is a delicate balancing act. The middle ground 
is hard to find, and you will often swing from one extreme to the other.
CASE STUDY: Politics and Partners
Within larger organizations, the politics around the channel can result in mas -
sive strategy shifts every 12–18 months, usually dependent upon the whims 
of senior executives and the input of analysts and VCs. For example, I saw a 
partner-friendly organization that did 40% of revenue through the channel 
3. Food and drink are the universal catalysts of all trusted relationships.

## Page 275

254 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Selling with and Through Partners 255
swing wildly to a negative partner mindset almost overnight when moving from 
one fiscal year to another. The new chief revenue officer created vertical sales 
teams to work on each deal directly and cut out the partners to increase revenue 
and margin. The result was catastrophic as the partners switched to a direct com-
petitor. The 19% revenue gain from the verticals was more than offset by a 30% 
drop in every other company area. The entire partner support team resigned 
over the next quarter, and the partner infrastructure disappeared. Six months 
later, the board terminated the CRO and reversed course. Unfortunately, it was 
almost too late. It took 2 years to rebuild the people and the trust. 
E E E E
Categorizing the Partners
A common approach, whether or not you have a partner organization, is to 
tier your partners. The classic categories are gold, silver, and bronze, with a 
sprinkling of titanium on top. Gold represents your premier partners, who 
receive the bulk of available time, people, and money and have priority 
over all others. Silver partners qualify for quarterly updates, invites to local 
events and user groups, and support for deals over a specific size or with a 
predetermined set of accounts. Bronze partners get standard offline training 
and as-needed deal support. In addition, companies have added heavy metal 
super levels such as titanium and platinum to reward partners based upon 
size, revenue, or marketing contributions. 
Part of your compensation structure, or that of your sales partner, may 
come from upselling partners to move from silver to gold. It is worth under-
standing the differences in marketing development funds (MDFs), discount-
ed event admission, certifications, directory listings, and so on between the 
levels. Think of them as the equivalent of features in a product set! 
You may not be able to create such tiers formally, but your local SE team 
can certainly mentally categorize your partners in this manner. Remember 
that everything is local, so a high-level partner based on a U.S. headquarters 
category may not be a prominent player in Spain or Portugal. A local partner 
may instead be the gold equivalent. 
In the long term, almost every direct sales organization sets up a parallel 
partner relations organization. The organization initially starts with business 
development roles and marketing/events coordinators. Then, specialized 
partner SEs are added to the mix as resource requirements climb and the 
field SE managers complain.

---

## Page 276

254 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Selling with and Through Partners 255
Partner Enablement
The apparent downside of selling with partners is that they must be trained, 
supported, and updated regularly. That is a time-consuming activity. Ma -
ture sales organizations have dedicated partner groups and specialized SEs 
to work exclusively with external partners. There is no such group within 
smaller organizations or only the business development managers with 
minimal technical ability. This structure places more stress on the field SEs 
because they become responsible for onboarding and upskilling the part -
ner’s SEs. 
Every year vendors allocate large sums of money to enable their part -
ners. This money is spent internally on infrastructure, collateral, and staff 
and externally on events, promotions, compensation, and even more col -
lateral. Unfortunately, most partner enablement strategies are built upon 
either one-size-fits-all or an on-the-fly approach. Neither of which are par-
ticularly effective for the SE organization. 
Partners bring scaling, and they provide massive leverage for your sales 
efforts. Whether you have three or 3,000 partners, the key is to develop 
a repeatable enablement process for their technical sales team. In a small 
company, you may need to build your system starting from ground zero, 
while in a large company, you can repurpose the assets already available. 
Rather than just bundling materials, create a plan for building the most ef -
fective partner technical team by understanding what they need to learn 
and how to achieve that goal. Put yourself in their place and ask, “If I were 
an SE at this partner of my company, what would I need to know and what 
tasks should I be able to perform that (1) makes me the most effective at 
selling the product and (2) minimized my dependency on others?” This is 
the first step to building a partner certification program. You are effectively 
onboarding a new SE, except they work for a partner rather than for your 
company.
Reverting to the heavy metal philosophy, you can use similar categories 
to measure the depth of knowledge you expect of the external partner SEs. 
Train and collaborate with the partners based on what you expect them 
to accomplish, not based on the available materials. Table 24.1 shows an 
outline of a technical categorization. Once you sign up a new partner, they 
likely have minimal knowledge of your product, positioning, and value 
proposition. Handing over a collection of website links, manuals, and mar -
keting glossies is good reference material but serves no immediate training 
purpose. For those first meetings with a partner, in the absence of any for -
mal onboarding program, the SE should supply the following:
• A personalized one-page product summary speech the partner can 
learn and personalize;

---

## Page 277

256 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Selling with and Through Partners 257
• A pain sheet of common business problems you can solve;
• High-level technical requirements;
• A “who do I call?” list (internal contacts);
• A summary of likely competitors;
• A sample six- to eight-minute whiteboard pitch;
• Access to prerecorded training and demo videos.
The current trend is to provide a series of on-demand and interactive 
video training sessions. Some hardware companies have amazing virtual 
reality setups to enable a holographic walkaround of their equipment and 
sample data center racks. Written materials follow this, an online lab, and 
then a final examination. Passing these tests results in a base-level certified 
professional award that guarantees the technical skills level of the partner 
and creates an ecosystem around your product. Plus, we SEs love to get ad-
ditional qualifications and certifications!
The Special Role of the Partner Engineer
The role of the partner SE is unique within the SE community. Your target 
customers are both the external partner(s) and internal SE and sales teams 
persuaded to work with those partners. Your ultimate goal is to direct as 
much business as reasonably possible through your partner. You are selling 
your partner internally and externally in conjunction with a partner man -
ager (the equivalent of a salesperson). It is a delicate balance of time and pri-

oritization, and you may spend as much time working inside your company 
as you do outside of it. 
Typical responsibilities will include:
• Work with partner managers to find and recruit partners in the mar-
ket to deliver innovative solutions to the customer.
• Develop the partners’ technical teams with both sales and technical 
competencies to be successful in the market.
• Keep partners up to date with product and technical knowledge and 
market differentiators.
• Advise on integration strategies, enterprise architectures, platforms, 
and application infrastructure needed to implement a complete solu-
tion successfully.
• Work with partner technical representatives in opportunities and 
projects, complementing their skill and guaranteeing high-quality 
customer deliverables.
• Act as a technical advocate for partners to help provide feedback 
from the field and present on their behalf.
• Up-level partners to commit more of their efforts toward your prod-
uct set (such as a move from bronze to silver level). 
Hint: Becoming the Trusted Advisor Partner SE 
Stay in touch with your partner contacts, even when there is no business currently on the 
table. Only engaging with partners when you need something (or they doing the same to 
you) shows a high self-orientation and low intimacy (see Chapter 19). You want to be the 
first person they think about when there is a lead to follow. The more positive things that 
happen when you are not in the room, the stronger the relationship. 
CASE STUDY: The Multiplier Effect
At MTS, we have our own partnership stories to share. For example, a mid-
sized technology company (500 salespeople, 250 SEs) was looking to imple -
ment a sales methodology and associated professional skills training for their 
entire sales team. The incumbent vendor submitted a proposal to (re)train all 
750 people. Their competitor, looking for some differentiation, contacted us 
and asked that we partner on a joint bid to deliver specialized role-based train-
ing wrapped around their proprietary sales process.
 We won the business together through an approach centered on using visual 
selling and a “why repeat the mistakes of the past 5 years?” message. The subse-
quent client debriefs revealed that the number one reason they selected us was 
that we were a “shining example of sales and presales working together”—which

---

## Page 279

258 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
is precisely what the client wanted our two companies to teach them. Neither 
company would have won this business had we operated independently, yet we 
acquired and now retain a loyal client. Since that initial success, the two compa-
nies have partnered on a half-dozen other joint proposals.
 It was also a humbling experience in that despite all our careful planning 
and strategy, the customer bought from us for such an obvious reason that we 
overlooked it!
E E E E
Summary
Used appropriately, partners can apply tremendous leverage to your sales 
activities. The initial investment of time and resources to onboard the SEs 
from your partners will be repaid in the long term as those partners bring 
in more business for you. Utilize all the resources available from the part -
ner community, such as centers of excellence, marketing events, executive 
access, and their general technical prowess. Establish contacts within both 
your partners’ technical and business ranks just as you would for a custom-
er. Develop a personal plan for interacting with the partner SEs, rather than 
relying on ad hoc processes. 
Remember that your company pays multiple people to channel business 
through their partners. So if you are a true partner SE, that group includes 
you! 
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Assemble the critical components for partner education. Repurpose onboarding.
 2. Develop your personal process and strategy for partner interactions.
 3. Cultivate both technical and business contacts within partners.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Adopt several key partners and become their go-to field person.
 2. Constantly repurpose internal materials to reduce partner dependencies on you and 
    your peers.
 3. Is it time to create a specialized partner SE team?
 4. Seek out speaking and promotional opportunities at partner events.
For the partner sales engineer:
 1. Spend time with your partner manager.
 2. Participate in joint sales calls to start up the partner relationship and judge skill gaps.
 3. Educate the field SE and sales teams on the capabilities of your partners.

Chapter Goals Competitive Tactics
Never interrupt your enemy when he is making a 
mistake.
         —Napoleon Bonaparte
E
very sales opportunity involves an element 
of competition. It may be the customer tak -
ing a token glance at your competitor to check 
off some purchasing compliance box, or it may 
be a vicious in-the-trenches fight for the busi -
ness. Frequently you are fighting one of your 
closest competitors for the right to convert a 
prospect into a paying customer. Throughout this 
book, I have emphasized the importance of sell -
ing a solution and positioning the resultant value 
to executives as high up the corporate hierarchy 
as you can reach. That is the perfect academic 
world. Just as often, the competitive environ -
ment resembles hand-to-hand combat, despite 
the merits of technology, ROI, and your under -
standing of the business problems. It is you and 
your sales partner against “them.” More often, 
the team with the best plan has the advantage, 
precisely the environment this chapter discusses. 
The Competitive Landscape
To develop anything more than a generic com -
petitive strategy, you need to know who else is 
Be able to identify, 
neutralize, and then defeat 
your competition.
Understand the five main 
competitive strategies.
Handle the competitive 
customer.

## Page 281

260 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 261
engaged in the opportunity. Even though you are promoting your strengths 
and nullifying your weaknesses throughout the sales cycle, you should do 
that with a potential competitor in mind. You need to have a target for your 
competitive weapons. 
Y our Number One Competitor 
One exercise we run during our business value discovery workshops is gath-
ering and listing a set of competitors. We usually end up with a list of three 
to five names on the board. Then we ask the students to think of all the deals 
they actively supported over the past year—and what eventually happened. 
The outcome is that with most technology companies, there is one other 
competitor responsible for 30%–50% of all lost deals. It is that little-known 
company we discussed earlier called Do Nothing, Inc. (DNI). You do not win 
the sale, nor do any of your classic competitors. Instead, the deal disappears 
or is deferred because the budget is directed elsewhere. The customer spends 
no money on the identified problem, and no one wins. Every sales review I 
have participated in over the past 25 years in various industries and market 
segments supports these numbers. 1 Take a few seconds to reflect on that 
number. Two out of every five deals that enter the top of the pipeline with 
some basic qualification never appear at the bottom end of the pipeline. 
So, the SE needs to start thinking about the concept of risk as perceived 
by the customer. Figure 25.1 illustrates that when the customer views the 
risk of making a change (R1) as more significant than the risk of doing noth-
ing (R0) and maintaining the status quo, then inertia (and DNI) wins (so R1 
> R0). Thus, a new dimension to your SE competitive strategy is to create a 
positive risk gap between DNI and buying from you (so R0 > R2). The wider 
the gap, the more powerful the impetus to buy. Ideally, you should increase 
the risk of going with DNI (as everyone benefits from that) and lower the 
risk of proceeding with your solution shown as moving from R1 to R2. 
Practically, you need to adopt a strategy to defeat DNI, just as you would 
one of your more classic, named competitors. It is not your role to scare your 
customer, but you can certainly do your best to make them fully aware of 
what might happen if they take no action. “What happens if you do noth -
ing?” followed by a long pause is a great discovery question. 
Identify Y our Competition
Although a significant proportion of the sales cycle is consumed with devel-
oping and proposing your solution, you must balance that against the com-
1. Challenger, Inc., reports a number of 38% of B2B purchase attempts end in no 
decision. See https://www.challengerinc.com.

---

## Page 282

260 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 261
petitive environment. In the early stages, you may be competing against 
several competitors—repeatedly. Add in a dozen smaller point solution 
competitors you meet only once or twice a year, and the landscape becomes 
crowded. Just as no professional sports team would ever play a match with-
out scouting the opposition, a competent sales organization should do the 
same. So, of course, step one is to figure out exactly who your competition 
is in any opportunity.
The most straightforward approach is usually the best, which means 
asking a direct question such as “Who else are you looking at for this proj-
ect?” I’d recommend that the sales team divide and conquer on this ques-
tion and that the salesperson asks their primary contacts, and you ask the 
question of your contacts. Pose this question when in a small group or a 1:1 
meeting rather than in front of a large audience. You are likely to have a 
better chance of a response rather than people feeling self-conscious about 
revealing information in front of other employees. The higher your trust 
score (Chapter 19), the better your chances. 
Hint: So How Does It Make You Feel?
Some customers are very reluctant to reveal anything about the competitive environment 
as they fear it may somehow give you leverage in the sales cycle, which may be true! When 
dealing with sales or marketing customers, you can play the guilt card and ask them 
how they feel when their customers won’t tell them anything about potential competitors. 
Then be quiet and see what happens. 
Figure 25.1 The impact of risk and Do Nothing, Inc.

## Page 283

262 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 263
Sometimes the customer is unwilling to share anything about the com-
petitive environment, particularly if a third party (like an SI or management 
consulting organization) is involved. It is then time to get creative and em -
ploy other forensic2 options. 
• Check the cc: list on outgoing customer emails.
• Review the permissions on any shared drives.
• Glance at the visitor’s log when onsite.
• Watch for literature, samples, or manuals from your competition on 
a desk.
• Review the document property tags for any email attachments.
• Request the information from any third-party consultants onsite.
• Probe for the information with other customers from the same com-
pany. 
• And above all, keep asking! 
Sometimes, despite best efforts, you cannot uncover who you are com -
peting against in the opportunity. It happens. Then pay special attention to 
the types of questions asked, functions requested in a demonstration, bench-
mark, standard requirements, and so on. There is always, eventually, a clue. 
Know Thine Enemy
Do you know the names of the competitor’s sales representatives who cover 
your territory and the names of your technical counterparts? Have you stud-
ied their sales strategies and looked for telltale patterns? Humans are crea -
tures of habit, and everyone has patterns they fall into when under stress. 
It is also human nature to continue down a winning path. If you have won 
five deals in a row with the same strategy, it is unlikely that you will pro -
actively deviate from that strategy for deal number six. That laziness means 
that your competitor will adapt.  
CASE STUDY: My Strategy Works, Until It Doesn’t
During the rise of the dot.com boom (1999–2001), I worked for Clarify, a cus -
tomer relationship management (CRM) company. Our number one competitor 
was Siebel Systems, the proverbial and literal 800-pound gorilla in the market. 
We had a new sales force automation (SFA) module that was, at best, distinctly 
2. Don’t do anything that strays into questionable legal or ethical areas. For example, 
you cannot offer a quid pro quo or bribe/payment for any kind of information.

---

## Page 284

262 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 263
average. The Siebel competitive intelligence team quickly realized that our ca -
pability to sync data from a salesperson’s laptop into the central corporate da -
tabase (this was 2000!) was unreliable. They made syncing data part of every 
demo and insisted that reliability and data integrity were major requirements 
for any SFA system. It was a masterstroke and cost us seven large deals within 
three weeks. We quickly formed an engineering SWAT team, fixed some UI 
bugs, and adapted. We started our next major demo by collecting business cards 
from everyone in the room. Even before the Siebel fans could mention data 
syncing, the salesperson entered their contact data and uploaded it to the cen -
tral system. Attack neutralized. We then went on the offense by giving every 
SE a poster-sized (2m × 3m) copy of our data model. We proudly showed this 
poster at every demo (of course, Siebel had a proprietary model and would not 
share it, but we never said that). It was enough to keep us in the game, and 
Nortel Networks eventually bought us for over $5 billion.3
E E E E
Researching the people as well as the product will tell you:
• What is their preferred competitive approach?
• Do they sell at the technical, business, or executive level?
• Are there success/reference stories they commonly use?
• How do they adapt when they compete against you?
• How do they find a coach, and how do they use that person?
Five Competitive Strategies for the SE 
A competitive strategy needs to be both focused and consistent. This means 
that you align your technical strategy with the overall sales strategy. Al -
though there are many types and variants of strategy, you can broadly place 
them into five different categories, each of which has implications for the 
SE team. Each approach will work some of the time. Unfortunately, none 
of the strategies are a panacea and work every time. Each customer is dif -
ferent, and your competitors constantly adapt.
3. The original Nortel offer was $2.1 billion in late 1999. By the time the deal closed 
it was worth $5 billion. Two years later, Nortel sold Clarify to Amdocs for $200 
million. By 2002, Nortel’s stock price had plunged from C$124 to C$0.47. I keep 
a share certificate for a single Nortel share in my downstairs bathroom as a re -
minder that all things are transient.

---

## Page 285

264 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 265
The Frontal Strategy 
The frontal strategy is precisely what you expect. It is a complete head-on 
attack4—on both the competitor and often the customer. This approach re -
quires you to have a crystal-clear advantage over your competitor in the 
areas of product, company, and trust. It also tends to be very resource in -
tensive, as you are constantly in front of the customer to press your advan -
tage. Salespeople love the frontal approach, as it is both the most direct and 
has the attraction of being a one-on-one fight against your competitor. It is 
just the two of you and let the best team win! It is commonly employed by 
extremely confident salespeople trying to break into an account or a new 
territory.
For the SE, this means that your product must have clear and compel -
ling business and technical advantages. There can be no doubt about those 
advantages, and they should be large enough that there can be no natural 
defense against them. You will often have to conduct multiple presentations, 
demonstrations, and possibly a POC to drive these advantages home. In ad-
dition, your constant presence in front of the customer yields the opportu -
nity to build trust and sell your company. 
CASE STUDY: A Regional Frontal Strategy
An example from one of our clients: “We had one main competitor, whose prod-
uct was realistically about as good as ours. Our significant advantage was that 
we were five times the size and had five times the SEs plus many more partners. 
We decided to adopt a theater-wide frontal strategy and force our competitor to 
engage in each account. We pushed customized demonstrations and complex 
POCs, knowing that they could not compete in every opportunity and would 
have to pick and choose. Over the next few months, about half of our deals 
were unopposed, and they were forced to fly in more SE heads from all over the 
world. They quickly adapted by preemptively using a flanking or fragmenting 
response, yet we were able to run up the score5 and demoralize our sales coun-
terparts for a short time.”
E E E E
Apart from the time requirements, the downside is that it is difficult to 
change strategy part way through the sales campaign. For example, sup -
pose your product superiority is not acknowledged by the customer (pos -
sibly because your competitor has adopted another competitive strategy in 
4. Known as “full speed ahead and damn the torpedoes.” Attributed to David Far -
ragut, a U.S. Union Naval Officer during the Civil War. His attack was successful, 
and he was promoted to vice admiral and later to a full admiral.
5. Continuing to score points or goals even after the outcome is no longer in doubt.

---

## Page 286

264 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 265
response). In that case, you are eliminating yourself from the deal instead 
of the competitor eliminating you. You can only afford to lose a few frontal 
opportunities before you lose the trust of your customers, the marketplace, 
and even the salespeople. 
The Flanking Strategy
A flanking strategy is a subtle approach that shifts the focus of the buying 
criteria to new and different issues that favor your portfolio. That shift in -
cludes other success criteria for the current set of products in play or adding 
new criteria that require additional products. You take a different approach 
than your competitor may expect and use their momentum as a defense 
in the case of a frontal attack. To execute this strategy, you need someone 
within the customer organization—a coach (see Chapter 9)—willing to in -
fluence the criteria. 
The perfect situation for an SE to execute a flanking strategy is during 
discovery. In Chapter 6, we looked at pain, gain, and priorities. One of the 
steps was to ask permission to add your own issues. You may recall:
“Many of my customers [who are in this same vertical/who are the 
same size as you/who are facing these kinds of business issues] are also 
looking to [solve a problem that you can help them with]. Is that something 
that might be of interest to you?”
The goal is to introduce the customer to a broader set of issues than just 
the immediate need to purchase a specific product or service that may al -
leviate the current pain. For example, suppose you are working for a larger 
company competing against a smaller one. In that case, you are likely to 
introduce an additional product or set of products to broaden the project 
scope. Conversely, an SE for a smaller company may flank by using simplic-
ity, responsiveness, speed of install, or faster time to value.
Hint: The Size of the Bus
Some huge enterprise companies are famous for sending many people to a sales call. There 
may be five or six salespeople and multiple SEs, partner reps, and overlay personnel. The 
joke is that they need a bus to hold everyone. A small company may only send a salesper-
son and an SE. You may feel outnumbered. However, their product is so complicated that 
it takes a dozen people to answer even the basic set of questions! Simplicity now becomes 
a criterion. 
The critical aspect of this strategy is that someone within the sales team 
needs to be trusted enough to execute the flank. Unless there is some long-
term preexisting relationship between the salesperson and the account, that 
someone is the SE. Be prepared.

---

## Page 287

266 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 267
The Fragment Strategy
A fragment strategy subdivides the primary opportunity into smaller pieces 
and focuses the different customer personnel on those strengths you can 
directly address. It is also known as a divide and conquer approach. You effec-
tively run parallel sales efforts with different customer teams and minimize 
the communication and interplay between those teams. This approach relies 
on the sales team finding unique features, advantages, and benefits within 
a specific part of the product and then selling the interested customers on 
those benefits. It requires advanced targeting and much forethought.
Fragmenting has come to the fore in the XaaS realm. It is effective at get-
ting your first deal with a small department or division. The barrier to entry 
and the cost of failure is so low that the customer is more prepared to take 
risks because you solve their specific problems. Once that customer is happy 
with you, then you can expand your footprint. It is a great way to break into 
a competitor’s reference account. 
Note there is still considerable investment upfront in the discovery 
phase, as you are seeking out the unique features of your product, compared 
to the competition, that will make a material difference for only a subset of 
the customer personnel. 
Hint: Beware the Committee
The fragment strategy is challenging to execute if the customer has a formal evaluation 
committee. You run the risk of being accused of “going behind my back” by intentionally 
selling different features to different departments or individuals. It is nice to have a coach 
or executive sponsor to guide you through that process. 
The Defend Strategy
Use a defend strategy within your current install base. It will protect you 
from the assaults of your competition. This approach relies on a trusted advi-
sor position between the sales team and one or more customer executives. 
It is not a last-minute strategy but a deliberate long-term approach and not 
one you can use within your entire base (as you don’t have the capacity). It 
involves the constant reinforcement of your benefits and value with those 
executives and their subordinates.
Many companies are forced into a defensive strategy to maintain their 
customer base in an incremental month-over-month revenue consumption 
model. This is a primary impetus of the rise of the customer success man -
ager, whose sole mission is supporting the happiness and resource onboard-
ing of a client. Whenever a competitor tries to gain a share of wallet, by

---

## Page 288

266 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 267
whatever strategy (including a frontal price attack with violent discount -
ing), you have a well-defined set of successes and internal champions to 
blunt the attack. 
This strategy requires the SE to have both a technical and a marketing 
focus, as it is necessary to promote the outcomes and positive results of 
your technology. Note that you are promoting the customer’s achievements 
rather than your own. (That is low self-orientation behavior in our trusted 
advisor terminology.) 
The Develop Strategy
The develop strategy is a catch-all for “what happens if nothing is happen-
ing?” It is designed to set you up for potential future opportunities. It is the 
strategy to employ when the sales team is convinced that there is real rev -
enue potential within the account but that it will not happen immediately. 
Develop is a slow process used to build up the credibility of you and your 
product set over months or even a year. It means that you are investing for 
the future, which not every sales organization is willing to do. 
Hint: Maintain a Constant Contact Customer Cadence 
It is much easier to restart an opportunity with a potential customer if you have regular 
contact with them, rather than just calling when they are ready to buy. A sizeable propor-
tion of our MTS business comes from “develop” customers who have been left to simmer 
for 12–18 months with occasional contact rather than being continuously pestered by a 
salesperson. 
Each strategy has a unique blend of SE time, planning, and effort. Fron-
tal usually requires the most engineering and commitment of your time in 
a short period against a defined set of criteria. It is a take no prisoners ap -
proach, and once you commit to it (having agreed that your product is the 
best and that you can prove it), there is no walking that statement back. If 
you are uncomfortable with the strategy, you need to speak up. Flanking 
and fragment are more nuanced and require equal parts sales and engineer-
ing. I always enjoyed winning with those two strategies rather than a fron-
tal strategy, but that may be a personality trait. Defend and develop I con -
sider as necessary approaches to keep your current customers and incubate 
your future customers. 
Hint: Read the Case Study and Apply the Learning 
Go back a few pages and review the “My Strategy Works Until It Doesn’t” case study. 
What were the competitive strategies employed by Siebel and Clarify throughout the 
engagement?

---

## Page 289

268 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 269
The Customer and the Competition
Often in the execution of the competitive strategy, the customer will directly 
ask both the SE and the salesperson why your solution is better than anyone 
else’s. Sometimes the customer is genuinely curious, and sometimes it is a 
negotiating/procurement tactic. The prime directive is to stay positive, con -
centrate on your strengths, and use customer-focused language. 
• The customer asks: “Why should I buy from you? Why are you better 
than [Company/Product X]?“
• You should respond with: “I am not an expert on [Product X]. How-
ever, I can share with you the top three reasons my customers tell me 
that make them buy from us. They are [1, 2, and 3].” 
Of course, items 1,2, and 3 should be not only your perfectly valid and 
applicable strengths but also weaknesses of that competitor. Unfortunately, 
your customer may still insist that you dish the dirt and give them some data 
for an upcoming review meeting. Whether or not your company has a policy 
about the direct use of competitive intelligence, I would strongly encourage 
you not to engage in this line of conversation. In 30-plus years of selling, I 
can only recall a handful of opportunities with a positive outcome after di -
rectly attacking the competition (even in a full-frontal strategy). There is so 
much downside and potential loss of trust. 
You can refer the customer to a third-party source, a news article, chat 
room/user group feedback, or an analyst report. Passing on irrefutable data 
from industry analysts or customer references supports your position and 
improves your credibility. If, for example, you are in the top right of the 
Gartner Magic Quadrant, 6 and your competitor is not, and the customer 
cares about it—that is important.
Reversing the Dirt
You may encounter the situation where the competitor has thrown the dirt 
or fear, uncertainty, and doubt (FUD) at you and given the customer a list 
of reasons why they should not buy from your company. Under no circum-
stances should you respond item by item to this kind of document. Instead, 
meet with your sales partner and carefully review the list. Typically, these hit 
lists focus on a feature level (we can do X and they cannot) or at a corporate 
level (size of company/breadth of support). Rarely do they look at the eco -
nomics and value of your solution.
6. See https://www.gartner.com/en/research/magic-quadrant.

---

## Page 290

268 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Competitive Tactics 269
Then select two or three items from the list which you can quickly 
discredit. Briefly and verbally respond to these items, stating that the docu-
ment is probably full of similar inaccuracies. You have then cast doubt on 
the integrity of your competitor. Should the customer insist on continu -
ing through the list item by item, offer to reconvene in 24 hours and ask 
the customer to prioritize the list and pick their top two to three items. In 
return, you will also do the same. Whenever possible, avoid a written re -
sponse. It is possible that if that customer has already accepted a list from 
your competitor that they may then share your answers. This might occur 
to allow that competitor another response or to correct apparent misinfor -
mation. Either circumstance damages your cause. 
The purpose of responding to competitive information is to discredit 
your competitor. Therefore, create the mental connection for the customer 
that if some of the information they have been given is incorrect, how can 
they 100% trust all of it? 
Summary
Credibility is a crucial component of trust for the sales engineer. It is hard 
to be effective in front of the customer if you do not have their confidence. 
You know your product very well; it is unlikely that you have that degree 
of knowledge about competing products. One misstatement can cost you 
months of works and trust. Take the high ground and refrain from bashing 
the competition, even if the customer or the salesperson asks. However, 
there is no reason that you cannot emphasize your strengths, which happen 
to be a weakness for someone else. 
Always take account of the impact of Do Nothing, Inc. They are a pow-
erful competitor, especially if you ignore their effect. Once you have identi-
fied your other competitors, develop a strategy for dealing with them. You 
do not have to use the same strategy for each competitor, nor does every 
strategy have to be a time-consuming frontal approach. The kinder and 
gentler sales engineer has many ways of influencing and ultimately win -
ning a deal.  
Hint: First Contact 
No competitive strategy survives contact with either the customer or the competition. So be 
prepared to adapt to changing demands and tactics. Your competitors are just as bright 
and intelligent as you are!

---

## Page 291

270 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Learn your product capabilities before talking about the competitors.
 2. Attend a competitor’s trade show or seminar. (But don’t hide who you are.) 
 3. Beware of the customer’s risk perception and how that can drive a DNI win.
 4. Know your top three strengths and references.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Learn your competitor’s product. Use it if possible.
 2. Never use the identical strategy twice in a row . Even if you win.
 3. Gather your competitor’s top three strengths and references. 
 4. Create a demonstration or presentation that showcases your strengths without explicitly 
    talking about them.

Chapter Goals Using the CRM System
Technology is a useful servant but a dangerous master.
      —Christian Lous Lange (historian)
C
ustomer relationship management (CRM) 
software has come a long way since the 
release of Automated Contact Tracking! (ACT!) 
by Mike Muhney and Pat Sullivan of Conductor 
Software way back in 1987. However, the pri -
mary purpose is still the same, to place the cus -
tomer at the center of all that you do. Every sales 
organization now uses a CRM system (which has 
engulfed the older sales force automation pro -
grams) to track customer interactions and sales 
opportunities. A functional and well-supported 
CRM system is a significant asset to the SE team. 
It can automate many tasks and serve as a long-
term corporate repository for all things about the 
customer.
This chapter will examine the SE-specific 
benefits of CRM, how to harness the power of 
CRM processes, and some helpful metrics and 
measures you can figure out from the data. 
Understand the benefits of 
using a CRM system.
Learn how to support your 
sales process with CRM 
features.
Know how to get the most 
out of your corporate CRM 
system.

## Page 293

272 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Using the CRM System 273
Why CRM Is Y our Best Friend
There is no such thing as the best CRM system or the best sales process. Any 
CRM system coupled with an inbuilt sales process is automatically the best 
friend of the SE. Look at some of the high-level benefits:
1. It creates a common language to prevent misunderstandings.
2. It injects a repeatable discipline into the sales process.
3. It helps you leverage other people’s knowledge and customer his -
tory.
4. It supplies a quantifiable form of measurement and revenue contri-
butions. 
Pursuing the FABulous sales engineer (Chapter 8), these benefits mean 
that you save time by streamlining your tasks, (theoretically) working on 
only qualified opportunities, and having full access to customer history, so 
you don’t repeat the mistakes of others. Used correctly, CRM is like having 
a bot sitting on your shoulder helping you through every step of your job. 
Misused, it becomes a vampire soullessly sucking data from your brain into 
the system for no apparent purpose. 
You can broadly divide a CRM system into three major components:
1. Operational. Handles leads, opportunities, customer master data, 
and product/support information, and enforces the sales process 
steps.
2. Analytical. Supplies functional sales and marketing data. Tracks 
pipeline, forecast, commit. Predicts future activity/business.
3. Collaborative. Allows team-based selling by supporting multiple 
functions within the company to align with the sales process and 
customer activities. 
As such, CRM acts as the central nervous system of the sales organiza -
tion by coordinating activities among the various participants in the sales 
process. It also allows you to map your sales process to the customer buy -
ing process. Unfortunately, a massive proportion of that buying process is 
unseen and never makes it into your CRM. It is like the hidden 90% of an 
iceberg. However, you can document part of it by recording what your cus-
tomers tell you has happened behind the scenes between sales calls. 
Progressing through a sales cycle (stages 3 through 8 of Table 2.1 in Chap-
ter 2) becomes less painful as you overlay best practices on each opportunity.

---

## Page 294

272 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Using the CRM System 273
Table 26.1 shows a sample mapping of a generic sales process to milestones. 
This form of mapping is why the CRM system becomes your friend. When 
correctly executed, the process ensures that no step is skipped, helping the 
SE and SE management avoid behaviors like the dash to demo and the push 
to present. It is far easier to ask qualification questions and say no when you 
have the backing of the sales process. 
The pipeline and forecasting functionality of CRM also supplies addi -
tional decision criteria to the SE team. Once a salesperson labels an oppor -
tunity as commit or forecast, that salesperson is putting their money where 
their mouth is by opening themselves up to risk and inspection. For ex -
ample, forecasted or committed implies that a deal will close in the current 
period, upside suggests that it might close, and pipeline indicates that it may 
close at some point in the future. That is a broad yet useful indication of 
opportunity prioritization.

Time and People Management
SE time is a limited resource.1 There is no open checkbook for unlimited SE 
hours and efforts to apply to an opportunity because the average SE has 12 
open and active opportunities at any given time. However, a well-regulated 
salesforce working with a disciplined SE team can use the CRM system and 
the business forecast to prioritize people and limit time, effort, and other 
assets. This approach allows sales and SE management to make decisions 
based on time, money, and commitment rather than pure emotion and the 
“buddy network.” A forecasted deal should always get SE time before an 
upside or pipeline deal. It is a wonderful filter.
In conjunction with the sales process, SE management can also place 
boundary conditions regarding where in the sales cycle an opportunity must 
be before an RFP is answered, a custom demo created, or a POC launched. 
Supplying these guidelines gives individual SEs the ammunition to push 
back on requests that, based upon the standard corporate process, represent 
a poor use of time or are viewed as premature.
The most common conflicts occur when balancing a minor committed 
deal with a much more significant (say three to five times) upside or pipe -
line deal. The two guiding principles here are your relationship with that 
salesperson or partner (what is their track record?) and whatever your SE 
manager advises. 
The Beauty of Repeatability
Active use of a sales methodology coupled with CRM can lead to the desired 
state of repeatable sales. This allows you to reuse materials and tactics from 
previous opportunities with only minor adjustments. You can then close ad-
ditional opportunities at other accounts in a shorter time. A sports analogy is 
that of a professional golfer. With comparatively little effort, a pro can hit a 
golf ball over 300 yards2 with a basic swing of a driver. Then, small changes 
in the grip and stance allow the golfer to hit the ball in a higher or lower tra-
jectory or fade/draw the ball around obstacles. Other than hours of practice, 
the key to this is a repeatable system of addressing the ball, holding the golf 
1. In this setting, time is a resource. SEs are not resources, they are people. Calling 
SEs resources, even with the best intention, is dehumanizing. Coal and oil are 
resources.
2. Rory McIlroy averages 320 yards (290m) compared to 216 yards for the average 
male amateur golfer.

---

## Page 296

274 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Using the CRM System 275
club, and then starting the swing—all of which the human body remembers 
(known as muscle memory). On the other hand, an undisciplined amateur 
golfer approaches each shot differently, has no set routine, and struggles to 
improve. As a result, the ball often ends up out of bounds, in a sand trap, or 
just in a poor position to play the next shot. 
An undisciplined salesforce pushes the SE team to approach every op -
portunity as a one-off, with little chance of learning from or reusing past 
successes and failures. The possibility of a poor sales call or demo is far 
higher when every activity starts from nothing. Conversely, the CRM sys -
tem can come to your aid differently. Just because you, or no one in your 
geography, has sold a particular configuration to a financial services com -
pany against your biggest competitor, it does not mean that no one else has. 
Searching through the CRM system with a find similar function can yield 
some fantastic results! 
Using the Features and Tapping Organizational Knowledge
The true benefit of CRM is that it allows you to leverage the knowledge and 
interactions of other people (including partners) both with your customer 
and with other customers. It is a system where the more people who use 
it, the better it becomes. An old heuristic from the CRM world of the 1990s 
was the 3:1 rule. It stated that for people to want to use a system, they 
had to get three times as much value (data) out of the system compared to 
what they entered. Entering your call reports, contact data, and reference 
architectures might be helpful to you in the long run as a form of corporate 
memory. Yet, it is more likely that others will benefit from that data more 
than you will. Multiple CRM mining and add-on tools can now extract in -
formation from your calendar and notes and automate much of the process 
for you. 
Hint: CRM Hygiene
A primary responsibility of the SE is to enter their activities into the CRM system. Sales 
operations folks refer to this as CRM hygiene. It is the equivalent of getting your expenses 
done on time, and your manager should never have to remind you to get either task done! 
You are being a good corporate citizen and helping others. Don’t settle for minimum 
compliance. Set a few minutes aside every day and upload/enter all relevant data. It is 
better inside the system than outside the system and living in your unreliable brain cells.
One natural starting point is interactions with customer or product sup-
port. You only need to be blindsided once by a supposedly friendly customer

---

## Page 297

276 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Using the CRM System 277
about some technical support issues. It is not a pleasant experience to lis -
ten to a customer complain about the quality of support personnel, callback 
times, resolution paths, and so on. You should always check their support 
profile before you visit a customer for the first time or one you have not seen 
for a few months. If you see anything that might raise a concern, then check 
out the type of questions and calls your customer is making into support. 
For example, you might see a training issue if many calls are of the “How do 
I do xyz?” type.
For the new SE or salesperson, the CRM system can tell you how many 
sales activities such as RFPs, demos, POCs, and sales calls were performed 
over the past year or two. You will also get a view of the corporate hierarchy 
and power structure, some trust scores, and at least an idea about poten -
tial coaches. You’ll also gain visibility into marketing campaigns and which 
contacts attended seminars, webinars, and user group meetings. For larger 
accounts that span multiple geographies, you may be able to see other active 
opportunities. The more you know, the less likely you will step on someone 
else’s deal or interfere with an in-process sales campaign. 
Hint: Who Owns What?
Companies struggle with keeping track of exactly what products and services their custom-
ers have already contractually purchased (and when they bought them). It doesn’t matter 
whether you are a 30-year-old hardware company or an up-and-coming cloud provider. 
It is a perennial problem. It is always worth checking what your company thinks your 
customer owns—whether physical equipment, onsite software installed, or rented com -
pute/storage capabilities. Then work with your sales partner to validate that list—and, 
of course, update the system for an accurate record. This activity yields significant benefits 
when you are launching a new product or upgrading an old one. You can now accurately 
profile the customer base. 
Many companies also use the CRM system as a high-level repository 
for handover materials when moving customers from presales to postsales 
functions. This ensures that the delivery side of the organization, whether 
in-house or external partner, knows what sales promised and has access to 
all the call notes, architectures, demo scripts, screen mockups, and anything 
else used to drive the sale. 
CRM and Personal Gain
CRM systems are a double-edged sword, especially when it comes to the ac-
tivity tracking of SEs. I am a big fan of activity tracking—which is what you

---

## Page 298

276 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Using the CRM System 277
do during the week. However, I’m not too fond of time tracking, which is 
how much time you spend on each of those activities. Activity tracking pro-
vides SE management with a mass of beneficial information. AI tools like 
Salesforce’s Einstein can then analyze that data for valuable insights. Activ-
ity tracking provides management with the data to determine if the SEs are 
doing the right things and push back on other organizations (like support, 
marketing, and services) when SE time is being misused or supporting poor 
behavior on the part of others. The data will flow if SEs see these actions 
occurring (we are handling way too many escalations). Once activity data 
is used to kick down the organization (“You only made two customer calls 
last week”) or have SEs record their time (“You only registered 31 hours 
last week”), the system falls apart, trust dies, and the numbers are gamed. 
SEs outshine any other department in finding holes, loopholes, and the 
unintended consequences of any metric by which they are judged. It is a 
professional talent.
Hint: Learn the CRM System
Just as any self-respecting SE will spend time learning about some of the most obscure 
menu options and administrative settings within their product, they should also be natu-
rally curious about the CRM system. There are usually hundreds of reports, views, and 
search functions available to you. You may also use some of the AI tools to run some 
large-scale data analysis. 
Annual Review Time
One significant CRM advantage for the competent SE is that it keeps a his -
torical record of your activities and contributions for each customer. A few 
weeks before your annual review,3 run reports to gather:
1. A complete list of all your revenue transactions;
2. Conversion rates of POCs;
3. RFP contributions;
4. The number of demos and presentations;
5. Post-sales support escalations;
6. Other recorded activities like marketing events and partner 
training.
Your direct manager will undoubtedly remember your top opportuni -
ties. A top-class manager will already have most of this data in front of 
3. Less than 50% of companies still run annual reviews, so this may be quarterly.

---

## Page 299

278 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Using the CRM System 279
them. However, they may need some help to recall the smaller contribu -
tions and all the other work. By showing that you were the lead SE on 25% 
of your area revenue in an eight-person team, you make the case that you 
were significantly more productive (two times) and deserve a pay raise and 
promotion. 
For the more senior SE, if you see a positive disparity between your 
numbers and the larger group number, that may allow you to share best 
practices and help others achieve your level of productivity. 
CASE STUDY: Clara’s Conversion Case
Clara, a three-year inside SE, was approaching her biannual review. This re -
view was important for her as she hoped for a sizeable pay raise and a transfer 
from the SMB group into a field enterprise SE team. She continues her success 
story: “I went through all my deal and activity data to sell my differentiators. I 
discovered that compared to my peers, my initial monthly recurring revenue 
(iMRR) stats were 30% higher, my deals closed four days faster, and my first call 
to the next demo conversion rate was 12% better. Although my manager was 
reluctant to let me go, she listened to the data and approved my transfer. We 
also spent some time reviewing why my numbers were better and uncovered 
that I always included a gain no matter how much pain the customer was suffer-
ing. That gain generated add-on revenue and more hope in the customer. Who 
knew? The CRM data made us all better SEs.” 
E E E E
Summary
A CRM system is truly the best friend of the sales engineer. By taking emo -
tion out of the sales process and providing an accurate measure of an oppor-
tunity against others within the company, SE prioritization and allocation 
become more science and less art. It also allows you to harness the collective 
knowledge and wisdom of other personnel within your company (and out-
side of it) and become more effective as a result. You can also use the CRM 
data to bolster your strengths and fill in skills gaps to eliminate weaknesses. 
Finally, remember the 3:1 rule and that you must give data to get data. With 
a predictable sales process, you do want history to repeat itself.

---

## Page 300

278 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Using the CRM System 279
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Learn the entire system, not just the sales/pipeline portion.
 2. Research all your customers and contacts.
 3. Contribute (willingly) your information.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Use the CRM repository to pass on expertise and documentation.
 2. Automate reports and alerts for nonsales activities.
 3. Run the numbers to improve your skills.
 4. Managers can monitor the data for coaching opportunities and input for headcount 
    decisions.

Chapter Goals Compensation (Show Me the

All I ask is the chance to prove that money cannot 
make me happy!
            —Spike Milligan
W
e are engineers. We love to solve problems 
and to make people happy. And we are 
paid for doing that every single day. That pay -
ment is essential. We have bills to pay, families 
to take care of, holidays to enjoy, and dreams 
to achieve. That is why being a sales engineer 
is genuinely one of the best professions in the 
world. How and why you are compensated is a 
critical part of the job. Few of us do not need to 
work, so the size of the paycheck makes a mate-
rial difference to our lives. This chapter covers 
the basic SE compensation structure, how to le -
verage the plan to best effect, and how to build 
the first compensation plan.
The Basic Components of a Plan
I divide an SE compensation plan into five broad 
components: fixed, variable, management by ob-
jectives (MBO), stock, and other. Each of these 
components plays an integral part in determining 
how the organization values your unique talents 
and how you function in a team environment. 
Understand the essential 
components of an SE 
compensation plan.
Leverage the plan to best 
effect.
Build the first SE 
compensation plan (for SE 
managers or the first SE).

---

## Page 303

282 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 283
This review excludes the standard HR-oriented benefits such as health, den-
tal, retirement/pension funding, employee stock purchase plans, and so on. 
Plans vary widely, even in the same target market (e.g., one firewall vendor 
compared to another), so I’ll look at standard down the middle  plans rather 
than a multitude of what-if corner cases. 
One acronym used throughout the chapter is on target earnings (OTE). 
That is defined as your overall compensation if you achieve all your set tar-
gets in the prescribed manner. OTE is usually a combination of fixed plus 
variable pay and 100% MBO payout (if you have them). 
Fixed (Base) Salary
Base salary is the regular periodic payment you receive from your com -
pany, regardless of sales achievements. As a general heuristic, base salary 
should fall between 60% and 80% of your OTE. Smaller startup organiza -
tions, especially on the software side of the business, may pay out on a more 
leveraged plan close to 50/50. Early career SEs and inside SEs may receive 
85%–90% of OTE as the base. You will also see that the more senior posi -
tions in the SE hierarchy (management or principal) have greater leverage. 
Your salary is your guaranteed income independent of sales achieve -
ment. In most cases, it pays to negotiate for as high a base salary as possible 
as most other payments key off that amount as an added percentage. In 
many countries, certain other benefits such as life insurance or disability are 
correlated to base salary and not OTE. 
Base SE salaries vary widely based upon experience, products sold, mar-
ket maturity, and geography. The good news is that a good SE will always be 
in demand. SE demand has outstripped supply for the past 20 years, even 
in economic downturns such as 2008 and 2019. A quick search on LinkedIn 
shows more than 40,000 open SE positions just in the United States and 
Canada, which extrapolates to over 80,000 jobs worldwide. 
Another quick search will yield expected salary ranges, grade levels, and 
an overall rating of company cultures. Again, there is plenty of information 
out there for the prospective SE. Larger companies will have a standard set 
of overlapping salary ranges based upon experience and seniority. (Senior -
ity is how long you have been around; experience is what you have done.) 
For example, the base salary range for a standard SE might be 105,000 to 
125,000, while a senior SE range is 118,000 to 147,000.
Variable Pay
Variable pay consists of your commission payments. There is a direct corre -
lation between the monetary value of sales closed and the amount of your 
compensation. Companies typically pay commissions as a percentage of rec-

---

## Page 304

282 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 283
ognized revenue related to a target (quota), often with those percentages 
rising as you sell more. 
Variable compensation is the upside of the job, the reward for excel -
lence. It needs to be significant enough to feel motivated by it, yet not so 
large that you think you are a salesperson. Your targets are based on one or 
more summary levels (territories):
1. Individual. Your sales targets are linked only to the achievements of 
the salesperson, or two or three salespeople that you support. 
2. Local geography. Your sales targets are linked to the overall achieve-
ment of a group of salespeople, supported by a team of SEs. Typical 
examples would be a vertical, a large city (San Francisco, Chicago, 
London, Tokyo), or a smaller country (Denmark, New Zealand).
3. Large geography. Your sales targets are linked to the overall achieve-
ment of a large group of salespeople, supported by multiple teams 
of SEs. Large geography may range from global for smaller compa-
nies to a region such as United Kingdom and Ireland or Northeast-
ern U.S. for larger companies.
The impact of your efforts diminishes as you move from an individual 
target plan to a large geography plan. 
When linked to a small group of salespeople who depend entirely upon 
you, every deal you close makes a significant difference in achieving your 
targets. This setup is known as “You eat what you kill.” You are paid for your 
efforts and no one else’s. When paired with an outstanding salesperson or 
blessed with a red-hot territory, you can make a lot of money. Conversely, 
when paired with an ineffective (or new) salesperson, you may struggle.
When tied to small geography, you can still make a difference. For ex -
ample, you might be part of a team of seven SEs supporting ten salespeo -
ple. Closing one or two extra opportunities results in a better overall team 
achievement—and everyone benefits. However, you are expected to con -
tribute your share when paid on a large geographical number, potentially 
shared with hundreds or thousands of SEs.
The advantage of a large geographic number is that it is more predict -
able because of the law of large numbers. 1 You are far more likely to hit 
100% +/– 10%. On the other hand, the smaller the target number and the 
smaller the sales population contributing to that number, the less predict -
able the outcome. Because of this effect, many SE compensation plans base 
1. The law of large numbers, in probability and statistics, states that as a sample size 
grows, the mean gets closer to the average of the whole population. The more 
you toss a coin, the more likely you are to get a true 50/50 heads/tails result.

---

## Page 305

284 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 285
their sales targets on two territories. Your variable pay is 60% based on small 
geography or individual achievement and 40% on a larger number. This split 
provides a more predictable payment stream and rewards helping other SEs 
and contributing toward the general good of the corporate SE community. 
Variable plans pay monthly or quarterly, based on the targets for that 
group. For the following examples, assume an SE has a base salary of 
$100,000 and variable pay of $25,000 for an OTE of $125,000. That is an 
80/20 plan, where 80% of OTE (100/125) is the base salary. The simplest and 
most common plan pays a quarterly bonus based on % target attainment. At 
100% of the target, the SE will receive $6,250 each quarter. Under a linear 
plan, at 60% of target you get 0.6 × $6,250 = $3,750 and at 150% you get 
1.5 × $6,250 = $9,375. It is common practice to institute a cutoff (doorstep) at 
25% or 30% attainment, under which you receive zero payment. 
You are not in sales just to achieve 100%, so the most exciting aspects 
of a compensation plan come into effect once you surpass your target. The 
plan increases your commission payments using accelerators. These multiply 
your payments by a specific factor. For example, payments may be doubled 
over 100% and tripled2 over 150%. When you have a blowout quarter and 
reach 200% of target, your commission payments increase by a factor of 3.5, 
as shown in Table 27.1.
It is a sad fact of SE life that the profession often does not receive the 
respect or the rewards for overachievement. Accelerators are a wonderful 
way to reward those SEs and geographies who significantly beat their quota 
without it costing the company a large amount of cash. You’ll hear many 
sales leaders say, “If you want to make money, then carry a quota,” which to 
me shows an amazing lack of understanding of the SE psyche and the gen -
eral concept of sales teamwork. An interesting risk-reward approach is the 
quota-squared accelerator model, which rewards overachievers and pun -
ishes underachievers. At 100% the accelerator is a flat 1.0, at 120% it is 1.44 
(1.2 × 1.2), and at 150% it is 2.25 (1.5 × 1.5). This plan is attractive for CFOs 
2. This is an incredibly generous plan you might only see at a smaller company but 
serves as a great example.
Table 27.1

Range
Payment

100–150% Double 2.0 $6,250 × 2.0 x 0.5 $6,250 
150–200% Triple 3.0 $6,250 × 3.0 × 0.5 $9,375 
Total $21,875
