and sales operations because at 80%, the accelerator is 0.64 (0.8 × 0.8), and 
at 40%, the factor is a mere 0.16. 
MBOs and General Performance 
More mature organizations use MBO payments to promote a longer-term 
view over the monthly or quarterly chase. You may also see these referred3 
to as objectives and key results (OKRs). MBOs are directed toward individu-
al achievements, such as training or education, although more enlightened 
organizations will use them to build SE infrastructure or nonstandard sales 
channels. You should regard MBOs as negotiable because they are regularly 
set in conjunction with your manager throughout the financial year. With 
some reasonable effort, you should claim 100% of your MBOs and effec -
tively move that amount into base salary. 
Hint: The Glass Half-Full MBO
If MBOs are part of your compensation plan, look out for two factors. First, is the MBO an 
all-or-nothing MBO? For example, if the MBO is to take 10 training days, and you only 
take 9, do you receive a 90% payment or a 0% payment? Second, you should not have 
more than 3 MBOs per quarter, mainly for focus. 
If MBOs are part of your compensation plan, they typically will not 
exceed 5% of your OTE. The two major exceptions are if you are part of a 
newly acquired company with uncertain sales targets or an SME incented 
to distribute your knowledge and wisdom. MBOs may then comprise 12%–
15% of your OTE.
Stock Options and Equity
Diving headfirst into a startup or smaller company can be a fabulous way to 
accelerate your career and your paycheck. Choose the right company, and 
there should be a significant financial reward in the future. On the other 
hand, choose the wrong company or one with poor management, and a lot 
of hard work goes to waste (yet you gain a lot of valuable experience). 
The possibility of a future financial gain allows the smaller companies 
to compete in the SE talent pool. 4 Therefore, you are offered a lower base 
salary and a more leveraged variable comp plan (70/30 or even 60/40) in 
return for the possibility of sharing in the company’s ownership. 
3. For more on this, read John Doerr’s 2018 book Measure What Matters, published 
by Penguin Publishing.
4. A 2021 SE compensation survey by Consensus noted between 50% and 64% of 
individual contributor respondents received options. That rose to between 55% 
and 75% for SE leaders depending on geography.

---

## Page 307

286 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 287
A stock option is different from owning stock in your company. An op-
tion gives you the right to buy a certain number of shares at a predetermined 
price in the future. When your company grows and becomes successful, go-
ing through an initial public offering (IPO) or a venture capital sale, those 
options gain value. For example, you are offered the option to buy 10,000 
shares of your new employer, Acme Transwarp Networks, common stock at 
$1.
You should consider the following terms and conditions (and consult a 
financial advisor for the actual monetary speeds and feeds, as this is not legal 
or tax advice) of Table 27.2. 
So, in this example, one year after the issue date, you have the right to 
purchase 2,500 shares of Acme from the company. Every month after that, 
you receive an additional right to another 208 shares (7,500 / 36), which 
you can exercise immediately.5 That continues until four years have passed, 
at which point you now own the right to buy 10,000 shares of Acme at $1 
each. You don’t have to exercise your options as they vest and can wait until 
the appropriate time (when the price is > $1!). There are many other rel -
evant tax issues beyond the scope of this book, and you should consult with 
a professional if the amounts at stake are significant. 
Hint: Understand the Percentage of Ownership
It is essential to know what your percentage of ownership will be. For example, a 
10,000-share option of a total float of 1,000,000 shares is quite different from a float of 
5. Technically, there may be a blackout period, typically surrounding earning an -
nouncements, when you are not permitted to sell your shares.

100,000,000 shares. Also, ask for the company’s latest valuation (if pre-IPO), as that 
valuation may be different from your strike price. Knowing the valuation helps you un -
derstand what a financially informed investment company has paid for the stock.
Instead of stock options, mid-size companies may offer you restricted 
stock units6 (RSUs). For example, suppose you are offered an SE position 
at CumuloNimbus, Inc. As part of the package, they give you 2,000 RSUs 
which vest over five years. The company stock is currently trading at $10, 
making the RSUs potentially worth $20,000. After a year, you receive 400 
RSUs (1/5 × 2,000) converted into regular company stock. That stock is 
then worth, in total, 400 times the current trading price. RSUs rarely have 
as much upside as stock options but can become a nice bonus on top of your 
regular compensation. 
Other Plans
”Other” is a catchall for various benefits. The first type is HR style ben -
efits. For example, you might receive a monthly car allowance in certain 
territories if required to do much driving. Membership in airline clubs is 
another great perk7 for those who travel frequently. In addition, you may 
be eligible for tuition reimbursement for MBA classes. A second benefit is 
directly performance related, such as a president’s club or circle of excel -
lence trip awarded to those sales individuals who overachieve their targets. 
This award, also designed to reward the spouse/partner of the employee, 
has taken me to Hawaii twice, Puerto Rico, and Paris. You never forget 
those trips.
The third benefit is miscellaneous compensation, known as special in -
centives for sales8 (SPIFS). These are special one-time bonuses and contests 
run by sales management. They may take the form of payments for selling a 
new product, beating a specific competitor, or accelerating revenue or con-
sumption into the first month of a quarter. Some SPIFS apply only to direct 
sales, but most forward-thinking organizations now include the SE team, 
albeit at a lower rate. An aggressive SPIF program can add another 5% to 
10% onto your final earnings for the year. 
6. Terminology varies by country and by tax code.
7. My personal favorite. As well as improving morale, there is over a 200% ROI 
based on productivity and food/drink consumption.
8. Also sales performance incentive funds. Its usage dates to 1859 as “the percent -
age allowed by drapers to their young men when they effect sale of old fashioned 
or undesirable stock.” (https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiff.)

## Page 309

288 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 289
Leveraging the Plan 
As noted a few times, compensation drives behavior. Should your plan force 
you to sell a particular product or drive consumption of a specific resource, 
there is a reason for the plan. Do not fight the plan. Instead, go along with 
it and let it be your guide. Every plan is different, but here are some sugges-
tions for reviewing your plan:
1. Read the plan carefully. This may sound obvious, yet more than 
50% of SEs don’t read their plan until there is a problem.
2. Note any limits, caps, or barriers.
3. Determine what counts toward the achievement of your target. For 
example, in a billing model, does it include professional services, 
education, or premium support?
4. Is the target set on an annual, a quarterly, or a monthly basis?
5. Are there any incentives for smoothing the target (e.g., four steady 
quarters) rather than getting one large deal? 
6. Are you credited for telesales or third-party sales into your territory?
CASE STUDY: The Commission Cap
Mick was incensed. His quarterly bonus payment was only half of what he ex -
pected. The sales team had closed one huge transaction with a big up-front pay-
ment and a multiyear commitment. It was the largest deal in the company’s his-
tory and brought Mick to 325% of target for the year. Mick’s line management 
was caught unawares and had no idea why he was underpaid. The sales VP 
hastily convened a call between the CFO, the sales VP, and the entire sales team. 
There was a windfall clause in the compensation plan, which stated that any pay-
ments above a 200% attainment were subject to the review and approval of se-
nior management. The CFO had triggered the windfall clause and compounded 
the error by neglecting to communicate the fact to the team. A brutal and direct 
conversation occurred, and the CFO only changed his mind after a long and 
painful three months. The moral is always to look for caps and clauses that place 
your compensation at the mercy of others. You cannot negotiate the clause out 
of your plan, but you can prehandle it if it is likely to become an issue. 
E E E E
Hint: Model the Plan
If your company does not provide a model, create an SE-based model of the compensa -
tion plan so you can perform what-if analyses against future events and double check 
payments.

---

## Page 310

288 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 289
Ramping
Ramping can impact your variable compensation. It refers to the modi -
fication of your targets across multiple periods. In the first instance, you 
may have an uneven distribution of your quarterly targets. For example, 
although your annual target may be 40, your quarterly targets are 8, 9, 11, 
and 12. This ramping may be in effect if your company has strong quar -
terly revenue variation (usually in a billing model) or is experiencing strong 
sequential growth (consumption model). The second form of ramping is 
when you join the company. For a certain period, a new hire SE is not ex -
pected to contribute materially to target achievement. For example, your 
hire date might be August 15, yet you start on quota on December 1. Mean-
while, you receive what is known as a draw, which assumes you are achiev-
ing 75% or 100% of the plan. Always ask about your ramping time and the 
type and amount of draw you receive.
Who Else Cares?
A cardinal rule of SE compensation plans is that you should never have a 
target that no one in sales shares. Instead, your targets should always be a 
sum (or multiple) of the targets of individual salespeople or territories. You 
then have someone on your side to reach that common target and a friend 
to help you investigate and fight any compensation errors or omissions. 
As usual, when both sales and sales engineers work together, good things 
happen. 
Negotiating the Plan 
The only time you can seriously negotiate parts of your compensation plan 
is before you accept the position. I’d caution you not to join a company if 
they cannot show you your compensation plan, especially several months 
into their fiscal year. You can negotiate base salary by asking to be moved 
up in the pay band or join at a higher job level. Variable compensation is 
harder to change, although I have seen sizeable payments added to a job 
offer to achieve first-year targets. A signing bonus is an often-used tactic if 
a candidate is either foregoing a bonus from their current employer or is 
taking a base salary cut and nervous about the compensation plan. Because 
signing bonuses are a one-off payment, they are easier to negotiate and seek 
internal approval. Finally, you can ask for more vacation or personal days. 
These are all items you should discuss once the company has expressed 
a desire to hire you. Don’t start with the details of the compensation plan in 
your first set of interviews.

---

## Page 311

290 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 291
Hint: Don’t Ask for a Round Number
When discussing base salary, do not ask for a round number. It is better to ask for 136,544 
rather than 135,000. That gives the interviewer a string to pull on and ask, “Why?” Have 
a rationale for the number, like “That is what my brother earns, and I’d like to make more 
than he does.” As ridiculous as this sounds, it has an amazingly high success rate. 
What Is a Target?
You may have noticed the judicious use of the word “target” as we speak 
about compensation plans. That is because, unlike ten years ago, there are 
now many forms of SE targets as companies flex between billing and con -
sumption models. For example, in the classic large enterprise portfolio sale, 
a company might agree to buy 5,000,000 worth of your products, together 
with a 1,000,000 first-year maintenance payment. That maintenance pay -
ment covers support and the rights to updates. Typically, the total 6,000,000 
is credited toward revenue attainment on the date the revenue was recog -
nized (usually the contract signature date). Easy! 
Hint: Is the Target Realistic?
When it comes to tech sales, 60% of salespeople achieved quota last year, according to a 
recent Harvard Business Review study. An Xactly9 survey shows that only 69% of enter -
prises hit their annual revenue goals and that Salesforce estimates 57% of reps miss their 
quota. 
The consumption model is different and more complicated. Laura Palm-
er, former head of sales at Google Cloud, says, “Customers use the cloud 
services they need when they need them and only pay for the services once 
they are consumed. Sounds simple enough. Except it changes everything: 
the entire sales cycle, roles and responsibilities, and even compensation.” 
At its simplest, the consumption model is all about customers using the ser-
vices. No revenue can be recognized until the customer consumes those 
services. That means signing the (cloud) contract is just the beginning, as 
now you need to ensure the customer adopts and ramps up the use of your 
technology. Selling has moved from being front loaded to continuous. 
Consumption targets typically have two components. The first is en -
suring that committed spending occurs. For example, Acme Rocketskates 
commits to spending 100,000 a month with you—that needs to happen. 
The second component is incremental spending, which is net-new revenue 
9. See https://www.xactlycorp.com/blog/quota-attainment-tips. Accessed Novem -
ber 14, 2019.

---

## Page 312

290 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 291
above the already committed amount. So, if by month 10 of usage, Acme is 
now spending 120,000 a month, then that 20,000 is incremental monthly 
recurring revenue (iMRR). Although both components (known colloqui -
ally as buckets) are important, the iMRR is commonly more highly prized 
and rewarded.
Building the First Plan  
This final section of the chapter supplies some guidelines for SE and sales 
operations leadership when they formulate the first SE compensation plan. 
There may be only one or two SEs in place or an SE manager with a small 
team. This plan has four sections.
1. Base salary. Dan Pink10 puts this succinctly: “The best use of money 
as a motivator is to pay people enough to take the issue of mon -
ey off the table.” The SE should not be looking around the in -
dustry (or inside the company) and feel underpaid and therefore 
undervalued. 
Hint: Is the Target Realistic?
If HR supplies a band/range of salaries for a comparable position, always ask for the 
companies and industries surveyed and validate that the data is a fit.  
2. Stock options. Options are the “if . . . then” kicker on top of the base 
salary. For a small company, they are the incentive for the SE to 
work extra hours and perform more non-SE-related tasks to get 
the deal done and aim for customer satisfaction. They should be 
meaningful enough to make an actual financial decision to leave 
them behind for another job. Anywhere from a potential of one to 
five times annual salary seems a typical range. 
3. Variable pay. Set either one or two targets for the SE and clearly de-
fine what is and what is not included in the target (such as services, 
third-party sales, and so on). One target should be the smallest 
scope realistic target the SE can achieve. This may be the same tar-
get as the salesperson or salespeople that the SE directly supports 
and is the type 1 target described in the earlier variable pay section. 
10.  Pink, D., Drive: The Surprising Truth About What Motivates Us,  New York: River-
 head Books, 2011.

---

## Page 313

292 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Compensation (Show Me the Money) 293
The second (optional) target is larger and is equivalent to the large 
geo type 3 target. The split between the two is two-thirds to one-
third (see Table 27.3). Add accelerators (Table 27.4) for 100%–plus 
achievement. 
4. MBO. Create one MBO linked to the creation of SE infrastructure. 
Examples are building and recording standard demos, a POC play -
book, and RFP templates.
Summary
Read your plan. It is about much more than your base salary and a few sales 
targets. It tells you the expected behaviors and outcomes for the following 
year. My general advice is to live off your base salary and less than 50% of 
your variable pay. Anything above that is a bonus you use to reward yourself 
and those you love. Spend the time to understand what counts as a quota or 
target attainment and how to fill the various buckets in the plan. Should you 
be fortunate enough to have stock options as part of your package, consult a 
professional to maximize them even if they are a small amount.

2.0 150% + 125%–plus

Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Read the plan and ask questions.
 2. Learn from other seasoned SEs about the pros and cons of the plan.
 3. Model the plan. Find out who in sales cares about your targets. 
 4. Stock options, or RSUs, are an upside—a reward for a lower base and hard work.
For the experienced SE or SE manager:
 1. Be an advocate for your staff in commission issues.
 2. Develop a written plan for bonuses or qualifications (like club) if there is subjectivity 
    involved.
 3. It is fine to have an SE make more than you do in a good year as a manager!

Chapter Goals Moving into Sales
Success is the ability to go from failure to failure with-
out losing your enthusiasm.
            —Winston Churchill
Y
es. You are reading a chapter about moving 
into a direct sales role and carrying a quota. 
Every SE has looked across the table and watched 
a sales representative stumble through the sales 
process and thought, “How hard can it be? I can 
do that, too!” This process is known in the trade 
as crossing over to the dark side, and some SEs will 
become incredibly successful, while others will 
fail miserably. However, regardless of the even -
tual outcome, an SE who even thinks through 
the transition process will gain a far deeper ap -
preciation of the art of selling. 
This chapter will help you uncover your po -
tential motivation to make a career change, pre-
pare for a move into sales, and leverage your su-
per SE powers when you are in sales. 
Y ou Want to Do What?
We ran a career development survey with mul -
tiple SE teams1 between 2016 and 2020. The sur-
1. Surveyed companies ranged from parts of Oracle, 
HPe, Cisco, and Salesforce down to several smaller 
Examine your motivation.
Learn how to prepare 
yourself for a transition into 
sales.
Understand what you 
should expect and how 
to make the change 
successful.

---

## Page 317

296 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Moving into Sales 297
vey asked over 4,100 SEs the question, “Thirty months from now, what 
role/position would you (realistically) like to have as the next step in your 
career?” More than 16% responded that they dream of moving into sales, 
which is one in six. So there is certainly demand and multiple inspirational 
success stories over the years. See Table 29.1 in the next chapter for the full 
results of the survey. 
Your first action is, in effect, that of discovery. What is your motivation 
to make this career change? Is this part of a long-term plan, or is it some -
thing born out of frustration with your current role or with the salespeople 
with whom you work? In many cases, SEs become preoccupied with the 
fame and fortune aspect of being a salesperson, which are two of the worst 
possible reasons to cross over. 
This book deals with building up both your technical and business skill 
sets with a future career path in mind. Moving into sales is neither a promo-
tion nor a demotion; it is a lateral move. However, it is a move that requires 
a different skill set than even the most experienced SE typically owns. Given 
the fact that many SEs even resent the sales nomenclature in their job titles 
and do not regard themselves as salespeople, asking, “Would you like to 
sell?” usually receives a loud no. Yet for one in six SEs, that answer is a yes 
or at least a strong maybe. Sometimes the opportunity to move into sales is 
indeed part of a longer-term life plan; on other occasions, it may be purely 
opportunistic. 
Either way, you need to periodically assess your skill set and career as -
pirations to decide if this is the right move for you. It certainly will open a 
wealth of future career opportunities and close down others. My first ven -
ture into sales was a matter of stepping up to fill a territory for the company’s 
greater good. It taught me that I would be a good salesperson but never a 
great salesperson. I also learned that in my case, it had a detrimental impact 
on my attitude and my personality. For others, it is a natural path forward. 
As part of your motivation and discovery process, try making out red/
green lists (such as in Table 28.1) for both your current SE job and your 
imaginary sales position. On the red side of the list, note all the things you 
don’t like, and on the green side, note down all the positives. Then compare 
the lists and see how many reds would disappear from being an SE and 
which new ones would appear in sales. Likewise, which greens would you 
lose if you gave up being an SE, and what would you gain in return? I will 
also mention that there will undoubtedly be a difference between what you 
perceive as +/– and what may occur.
As mentioned earlier, the transition is affectionately known as crossing 
over to the dark side,  which takes its phrasing from the first Star Wars movie 
$200 million companies.

---

## Page 318

296 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Moving into Sales 297
(George Lucas, Paramount Pictures, 1977). It is not to imply that SEs stand 
for the good side and that salespeople are evil. Instead, it mimics the con -
sensus view of SEs that we are indeed always righteous and are “the good 
folks.” Each side certainly takes great delight whenever a conversion occurs! 
Preparing for the Transition
Sales is a noble and challenging profession with its own set of unique skills 
to learn and master. Contrary to popular SE opinion, it is not simply a mat-
ter of having a big expense account, playing golf, driving a BMW, and dress-
ing nicely. As well as learning new sales skills, there are some significant 
psychological adjustments to make.
First and foremost, following all the other advice in this book, you need 
a plan. Make a list of all the sales-related skills you believe that you will 
need, and then grade yourself from 1 to 5 on that list. Then speak with your 
local sales manager, several successful sales representatives, and of course, 
your SE manager about what it would take to make a move. Ask them to 
rate you on that same 1 to 5 scale. Be prepared for some harsh feedback and 
reality and stark differences of opinion over your capabilities. You should 
also engage your mentor, either inside or outside the company, and talk 
through the process. Don’t keep your hopes and dreams a secret. The more 
you share, the more help you are likely to receive.
Hint: Revisit Your Brand 
Review the brand statement and list you created in Chapter 23. What, if anything, would 
change if you moved from sales engineering to sales? 
Table 28.1 
Sample Red/Green Analysis of Being a Salesperson Compared to Being an SE
Green (Perceived Positives) Red (Perceived Negatives) 
I make more money. I must forecast and commit deals.
I don’t have to account for my time. I’ll eventually lose my technical mojo.
I’ll learn a lot of new skills. I will no longer be a technical superstar.
More contact with customers, less with 
code.
Customers may not want to talk to me.
I’m now COO of my own business. Not every problem is now solvable with 
technology.
I know how the product works and what 
customers do with it. 
I’ll make a lot of mistakes (and I am not 
used to that). 
I can work better with (and have more 
respect for) SEs than most other reps. 
I will hear a lot of nos and negativity.

## Page 319

298 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Moving into Sales 299
Because of these initial conversations, you will have a list of skills, a 
frank assessment of your gaps, and an indication of how the organization 
rates you on your skill sets and aptitude. You are now able to start planning 
the move. Look ahead over the next 6 to 12 months and see if there are any 
courses (internal/external), specialized training, sales meetings, or general 
programs you can attend that will help you fill the gaps. Revisit your conver-
sations with local sales management and ask for their help and advice. You 
can get involved in contracts, negotiations, and pricing as a silent observer. 
When working on smaller opportunities with a salesperson, volunteer to 
take on some more traditional sales-related tasks.
Hint: Expand the Network 
No matter how effective you feel your networking skills have been over the past year—re -
double your pace of meeting new people. The more contacts (inside and outside the com -
pany) you can make who are willing to help you out, provide leads, advice, give favors, 
and so on, during your first few months, the easier the transition.
Psychologically, becoming a salesperson is a mentally wearing process. 
A significant part of the trust and credibility you build up as a successful 
SE dissipates once you are known to carry a quota and you become a mere 
salesperson. It would be best if you prepare for plenty of rejection, cynicism, 
objections, and repeatedly hearing the word “no.” There is a reason that 
many motivational sales quotes revolve around hearing no, as that can be 
a confidence draining act. However, I remember that “a good no is better 
than a bad yes,” and “every no is a chance to learn.” You’ll also need more 
mental energy as you are now in charge of your own business. Even as a 
successful SE, you are often a follower, acting your part in a grand plan. As 
a salesperson, you can set the strategy and lead the charge for each sales op-
portunity—with unbridled enthusiasm and optimism. 
What Are My Chances?
Every SE I know who attempts the transition is surprised by the level of 
distrust they face in their daily routine. Once friendly customers now won -
der why you are calling them. This change in relationships happens even 
though you feel fully prepared to handle it. The actual success rate of SEs 
making a move within the same company is about 30%, and that number has 
remained almost unchanged for several decades. I define success as:
1. Achieving primary sales targets by the end of year 2;
2 Still being in the job after year 3;
3. No material negative personal/home life changes;
4. The SEs still like you!

---

## Page 320

298 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Moving into Sales 299
Together with all this preparation is the realization that you will need to 
commit to this process for a minimum of two years. During those two years, 
your technical skills will fade, making it more complicated (but not impos -
sible) to revert to a technical position. However, that should not hold you 
back from your dream, as you don’t want to reach the end of your career 
regretting that you never tried. Five notable factors will raise your chances 
of success:
1. Having the buy-in from the home front. The support of your 
friends, family, and spouse/partner is critical and will get you 
through the dark days.
2. If you are technical, look for a technical product to sell. Alterna -
tively, become a technical (overlay) sales specialist. 
3. Leverage any industry-specific knowledge or vertical expertise you 
may have.
4. You need time. Having a concurrent “big ask” like finishing an 
MBA, marriage, or birth of a child will detract from your mission. 
5. You need a great boss. 
What Should I Expect?
Life will be different. You are no longer the knowledgeable SE that every 
customer wants to meet. You are a salesperson. You can no longer expect 
everyone to believe what you say. You should no longer expect to answer 
technical questions (even though you can because you still know the an -
swer). Your circle of friends and peers will change, and you will spend a lot 
of time with your sales manager and dealing with finance, legal, revenue 
recognition, and sales operations. You should pay particular attention to 
five areas.
1. The forecast. Once you accept your position, you receive one or 
more sales targets (quota). Usually, you will have a three- to six-
month grace period with zero quota and then ramp up to a full 
target. If you inherit an active territory with run-rate business or 
existing consumption, you may immediately start on or close to 
quota. You can negotiate this. From day one, you need to adjust 
to building a pipeline of business and forecasting opportunities. A 
forecast is a commitment to the company that you will bring in 
revenue, and most converted SEs are either wildly optimistic or

---

## Page 321

300 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Moving into Sales 301
ultraconservative in their first year. Forecasting as an SE is easy, 2 
as you have minimal accountability if you are wrong; missing your 
forecast as a salesperson has serious repercussions. 
  Seek your sales manager’s guidance before committing to any 
large deals, and run a reality check with the local SE manager to 
ensure you have not missed any steps. Bear in mind that your sales 
manager may also be under pressure to forecast their number, and 
that pressure cascades downhill onto their reps. 
CASE STUDY: Dealing with Legal
Every customer will insist on a full legal review of any contract. Sometimes it 
will be on your paperwork. More often, it will be through theirs. This means 
that two legal teams become involved, which causes much back-and-forth over 
terms and arcane language that you will find frustrating. It pays to be creative, 
friendly, and patient with your legal team. MTS is a small business, so we are 
often at the mercy of our larger customers. This story is an amusing example of 
how the creative problem-solving abilities of the SE can come into play—even 
with lawyers. Our client sent us a contract, and paragraph 6(d) granted them the 
rights, under certain conditions, to re-use our intellectual property internally 
after just a single workshop with no further payments. The conversation went 
as follows.
 Us: “We cannot sign that. Paragraph 6(d) can be interpreted as meaning that 
you can deliver subsequent workshops with our material at zero cost.”
 Client: “Ignore it. It is poorly written, and we would never enforce it.”
 Us: “We cannot do that. A contract is a contract. So why not remove that 
clause?” 
 Client: “Oh no. It requires the approval of our chief legal officer to remove 
anything from the standard contract. That might take months. You have to sign 
it if you want our business.”
 Us (pausing and going into SE mode): “Wait, if you cannot remove anything, 
can you modify or add to the contract?”
 Client: “Yes. We make those changes all the time.”
 Us: “Can you add a paragraph 6(e) that says you agree never to enforce 6(d)?”
 Client: “Yes, we can.”
E E E E
2. Prospecting. Potentially one of the least pleasant parts of sales is cold 
calling. That is the act of calling a company and asking to speak with 
buyers with whom you have no prior contact. Most SEs have no 
experience with this task, so make some friends in the telemarket-
2. Although some companies now ask their first-line SE managers to forecast incre-
mental consumption in parallel with their sales counterparts.

---

## Page 322

300 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Moving into Sales 301
ing or outbound sales team and learn from them. Although most 
mature sales organizations now have massive lead generation pro-
grams (see Chapters 2 and 3) led by corporate and field marketing 
teams, sometimes working the phones/email/social media is the 
only way to break into a stubborn account. Even though it is hard 
to pick up the phone when you face a 97% rejection rate, don’t au-
tomatically default to electronic communications, as that is a bad 
habit. Sometimes you still need to work the territory physically. 
On the other hand, you benefit from knowing how the product 
functions and covering multiple use cases and references that most 
salespeople cannot.
3. Leads. A new salesperson will typically have few leads and even 
fewer active opportunities on which to work. Therefore, it is easy 
to aggressively follow up on all leads in this position, whether 
qualified or not. Although this is good practice and training for 
you, wasting other people’s time, especially your former SE col -
leagues, is a surefire way to consume whatever goodwill you may 
have. 
4. Discounting. Competing on price, especially in smaller transactions, 
is a common mistake. SEs, because they have little exposure to 
contracts and price schedules, tend to be more price sensitive than 
experienced salespeople. Remember the principles of business val-
ue discovery and value engineering. Discounts usually increase, 
rather than decrease, over time, so be mindful of setting unneces -
sary precedents. 
Hint: Account Control Is a Difficult Concept
An attractive part of the sales position is the apparent increase in control during the sales 
cycle. After all, you now set the overall strategy. However, be prepared for the transitory 
nature of control once you face items such as pricing, discounts, legal terms and condi -
tions, and internal revenue recognition. These are the hidden parts of the sales cycle that 
SEs rarely see (and often because SEs think salespeople are lazy because they don’t rec -
ognize the internal selling that occurs to overcome these impediments). Yet, they consume 
hours of the sales week. 
5. Saying no. It is difficult, almost traumatic, to say no to a prospect 
when you are pursuing your first few deals. You are so eager to 
please (the engineer still burns brightly within you) and build the 
relationship that you will give away products, services, and other 
benefits too early in the sales cycle. Unfortunately, converted SEs 
often forget their bad experiences of pointless POCs and out-of-
the-box demos. They offer these to progress the deal rather than

---

## Page 323

302 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Moving into Sales 303
focus on true solution and value selling. I have learned, over time, 
that it is perfectly acceptable to say no (culturally adjusted) to a 
customer and that many customers will keep asking for things until 
they receive their first no. 
Making It All Work
Moving across to sales is hard work, yet it is not all doom and gloom. In ad-
dition to any innate sales skills you own, you have your SE superpowers to 
help you through the process. Success in sales is as much about using your 
strengths as it is filling in your weaknesses. Think about what got you into 
the position where you can even make this transition.
1. You know the products. Rather than professing your technical 
knowledge, use it to be quietly confident. Understanding the port -
folio’s capabilities can keep you out of a lot of trouble, plus quickly 
qualify you into or out of deals. 
2. You can run business value discovery. Unfortunately, a massive 
proportion of salespeople are unable to conduct an entire BVD ses-
sion and gather enough information to satisfy their SE partners.
3. You don’t always have to wait on SE availability. For example, al-
though you may not be doing demos or technical presentations, 
there will be a subset of sales calls where you may not need SE 
help—that can speed up the sales cycle.
4. You can manage your time. Your SE career has taught you how to 
get the most things done in the least amount of time. 
5. You already have contacts. You know people in product marketing 
and management, development, engineering, services, and sup -
port. Ask for help.
6. You can seek advice. Speak with your new sales peers and ask for 
their advice around deal strategy and how to deal with all the inter-
nal hurdles. 
7. You understand the outcomes. You can speak about implementa -
tion successes and therefore walk the customer into the future by 
removing any risk they see in that area.
8. You don’t need slides because you are comfortable with the white-
board and using visual selling.

---

## Page 324

302 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Moving into Sales 303
9. You can learn new things quickly. Although you will have many 
new skills to learn, sales skills do not change as fast as technology. 
You’ll catch up! 
Summary
If your next career goal is to move into sales, start preparing early. Catalog 
your sales skills and be prepared to ask many people for help, advice, and 
honest feedback (which you may not always like to hear). Let local sales 
management and your SE management know about your intent and solicit 
their help. Remember that salespeople love to be sold to themselves, so 
sell yourself hard for the position you want. They will respect you for that 
direct approach. You should certainly set your expectations for a challeng -
ing first year, both psychologically and financially, and rely on your support 
network to get you through many dark days. You are traveling down a road 
that many have successfully navigated before you, so if you persevere and 
have the skills and bit of luck, you will succeed. In keeping with the theme 
of the chapter—may the force be with you. 
Skill Building
For all: 
 1. Check your motivation and revisit your brand.
 2. Work on filling in your skills gaps and continue to work on your strengths.
 3. Constantly ask for help and advice—inside and outside the company .
 4. Volunteer for some junior sales responsibilities.
 5. Review how you handle rejection, distrust, and hearing no.
 6. Practice having conversations with random strangers.

People don’t get promoted for doing their jobs well. 
They get promoted by demonstrating their potential to 
do more.
            —Tara Jayne Frank
A
s I journeyed through the English public 
school system, my teachers told me to let 
my hard work speak for itself. But unfortunately, 
when you are angling for a work promotion, that 
humility may not be enough. While corporate 
ladder climbers do not have a good reputation, 
the truth is that there is nothing wrong with 
wanting to be recognized for your performance. 
If your future depends on a better title or bigger 
salary, being a valuable member of the organiza-
tion is a critical first step. But if you genuinely 
want to stand out as a future star, that hard work, 
even in the most self-effacing culture, requires a 
solid dose of self-promotion and planning to go 
hand in hand.
This chapter examines typical SE organiza -
tional structures and titles and then references 
those positions against essential behavioral and 
functional competencies. It is intended for the 
individual contributor whose goal (short or long 
term) is to “become the best SE that I can be.”
Earlier, I referenced a survey of over 4,100 
SEs. We asked the question, “Thirty months from 
now, what role/position would you (realistically)

structures and roles.

functional competencies.

paths to SE greatness.

306 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Career Progression 307
like to have as the next step in your career?” The results (Table 29.1) show 
that more than 30% responded that they intended to remain in sales 
engineering. 
Some Typical Organizational Structures
Unsurprisingly, the complexity of an SE organizational structure is propor -
tional to the size of the team. A small company may have only a few SEs, 
all at the same level. On the other hand, the largest SE teams at compa -
nies such as Oracle or HP have five or more job levels with a whole hierar -
chy of first-line managers, second-line directors, and regional or global vice 
presidents. Usually, there is a connection point where the most senior SE 
leader(s) reports to their sales counterpart. For example, in multinational 
companies, the sales organization may be split into the Americas (North, 
Central, South), Europe, Middle East, and Africa (EMEA), and Japan, Asia-
Pacific, Australia, and China (JAPAC). Each SE leader of these respective 
geographies reports to their respective local sales leader or general manager. 
A few companies have a separate SE organization, and the global SE leader 
reports to the COO or executive vice president of sales/chief revenue officer. 
There are many positives to this structure. The SE team has a voice and a 
vote at the most senior executive levels and a single decision-making point 
to ensure consistency across the regions.
In terms of growth, the prime/original SE eventually needs help, and 
the team expands to five or six individuals with an SE manager. Shortly after 
that, either for hiring or HR purposes, the role will split into SE and senior 
SE. The company then adds new functions above and below those two basic 
positions. Like everything to do with SE terminology, there are many alter-
native job titles for these roles. An associate SE covers entry-level applicants 
moving into the function directly from a university or a few years of busi -
ness experience. Leadership then creates positions such as principal SE and 
senior or master principal for the highly experienced and tenured SE. These

top two levels are equivalent to a first- or second-line manager in rank, 
compensation, and overall seniority.
The largest companies have an added position at the very top of the 
organizational hierarchy, reserved for the top 0.25% crème-de-la-creme of 
SEs. That is the distinguished engineer, master strategist, or field CTO. This 
position is equivalent to a regional VP level. It takes an exceptional and 
dedicated individual to reach these heights, so sit back and learn if you are 
working with one of these unicorns! 
Hint: It Is About Growth—Not Titles
The basic rule of thumb is that at least one (if not two) additional growth level(s) should 
be available when you take on an SE role. So, if you are joining a team at the highest 
possible HR level, what is your challenge—except proving that you are worth the title? 
Career progression now becomes the match between your talents and 
those that the company believes it needs for any given position. As a first 
step, print out/download the official HR-approved job description for your 
job role and the levels above it. Next, map your talents and skills against the 
requirements, look for gaps and areas where you exceed the requirements. 
Then, make the first move to put a plan in place to address them. The plan 
should align with your trust profile and factor strengths (Chapter 19) and 
your personal brand (Chapter 23).
The following two sections supply some specifics for the behavioral and 
functional competencies expected of top-performing sales engineers.
CASE STUDY: Getting Your Manager’s Buy-in 
Alexa walked into her manager’s office and, after a few minutes of small talk, 
blurted out, “It’s time I was promoted. How do we make that happen?” Her 
manager was somewhat taken aback, as she thought this was something she 
had been working on together with Alexa over the past 12 months. Unfortu -
nately, the conversation did not go well. Alexa felt her talents were unrecog -
nized, and her manager was frustrated and felt that Alexa was ungrateful (and 
not ready for a promotion). A few days later, Alexa had lunch with her mentor 
and received some career counseling. The following week she again walked 
into her manager’s office, apologized for her abrupt behavior, and asked for 
help. “I am seeking a promotion to principal SE, and I’ve printed out the job re-
quirements. I understand you feel there are gaps, and I’d like your explicit and 
direct feedback to identify those, and then perhaps we can put a plan in place 
to remediate them. Also, I understand that the official HR requirements are not 
the only criteria, but that’s a good start.” Everyone felt better and consequently 
made progress. The career advice Alexa received was “The only person 100% 
responsible for your career development is you. It is not learning and develop-
ment, HR, or your manager. It is you.” 
E E E E

---

## Page 329

308 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Career Progression 309
Behavioral Competencies
Behavioral competencies are the softer, or human side, of the job. They re -
late to your personality and enthusiasm as you perform the job. I look at this 
aspect in five major categories, with an optional score rating (Table 29.2) in 
each item ranging from a 1 (needs development) to a 3 (mastered) up to a 
5 (far exceeds). 
Everything that follows is common sense, yet we can all do better.
1. Accountability. Holding self and others accountable for keeping com-
mitments and delivering the best possible performance.
a. Makes no excuses and takes full responsibility for their actions 
and results.
b. Is reliable and can be counted on to do as they say.
c Follows up and monitors progress to ensure that things stay on 
track.
d. Acts with a sense of purpose and sets clear direction for others.
e. Effectively deals with performance challenges and setbacks.
f. Works through ambiguity to maintain momentum and achieve 
progress.
2. Authenticity. Showing a genuine, accessible, and caring side that 
builds trust, mutual support, and credibility.
a. Is consistently approachable and transparent. Refuses to let ego 
undermine relationships.
b. Shows concern and respect for people, their knowledge, and 
their contributions.
c. Remains calm and composed even in stressful circumstances. 
d. Is confident yet also shows grace and humility.

Rating Description

1.5 Falls below requirements
2.0 Meets most requirements

3.0 Meets all requirements satisfactorily
3.5 Sometimes above expectations
4.0 Exceeds expectations
4.5 Consistently well above requirements; can guide others
5.0 Far exceeds requirements; actively guides others

e. Acts ethically and above reproach.
3. Communication. Ensuring that communications channels are open 
and that information and ideas are flowing in all appropriate direc-
tions. 
a. Shares the pertinent information that helps others.
b. Fosters open communications and stays receptive to bad news 
as well as good. 
c. Uses clear, direct, and straightforward communications with 
others.
d. Actively seeks and provides honest and constructive feedback.
e. Encourages constructive dialogue to ensure all opinions and 
possibilities are heard.
4. Excellence. Striving to be the best one can be and maximizing po -
tential in helping [company] succeed.
a. Shows persistence and tenacity in working toward goals.
b. Pursues goals beyond what is typically expected or expressed. 
c. Is results oriented, with a solid drive to accomplish objectives 
and meet high standards.
d. Strives to improve performance and to have a significant im -
pact on our success.
e. Continuously seeks to enhance the quality and detail of ser -
vices, products, and processes.
5. Teamwork. Working in concert with others and creating group syn-
ergy in pursuit of collective goals. 
a. Looks for ways to create organizational collaboration and har -
ness cross-functional efforts.
b. Is consistently cooperative, collegial, and professional in work-
ing with people inside and outside of the immediate team.
c. Supports higher-level agendas and makes it their interest to 
support corporate interests.
d. Respects and relates well to others from varied backgrounds. 
The issue with these softer, professional skills is that they are hard to 
measure accurately, even with a comprehensive metric. However, much 
like the trust factors, what matters most is what other people think, rather 
than how you judge your performance.

Functional Competencies
Functional competencies relate to the job and task-oriented aspects of your 
job. These are the more classic sales and engineering components. I have 
supplied examples when working at the 4.5 or 5.0 level for these skills. Be 
warned, this is a long list across the eight functional competencies, and the 
word proactive occurs in multiple places. Adapt according to your responsi -
bilities and corporate go-to-market philosophy.
1. Account engagement. Develops, supports, and executes strategies to 
establish the right relationships, strategically position [company], 
and maximize revenue within the account based on knowledge 
about and effective navigation of customer organizations. 
a. Is viewed by the customer as part of their core IT strategy team.
b. Develops strategic relationships with multiple senior IT and 
business executives.
c. Positions our solution/portfolio strategically.
d. Leverages a documented account plan that includes other team 
members.
e. Has insider access. 
f. Leads team meetings at the CxO or xVP level. 
2. Competitive selling. Leverages partner, customer, industry, internal, 
and competing product knowledge to position and sell our solu -
tions.
a. Anticipates competitive situations and works with account 
manager/partner to develop and execute proactive strategies to 
avoid competitive bake-offs.
b. Trains and prepares other account team members to avoid in -
appropriate conversations and focus on the true competitive 
differentiators.
c. Seeks out and creates a strong presence for [company] in critical 
industry showcases and events.
d. Attracts, hires, and develops individuals known to be 
competitive.
e. Masters content in all sales tools (whiteboards, guides, sales play 
sheets) and seamlessly incorporates them into various scenarios 
and conversations.

Hint: Ask Your Customers
One of the best sources of competitive data is your customer base. Rather than asking 
for a direct head-to-head comparison (which you will rarely receive), ask instead about 
what your competitor did well in their demo or presentation and what stuck in the head 
of your customer. 
3. Consultative selling. Uses business acumen to strategically position 
our portfolio to the customer and/or position us as a strategic 
partner to the channel. Understands the customer’s implicit and 
explicit needs, provides advice and consultation regarding the best 
approach to addressing them, and positions us as the ideal solution. 
a. Works collaboratively with a partner to understand business 
opportunities and help develop implementation strategies us -
ing our technology. 
b. Works with the customer to develop a compelling enterprise-
wide business justification.
c. Validates needs and suggests various options. Recommends 
a “best choice” based on the customer’s current and future 
requirements. 
d. Articulates and presents to the full scale of a customer orga -
nization how we address their specific needs and priorities to 
create real business value.
4.  Opportunity management. Develops and executes plans to pursue 
and win opportunities by understanding and proactively leading 
the customer buying process. 
a. Establishes detailed technical account plans. Includes relation -
ship, competitive, and product strategies.
b. Consults with all other team members and includes them in the 
opportunity strategy.
c. Leverages a complete team to anticipate and proactively ad -
dress customer concerns and problems.
d. Leads regular team meetings to discuss the state, next steps, 
and necessary modifications to the plan.
e. Maintains or increases the velocity of opportunity through the 
sales and buying cycles.
5. Orchestrating assets.  Develops collaborative, engaged, customer-
focused teams of [company] and [partner] assets to deliver high-
quality solutions and services to the customer.

The Many Paths to Greatness
Should you choose to remain and grow on the standard SE path and not 
divert into management, sales, or other job roles, there are many ways to 
get there from here. The three most common are as follows:
1. To specialize in one technology and stay focused (analytics, backup 
and recovery, firewalls);
2. To specialize in a vertical or industry (media, financial services, 
pharmaceuticals);
3. To specialize in being a sales engineer and be a generalist across 
multiple technologies or verticals.
Every company needs the technical specialist. They are that one person 
who always has the answer to every technical question and can go head 
to head with the customer’s technical experts to gain their respect. Get -
ting to this level requires years of dedication, learning, and innate curios -
ity. A certain proportion of deals require a technical specialist and will not 
close without their engagement. Liam Mahoney, a senior SE and long-time

---

## Page 335

314 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Career Progression 315
technical specialist, explains it best: “Not everyone is cut out for this kind of 
role, as it goes far beyond the generalist level of knowledge to the Malcolm 
Gladwell 10,000 hours level of mastery. We provide a wealth of knowledge 
and expertise on demand that other SEs might have to go learn or build.”
The industry specialist or vertical specialist is essential once a company grows 
and sees significant penetration in a particular market segment. This indi -
vidual then becomes a thought leader within the company (and potentially 
externally) in linking the portfolio to specific industry trends and challenges. 
This path is appealing to those with prior industry experience, and you will 
commonly see individuals recruited directly from the customer base estab -
lish themselves in these positions. You can, however, pick up this knowl -
edge on the fly as you opportunistically work on sales transactions in the 
same vertical area. For example, during my career at Oracle and Sybase, I 
learned enough about the pharmaceuticals and financial services verticals to 
become at least industry-knowledgeable, if not an expert. One of the value-
add benefits of this type of position is that you may become the creator of a 
new product or service for that vertical if you see the need and can convince 
product management and development. 
Finally, there is the generalist. I think of the generalist as another special-
ist position. The specialty is in making it all make sense and linking together 
disparate pieces of technology. That is a unique skill set, and although more 
than 75% of SEs start in generalist positions, the breadth of the portfolio 
and learning requirements often defeat them, and they’ll move onward. 
Pabel Martin, a generalist SE in the United Kingdom, explains: “The cross-
pollination of ideas and concepts that we bring to an organization is critical 
for continued growth. Typically, we learn new things relatively quickly and 
have a wide range of knowledge on many topics and experiences. Over time 
that makes us valuable (and employable) across many different situations, 
organizations, roles, and geographies.”
Summary
The sales engineering job prepares you for many other roles and responsibil-
ities within the company. In some ways, it is a generalist position in that you 
gain exposure to every other part of the company. Growing and developing 
within the SE ranks can prepare you for many different career choices, in -
cluding staying on the technical track and moving up the SE hierarchy. Do 
not get overly focused on the pure HR definition of the various job levels. 
Although they are important, the functional and behavioral competencies 
are ultimately what will drive your eventual promotion.

---

## Page 336

314 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Career Progression 315
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Access and download the official HR job descriptions.
 2. Map your skillset(s) to your current role and the subsequent promotional role.
 3. Delay any specialist/generalist decision until you understand the implications.
 4. Score yourself against the functional and behavioral competencies.
For the experienced SE:
 1. Where do you score a 4.0 or above in the competencies?
 2. Address any gaps (realistically a 3.0 or lower).
 3. Is there a benefit in switching roles (specialist/generalist), not just at your current 
    employer, but in the future?
 4. Y our next step is not necessarily management. Look at all the options.
For everyone:
 1. Document the great things you’ve done. Collect stories, anecdotes, and metrics to prove 
    your value. 
 2. Always have an ongoing career development discussion with your manager. Please don’t 
    assume that they know what you want or expect.

## Page 339

318 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Becoming an Effective Subject Matter Expert/SE Specialist  319
The Drivers of SE Specialization
SE teams of subject matter experts face a high bar. First, they are presumed 
to be the steward of superior knowledge on industry trends, competitors, 
best practices, use cases, and customer outcomes—all in addition to their 
offerings. Second, specialists generally are (incorrectly) expected to service 
opportunities rather than help create them, are usually rich on opportunities 
while poor on capacity, and, as a primary account team often defines them, 
are frequently poorly positioned1 with buyers. Third, account teams believe 
they bring excellently qualified opportunities to the SME when often that 
isn’t actually the case. 
Hint: Opportunity Qualification Is Like Learning
Just like learning, business qualification never ends. Qualification is the responsibility of 
everyone given the gift of customer interaction. However, at some point, qualification needs 
to pause. It pauses at the point where the team feels it has the detail sufficient to produce 
a sound proposal (see Chapters 10–13), then picks up once more through to closure and 
onto value realization.
Today, SE leaders are scrambling to build out specialist organizations for 
many reasons:
• They are responding to an insatiable demand presented both by cus-
tomers and vendors’ new go-to-market strategies. 
• Technology consolidations through acquisitions are broadening ven-
dor portfolios to a point beyond the technical reach of an account SE. 
• Driven by demands to lead and grow cloud and XaaS offerings, spe -
cialist SE teams are established to support new selling partners. 
• Industry verticals are expecting that their unique outcomes are solu-
tioned by knowledgeable vendor experts.
• Vendors needing to safeguard their position as market leaders require 
long-term thinkers aligned with buyer architects with the remit to 
future-proof enterprises three to five years out. 
Specialists/SMEs are critical for all these strategies. Those same SE lead-
ers address these trends while also facing internal demands to contain costs 
and meet acceptable expense to booking ratio (E2B) ratios. 2 Once in play, 
specialist teams face external challenges:
1. Account SEs have been known to offload key activities to specialists mostly to 
manage their own workload but sometimes for more nefarious reasons.
2. As sales organizations are run as an expense team rather than a profit center, 
sales leaders are being held to balance their staff costs to higher levels of

---

## Page 340

318 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Becoming an Effective Subject Matter Expert/SE Specialist  319
• Customers expect specialists to be fully coached by the primary ac -
count holder team members and understand their role or value. 
• Customers can be hesitant to invest in a new relationship. 
• Selling partners can become impatient, believing adding others may 
delay the sale.3
• Different compensation plans with varying motivations and SPIFs 
can create competition for the same customer spend.4
Hint: Seize More Ground
As a specialist/SME, and with the higher presumed value from buyers in general from the 
technical team, use your industry expert position to bring additional customer stakehold-
ers into the opportunity. Adding these contacts will not delay the sale but will expand the 
sales team’s reach into the customer with you and increase win probability.
All these conflicts are often played out right in front of the customer 
and can combine to create a contentious environment that disrupts the 
most critical goal: serving the best outcome for the customer.
Hint: The Specialists’ Dilemma
Internal business units are thrilled to have their own technical sales teams dedicated to 
their products. And that is the dilemma; they can think of you as their own technical 
sales teams. Think about the time you spend with customers versus the time you spend on 
internal activities. As a specialist, if you are not devoting about 60% of your time with 
customers with the next most significant chunk of time on your development, it is time to 
reprioritize your calendar. 
Becoming the Subject Matter Expert
You can staff specialist teams from a variety of sources:
• Long tenured SEs looking to direct their focus to specific technology 
domains rather than becoming a less technical generalist;
recognizable bookings.
3. Adding others with no value might just delay the sale. But if those new on the 
sales team access new customer personae, the likelihood of closing the sale in -
creases 5% for each new personae added. Source: 2021 People.AI analytics. See 
https://bit.ly/3xrQKZD.
4. “Is it hardware?” “No! It’s software,” “Wrong! It’s XaaS!” and all get paid differ-
ently on how the fixed customer spend ultimately books.

• Cross-functional transfers from professional services and product 
management looking or asked to consider a career change;
• Academy SEs new to the SE profession, often in their first job from 
university, focusing on demos and POCs;
• Recruits from the industry with deep business domain experience, 
but new to selling and competitive threats. 
Various Types of SME Teams and Positions
Here are a few common SME/SE specialization teams you may want to con-
sider if you are going to become an SME: 
1. The sales channel SE. These SEs are assigned to a set of value-added 
distributors (VARs), managed service partners (MSPs), distributors/
large account resellers (LARs), vendors with your embedded prod-
ucts (e.g., public cloud providers), or SIs. The sales channel SE’s 
job is to influence these selling partners and enable their technical 
teams to choose your company over your competitors as their de -
fault solution within their own go-to-market strategy.
2. A deep product SE. Proficient in the detailed use cases, integration 
points, and “-ilities ”5 of a specific product set within your com -
pany’s portfolio. The SE must maintain and grow the adoption of 
new features and advantages of this product with customers.
3. An SE of a newly acquired company. Now out of your post-integration 
swim lanes, you are working with core selling teams to ensure that 
your new employer’s strategy in acquiring you is successful. You 
enable core sellers and partners with use cases and success stories, 
and you are called in as the irrefutable expert to move a deal for -
ward, run a POC, or magic wand6 faltering sales motions. This path 
might not be a choice for you in becoming an SME. However, inte-
gration plans may demand you become one.
4. An SE among the breed of consulting engineers or enterprise architects. An 
SE to bring a broader view of industry trends, assuming an archi -
tectural and platform point of view, bringing a deep understanding 
of technology domains, all to build mindshare with customers and 
establish your company as a thought leader without peer in the 
industry.
5. The “…ilities”: reliability, maintainability, useability, scalability, availability, ex -
tensibility, supportability, and so on.
6. Expecting you to magically solve problems and progress the deal.

---

## Page 342

320 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Becoming an Effective Subject Matter Expert/SE Specialist  321
5. An SE focused solely on an industry vertical,  like hospitals/healthcare, 
telcoms, education, financial services, or government. In addition 
to all the skills mentioned here, the industry expert brings a deep 
and intimate understanding of the business operations, notewor -
thy trends, and service architectures specific to these industries.
In many ways, the SE owning a territory or named accounts teamed 
with “first dollar ”7 sales reps are a specialist/SME themselves, expected to 
bring insight into the buyer stakeholder map. These SEs understand the 
technical and business hurdles necessary to move the opportunity more 
quickly through the sales stages. And, they do everything else that is within 
these pages.
In some staffing models, these generalist SEs carry a specialization for 
their larger territory; in other models, generalists are asked to specialize en-
tirely and focus solely on one segment of their company’s portfolio.
Such is the state of the maturing nature of the SE profession. As with 
many other technical professions, from automobile mechanics to physi -
cians, science and computing power bring advances that no individual can 
master. And just as with those mechanics and physicians who long prided 
themselves on their capability to diagnose and solve every issue, they now 
need to reconcile themselves to solving those issues as a team.
Team Technical Selling—and Why It’s Hard to Do
The ante8 for being a specialist/SME is your technical excellence and grow-
ing industry knowledge within your area of specialization. You can achieve 
that ante through your technical enablement plan and by paying attention 
to customers on why they adopt or do not adopt your products and services. 
CASE STUDY: Customers Are the Ones Spending the Money
When I was an account SE, a specialist SE came to me and told me that I was 
their “customer.” They told me that they were there to serve my needs and to 
ensure I was successful. It was meant to be flattering. But I wasn’t a customer 
of theirs. Customers write contracts to buy things. Customers spend money; 
customers solve business problems with the things of ours that they buy. So 
7. “First dollar” teams typically refers to the sales executive/SE with primary re -
sponsibilities and the discrete quota for an account within a defined territory. 
This sales executive has the responsibility of negotiating, closing, booking, and 
ensuring the revenue recognition of the sales contract.
8. In poker, a forced bet, equal in amount for all players, before cards are dealt to 
simply play the game.

---

## Page 343

322 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Becoming an Effective Subject Matter Expert/SE Specialist  323
I responded: “I am not your customer; I’m your sales partner. We each have a 
job to do to make our customer and our company successful.” I learned my first 
lesson about SE team selling.
E E E E
Hint: Why Your Experience Needn’t Always Be Your Experience
While you are expected to have strong technical skills within your domain and bring 
experience in how customers will be successful with your products, you needn’t wait until 
you have all this experience yourself. As part of your ongoing enablement plan, include 
other SMEs to dialogue what they have learned from customers. Learn the good and the 
bad, successful use cases, and why customers may have failed with the root cause9 of those 
failures.
SEs are known for their willingness to share experiences, internal rela -
tionships, and even their time to support another SE in need. However, they 
are not coached on how to sell as a team. Instead, specialist selling strate -
gies are crafted in an ideal world far from customers and whose flaws are 
exposed once confronted with the three-headed beast of sales urgency, silos, 
and account control.
Sales urgency is a noble call to action when used in the context of 
matching our selling activity and responsiveness to a customer’s appetite to 
consume our deliverables. In the context of team selling, “lacking sales ur -
gency” is a blaming tactic intended to exclude the potentially offending team 
member, usually because of a misunderstood strategy or a flawed process. 
More extensive, complex opportunities require a balance between precise 
account planning with reasonable, shared expectations for each team mem-
ber, the patience necessary to execute the plan, and the agility to adapt to a 
customer pace to exceed their expectations. Proper, inclusive sales planning 
eliminates the need to be engaged solely by a CRM-based process.
Silos push us to our corners, 10 birthing competing and high-minded 
strategies that only increase the sense of separateness within the team. Silos 
of capable team members operating at their best can produce exceptional 
innovation. Unfortunately, the silos pushing us to the corners are devel -
oped by narrow perspectives that keep sales teams from serving a greater 
outcome. 
9. Root cause: the core issue that sets in motion all other cause-and-effect reactions. 
When seeking to understanding a problem, challenge yourself on whether you 
reached the root cause of the problem.
10. From the sport of boxing, where in between fighting the boxers retreat to the 
rope corners to recover from the beating they’re receiving.

## Page 344

322 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Becoming an Effective Subject Matter Expert/SE Specialist  323
CASE STUDY: Presume Trust
”Presume Trust” was the motto of the global sales leader when launching the 
new specialist organization. Presuming trust is a call for civility as you earn trust 
with others. “Show me your compensation plan, and I’ll tell you how you be-
have,” was the motto of my sales leader at a former company. Presuming trust 
and then seeing someone acting solely in the interest of their compensation 
plan is a fatal blow to that trust. Developing teamwide selflessness in pursuit of 
achieving the best outcome for customers is an amplifier of trust.11
E E E E
Obsessive account control stifles collaboration, results in teams reas -
sessing each others’ motives and value, and most fatally ends all cross-team, 
bilateral coaching. While we think of egoistical account control as the pur -
view of sales, it manifests itself within any team member, usually driven by 
internal pressures or processes. 
Adding more voices effectively to the opportunity chase team is hard. 
But when the opportunity is complex, we don’t win it by doing the easy 
things. And we certainly don’t win fooling ourselves into thinking it’s just 
easier to do it ourselves than to ask for help.
Hint: Receive and Give Coaching
All SEs, and especially specialist SEs, benefit from becoming better coaches. Since the spe-
cialist’s contribution to other selling partners and customers might be tightly orchestrated 
and trust levels are just beginning to build, leading with a cross-functional discovery/
coaching mindset can have better results than the typical SME/professorial approach.
Team Technical Selling—the Effective SME
Team SE selling is imperative to strengthen further the value buyers have 
in the technical team, expand customer touch to qualify the business fit for 
your technology better, add to your win likelihood, 12 and grow the deal 
size. Simply put, we must train ourselves to shed the old habits taught from 
the three-headed beast and adopt new inclusive, collaborative practices 
within a culture of effective coaching. 
Consider the MTS concept of Seven Sins in Team Selling . These sins are 
not committed by the primary account SE, the product specialist SE, or 
11. Care, J., The Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer, John Care, 2020.
12. op. cit. People.AI analytics on adding customer stakeholders. See https://bit.
ly/3xrQKZD.

---

## Page 345

324 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Becoming an Effective Subject Matter Expert/SE Specialist  325
the partner SE but by the entire technical selling team. Still, because of the 
focused activity of SMEs, specialists teams need to be more aware of them.
The Seven Sins of Team Selling
First is the foundation. “I’m special.” Special? Yes. Separate, no. Challenge 
those who insist that the specialists need a particular sales process, define 
different sales stages, or need different skills enablement. This separateness 
wastes time as you rationalize: “that’s not the way we do it” with other sell-
ing partners. What’s better? One team, one process, and a common sales 
language. This entire book applies to SMEs as it does for all SEs.
1. Waiting for the phone to ring.  Looking to a process or the CRM to 
engage the specialist. What’s better? Reach out and educate both 
specialists and core teams in advance so you help define yourself 
rather than others. Own your pipeline; don’t outsource your suc -
cess to others. 
2. Risky business. Engaging without a clear customer business objec -
tive—or a fuzzy objective. What’s better? Work with customers 
to mature a defined objective with business purpose and impact. 
Qualification usually is declared “done” way too soon. What’s bet-
ter? Learn to read the qualification as sufficient to move ahead with 
a good ROI, then subtly requalify as you move ahead with new 
customer stakeholders. Discovery never ends. It just stops.
3. The standard eval. Taking a meeting, agreeing to a demo, starting a 
standard POC just like others and not tailored to what they need to 
see. It annoys everyone. What’s better? Fit the evaluation to achieve 
the required customer outcomes in the shortest amount of time. 
4. The puppy dog POC. Load and go and assume the demo, assessment, 
or POC will go fine. Like a puppy will train themselves (they don’t). 
What’s better? Project plan the effort, calendar ongoing stakeholder 
contact, stay available, follow up, manage time expenditures and 
risks, and drive to the results you believe need to be achieved. 
5. Nobody listens to me. Perhaps the SME is not saying things in the right 
way to the right people. Such a lack of clarity could include cus -
tomers, engineering, selling partners, or external partners. What’s 
better? Stop talking and extend your discovery skills more broadly. 
Build trust by listening, understanding, and showing empathy.
6. Not my job to find more customer stakeholders. The SME is content 
to work with whoever the sales plan says they should. What’s bet-
ter? You have a position of presumed value and are recognized as 
the expert within your specialization. Many want to hear from you.

---

## Page 346

324 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Becoming an Effective Subject Matter Expert/SE Specialist  325
Walk through those open doors and talk to those you find essential 
to the deal that the sales plan didn’t even know existed. A stranger 
may be the critical stakeholder you haven’t yet met.13
7. BFFs are (not) forever.  Assuming a “sale” will stay sold on and on 
without your involvement. Very dangerous when you are paid on 
recurring revenue and not bookings at the time of sale. The cost 
of acquiring a new customer is enormous compared to keeping an 
existing one.14 What’s better? Stay close to customer onboarding, 
conduct SE/SME-led quarterly value reviews to ensure your stuff 
is the stuff that keeps giving.
Summary
The SE role as an SME is a critical source of selling influence. However, its 
responsibilities are very complex, serving many different functions that can 
cause SMEs and their organizations to falter. 
Habits are those things that we do over and over again until they be -
come instinctual. But, just like the three-headed beast has pushed us to 
habits in committing these sins, we can build new habits starting with a 
focus on customer value. 
Skill Building
For the new SE specialist/SME: 
 1. As an SME, you are still an SE. Grow skills and habits detailed in all other chapters.
 2. Surround yourself with mentors knowledgeable in uses cases, success stories, and 
    pitfalls with your specific subject.
 3. Fight back when others suggest you only play in certain sales stages. Instead, you serve 
    the customer’s outcomes across all sales stages.
 4. Own your territory , your opportunities, and your pipeline. Accordingly , engage other 
    technical sellers earlier than a process might prescribe.
For the experienced SE specialist or SE specialist manager:
 1. Become a model team member.
 2. Ensure coaching skills are on your development plan.
 3. Grow your curiosity of business outcomes realized by your subject area. Build a list of 
    these use cases. Please share it!
 4. Engage other technical selling partners with humility backed by your personal brand. 
    (See Chapter 23, “Creating Y our Personal Brand. ”)
13. Paraphrased from “A stranger is just a friend you haven’t met,” Roy E. Stolworthy.
14. It costs five times as much to replace a lost customer than to retain them 
before they leave. From several sources, including Bain Capital and  
Consero, 2020 summarized at https://www.europeanbusinessreview.com/is-  
acquiring-new-customers-more-expensive-than-keeping-them.

H
iring the right people is the most important 
job of the first-line manager. Therefore, it is 
critical to examine the process from both sides 
of the interview desk. On one side, there is the 
hiring manager. They are undoubtedly under 
pressure from both sales and their leadership to 
fill an open headcount. On the other side of the 
desk is the prospective employee, seeking reas -

right manager for them over the next few years. 
It is a complex emotional dance—as everyone 
has something critical at stake. 
In this chapter, we will briefly examine the 
process from the standpoint of the hiring man -
ager and then switch sides and provide the pro -
spective new SE employee with some tips and 
guidelines to ensure they make the best decision.
