The Hiring Process—Manager View
When looking at the sales engineer lifecycle, I use the mnemonic RADAR. 1 
Created explicitly for SE leadership, it means recruit, attract, develop, ad -
vance, and retain. Recruit and attract are the managerial equivalents of 
generating a sales pipeline and then moving to a win. Every SE leader is 
expected to be operating at full headcount and has a bench or channel of 
potential candidates. Given the perennial global shortage of SEs, that is a 
tough proposition. 
It is not just a matter of hiring people. It is a matter of hiring the right 
people. Mistakes are expensive, both in terms of time and money. Tony 
Hsieh, CEO of Zappos (now part of Amazon), once estimated that his bad 
hires had cost his company over $100 million. My customers tell me that 
the cost of losing a good SE is between $3.5 million and $6.0 million, and 
that the cost of making a bad hire who leaves after a year is equivalent to 
their annual target. That is independent of any soft effects around morale 
and workload. 
The Job Description
The human resources department, or a fellow manager, may have an al -
ready completed a job description, in which case use that as your starting 
point. I’ll assume you are starting with nothing but a blank sheet of (elec -
tronic) paper. Create a simple three-by-three grid (see Table 31.1) and label 
the middle column Technical and the right column as Business. Then add both 
a Requirements and a Pluses (nice to have) row. Then fill in each quadrant 
with aspects of the job profile.
Once you have constructed the outline profile, you can use it as the 
basis to build a set of discrete job requirements for the position. This job 
description is an area where you should call on the help of your HR or re -
cruiting partner. You can also research job postings on online recruiting sites, 
LinkedIn, and your competitor’s websites. In addition, many countries have 
an official government site which will supply further details about sales en-
gineering—although their definition may be broader than yours. Examples 
are the U.S. Department of Labor2 and the U.K. Graduates Prospects3 site. 
If you are working from an existing (mandatory) job description, then 
prioritize the requirements. For example, I have used green, yellow, and red 
highlighters to differentiate between the necessities and the nice to haves for 
any given position. 
1. Care, J., The Sales Engineer Manager’s Handbook, Chapter 10, John Care.
2. See https://www.bls.gov/ooh/sales/sales-engineers.htm.
3. See https://www.prospects.ac.uk/job-profiles/technical-sales-engineer.

---

## Page 350

328 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 329
Hint: Beware of the Standard Requirements
Do not automatically include requirements such as “four-year college degree” or “five 
years of experience with Amazon EC2.” Instead, question why you need such conditions 
rather than copying them from others. Similarly, ask yourself if “excellent communica -
tions skills” should even make the list. 
The Real Job Description
There is always a gap between the official externally facing job description 
and what an SE actually does in a typical month. Therefore, the hiring man-
ager should take the stance that you are filling the actual position rather 
than the ideal position. One starting point is to note down what you would 
expect the SE to do in a typical week or month (accepting there is no such 
thing as a typical day). Of course, examples will vary depending upon the 
breadth of portfolio, account type, and so on. Still, they will generally look 
like “Be able to present the product to an audience of 20 technicians,” or 
“Be able to perform systems analysis during the discovery phase.” 
You can use some of the functional and behavioral competencies de -
scribed in Chapter 29 as input into the job description or the roles and re -
sponsibilities section. However, please don’t create a job role with 22 4 key 
responsibilities! 
CASE STUDY: The Sales Engineer Value Proposition
Martina and her local SE leadership team were struggling to fill 15 open head -
counts across Europe. It was a challenging competitive environment. Although 
they managed to get many candidates into the interview process through an 
aggressive recruiting outreach program, their ability to attract and hire them 
was poor. They were losing out to other technology vendors, in some cases 
their direct competitors and partners. A quick review of the overall compensa-
tion and benefits package showed that money was not the issue. The candidates 
never found a compelling reason why they should work for Martina’s company. 
She decided to create a sales engineer value proposition (SEVP) to sell the posi-
tions and have a consistent message across the team. Within a week, they built 
4. I see this all the time. Examine the math. If there are truly 22 keys, then in any 
workweek you can devote 2 hours to that key responsibility. There is no focus. It 
looks bad and does not support a value proposition.
Table 31.1 
Job Description Matrix—Starter Version
Technical Business
Requirements VMware Certified Professional 50% travel; finance, brokerage, 
and insurance experience
Pluses C++/Javascript; advanced VCP Second language (Spanish)

---

## Page 351

330 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 331
a framework (and collateral) that answered the question, “Why is this a great 
place to work?” The SEVP covered the five areas of teamwork, rewards and 
recognition, development, coaching, and productivity. It was an instant hit! By 
formalizing “Why this is a great place to work,” and answering that question in 
the mind of the prospective SE, they moved enough candidates into the attract 
phase. Eventually, they hired sufficient SEs to fill their headcount. Plus, within 
three months, both the Americas and JAPAC SE leadership teams adopted the 
SEVP, and the HR team, to their credit, also jumped on board and created their 
version for software engineers. 
E E E E
Looking in All the Usual and Unusual Places 
In addition to the standard channels, you can seek out candidates internally 
or externally by using references or word of mouth. Depending on com -
pany policy, you may have to post all job openings internally before look -
ing outside the company. Internal candidates can appear from the strangest 
places, but I have had the most remarkable success rate taking employees 
from the customer service and product engineering departments. I rational-
ize that by assuming that hotline personnel have the people skills with some 
product knowledge, while an engineer has product knowledge and some 
people skills. Both have a strong desire to meet the people who are buying 
and using their technology. Transfers from marketing and professional ser -
vices seem to have less success. To expand on that, former consultants can 
become amazing SEs, once they realize their role is to solve and sell rather 
than educate.
External candidates enter the pipeline (the recruit phase) either via 
some form of intake media such as your website, LinkedIn, or CareerBuilder 
equivalent or else via a recruiter. That may be an internal recruiter, or an 
external paid search, or a freelance recruiter. My advice for the hiring man-
ager is to set up an SLA with your HR/recruiting team to respond to resumes 
within 48 hours. Speed is of the essence in the current economy, and wait -
ing 7–14 days to contact a potential employee means that a more aggressive 
and organized company will beat you. Tracking your candidate pipeline is 
an essential SE leadership metric and holds the recruiting team accountable. 
Hint: SE References Are Golden
The SE network and rumor mills are some of the most efficient in the company. You should 
constantly be tapping into this network and seeking out referrals from the current SE 
team. Referrals from the salespeople are also precious, so don’t neglect that source. Your 
company may also sweeten the pot by offering referral bonuses for a successful hire. One of 
my former SEs made an additional €20,000 over two years through referrals—enough to 
pay for a nice summer family vacation each year.

---

## Page 352

330 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 331
CASE STUDY: The Power of Referrals
Dr. John Sullivan,5 known as the father of HR metrics, states that internal refer-
rals are hired 10–15 days faster than outside candidates, have 33% better reten-
tion after twelve months on the job, and comprise 46% of all top-performing 
hires at companies. He also cites evidence that internal referrals promote em -
ployee diversity and are the best source for hiring quality. 
 One geography within a leading cloud services provider in our industry re -
cruited 55% of nearly 300 SEs using internal referrals. Meeting the definition 
of “this is a great place to work”! 
E E E E
The Interview—Manager Side
Preparation is everything in the interview process (which may span several 
weeks and many interactions). Just as you would expect the candidate to be 
fully prepared, the candidate wishes the same for your organization. Have a 
plan and a process, which is more than sending out the candidate’s resume 
to all involved. Larger companies have a formal and documented approach 
to the hiring process to show fairness and that diversity, equity, and inclu -
sion (DEI) guidelines are followed 100%. The number one piece of advice 
in this environment is to keep contemporaneous notes. 
There are many items in the manager’s control.
1. Share any notes from prior interactions with a recruiter and video 
prescreening.
2. Share the candidate’s resume and LinkedIn profile. Also, note any 
public blogs and other professional social media (e.g., Twitter). 
3. Ensure everyone the candidate meets has a goal and focus for their 
meeting. There is nothing more annoying than being asked the 
same question four times by four different people unless you are 
being tested for consistency. 
4. Share a scorecard and rubric so that you can capture an objective 
score.
5. Leave room for subjective gut-feel input as well.
6. Decide how much of the process you wish to share with the candi-
date.
5. “A War for Talent,” https://drjohnsullivan.com.

---

## Page 353

332 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 333
Unlike the classic job interview of yesteryear, the SE interview is now a 
two-way affair, and you should expect to receive as many questions as you 
ask of the candidate. You may also introduce a 30-90-180 onboarding plan 
(Chapter 22) and an SE value proposition document, especially if you are 
moving into the attract part of the recruitment cycle. 
Hint: Sharing Is Caring.
You should provide a clear view of your company’s hiring process to the candidate. This 
explanation includes the number of interview steps and the timing of those steps. Ensure 
that matches the candidate’s plans and expectations. 
In terms of actual interview structure, I’d suggest that most of your 
questions be situational, such as “Tell me about a deal when . . . ,” or “What 
happened the last time you had a conflict with a salesperson.”  One of the 
critical performance components of the interview is the presentation phase. 
Typically, this involves a 10–15-minute presentation on a topic of their 
choosing. If viable candidates select a subject with domain expertise, this 
process allows you to focus on their communication abilities. Over the years, 
I have seen presentations on such varied topics ranging from the intricacies 
of Kubernetes to the far more intriguing “etiquette when having tea with 
the Queen,” “the merits of the 4-4-2 formation in soccer,” and “the perfect 
BBQ.” 
Bring three or four people into the room, preferentially including a sales-
person and an SE peer. Assign roles and encourage the audience to ask ques-
tions without being too aggressive. Pose at least one challenging question 
to each candidate. Nervous or highly confident candidates should be stress-
tested with more aggressive questions to mimic real-life situations. There is 
nothing to gain by making your interview process too easy, although the 
other extreme of constant interruption may not show your corporate cul -
ture in the best light. A typical stress factor is to tell the candidate that the 
projector or display screen is broken, hand them a set of pens, and ask for a 
whiteboard pitch. Bonus points if they bring their own set of marker pens or 
are ready with their favorite visual drawing app.
Hint: Enter IRL (In Real Life) Mode
We know that discovery is an essential element to set up the rest of the sales cycle. Yet more 
than 90% of SE interviews never put this SE skill set to the test. If it is important, you 
should test for it.

---

## Page 354

332 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 333
The Hiring Process—Candidate View
So, what should you do when you are on the other side of the desk? Mim-
icking the manager aspect of the interview process—preparation is every -
thing. You certainly want to portray an efficient, effective, and organized 
persona through all your interactions. As a starting point, you must have 
a professional resume 6 that focuses as much on outcomes and results as 
it does on qualifications and skills. The resume should match your online 
professional business profile on sites like LinkedIn. You should then be pre-
pared for a variety of calls and meetings as you move through the process. 
The Screening Call
This call is usually the first person-to-person interaction once your resume/
application has been processed and passed through the first human and bot 
filters. Anything you can do to build a relationship with this gatekeeper is 
a plus.
1. To whom are you speaking? Is it a recruiter or (rarely) the hiring 
manager? Check out their public profile and look for any com -
monalities or talking points.
2. Prepare to supply a drill-down on any accomplishments or num -
bers in your resume.
3. Have some stories ready to humanize your achievements. No one 
remembers plain facts and figures. Also, create stories for areas 
of weakness. For example, Ramzi Marjaba, host of We The Sales 
Engineers, notes, “When I interviewed for positions and was not 
familiar with their technology, I used a story about how I had to 
learn two new protocols in a week to perform my first demo.” That 
is far more effective than stating you are a fast learner.
4. Have some common/standard questions ready. Write them down.
a. Why are you looking to hire?
b. What happened to the last person who had this position? If it is 
a new position, why was it created? 
c. Describe the sales cycle.
d. How many reps would I support?
5. Have some common/standard answers ready. Write them down as 
well. 
a. Why are you looking to work for us?
6. Known as a CV, or curriculum vitae (source of life) in British English.

---

## Page 355

334 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 335
b. Why are you in the market right now? Are you seriously 
looking?
c. Have you done x before? Do you know y?
d. Salary range and general requirements (may be illegal in some 
locations).
The Hiring Manager
This is the next call or meeting after you pass through screening. You will 
usually receive one or more intermediate and instructional communications 
to prepare you for this meeting (e.g., presentation/demo requirements). Use 
those calls as an opportunity to conduct interview discovery (i.e., what types 
of questions you may expect). Some companies supply instructional guides. 
Google Cloud will tell you what being “Googley” means and has interviews 
with HR and current employees to help you out!
1. Research the hiring manager as if you were meeting with an 
executive. 
2. Research the technology and market.
a. Who are their customers? Do you know anyone who uses their 
technology? Read some success stories.
b. Scan through a collection of whitepapers. They’ll usually give 
you more detail than standard glossy marketing documents. 
You will also gain insights into common customer pains and 
gains.
3. Is there any demo software available? If you have the equipment, 
play around.
4. Have some common/standard answers available for:
a. More depth on the screening questions.
b. How you build relationships and deal with conflict.
c. Customer success (and failure) stories.
d. Networking.
e. The importance of value versus features.
5. Have some common/standard questions ready. Write them down.
a. Tell me about your team—the good, the bad, and the ugly.
b. How is business this year? Hitting targets? What are those 
targets?
c. What does a successful SE hire look like?

## Page 356

334 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 335
d. Process questions. How many rounds of meetings? How many 
candidates? What is the timeframe? It is fine to ask qualifica -
tion and closing questions.
SE Leadership (Director/VP)
“I’d like you to meet with my boss.” This meeting is usually a good sign 
because the hiring manager wants to hire you but seeks reassurance from 
their management chain. Occasionally, it is a tiebreaker in that the hiring 
manager wants you, and someone else has said no. So what do senior SE 
leaders seek? Lara Meadows, sales engineering VP at a rapidly growing cy -
bersecurity vendor, says, “The biggest things I look for in a candidate are 
personality and a willingness to learn. Are they conversational? Do they 
like to build and tinker (even beyond technology)? Are they going to try to 
solve problems on their own before reaching out for help? And, last but not 
least, are they excited to join our team?” 
The Salespeople
The primary intent of these meetings is for sales to judge “Is this someone 
we can work with and someone who will make us successful?” Focus on 
your network, past successes, or occasions when you have uncovered new 
opportunities or grown a deal. Stories, results, and outcomes will usually 
matter far more than the details on your resume. Usually, the local SE man-
ager offers these interviews to their sales counterparts as a courtesy, so your 
MARS (see Chapter 20) is “do no harm.” 
Hint: Speak with the Other Sales Engineers
Some companies have a conversation with other SEs as part of the interview process. I 
believe that is a valuable step for both sides. The SEs may be in a better position to test 
your technical abilities but are also far more likely to give you some genuine insight into 
working conditions, the relationship with sales, and your potential new boss. So if you are 
interested in a job, ask for an opportunity to speak with other SEs. 
After each one of these interactions, you should use proper follow-up 
etiquette. Contact the interviewer to thank them for their time, supply a 
summary of why they should hire you, and provide any follow-up details 
or documents you may have promised. If you are unsure of how to reach 
someone, you can ask, “What is the best way to follow up with you?” dur-
ing your meeting.

---

## Page 357

336 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 337
Closing the Deal—Moving from Recruit to Attract
It takes both sides to come to an employment agreement. The employer 
wants to proceed to a job offer, and you are at least willing to entertain the 
offer. First, you need to get that offer in writing before you take any action. 
A verbal job offer is dependent on trust and has no legal standing. Second, 
you should respond to acknowledge receipt of the offer within 24 hours. 
Now, take the time to read the complete job offer, including benefits, statu-
tory noncompete requirements, and any conditional tests or documentation 
your new employer might need before you start. Even if you absolutely 
want the job, you should still ask for a few days to evaluate the offer and 
ensure that the position is what you want and that you are doing the right 
thing, especially if you are balancing the timing and contents of competing 
job offers. 
As a potential employee, the balance of power now shifts in your favor, 
so if you choose to negotiate for more compensation or benefits, this is the 
time to take that action. You are joining a sales organization, so it is (in most 
countries) acceptable to negotiate for a better offer. However, you do need to 
decide what happens7 if the employer refuses to change their offer—do you 
accept the position as is, or do you continue your search?
Once you have decided to accept a job offer, you will need to coun -
tersign the job offer, plus an employment agreement and other necessary 
paperwork. Then, you should contact your new manager, express your level 
of excitement, agree on a start date, and ask what you can do to get a head 
start on your onboarding. Congratulations!
Summary
Getting the right person into the right job is just as important for the hiring 
manager as it is for the candidate. There are negative consequences to a poor 
decision on either side. For the manager, it is essential to be realistic about 
the actual job responsibilities and match those to a potential candidate. The 
manager also needs to field-test the candidate to ensure that paper quali -
fications manifest themselves in real life. Once you are sure you have the 
right person, act quickly and get the offer letter to the candidate as soon as 
possible. 
For the candidate, the process is full of unknowns. You must convince 
yourself about the technology, people, growth opportunities, and overall 
7. Known in the negotiating world as best alternative to negotiated agreement 
(BATNA).

---

## Page 358

336 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook The Hiring and Interview Process 337
corporate culture combined with a more than satisfactory compensation 
package. Don’t allow the possibility of working for a hot company with 
stock options to dazzle you so that you see nothing else. If it is the right job, 
then evaluate, negotiate, and ultimately joyfully accept it!
Skill Building
For the candidate: 
 1. Prepare and research. Then research the company and the people some more.
 2. Questions are important—both the ones you ask and the ones you answer.
 3. Ask “Why should I come to work for you and your company?” (Look for the value.)
 4. Y ou are joining a sales organization. Therefore, it is acceptable to demonstrate your sales 
    abilities around discovery , positioning, negotiation, and even closing the deal.
 5. Be clear about your motives. For example, are you leaving a bad situation at your current  
    employer or moving toward something more exciting?
For the hiring SE manager:
 1. Create a job description and actual requirements matrix. 
 2. Build a pipeline of candidates. Always be recruiting.
 3. Construct an SE value proposition. Then, use it in the recruiting process.
 4. Hiring is a team effort, but ultimately your responsibility . Don’t procrastinate.

CHAPTER

Chapter Goals Time Management for the 
Sales Engineer
The bad news is that time flies. The good news is you’re 
the pilot.
            —Michael Altshuler
T
he eternal conflict of the SE is managing their 
limited time against seemingly infinite de -
mands. The precious minutes and hours of your 
days (and nights) are resources against which 
others feel they can write unlimited checks. Yet 
delivering on those demands is the hallmark of 
the cool, calm, and competent professional SE. 
This chapter will supply you with a structure to 
qualify and manage your sales activities. In ad -
dition, you can use the structure to prioritize 
your efforts across opportunities. This approach 
increases the effectiveness of your efforts, lowers 
the stress you feel when looking at your calen -
dar, and certainly improves your reputation for 
getting it done.
CASE STUDY: It Pays to Be Organized
That basic tenet stands behind one of the only 
studies on the cold, hard ROI of being organized. 
Express Employment Professionals, a staffing 
agency, surveyed 18,000 business leaders to learn 
that companywide disorganization cost 57% of 
respondents six working hours per week. Disor -
ganized employees can cost their company more 
Measure where your time 
flies.
Develop a prioritization 
scheme with your sales 
partner and your manager.
Learn time-saving 
strategies.

## Page 361

340 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Time Management for the Sales Engineer 341
than one-fifth their actual salary: $44,000 per year out of their $200,000 an -
nual earnings. Elsewhere, a report by SaaS company MediaValet says employees 
spend 8% of their workdays searching for files and end up wasting time re-
creating between 5% and !5% of their company’s digital assets after giving up 
the hunt.1
Tempus Fugit (Time Flies)
Just as the discovery phase is essential to achieving a successful sales out -
come, discovery is also important for time management. You cannot man -
age your time if you do not know where and when you are spending your 
time. Our survey data from SE leadership workshops shows that only 25% 
of participants ever go back and revisit their calendars for review purposes. 
Instead, they use their calendar as a current event and future planning tool 
rather than providing a retrospective viewpoint. And these are our leaders! 
For an individual SE, it is hard to complain about “I am doing too many 
introductory demos” (that lead to zero business) if you cannot determine 
how much time you are spending on the preparation and delivery of those 
demos.
The most recent Consensus “State of The SE Union” Survey 2 reported 
that the median SE work week was 45 hours. At least 66% of the respon -
dents reported one recent work week of more than 60 hours. It is no sur -
prise that the U.S. Bureau of Labor. 3 makes the following comment about 
our profession, “Sales engineers often work under stressful conditions . . . 
some may work additional and irregular hours to meet sales goals and satisfy 
client needs.”
The first steps are to analyze exactly where you spend your time and 
then match that data against your goals and priorities. Choose three or four 
recent representative weeks as input to the process. I would recommend 
that you print out4 your calendar as it makes the output far more impactful. 
If you are not a calendar person, then for the next three or four weeks, keep 
track of your activities in a diary or log. There are many ways to track the 
data. For example, you can measure it by specific activity, client, salesperson 
or partner, or sales stage. 
1. https://www.mediavalet.com/blog/employee-productivity-report/.
2. See https://www.goconsensus.com/resources#research.
3. See https://www.bls.gov/ooh/sales/sales-engineers.htm.
4. You may be lucky or are already very organized and can auto-generate a report 
from your calendar.

Hint: Beware of Estimates
When estimating your time allocation, pay particular attention to email, customer calls, 
and internal meetings if you do not have hard data. These are the areas where memory is 
unreliable and can result in under-reporting by up to 50%. 
One sample matrix is shown in Table 32.1. This measures activity across 
the sales stage. I would suggest two guidelines. First, to track your time in 
reasonable intervals (either 15 or preferably 30-minute chunks—you are 
not a billable time lawyer), and second not to create too many categories. 
The matrix already has 50 cells—such is the complexity of the role.
There is no perfect time allocation. All you are doing is measuring the 
current state to see some apparent mismatches and areas to investigate. We 
will revisit this matrix when we look at goals and priorities. 
The Distractions, Objectives, and Gains (DOG) Framework
Now we need to decide what is a good use of our time. So, rather than em-
ploy a complicated prioritization scheme or work through the limitations of 
the urgent-important matrix, let me introduce you to DOG theory. 
CASE STUDY: Does It Make the Boat Go Faster?
After consistently failing to reach the finals of any major competitions, in 1998, 
the British Men’s Rowing Eight decided to fundamentally change the way they 
worked and how they worked with each other. Their focus became purely 
about performance, and the results they hoped would follow. As a result, they 
approached things differently, critically asking the same question about every 
single action they undertook: “Will it make the boat go faster?” Along the jour-
ney, they learned the sustainable, dependable techniques that drove continuous 
Table 32.1 
Time Tracking Matrix
Sales Stage > Phase 0 Phase 1 Phase 2 Phase 3 N/A
RFx
Demo preparation
Demo
Discovery
Customer calls/
presentations
POC
Support
Internal meetings
Development/training
Other customer events

## Page 363

342 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Time Management for the Sales Engineer 343
team improvement. The outcome, 18 months later, against all reasonable odds, 
was a stunning Olympic gold medal on the waters of Sydney, Australia. 
 What is your version of “Will it make the boat go faster?” Can you define your 
boat and how fast you are going (and how quickly you would like to go)? 
E E E E
A distraction is something that lies outside of your core processes and 
your mission. It does not make the boat go faster. General examples are 
working trade shows, getting involved in needless escalation requests, at -
tending pointless corporate meetings, and about 50% of the internal virtual 
meetings you attend. Specific examples are going on sales calls because the 
rep needs “technical backup just in case,” responding to RFPs from a strate-
gic account that you will never win, pricing, fixing license and configuration 
issues, and the list goes on.
An objective is something that makes the boat go faster. It is a task that fits 
into your core processes and mission. They are usually obvious in terms of 
day-to-day SE activities. Examples are activities that progress an opportuni-
ty, create a pipeline, and leverage partners. The danger areas are distractions 
disguised as objectives. For example, it’s tempting for a salesperson to label 
every client activity such as a meeting, a demo, or proof of concept as vital 
to closing the deal. That is not always, as we all know from bitter experience, 
the case. Post-sales activities involving customer support, license keys, pro -
fessional services monitoring—all in the name of customer relationships and 
satisfaction—are generally distraction rather than objective based.
The last category of gain is the most interesting. This absolutely makes 
the boat go faster (quite possibly a small fleet of ships). It can be something 
that extends an objective, creates a new targeted objective, gains a competi-
tive advantage, or boosts your career. It often fits into the sudden flash of 
insight “Gee, I wish I’d thought of that” category. If you are honest with 
yourself, you’ll see several every week if you are willing to look for them. 
The separation of the distractions from the objectives can cause you to over-
look the gains. Gains can either be short-term tactical or longer-term strate-
gic. For example, one of my SEs had an objective to find a way to speed up 
the reset and refresh of a demo dataset that 100 of his peers used. He did that 
(saving 100 people a few hours every month), and, as a side benefit, he also 
found a way to build a million-record randomized dataset. He mentioned 
that outcome at a staff meeting. Another SE jumped up, and she explained 
we could use that for a scalability demo (massive competitive advantage) 
and give it to engineering for performance testing. Double gain. 
Not to state the obvious, but I will as we are engineers. Objectives and 
gains are good; distractions are bad. The more time you can move from

---

## Page 364

342 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Time Management for the Sales Engineer 343
distractions, which rarely align with speeding up the boat, to the objectives 
and gains, the more effective you become. It sounds so simple.
Taking the DOG for a Walk
Examine your task and activity list. First, look for small leaks. These are ac-
tivities where you spend a small percentage of time doing something that is 
an actual distraction, or else you are doing it at the wrong time. Examples 
might be time allocated to a POC in phase 0 or RFxes in phase 3. Suppose 
you can save yourself an hour per week. That is a significant payback. Next, 
drill down on the areas where you spend a substantial percentage of time (> 
10% to 15% a week). You are now looking for ruptures where you are los-
ing a significant amount of time. Typically, these are distractions in that you 
are doing the work of some other individual or organization, and therefore 
enabling bad behavior. Remember, just because you can do that work bet -
ter and faster does not mean that you should do it. The SE team is often the 
team of last resort, in that if something needs to happen to help a customer, 
and no one else is doing it, then the SE team will engage because we like to 
make people happy and fix things. 
You should now have a preliminary of activities to examine. Then, re -
alistically, ask yourself the following set of questions (not all may apply) if 
the same action were to reoccur. 
1. What would happen if I don’t do this? 
2. An hour from now, will I feel like this is a good use of my time?
3. Do I really need all this time (meetings/calls and events)? 
4. Will this make the boat go faster?
Hint: It’s Not Just About You.
There is a fifth and more unpleasant question: “What do I do that wastes other people’s 
time without contributing to their effectiveness?” If you genuinely believe in effective time 
management, then the review needs to work in both directions. 
Most of the time and productivity gains come from self-awareness. 
Once you realize where the time is spent and who consumes it, it is easier 
to modify behavior. That behavior change is both yours and that of your 
customers—internal and external. If you are so busy doing “stuff” that you 
don’t have the time to measure, monitor, and modify, start with the obvi-
ous items that make the boat go faster and discard the heavy and unwanted 
boat anchors that slow you down.

---

## Page 365

344 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Time Management for the Sales Engineer 345
Creating a Tactical Time System
So far, this chapter has focused on examining the past and adjusting to en -
sure a better and more efficient future. However, much of time management 
is composed of daily, more tactical decisions. How do you start your day? 
How do you adjust and prioritize events during the day, and how do you 
finish the day, so you are ready for the next day? It’s important to note that 
there is no perfect time management system; what is critical is that you have 
a process to ensure consistency and completion of tasks.
Hint: Setting Work Hours
Set your work hours within your calendar, primarily if you work across multiple time 
zones. By selecting a start time and an end time, you stand a much better chance of keeping 
your time as your time, rather than allowing people to grab an extra 30 or 60 minutes at 
the start or end of your day (which might not be the start or end of their day). 
Starting the Day
Some SEs begin by booting up their laptop and either continuing the previ-
ous day’s last task or (more likely) rummaging through email or Slack chan-
nels, looking for new and exciting things to do. It is part of human nature 
to avoid unpleasant tasks and preferentially act on those that bring us joy. 
However, starting your day with email can make you reactive, as the natu -
ral tendency is to respond only to those customers or salespeople who have 
something urgent for you. This can lead to a frenetic lifestyle, working only 
on the most critical items at any time, with occasional overloads resulting in 
long nights and weekends. (This is known as the heroic efforts approach.)
Time management gurus, such as Craig Jarrow 5—known as the Time 
Management Ninja, recommend starting your day the previous evening, 
which means planning your day before it unfolds. This plan will give you an 
overview of what appointments, calls, and tasks you have, and that way you 
will not be caught off guard. You will know how much free time is on your 
calendar and be aware of any deadlines. Your goal is then to stick to that 
plan as best you can. If I have not already boxed out some time for my spe-
cific tasks (such as writing this book), I do that the night before. Reserving 
time means that my calendar is no longer subject to the whims and vagaries 
of someone else scheduling my time. I usually time box 6 limit each task to 
prevent work from intruding on other activities or my free time. 
I also group similar activities to prevent too much context switching and 
multitasking. For example, I divide my work into multiple categories. And 
5. See http://timemanagementninja.com.
6. Setting a duration, such as no more than 55 minutes.

---

## Page 366

344 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Time Management for the Sales Engineer 345
I have learned that I can waste a lot of time continually switching between 
these categories. Although it is tempting to jam in 15 minutes of adminis -
trative work between two longer and larger business tasks, my error rate 
triples, and it causes me more work in the long run. So, I batch tasks into 
work buckets such as:
• Administrative (expenses, billing, infrastructure);
• Writing (books, articles, blog posts);
• Business development (marketing campaigns, partner events, gen -
eral email);
• Workshop development (creating or updating IP);
• Customer calls and meetings (phone, calls, meetings).
Hint: Using the Calendar
Some calendars have the automatic facility to email a copy of the day’s schedule first thing 
in the morning. With some creative color-coding of events and types of activities, it is a 
physical day-at-a-glance view of your time. 
Running the Day 
Unless you are in an all-day customer meeting, having a perfect day with 
zero interruptions or distractions is a rare occurrence. I do, however, retro-
spectively mark those days on my calendar so I can review why I had such a 
perfect day in the hopes of replicating it. Assuming that your day is already 
mapped out, your primary intent should be to stick to your plan unless 
something else of importance happens. This means deciding precisely what 
is important and how that relates to your other tasks for the day. There is no 
hard and fast prioritization scheme, so look at:
1. Is it customer related?
2. Is there committed or forecast revenue at risk?
3. Is this a directive from your chain of command?
4. What happens if you don’t do this?
5. Can someone else (realistically) do this or assist you?
6. Do you need your manager’s input or guidance?
7. If you take on this new task, what other tasks will be delayed or 
not completed?

---

## Page 367

346 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Time Management for the Sales Engineer 347
CASE STUDY: The Power of Choice
Making decisions about customer priorities is often a tricky task for an SE. It is 
even more challenging for a new-hire SE, as you certainly do not wish to be seen 
as uncooperative or difficult. Sometimes it is easier to force the decision onto the 
requestor, who may be better positioned to make that choice. Charlie, an SE for 
a network infrastructure company, tells their story: “I was asked at the start of 
the week to prepare for two demonstrations on the following Friday, both for 
the same salesperson. I had just enough time to research, practice, and prepare 
for the calls. All good! Then the same rep asked for another demonstration on 
Thursday. As a newbie, I was ready to show my dedication and work through 
the nights, cancel a personal event and reschedule a medical appointment. My 
manager told me I was crazy and needed to have the rep prioritize the meetings. 
I listened in to her conversation with Harry, the salesperson.
 “Harry, Charlie can only prepare and deliver two of these demos, and you 
know there is no one else available. So you need to defer one of them.”
 “I can’t do that. I committed a demo to each of the customers,” he responded.
 “Understood,” replied my manager, “which is the most important?”
 “They are all important!” huffed Harry. 
 “OK,” the manager continued, “which demo should Charlie start working on 
now?”
 “The one for Friday morning,” Harry replied, adding, “and then Thursday is 
critical, too.”
 “Great, then Charlie will focus on those two demos, and you will need to re -
schedule Friday afternoon to next week. Who attends a Friday afternoon demo 
anyway? Charlie, which days are you available next week?”
 It was a marvelous example of reversing the prioritization decision and ensur-
ing that I could deliver three fantastic demos. As a postscript, the demo meeting 
that Harry deferred into the following week was a terrible opportunity. The two 
demos we kept on the schedule both turned into business. Harry did understand 
his priorities. 
E E E E
The final filter is to use the DOG categories. Reviewing what you already 
have scheduled for the day against incoming tasks, how do they rank on the 
DOG scale? Unless the distractions are coming from senior management, 7 
you can discard or defer them for the day. You can also map the incoming 
tasks against your time utilization matrix. If the activity falls into one of the 
boxes you identified as prone to time leaks or rupture, then there is a high 
probability that it is also a distraction. Act accordingly.
7. One group of first-line SE managers reported 7 hours a month wasted on fire 
drills and random distractions.

---

## Page 368

346 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Time Management for the Sales Engineer 347
Hint: Manage Your Downtime
Sales is cyclical, so don’t feel that you are doing something wrong if you have some free 
time on your calendar when the salespeople may be extremely busy. Instead, relax and 
retool. You should have a list of self-improvement items like industry reading, technical 
updates, and training videos to consume that time after taking care of self-maintenance 
like visiting the dentist or optician. After a while, you will learn the cadence of the sales 
cycle and estimate the quarter’s busy and slow times. Weeks 1–8 of the quarter may be 
your busiest as you conduct POCs, demos, and time-critical tasks, while weeks 9–13 may 
be slower as deals move into the pricing and negotiation phase. I’d also recommend hav-
ing either an electronic or physical file of discretionary reading, new technical updates, 
white papers, and corporate policy updates. Then, before a trip or during your downtime, 
take that folder with you and empty it.
End of Day 
Whenever possible, block out 15–30 minutes at the end of the day to close 
out all activities, tidy up notes, send reminders, and plan out the following 
day. If you have been in back-to-back meetings, you will need time to pro-
cess all the to-dos and follow-up items you have assigned. Relying on your 
memory and a few notes the following morning makes for errors and takes 
away from the next day. Next, double-check [three plus one] necessary 
items for the next day.
1. Review all travel plans and conference calls to ensure the logistics 
are complete. For example, a call may be on your calendar with no 
corresponding virtual room.
2. Verify all your presentation and demo materials are complete and 
that you have a backup copy on different media.
3. Mark all tasks that you completed today as done (it supplies dopa-
mine to the engineering brain) and reschedule any items you did 
not complete.
4. The plus one is to have an answer prepared for your spouse, part-
ner, friends, or family when they ask, “How was your day?” Find 
something positive about your day—that they would understand. 
You now revert to “start of day” planning and map out your following 
day. One thing you can be sure of is that no one day is ever like another. 
That is one of the beautiful things about our profession. 
CASE STUDY: A Time for Reinvestment
Stefanie is an amazing SE. She is highly valued by her manager, well-loved by 
the salespeople, commonly requested by the customers, and always invited to 
participate in cross-functional teams. She was that unique mix of a strongly

---

## Page 369

348 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
determined get it done  individual and a collaborative team player. However, as 
the months went by, Stefanie’s workload gradually increased due to the volume 
of her commitments, and both the quality of her work and her relationships suf-
fered. Stefanie’s manager gave her one piece of advice: “It is OK to say no.” Feel-
ing newly empowered, she went through the time and prioritization exercise. 
She realized that not only was she overstretched, but she had also allocated 0% 
of her time over the past four months to learning and development. She was not 
investing in herself. She ruthlessly cut internal meetings and just-in-case sales 
calls from her schedule and blocked out time each week just for herself. The old 
Stefanie reappeared. SEs hate to say no. It is an acquired skill. 
E E E E
Summary
Have a time management system and be as proactive as you can. You are far 
more in control when you set out your activities and time allocation than if 
you allow others to manage your time. You certainly need to react to stake-
holder demands, yet not 100% of the time. I would encourage you to take a 
retrospective view of your calendar and seriously examine where you spend 
time, mapped across multiple factors such as client, salesperson, and general 
activity. The final words on the topic are don’t be afraid to say no. It is only 
by saying no8 that you can complete all the yeses. 
Skill Building
For the New SE: 
 1. Build a structure and process that makes sense to you.
 2. Learn the prioritization scheme of your direct manager and your sales partners.
 3. Take time to view time/resource demands from the requestor’s point of view . 
 4. Practice saying “no”—nicely . 
For the Experienced SE or SE Manager:
 1. Review your past month in detail. Look for leaks and ruptures. (And for good!)
 2. Take the DOG for a long walk.
 3. Block out “me time” for any activity that makes you a better SE. That is not reading  
    an email. 
 4. Watch the people you regard as effective and efficient. Ask questions.8. For some great tips, read Claire Diaz-Ortiz, https://clairediazortiz.com/ 
50-ways-to-say-no/.

CHAPTER

Chapter Goals Managing Y ourself by the 
Metrics
The numbers should speak for themselves, but they 
don’t.
          —Molly Norris Walker
E
very day, SEs must decide how best to dis -
tribute their time to achieve their objectives. 
Managing by the metrics refers to identifying 
the key activities that contribute to success and 
directing employees to prioritize those activi -
ties. Yet it is not just a management task. It is 
essential that the individual SE also uses metrics 
to manage their time, relationships, and career. 
You can use metrics to take some of the heat out 
of emotionally charged decisions and instead ap-
ply logic and facts to those decisions. This chapter 
will cover a basic metrics philosophy for the indi-
vidual SE, how to use metrics to your advantage, 
and close with some of the pitfalls to avoid. 
Some Basic Metrics Philosophy
You should read this chapter and be aware of 
your limitations as you start to examine your 
candidate metrics. Metrics are only helpful if 
they apply to activities within your control or 
Lay out a simple 
mechanism to select 
meaningful metrics.
Understand how to use your 
metrics.
Understand and avoid the 
pitfalls of metrics.

---

## Page 371

350 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Managing Y ourself by the Metrics 351
your influence. For example, measuring your high-level success on whether 
the salespeople you most closely work with meet their targets is within your 
control. Success at a local geographic or country level is probably something 
you can influence, while reaching a global sales target is too lofty a goal for 
an individual SE (except in the smallest of companies). 
If your organization uses metrics to make sensible business decisions 
that run presales as a business, then you already have a head start on this 
process. You can repurpose some of the grander strategic measures for your-
self. Conversely, if your organization does not use metrics, creating your 
own will put you ahead of the game and make you a thought leader. 
I base the metrics approach upon The Balanced Scorecard,1 popularized by 
Robert Kaplan and David Norton back in the 1990s. At its heart is a simple 
tool for looking at strategy (in this case, your strategy) and mapping that into 
key performance indicators (KPI) or metrics. Then, you can subdivide those 
metrics into four categories (see Figure 33.1) or buckets.2 That is where we 
will start. The first bucket is learning and growth (L&G), measuring your skills 
and capabilities. The second bucket is process, which covers your tasks and 
activities. The third bucket is customer, relating to the strength of your rela -
tionships and how your customers act. The final bucket is finance, looking at 
the overall monetary aspects of the job. 
1. Kaplan, R. S., and D. P. Norton, The Balanced Scorecard: Translating Strategy into Ac-
tion, Cambridge, MA: Harvard Business School Press, 1996.
2. Bucket is my nontechnical terminology.
Figure 33.1 The balanced scorecard.

---

## Page 372

350 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Managing Y ourself by the Metrics 351
Getting Started
For each category, make a list of all the metrics that you believe might 
prove helpful. Bear in mind the classic advice “Just because you can mea -
sure something does not mean that you should.” You should be looking 
for a mix of historical metrics (“How did we do last quarter?”), of current 
metrics (“How are things right now?”), and of predictive metrics (“What 
will happen next quarter/year?”). Also, consider three personal and more 
emotional guidelines.
1. Please keep it simple. The easier it is to track and measure, the 
more likely you are to do it. Balance the utility of the metric against 
calculation time.
2. Don’t get intimidated. Some SEs avoid metrics that are difficult to 
achieve or may overwhelm them. Instead, include these metrics as 
they form a massive step in self-improvement. 
3. Do be realistic. Not every metric is 100% quantitative. The value of 
the metric is proportional to your level of honesty and patience. 
Learning and Growth
L&G metrics relate to your skills and capabilities, how well you apply those 
to your job, and the hours you spend not working. In the words of Steve 
McKellar, a former SE operations director, “How do I know if I am any 
good, and how can I get even better?” Some starter ideas are:
1. Technical/product training. Number/hours spent in courses, semi -
nars, or other programs. You can divide these by sources such as 
internal and external. 
2. Business skills/professional training.  Number/hours spent in SE-spe -
cific or industry-specific courses, workshops, or other programs. 
3. Reading. How many books have you read? 
4. Podcasts. How many podcasts have you listened to (that don’t in -
clude general news)?
5. Content created. Original content you created and shared with the 
community.
6. Content delivered. Training/updates delivered. Calculate from a util-
ity point of view (e.g., 1 hour training 10 people is 10 hours con -
sumed).

---

## Page 373

352 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Managing Y ourself by the Metrics 353
7. Sleep.3 How many hours of sleep do you average each night?
8. Feedback/coaching. How much feedback did you receive and pro -
vide? Similarly, for coaching opportunities. You can also track the 
frequency of 1:1 with your manager.
9. Food. The ratio of high-quality lunches versus quick snacks or high-
fat fast food.
Hint: Sleep on It
During one stressful time in my life, I discovered that “get more sleep” was an ineffective 
goal. So I eventually measured the number of nights I was in bed (with no electronics) 
before 10:15 pm.
Process
Process metrics examine how effectively and efficiently you are running 
your small part of the business as a business. Some thoughts to get you 
started are:
1. RFP win rate;
2. POC win rates;
3. Number of demos or sales calls (measuring a pure quantity rarely 
has utility except as a measure of demand);
4. Percentage time on postsales calls;
5. Demo preparation and delivery time;
6. Demo re-use;
7. Solo sales calls (no rep present);
8. The overall percentage of time that is customer-facing (or prepara-
tion for customer-facing);
9. The overall quality of calls, measured on 1–10 or A–F scale;
10. Speed of deal progression through initial stages;
11. Technical win rate;
12. Technical to business win progression, such as days delay or  per -
cent conversion rate;
13. Overall competitive win rate.
3. Level of sleep affects your performance. This has been studied extensively for 
athletes. See https://journals.lww.com/acsm-csmr/fulltext/2017/11000/sleep_
and_athletic_performance.11.aspx.

---

## Page 374

352 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Managing Y ourself by the Metrics 353
Customer
Customer metrics refer to the quality of your relationships with all your 
direct and indirect stakeholders. Direct stakeholders are typically customers, 
partners, and salespeople, and potentially your manager and your peers. 
Indirect stakeholders are everyone else!
1. The number of customer references—personal and professional;
2. Partner engagement—can be training, number of calls, enable -
ment;
3. Trusted advisor T-scores (see Chapter 19);
4. Nontechnical ad hoc customer meetings (casual lunches, tea/cof -
fee breaks);
5. Consumption variations;
6. NPS scores (may be at a higher level);
7. User group/conference attendance;
8. Salesperson satisfaction score (dangerous as can be easily gamed);
9. Various gamification/badge contests. 
Finance
Finance metrics link directly to the monetary aspects of the role. Usually, 
you will have a head start in this area as sales operations can slice and dice 
the forecast and funnel in multiple ways. You may also have your view and 
reports within your CRM system.
1. Generated pipeline—the monetary amount of leads generated;
2. Expenses;
3. Upsell opportunities where you increased the deal size or monthly 
commit;
4. Revenue cross-referenced to geography, industry, product, sales -
person;
5. Return on presales effort (ROPE)—if you had an incremental two 
hours a week, where are they most productively deployed?
6. Achievement of quota and sales targets.
Within each area, my recommendation would be to select two or three 
metrics as a starter set. Then, decide who owns the data (if you don’t), how 
to access it, and set a good target, or range, for the metric. For example, 
you may decide to measure percent of customer-facing time. You own that data 
in your calendar and CRM entries. You also decide that a range of 50% to

---

## Page 375

354 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Managing Y ourself by the Metrics 355
65% for a month is suitable. If you consistently spend more than 65%, you 
may burn out; you are not selling hard enough if it is less than 50%. Your 
final two action items are first to understand what you need to do to remain 
in range and on target, and second, what actions to take if you are outside 
of range. 
Maximizing the Metrics for Personal Gain 
As you run through the metric generation and selection process, you will 
note that some metrics cross over categories, and others bridge the work-
life balance. These metrics are for your personal use, so you do not need to 
share them with others. However, as you seek to optimize your calendar and 
employ time management and prioritization techniques, they can provide 
beneficial insight into decision making. Unfortunately, despite all the tech -
nological advances we are seeing, sales are still one of the least data-driven 
roles in the company. Every year, salesforce publishes a state of sales4 white 
paper. The latest report notes that “57 percent of sales reps are expected 
to miss quota this year, and data-driven sales teams outperform non-data-
driven sales teams by a ton. In fact, reps who are expected to perform at or 
above quota are 1.6 times more likely to be data-driven. Yet only 33 percent 
of sales leaders are leveraging data analysis as their primary means of run -
ning their sales playbook.” An alternative take on the data is that if you can 
take the emotion out of sales decisions, particularly as it relates to time and 
people, and use data instead—you are far more likely to be successful. 
CASE STUDY: The Fix Is In
Valery was one of the most tenured individual SEs in the company. However, he 
grew stale and bored in his current position and was looking for a challenge. “I 
need a metric that will motivate me to learn new things and reinvigorate my zest 
for the profession.” We asked Valery what brought him joy and made him want 
to get out of bed in the morning. He smiled and answered, “Coffee, and fixing 
problems. I am an engineer at heart, and I love to fix things at work and home. 
For my friends, my family, my customers, and even for perfect strangers. I am 
solution driven!” So Valery decided to measure the number of problems he fixed. 
He categorized them into tall, grande, venti, and global. After just the first 6–7 
weeks, Valery could immediately see the number of people he had helped and 
the impact on their lives. For an engineer, not every metric is an obvious one. 
E E E
4. See https://c1.sfdcstatic.com/content/dam/web/en_us/www/documents/reports/ 
/sales/state-of-sales-3rd-ed.pdf.

---

## Page 376

354 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Managing Y ourself by the Metrics 355
It is essential to realize that not everyone is like you. You might like to 
make data-driven decisions. However, many of your sales partners may still 
favor the emotional gut-feel approach, and you will never convince them 
otherwise. That is part of the beauty of the sales + sales engineer partner -
ship—each brings a strength that the other does not possess. If you want 
others to listen to your brain, you will need to listen to their hearts. 
Here Be Dragons (Pitfalls to Avoid)
”Here be dragons” was a phrase allegedly used in the 1700s and earlier by 
cartographers on faraway, uncharted map corners. The intent was to warn 
travelers away from dangerous areas where sea monsters existed. Everyone 
knows the story. However, further research shows that the Latin hic sunt 
dracones is only found on a single globe. Thus, it is a myth about mythologi-
cal creatures. It is also a perfect example of the single data point fallacy, just 
one of the pitfalls to avoid when managing metrics. 
Here are some other typical examples of the downside of working by 
the numbers. 
1. Goodhart’s law. When a measure becomes a target, it ceases to be a 
good measure. The classic example cited is that you will get thou -
sands of tiny nails if you measure people on the number of nails 
they produce. On the other hand, if you measure people on the 
weight of nails they make, you will get a few giant, heavy nails. 
2. Unintended consequences. If you set a 65% customer-facing time tar-
get, and your primary customer for the quarter is a 3-hour plane 
flight away, will that make you reluctant to travel, will you travel 
out of hours, will you do nothing, or will you change your mea -
sure for the quarter? When you set a target against an activity, 
what else might happen due to reaching the target? 
3. Metrics don’t give advice . Metrics are numbers that tell you some -
thing about a current situation, but they do not tell you what you 
should do. The analogy is that of a smoke detector. The alarm may 
sound, but it does not tell you how to escape a fire. 
4. The single data point. When your metric is based on a single or small 
number of data points, you may not have a statistically meaningful 
outcome. This is because some metrics take time to acquire mean-
ing unless you can access accurate historical data.
5. Vanity metrics. Do you measure something just because it makes 
you look and feel good?

---

## Page 377

356 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook
You should also cross-match your metrics against your personal brand 
(they should obviously support that brand) and any metrics your chain-of-
command follows. The final set of questions are to ask precisely why you 
measure something? What is the intended outcome? If you hit the target for 
the metric, what will happen as a result? This is a big picture extension of 
the Law of Unintended Consequences. 
Hint: Copy and Steal Everything (Revisited)
This area is a classic example of seeking out others who use metrics to run their SE busi -
ness within your company and to repurpose any of their exciting metrics. In addition, they 
may already have a track history and can pass on their learnings and outcomes, saving 
you some time and effort. 
Summary
You cannot run your job, or your entire life, based on metrics in a spread -
sheet or a dashboard. Metrics can, however, provide you with some direc -
tion and allow you to answer Steve’s question, “How do I know if I am any 
good, and how can I get even better?”  Building a personal set of metrics, 
whether you have two months, years, or decades of SE experience, will help 
you become an even better SE and will likely improve the work of those 
around you. They will also prove invaluable in performance reviews, team 
reviews, and casual conversations with executives. Their value comes from 
reinforcing something you already knew and giving you a clue into some -
thing unknown. 
Skill Building
For the new SE: 
 1. Build your initial list of metrics. Then, cover each of the four buckets.
 2. Set a target or range for each metric.
 3. Determine your actions when you are on target and off target on each metric.
 4. Specifically , how does each metric make you a better SE?
For the experienced SE or SE Manager:
 1. How do your chosen metrics align with your manager’s and with corporate?
 2. Consider sharing your metrics with friends, family , and peers.
 3. Include at least one health metric (sleep, food, vacation time used).
 4. Metrics are just numbers. How do you gain insight? 
For everyone:
 1. Set aside calendar time to review your progress against the metrics and the continued 
    validity of your chosen metrics.

There are now dozens of companies developing and selling tools to help

money into our market segment, whether it is demo automation, RFP re -
sponse technology, POC trackers, leadership dashboards, CRM add-ins, or 
virtual reality presentation environments. This activity means that there is a 
lot of positive momentum driving the profession forward, and it is changing 
faster than ever before. 
What can you do to support your skills and keep your career on the 
move? I’ll conclude with a few suggestions to help you tie it all together and 
present the proverbial big picture view.
Show Y our Passion
Back in Chapter 1, I noted that hiring managers describe the perfect candi -
date as someone who has a positive attitude, which translates into adapt -
ability. Passionate people attract and inspire other people. Your customers 
are buying the opportunity to improve some aspect of their business (and 
hopefully their lives). If you are passionate about your product, your com -
pany, and their suitability for the project at hand, you are more likely to win 
the deal. That passion needs to shine brightly through in all your verbal and 
nonverbal interactions with the customer. 
Find those products, customers, and companies who excite you. Use 
that passion to light up your coworkers and your customers. Balance that 
with an equal passion and level of engagement in your personal relation -
ships. Pursuing your dream outside of work makes you a more exciting per-
son to be around when you are at work. Passion does not mean devoting 
100% of your time to work, as if you allow that to happen, you will burn 
out—and your friends and family will still have to live with you! 
A high-energy employee is contagious, as you make your environment 
invigorating for others. So be the “and” SE rather than the “but” SE (re-read 
Chapter 6, “Business Value Discovery 1: Pains and Gains”) and do your best 
to make everyone around you just that little bit better.
The Work-Life Balance
Keeping your work and nonwork life in balance is particularly complex 
within a sales environment. You must take care of your inner self. As a tech-

---

## Page 380

358 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Final Words 359
nical analogy, imagine that you are your own operating system. If you do 
not perform upgrades, fail to patch your OS and applications, and neglect 
security, you will eventually crash or be hacked. There is always time for 
your friends, your family, and your pets. 
I would recommend that you share your career and business goals with 
your close friends and loved ones. By doing so, you can jointly agree on 
any sacrifices you make and the support you will provide to each other. 
Even though your friends and family may have no real idea about what 
you do on a day-to-day basis, they are still a fabulous resource to talk things 
through and get advice about interpersonal relationships. 
Always keep your career and the latest quarter in perspective. You are 
working for your manager and, by extension, your company, for your mu-
tual benefit. If you contribute less to the company than they contribute to 
you in the long run, you may be asked to move on. On the other hand, if 
you feel you are adding value beyond your expected roles and responsibili-
ties, build a case that will get you a promotion or a raise. 
Show Y our Time to Value 
Time to Value (TTV) is an essential concept for your customers, as discussed 
in Chapter 21. It is also a necessary concept for your leadership team. If you 
are a new hire SE, the faster you can engage in sales opportunities, build re-
lationships, and offload activities from the experienced SEs, the more value 
you provide. Don’t be passive and reactive, waiting for tasks to arrive while 
you sit behind your desk. Instead, get engaged, seek out work, and try to 
help everyone else be better. 
We have seen through several recent economic downturns that the ex-
ceptional SE never has a problem finding work. There is a direct relation -
ship between value and performance. Always ask if you provide unique 
value to your customers (remember those are classic customers, salespeo -
ple, and partners). As the low-end parts of the SE job become automated, 
your higher-end value lies in creating relationships, complex solutioneer -
ing, and becoming an advocate for your customer. 
Keep performing for your customers and partnering with the salespeo-
ple, and you will be in an excellent position to hit your targets and beat 
your quota. Making your revenue target and helping your customers suc -
ceed are two metrics that define the successful SE and will never go out of 
fashion.

---

## Page 381

360 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Final Words 361
Build the Relationships
You should constantly be expanding your network inside and outside the 
company. If you are friendly and professional and take an interest in advanc-
ing the goals of those around you, you’ll find that a network will naturally 
form when you need help. Sales engineering is a competitive sport, with the 
difference that you want all parts of the sales organization to win. Surround 
yourself with talented people, and you will have the opportunity to learn 
some fantastic skills from them. They will also learn from you, even if this 
is your first week on the job, simply because you have a fresh and different 
perspective. An innocently posed “Tell me why do we do it that way?” can 
be such a powerful question. 
CASE STUDY: My Lesson at Starbucks
As well as being an impromptu meeting place for many of the world’s SEs, Star-
bucks, that ubiquitous coffee house, serves as a powerful reminder of the power 
of listening to your customer and the power of your brand. The MTS team af -
fectionately knows the Newtown, PA, location as the Mastering Technical Sales 
North office. A few years back, I had the opportunity to watch a new-hire ori -
entation session for baristas. I expected most of the day to be spent teaching 
them how to make the hundreds of coffee drinks on offer. Instead, I witnessed 
a focused presentation on the importance of connecting with the customer. The 
major takeaway lessons were to smile, look the customer in the eye and greet 
them, engage the customer if they were unsure about their choice of beverage, 
and make a connection between their name and the drink or some other visual 
(like a baseball cap). This customer-focused training is for employees making 
$15 an hour. Compare that with your take-home salary and draw your conclu-
sions about the importance of building relationships. 
E E E E
Challenge Y ourself and Others
Constantly push yourself by benchmarking your skills against the competi -
tion. As you master one set of skills, look for the next set of skills to install 
and bring onboard your personal operating system. The best SEs, the ones 
who have remained at the top of the field for years, achieve that lofty posi -
tion by never settling for the current state. They avoid complacency and are 
constantly learning and challenging themselves and others. Remember the 
Law of Two Feet: “If at any time you find yourself in a situation where you

---

## Page 382

360 Mastering Technical Sales: The Sales Engineer’s Handbook Final Words 361
are neither learning nor contributing, use your two feet to move someplace 
else where you can learn or contribute.” 
After 34 chapters and more than 150,000 words, my final piece of ad -
vice may be the most important. Smile and enjoy yourself! Sales engineer-
ing is a unique and challenging profession—and you are blessed with one 
of the best jobs in the world if you choose to make it so.
Be FABulous.

About the Author
J
ohn Care  is the managing director of Mastering Technical Sales. The 
company serves the needs of sales engineers across the globe through a 
combination of professional skills enablement, management consulting, and 
keynote speeches. 
John built world-class sales engineering organizations at Oracle, Sybase, 
Business Objects, Nortel, CA Technologies, and HP during his SE career. His 
responsibilities have varied from an individual level up to a vice president 
of presales running organizations of over 200 people. Additionally, he has 
diverse experiences as both a quota-carrying salesperson and a senior IT 
executive/chief information officer listening to salespeople and presales en-
gineers trying to sell him their “solutions.”
To complement Mastering Technical Sales, John has written articles in 
various media ranging from Infoworld and CIO Magazine to the Wall Street 
Journal. He is widely credited with creating the “First Law of Discovery” 
and the original “Demo Crime Files” list in 1995. In addition to this book, 
he published The Trusted Advisor Sales Engineer in July 2016 and Mastering 
T echnical Sales: The Sales Engineer Manager’s Handbook in April 2020. He is 
now working on a murder-mystery novel featuring a sales engineer amateur 
detective. 
John holds a bachelor of science (engineering) degree in chemical engi-
neering from Imperial College, London. In addition, he served on the advi -
sory board of the Fox Business School of Temple University, Philadelphia, as 
a contributor to the International and Executive MBA program. John cur -
rently lives in Longboat Key, Florida, with his wife and various pets.
